<t0>CHAPTER 1</t0>
<t1>It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single man in possession of a good fortune, must be in want of a wife.</t1>
<t2>However, little is known about the feelings or opinions of a man of such conditions when he becomes part of a neighborhood.</t2> <t3>This truth is so ingrained in the minds of some of the families around him, that some consider him their rightful property and others that of their daughters.</t3>
<t4>––My dear Mr. Bennet ––his wife said to him one day––, did you know that Netherfield Park has finally been rented?</t4>
<t5>Mr. Bennet replied no.</t5>
<t6>––Well, that's how it is ––she insisted––; Mrs. Long was here a moment ago and told me everything.</t6>
<t7>Mr. Bennet made no move to reply.</t7>
<t8>––Don't you want to know who rented it? ––his wife grew impatient.</t8>
<t9>––You are the one who wants to tell me, and I have no problem listening to it.</t9>
<t10>This suggestion was enough for him.</t10>
<t11>––Well, you should know, dear, that Mrs. Long says that Netherfield has been rented by a very rich young man from the north of England; that he came on Monday in a four-horse landau to see the place; and that he was so delighted with it that he immediately came to an agreement with Mr. Morris; that he will come to occupy it before Michaelmas; and that some of his servants will be in the house by the end of next week.</t11>
<t12>––What is your name?</t12>
<t13>––Bingley.</t13>
<t14>––Are you married or single?</t14>
<t15>––Oh! Single, dear, of course.</t15> <t16>A single man of great fortune; four or five thousand pounds a year.</t16> <t17>What a good match for our daughters!</t17>
<t18>––And what?</t18> <t19>How can it affect them?</t19>
<t20>––My dear Mr. Bennet ––replied his wife––, how can you be so naive?</t20> <t21>You must know that I am thinking of marrying him to one of them.</t21>
<t22>––Is that the reason that brought you here?</t22>
<t23>––"Reason! Nonsense, how can you say that?"</t23> <t24>"It is very possible that he may fall in love with one of them, and that is why you must go visit him as soon as he arrives."</t24>
<t25>––I don't see the reason for it.</t25> <t26>You can go with the girls or send them alone, which might be better; since you are as pretty as any of them, maybe Mr. Bingley will prefer you.</t26>
<t27>––Dear, you flatter me.</t27> <t28>It is true that at one time I wasn't bad at all, but now I can't pretend to be anything out of the ordinary.</t28> <t29>When a woman has five grown daughters, she must stop thinking about her own beauty.</t29>
<t30>––In such cases, most women don't have much beauty left to think about.</t30>
<t31>––Well, dear, really, you have to go visit Mr. Bingley as soon as he settles in the neighborhood.</t31>
<t32>––I don't guarantee it.</t32>
<t33>––But think of your daughters.</t33> <t34>Realize what a match it would be for one of them.</t34> <t35>Sir William and Lady Lucas are determined to go, and only for that purpose.</t35> <t36>You know they usually don't visit new neighbors.</t36> <t37>Really, you must go, because it will be impossible for us to visit him if you don't.</t37>
<t38>––You are too modest.</t38> <t39>I am sure that Mr. Bingley will be very glad to see you; and you will take him a few lines from me to assure him that he has my most sincere consent to marry one of them; although I will put in a good word for my little Lizzy.</t39>
<t40>––I refuse to let you do such a thing.</t40> <t41>Lizzy is no better than the others, she is not half as pretty as Jane, nor half as cheerful as Lydia.</t41> <t42>But you always prefer her.</t42>
<t43>––None of the three are very recommendable ––he replied––.</t43> <t44>They are as silly and ignorant as the other girls; but Lizzy has a bit more sharpness than her sisters.</t44>
<t45>––Mr. Bennet! How can you speak like that about your daughters?</t45> <t46>You love to upset me.</t46> <t47>You have no compassion for my poor nerves.</t47>
<t48>––You are mistaken, dear.</t48> <t49>I have a lot of respect for your nerves.</t49> <t50>They are old friends of mine.</t50> <t51>For at least twenty years, I have heard you mention them with much consideration.</t51>
<t52>––You don't know how much I suffer!</t52>
<t53>––But you will get well and live to see many young men with four thousand pounds a year come to this place.</t53>
<t54>––It would be useless if those twenty young people came and you didn't go to visit them.</t54>
<t55>––If it depends on that, dear, as soon as the twenty are here, I will visit them all.</t55>
<t56>Mr. Bennet was such a rare mixture of witty, sarcastic, reserved, and capricious that twenty-three years of experience had not been enough for his wife to understand his character.</t56> <t57>However, hers was less difficult; she was a woman of little intelligence, rather uneducated, and of uneven temperament.</t57> <t58>Her goal in life was to marry off her daughters; her consolation, visits and gossip.</t58>
<t59>CHAPTER II</t59>
<t60>Mr. Bennet was one of the first to pay his respects to Mr. Bingley.</t60> <t61>He always intended to visit him, although, in the end, he always assured his wife that he would not; and until the evening after his visit, his wife knew nothing about it.</t61> <t62>The matter came to light in the following way: observing how his daughter was putting on a hat, Mr. Bennet said:</t62>
<t63>––I hope Mr. Bingley likes it, Lizzy.</t63>
<t64>––How can we know what Mr. Bingley likes ––said his resentful wife–– if we haven't visited him yet?</t64>
<t65>––You forget, mom ––said Elizabeth–– that we will see him at the parties, and that Mrs. Long has promised to introduce him to us.</t65>
<t66>––I don't believe Mrs. Long would do such a thing.</t66> <t67>She has two nieces to think about; she is selfish and hypocritical and does not deserve my trust.</t67>
<t68>––Nor is mine ––said Mr. Bennet–– and I am glad to know that you do not rely on her services.</t68> <t69>Mrs. Bennet did not deign to reply; but unable to contain herself, she began to scold one of her daughters.</t69>
<t70>––For the love of God, Kitty, stop coughing like that!</t70> <t71>Have mercy on my nerves.</t71> <t72>You are destroying them.</t72>
<t73>––Kitty is not at all discreet when coughing ––said her father––.</t73> <t74>She always does it at an inopportune moment.</t74>
<t75>––I don't find coughing amusing ––complained Kitty.</t75>
<t76>––When is your next dance, Lizzy?</t76>
<t77>––In fifteen days from tomorrow.</t77>
<t78>––Yes, that's right ––exclaimed the mother––.</t78> <t79>And Mrs. Long won't return until the day before; so it will be impossible for her to introduce us to Mr. Bingley, because she still won't know him.</t79>
<t80>––Then, Mrs. Bennet, you can get ahead of your friend and introduce him to her yourself.</t80>
<t81>––Impossible, Mr. Bennet, impossible, when I don't know him either.</t81> <t82>Why are you mocking me?</t82>
<t83>––I commend your discretion.</t83> <t84>A friendship of fifteen days is truly very short.</t84> <t85>In reality, after only two weeks, one cannot really know what kind of man he is.</t85> <t86>But if we do not take the risk, others will.</t86> <t87>After all, Mrs. Long and her nieces can wait for their opportunity to present itself; but, nevertheless, as she will believe it to be an act of delicacy on her part to decline the attention, I will be the one to introduce him to you.</t87>
<t88>The girls stared at their father intently.</t88> <t89>Mrs. Bennet simply said:</t89>
<t90>––Nonsense, nonsense!</t90>
<t91>"––What does that emphatic exclamation mean? ––asked Mr. Bennet––."</t91> <t92>"Do you consider the formalities of introduction as nonsense, given their importance?"</t92> <t93>"I do not agree with you on that."</t93> <t94>"What do you say, Mary?"</t94> <t95>"I know that you are a very thoughtful young woman, and that you read great books and summarize them."</t95>
<t96>Mary wanted to say something sensible, but she didn't know how.</t96>
<t97>––While Mary clarifies her thoughts ––he continued––, let's return to Mr. Bingley.</t97>
<t98>––I'm fed up with Mr. Bingley! ––shouted his wife.</t98>
<t99>––I'm very sorry to hear that; why didn't you tell me earlier?</t99> <t100>If I had known this morning, I wouldn't have gone to their house.</t100> <t101>Bad luck!</t101> <t102>But since I have already visited them, we cannot give up their friendship now.</t102>
<t103>The astonishment of the ladies was precisely what he desired; perhaps Mrs. Bennet's surpassed the rest; although once the commotion caused by the joy was over, she declared that deep down it was what she had always imagined.</t103>
<t104>––My dear Mr. Bennet, how good you are!</t104> <t105>But I knew that in the end I would convince you.</t105> <t106>I was sure that you love your daughters enough not to neglect this matter.</t106> <t107>How happy I am!</t107> <t108>And what a funny joke, that you went this morning and didn't tell us anything until now!</t108>
<t109>––Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you want ––said Mr. Bennet; and he left the room, tired by his wife's enthusiasm.</t109>
<t110>––What an excellent father you have, daughters! ––she said once the door was closed––.</t110> <t111>I don't know how you will ever be able to thank him for his kindness, nor do I, for that matter.</t111> <t112>At this point, I assure you that it is not pleasant to make new acquaintances every day.</t112> <t113>But for you, we would do anything.</t113> <t114>Lydia, dear, even though you are the youngest, I would bet that Mr. Bingley will dance with you at the next ball.</t114>
<t115>––I am calm ––said Lydia firmly––, because although I am the youngest, I am the tallest.</t115>
<t116>The rest of the afternoon was spent making guesses about whether Mr. Bingley would soon return Mr. Bennet's visit, and deciding when they could invite him to dinner.</t116>
<t117>CHAPTER III</t117>
<t118>No matter how much Mrs. Bennet, with the help of her daughters, inquired about the matter, she could not get any satisfactory description of Mr. Bingley from her husband.</t118> <t119>They attacked him in various ways: with very clear questions, clever suppositions, and hints; but no matter how skillful they were, he evaded them all.</t119> <t120>And in the end, they were forced to accept second-hand information from their neighbor Lady Lucas.</t120> <t121>Her impression was very favorable, Sir William had been delighted with him.</t121> <t122>He was young, very handsome, extremely pleasant, and to top it off, he planned to attend the next ball with a group of friends.</t122> <t123>There could be nothing better.</t123> <t124>His fondness for dancing was truly an advantage when it came to falling in love; and thus, lively hopes were kindled to win Mr. Bingley's heart.</t124>
<t125>––If I could see one of my daughters living happily in Netherfield, and the others equally well married, I would desire nothing more in life––said Mrs. Bennet to her husband.</t125>
<t126>A few days later, Mr. Bingley returned Mr. Bennet's visit and spent ten minutes with him in his library.</t126> <t127>He had hoped to be allowed to see the young ladies whose beauty he had heard so much about; but he saw no one but the father.</t127> <t128>The ladies were a bit more fortunate, as they had the advantage of being able to observe from a high window that Mr. Bingley wore a blue coat and rode a black horse.</t128>
<t129>Shortly afterwards, they sent him an invitation to come to dinner.</t129> <t130>And when Mrs. Bennet had already planned the dishes that would credit her good housekeeping, they received a response that ruined everything.</t130> <t131>Mr. Bingley was obliged to go to town the next day, and consequently could not accept the honor of their invitation.</t131> <t132>Mrs. Bennet was quite disconcerted.</t132> <t133>She could not imagine what business called him to town so soon after his arrival in Hertfordshire; and she began to fear that he would always be flitting about without settling down properly and as expected at Netherfield.</t133> <t134>Lady Lucas somewhat allayed her fears by concluding that he was only going to London to gather a group of friends for the party.</t134> <t135>And soon the rumor spread that Bingley was going to bring twelve ladies and seven gentlemen for the ball.</t135> <t136>The girls were distressed by such a number of ladies; but the day before the ball, they were consoled to hear that instead of twelve, he had brought only six, five sisters and a cousin.</t136> <t137>And when the day of the ball arrived and they entered the hall, there were only five in total: Mr. Bingley, his two sisters, the husband of the eldest, and another young man.</t137>
<t138>Mr. Bingley was handsome, had the appearance of a gentleman, a pleasant countenance, and simple, unaffected manners.</t138> <t139>His sisters were beautiful women of undeniable elegance.</t139> <t140>His brother-in-law, Mr. Hurst, hardly had the appearance of a gentleman; but it was his friend Mr. Darcy who soon drew the attention of the room with his distinguished personality. He was a tall man, with handsome features and an aristocratic bearing.</t140> <t141>A few minutes after his entrance, a rumor was already circulating that his income was ten thousand pounds a year.</t141> <t142>The gentlemen declared that he was a man of great class; the ladies said he was much more handsome than Bingley, and he was admired for almost half the evening, until his manners caused such displeasure that they changed the course of his good reputation; it was discovered that he was a proud man, who pretended to be above everyone else and showed his dissatisfaction with the environment around him; not even his extensive estates in Derbyshire could save him from appearing odious and unpleasant, and from being considered worthless compared to his friend.</t142>
<t143>Mr. Bingley immediately made friends with the main people in the room; he was lively and frank, did not miss a single dance, regretted that the party ended so early, and spoke of giving one himself at Netherfield.</t143> <t144>Such pleasant qualities spoke for themselves.</t144> <t145>What a difference between him and his friend!</t145> <t146>Mr. Darcy danced only once with Mrs. Hurst and once with Miss Bingley, refused to be introduced to any other lady, and spent the rest of the night wandering around the room and occasionally talking to some of his companions.</t146> <t147>His character was definitively judged.</t147> <t148>He was the proudest and most disagreeable man in the world, and everyone hoped he would not return there again.</t148> <t149>Among those most offended by Darcy was Mrs. Bennet, whose displeasure at his behavior had intensified, turning into a personal offense for having slighted one of her daughters.</t149>
<t150>There were so few gentlemen that Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged to sit down during two dances; during that time, Darcy was close enough to her for the young lady to hear a conversation between him and Mr. Bingley, who left the dance for a few minutes to persuade his friend to join them.</t150>
<t151>––Come, Darcy ––she said to him––, you have to dance.</t151> <t152>I can't stand seeing you standing there alone with that stupid attitude.</t152> <t153>It's better if you dance.</t153>
<t154>––I don't intend to do it.</t154> <t155>You know how much I hate it, unless I personally know my partner.</t155> <t156>At a party like this, it would be impossible for me.</t156> <t157>Your sisters are engaged, and dancing with any other woman in this room would be like a punishment for me.</t157>
<t158>––You shouldn't be so demanding and fussy ––complained Bingley––.</t158> <t159>For heaven's sake!</t159> <t160>Honestly, I've never seen so many charming girls as tonight; and there are some who are especially pretty.</t160>
<t161>––You are dancing with the only pretty girl in the room ––said Mr. Darcy, looking at the eldest of the Bennet sisters.</t161>
<t162>––Oh!</t162> <t163>She is the most beautiful creature I have ever seen in my life!</t163> <t164>But right behind you is sitting one of her sisters who is very pretty and I would bet very nice.</t164> <t165>Let me ask my partner to introduce you to her.</t165>
<t166>––What are you saying? ––and, turning around, he looked at Elizabeth for a moment, until their gazes met, he immediately looked away and said coldly: ––She is not bad, although she is not pretty enough to tempt me; and I am not in the mood to pay attention to young women who have been turned down by others.</t166> <t167>It is better that you return to your partner and enjoy their smiles because you are wasting your time with me.</t167>
<t168>Mr. Bingley followed his advice.</t168> <t169>Mr. Darcy walked away; and Elizabeth was left there with her not very cordial feelings towards him.</t169> <t170>However, she recounted the story to her friends with much humor because it was funny and very cheerful, and she had a certain disposition to make ridiculous things amusing.</t170>
<t171>In short, the evening passed pleasantly for the whole family.</t171> <t172>Mrs. Bennet saw how her eldest daughter had been admired by those from Netherfield.</t172> <t173>Mr. Bingley had danced with her twice, and his sisters were very attentive to her.</t173> <t174>Jane was as satisfied as, if not more than, her mother, but she kept it to herself.</t174> <t175>Elizabeth was happy for Jane.</t175> <t176>Mary had heard Miss Bingley say that she was the most cultured girl in the neighborhood.</t176> <t177>And Catherine and Lydia had the good fortune of never being without a partner, which, as they had been taught, was the only thing they should worry about at dances.</t177> <t178>So they returned happily to Longbourn, the village where they lived and of which they were the principal inhabitants.</t178> <t179>They found Mr. Bennet still up; with a book in front of him, he lost track of time; and on this occasion, he was very curious about the events of the night that had aroused so much expectation.</t179> <t180>He came to believe that his wife's opinion of the newcomer might be unfavorable; but he soon realized that what he was about to hear was quite the opposite.</t180>
<t181>––Oh, my dear Mr. Bennet ––said his wife as she entered the room––.</t181> <t182>We had a delightful evening, the ball was splendid.</t182> <t183>I would have liked you to be there.</t183> <t184>Jane aroused such admiration, nothing like it had ever been seen.</t184> <t185>Everyone was commenting on how beautiful she looked, and Mr. Bingley found her gorgeous and danced with her twice.</t185> <t186>Just imagine, dear; he danced with her twice.</t186> <t187>She was the only one in the entire hall he asked to dance a second time.</t187> <t188>The first one he asked was Miss Lucas.</t188> <t189>It quite upset me to see him dance with her, but he didn't like it at all.</t189> <t190>Who could like her, don't you think?</t190> <t191>However, he seemed to be captivated by Jane when he saw her dance.</t191> <t192>So he asked who she was, they introduced her to him, and he asked her for the next dance.</t192> <t193>Then he danced the third with Miss King, the fourth with Maria Lucas, the fifth again with Jane, the sixth with Lizzy, and the boulanger...</t193>
<t194>––If she had had any compassion for me ––shouted the impatient husband–– she wouldn't have spent so much!</t194> <t195>For the love of God, don't talk to me anymore about her partners!</t195> <t196>I wish she had twisted an ankle at the first dance!</t196>
<t197>––Oh, my dear!</t197> <t198>He has me fascinated, he is incredibly handsome, and his sisters are charming.</t198> <t199>They wore the most elegant dresses I have ever seen in my life.</t199> <t200>The lace on Mrs. Hurst's...</t200>
<t201>Here she was interrupted again.</t201> <t202>Mr. Bennet protested against any description of attire.</t202> <t203>Therefore, she was forced to move on to another chapter of the story, and recounted, with great bitterness and some exaggeration, the scandalous rudeness of Mr. Darcy.</t203>
<t204>––But I can assure you ––he added–– that Lizzy doesn't lose much by not being his type, because he is the most unpleasant and horrible man that exists, and he doesn't deserve anyone's sympathy.</t204> <t205>He is so stiff and so conceited that there is no way to tolerate him.</t205> <t206>He did nothing but strut from one side to the other like a peacock.</t206> <t207>He's not even handsome enough to be worth dancing with.</t207> <t208>I would have liked you to have been there and given him a good lesson.</t208> <t209>I detest him.</t209>
<t210>CHAPTER IV</t210>
<t211>When Jane and Elizabeth were alone, the former, who had been cautious in praising Mr. Bingley, expressed to her sister how much she admired him.</t211>
<t212>––He is everything a young man should be ––she said––, sensible, cheerful, with a sense of humor; I had never seen such relaxed manners, so much naturalness with such perfect education.</t212>
<t213>––And he is also handsome ––replied Elizabeth––, which is never a bad thing in a young man.</t213> <t214>So he is a complete man.</t214>
<t215>––I felt very flattered when he asked me to dance for the second time.</t215> <t216>I didn't expect such a compliment.</t216>
<t217>––Didn't you expect it?</t217> <t218>I did.</t218> <t219>That is the big difference between us.</t219> <t220>Compliments always catch you by surprise, but never me.</t220> <t221>It was the most natural thing for him to ask you to dance for the second time.</t221> <t222>It couldn't have escaped his notice that you were five times more beautiful than all the other women in the room.</t222> <t223>Don't thank him for his gallantry because of that.</t223> <t224>Well, the truth is that he is very pleasant, I approve of you liking him.</t224> <t225>You have liked many stupid people.</t225>
<t226>––Lizzy, dear!</t226>
<t227>––Oh! You know perfectly well that you have a certain tendency to like everyone.</t227> <t228>You never see a flaw in anyone.</t228> <t229>Everyone is good and pleasant in your eyes.</t229> <t230>I have never heard you speak ill of a human being in my life.</t230>
<t231>––I would not want to be imprudent in criticizing someone; but I always say what I think.</t231>
<t232>––I already know; and that's what makes it amazing.</t232> <t233>To be so blind to the follies and foolishness of others, with the good sense you have.</t233> <t234>Pretending candor is quite common, you see it everywhere.</t234> <t235>But to be candid without ostentation or premeditation, to take the good from everyone, improve it even, and say nothing of the bad, only you do that.</t235> <t236>And you also like his sisters, don't you?</t236> <t237>Their manners are nothing like his.</t237>
<t238>––At first, of course not, but when you talk to them, they are very kind.</t238> <t239>Miss Bingley is going to come live with her brother and take care of his house.</t239> <t240>And, unless I am very much mistaken, I am sure we will find in her a charming neighbor.</t240>
<t241>Elizabeth listened in silence, but she was not convinced.</t241> <t242>The behavior of Bingley's sisters had not been intended to please anyone.</t242> <t243>A better observer than her sister, with a less flexible temperament and a judgment less prone to being influenced by flattery, Elizabeth was unwilling to approve of the Bingleys.</t243> <t244>They were, indeed, very fine ladies, quite cheerful when not contradicted and, when they wanted to be, very pleasant; but proud and conceited.</t244> <t245>They were quite pretty; they had been educated at one of the best schools in the capital and possessed a fortune of twenty thousand pounds; they were accustomed to overspending and associating with people of rank, which made them believe they had the right to hold a good opinion of themselves and a poor opinion of others.</t245> <t246>They belonged to an honorable family from the north of England, a fact that was more deeply ingrained in their memory than the fact that both their fortune and their brother's had been made in trade.</t246>
<t247>Mr. Bingley inherited nearly one hundred thousand pounds from his father, who had already intended to buy a mansion but did not live to do so.</t247> <t248>Mr. Bingley thought the same way and sometimes seemed determined to make the choice within his county; but as he now had a good house and the freedom of a property owner, those who knew his calm character well doubted that he would not spend the rest of his days in Netherfield and leave the purchase for the next generation.</t248>
<t249>His sisters were eager for him to have a mansion of his own.</t249> <t250>But although he was currently nothing more than a tenant, Miss Bingley was still eager to preside over his table; nor was Mrs. Hurst, who had married a man more elegant than wealthy, any less willing to consider her brother's house as her own whenever it suited her.</t250>
<t251>Two short years after Mr. Bingley had come of age, a casual recommendation induced him to visit the Netherfield estate.</t251> <t252>He viewed it inside and out for half an hour and was satisfied with the owner's praises, renting it immediately.</t252>
<t253>Between him and Darcy, there existed a firm friendship despite having such opposite characters.</t253> <t254>Bingley had gained Darcy's sympathy due to his open and docile temperament and his naturalness, even though there was no way of being that offered a greater contrast to his own, and even though he seemed very satisfied with his character.</t254> <t255>Bingley knew the respect that Darcy had for him, which is why he trusted him completely, as well as his good judgment.</t255> <t256>He understood Darcy like no one else.</t256> <t257>Bingley was not at all foolish, but Darcy was much more intelligent.</t257> <t258>He was at the same time arrogant, reserved, and picky, and although he was very polite, his manners did not make him at all attractive.</t258> <t259>In this regard, his friend had all the advantage; Bingley was sure to be liked wherever he went, whereas Darcy was always offensive.</t259>
<t260>The best example is the way they talked about the Meryton party.</t260> <t261>Bingley had never met more charming people or prettier girls in his life; everyone had been extremely kind and attentive to him, there had been no formalities or stiffness, and he soon became friends with the whole room; and as for Miss Bennet, he couldn't conceive of an angel who was more beautiful.</t261> <t262>On the contrary, Darcy had seen a collection of people in whom there was little beauty and no elegance, he had felt the slightest interest in none of them, and had received no attention or pleasure from any of them.</t262> <t263>He acknowledged that Miss Bennet was beautiful, but she smiled too much.</t263> <t264>Mrs. Hurst and her sister admitted it, but still liked and admired her, they said she was a very sweet girl and that they would not mind getting to know her better.</t264> <t265>It was established, then, that Miss Bennet was a very sweet girl and for this reason the brother felt authorized to think of her as and when he wanted.</t265>
<t266>CHAPTER V</t266>
<t267>A short distance from Longbourn lived a family with whom the Bennets had a special friendship.</t267> <t268>Sir William Lucas had previously been in business in Meryton, where he had made a modest fortune and had been elevated to the rank of knight by petition to the king during his mayoralty.</t268> <t269>This distinction had gone a bit to his head, and he began to dislike having to engage in business and live in a small commercial town; so, leaving both behind, he moved with his family to a house a mile from Meryton, henceforth called Lucas Lodge, where he could indulge in thinking with pleasure about his own importance, and, detached from his business, occupy himself solely with being kind to everyone.</t269> <t270>For although he was proud of his rank, he had not become conceited; on the contrary, he was all attentiveness to everyone.</t270> <t271>Of an inoffensive, sociable, and helpful nature, his presentation at St. James had also made him courteous.</t271>
<t272>Mrs. Lucas was a good woman, although not intelligent enough for Mrs. Bennet to consider her a valuable neighbor.</t272> <t273>They had several children.</t273> <t274>The eldest, an intelligent and sensible young woman of about twenty years, was Elizabeth's close friend.</t274>
<t275>That the Lucases and the Bennets should gather to chat after a ball was absolutely necessary, and the morning after the party, the Lucases went to Longbourn to exchange impressions.</t275>
<t276>––You started the night well, Charlotte ––said Mrs. Bennet, feigning all possible kindness towards Miss Lucas––.</t276> <t277>You were the first one Mr. Bingley chose.</t277>
<t278>––Yes, but she seemed to like the second one more.</t278>
<t279>––Oh!</t279> <t280>You mean Jane, I suppose, because he danced with her twice.</t280> <t281>Yes, it seems he liked her; yes, I think so.</t281> <t282>I heard something, I don't know, something about Mr. Robinson.</t282>
<t283>––Perhaps it refers to what I heard between him and Mr. Robinson, haven't I told you?</t283> <t284>Mr. Robinson asked him if he liked the Meryton parties, if he didn't think there were very beautiful girls in the hall, and which one seemed the prettiest of all to him.</t284> <t285>His answer to this last question was immediate: "The eldest of the Bennets, without a doubt.</t285> <t286>There can be only one opinion on that matter."</t286>
<t287>––Don't tell me!</t287> <t288>It seems determined to...</t288> <t289>It's as if...</t289> <t290>But, in the end, everything could come to nothing.</t290>
<t291>––What I heard was better than what you heard, right, Elizabeth? ––said Charlotte––.</t291> <t292>It's more worthwhile to listen to Mr. Bingley than to Mr. Darcy, don't you think? Poor Eliza!</t292> <t293>To only say: "Not bad.</t293> <t294>"</t294>
<t295>––I beg you not to put it into Lizzy's head to be upset about Darcy.</t295> <t296>He is such an unpleasant man that it would be a misfortune to like him.</t296> <t297>Mrs. Long told me that he had been sitting next to her and hadn't opened his mouth.</t297>
<t298>––Are you sure, Mom? Aren't you mistaken?</t298> <t299>I saw Mr. Darcy talking to her.</t299>
<t300>––Yes, of course; because she eventually asked him if he liked Netherfield, and he had no choice but to answer; but Mrs. Long said that he was not at all pleased that she spoke to him.</t300>
<t301>––Miss Bingley told me ––commented Jane that he did not usually talk much, except with his close friends.</t301> <t302>With them, he is incredibly pleasant.</t302>
<t303>––I don't believe a word of it, dear.</t303> <t304>If he were so pleasant, he would have talked to Mrs. Long.</t304> <t305>But I can already imagine what happened.</t305> <t306>Everyone says that his pride knows no bounds, and I would bet that he heard that Mrs. Long doesn't have a carriage and that she went to the ball in a rented one.</t306>
<t307>––I don't mind that he didn't speak with Mrs. Long ––said Miss Lucas––, but I wish he had danced with Eliza.</t307>
<t308>––If I were you, Lizzy ––added the mother––, I would never dance with him again.</t308>
<t309>––I believe, mom, that I can promise you that I will never dance with him.</t309>
<t310>––Pride ––said Miss Lucas–– always offends, but his pride doesn't seem so offensive to me.</t310> <t311>He has an excuse.</t311> <t312>It is natural for an attractive man, with family, fortune, and everything in his favor to have a high opinion of himself.</t312> <t313>In a way, he has the right to be proud.</t313>
<t314>––It is very true ––replied Elizabeth––, I could easily forgive his pride if he had not mortified mine.</t314>
<t315>––Pride ––observed Mary, who prided herself greatly on the solidity of her reflections––, is a very common defect.</t315> <t316>From all that I have read, I am convinced that it is indeed very frequent for human nature to be especially prone to it; there are very few who do not harbor a feeling of self-sufficiency for one reason or another, whether real or imagined.</t316> <t317>Vanity and pride are different things, although they are often used as synonyms.</t317> <t318>Pride relates to the opinion we have of ourselves; vanity, to what we would like others to think of us.</t318>
<t319>––If I were as rich as Mr. Darcy, exclaimed a young Lucas who had come with his sisters––, I wouldn't mind being proud.</t319> <t320>I would have a pack of hunting dogs, and I would drink a bottle of wine a day.</t320>
<t321>––Well, you would drink much more than you should ––said Mrs. Bennet–– and if I saw you, I would take the bottle away from you immediately.</t321>
<t322>The boy said he wouldn't dare, she said she would, and so they kept arguing until the visit was concluded.</t322>
<t323>CHAPTER VI</t323>
<t324>The ladies of Longbourn soon went to visit those of Netherfield, and the latter returned the visit as is customary.</t324> <t325>The charm of Miss Bennet increased the esteem that Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley felt for her; and although they found the mother intolerable and not worth speaking to the younger sisters, they expressed a desire to deepen their relations with them out of regard for the two eldest.</t325> <t326>This attention was received by Jane with pleasure, but Elizabeth continued to see arrogance in their treatment of everyone, except, with reservations, her sister; she could not like them.</t326> <t327>Although she valued their kindness to Jane, she knew it was probably due to the influence of the admiration the brother felt for her.</t327> <t328>It was evident, wherever they met, that Bingley admired Jane; and it was also evident to Elizabeth that in her sister increased the inclination she felt for him from the beginning, which predisposed her to fall in love with him; but she realized, with great satisfaction, that people could not notice it, since Jane would combine the strength of her feelings with moderation and a constant joviality, which would drive away the suspicions of the impertinent.</t328> <t329>She commented this to her friend, Miss Lucas.</t329>
<t330>––Perhaps it is better in this case ––replied Charlotte–– to be able to escape people's curiosity; but sometimes it is bad to be so reserved.</t330> <t331>If a woman conceals her affection for its object, she may lose the opportunity to win him over; and then it is a poor consolation to think that others are equally ignorant.</t331> <t332>There is so much gratitude and vanity in almost all affections that it is not at all convenient to leave them adrift.</t332> <t333>Normally we all start with a slight preference, and that can simply be for no reason; but there are very few who have enough heart to fall in love without having been encouraged.</t333> <t334>In nine out of ten cases, a woman must show more affection than she feels.</t334> <t335>Bingley likes your sister, undoubtedly; but if she does not help him, it will go no further.</t335>
<t336>––She helps him as much as his nature allows.</t336> <t337>If I can notice her affection towards him, he, certainly, would be foolish not to discover it.</t337>
<t338>––Remember, Eliza, that he does not know Jane's character as you do.</t338>
<t339>––But if a woman is interested in a man and does not try to hide it, he will eventually have to find out.</t339>
<t340>––Maybe so, if he sees her enough.</t340> <t341>But although Bingley and Jane are often together, it is never for long; and besides, since they only see each other at crowded parties, they cannot talk alone.</t341> <t342>So Jane should make the most of every minute she can catch his attention.</t342> <t343>And when she has him for sure, she will have time––to fall in love with him as much as she wants.</t343>
<t344>––Your plan is good ––replied Elizabeth––, when the matter is only about marrying well; and if I were determined to get a rich husband, or any husband, I can almost say that I would carry it out.</t344> <t345>But those are not Jane's feelings, she does not act with premeditation.</t345> <t346>She still cannot be sure to what extent she likes him, nor why.</t346> <t347>She has only known him for fifteen days.</t347> <t348>She danced with him four times in Meryton; saw him one morning at her house, and since then has dined in his company four times.</t348> <t349>This is not enough for her to know his character.</t349>
<t350>––Not as you put it.</t350> <t351>If I had only had dinner with him, I wouldn't have discovered anything other than whether he has a good appetite or not; but you mustn't forget that they spent four evenings together; and four evenings can mean quite a lot.</t351>
<t352>––Yes; in those four evenings, all they could do was find out what kind of dances each one liked, but I don't think they were able to discover the really important things about their character.</t352>
<t353>––Well ––said Charlotte––.</t353> <t354>I wholeheartedly wish that things go well for Jane; and if she were to marry him tomorrow, I believe she would have a better chance of being happy than if she spends twelve months studying his character.</t354> <t355>Happiness in marriage is only a matter of luck.</t355> <t356>The belief that a couple is equal or knows each other well beforehand is not going to bring them happiness at all.</t356> <t357>The differences become more and more pronounced until they become unbearable; it is always better to know as little as possible about the person with whom you are going to share your life.</t357>
<t358>––You make me laugh, Charlotte; it doesn't make sense.</t358> <t359>You know it doesn't make sense; besides, you would never act that way.</t359>
<t360>Occupied with observing Bingley's attentions towards her sister, Elizabeth was far from suspecting that she was also the object of interest in the eyes of Bingley's friend.</t360> <t361>At first, Mr. Darcy barely deigned to admit that she was pretty; he had shown no admiration for her at the ball; and the next time they met, he only noticed her to criticize her.</t361> <t362>But as soon as he made it clear to himself and to his friends that he barely liked her facial features, he began to realize that the beautiful expression of her dark eyes gave her an air of extraordinary intelligence.</t362> <t363>This discovery was followed by others equally mortifying.</t363> <t364>Although he detected with a critical eye more than one flaw in the perfect symmetry of her form, he had to acknowledge that her figure was graceful and slender; and despite claiming that her manners were not those of refined people, he felt attracted to her naturalness and cheerfulness.</t364> <t365>She had not the slightest idea of this matter.</t365> <t366>To her, Darcy was the man who made himself disagreeable wherever he went and the man who had not considered her beautiful enough to ask her to dance.</t366>
<t367>Darcy began to want to get to know her better.</t367> <t368>As a preliminary step to talking to her, he dedicated himself to listening to her talk with others.</t368> <t369>This fact caught Elizabeth's attention.</t369> <t370>It happened one day at Sir Lucas's house where a large group of people had gathered.</t370>
<t371>––What could Mr. Darcy want ––she said to Charlotte––, as he has been listening to my conversation with Colonel Forster?</t371>
<t372>––That is a question that only Mr. Darcy can answer.</t372>
<t373>––If he does it again, I will let him know that I understand what he intends.</t373> <t374>He is very satirical, and if I don't start by being impertinent myself, I will end up being afraid of him.</t374>
<t375>Shortly afterward, he approached them again, and although he did not seem to have any intention of speaking, Miss Lucas challenged her friend to bring up the subject, which immediately prompted Elizabeth, who turned to him and said:</t375>
<t376>––Don't you think, Mr. Darcy, that I expressed myself very well a moment ago when I insisted to Colonel Forster that he give us a ball in Meryton?</t376>
<t377>––With great energy; but that is a topic that always fills women with energy.</t377>
<t378>––You are strict with us.</t378>
<t379>––Now it's our turn to insist to you ––said Miss Lucas––.</t379> <t380>I'm going to open the piano and you know what comes next, Eliza.</t380>
<t381>––What kind of friend are you?</t381> <t382>You always want me to sing and play in front of everyone.</t382> <t383>If God had called me to the path of music, you would be a friend of incalculable value; but since that is not the case, I would prefer not to play in front of people who must be used to listening to the best musicians ––but as Miss Lucas insisted, she added––: Very well, if it must be, it will be ––and looking coldly at Darcy, she said––:</t383> <t384>There is an old saying that everyone here knows very well, "save your breath to cool your soup," and I will save mine for my song.</t384>
<t385>Elizabeth's concert was pleasant, but not extraordinary.</t385> <t386>After one or two songs and before she could satisfy the requests of some who wanted her to sing again, she was replaced at the piano by her sister Mary, who, being the least brilliant of the family, worked hard to acquire knowledge and skills that she was always eager to demonstrate.</t386>
<t387>Mary had neither talent nor taste; and although vanity had made her diligent, it had also given her a pedantic air and affected manners that would mar any brilliance superior to what she had achieved.</t387> <t388>Elizabeth, although she had played only half as well, had been listened to with more pleasure because of her ease and simplicity; Mary, at the end of her long concert, received no more than a few compliments for the Scottish and Irish melodies she had played at the request of her younger sisters who, along with some of the Lucases and two or three officers, were dancing merrily at one end of the room.</t388>
<t389>Darcy, who was indignant at that way of spending the evening, was silent and not in the mood to talk; he was so absorbed in his own thoughts that he did not notice Sir William Lucas was beside him until the latter addressed him.</t389>
<t390>––What a delightful amusement for the youth, Mr. Darcy!</t390> <t391>When you look at it closely, there is nothing like dancing.</t391> <t392>I consider it one of the finest refinements of the most distinguished societies.</t392>
<t393>––Certainly, sir, and it also has the advantage of being fashionable among the less distinguished societies of the world; all the savages dance.</t393>
<t394>Sir William sketched a smile.</t394>
<t395>––Your friend dances wonderfully ––she continued after a pause, seeing Bingley join the group–– and I do not doubt, Mr. Darcy, that you yourself are an expert in the matter.</t395>
<t396>––He saw me dance in Meryton, I believe, sir.</t396>
<t397>––Of course, and it gave me great pleasure to see him.</t397> <t398>Do you often dance at Saint James?</t398>
<t399>––Never, sir.</t399>
<t400>––Don't you think it would be a compliment to that place?</t400>
<t401>––It is a compliment that I never give anywhere, if I can avoid it.</t401>
<t402>––I believe he has a house in the capital.</t402> <t403>Mr. Darcy nodded his head.</t403>
<t404>––I sometimes thought about settling in the city, because I love high society; but I wasn't sure if the air of London would agree with Lady Lucas.</t404>
<t405>Sir William paused in the hope of a response, but his company was not willing to make any.</t405> <t406>Seeing Elizabeth approaching, it occurred to him to do something that seemed very gallant on his part, and he called her.</t406>
<t407>––My dear Miss Eliza, why are you not dancing?</t407> <t408>Mr. Darcy, allow me to introduce you to this young lady who could be an excellent partner.</t408> <t409>I am sure you cannot refuse to dance when you have such beauty before you.</t409>
<t410>He took Elizabeth by the hand with the intention of giving her to Darcy; who, although extremely surprised, was not going to refuse her; but Elizabeth turned her back on him and said to Sir William, somewhat disconcerted:</t410>
<t411>––Really, sir, I had no intention of dancing.</t411> <t412>I beg you not to assume that I came here to find a partner.</t412>
<t413>Mr. Darcy, with all propriety, asked her to grant him the honor of dancing with him, but it was in vain.</t413> <t414>Elizabeth was determined, and not even Sir William, with all his arguments, could persuade her.</t414>
<t415>––You are excellent at dancing, Miss Eliza, and it is very cruel of you to deny me the satisfaction of watching you; and although this gentleman does not like this entertainment, I am sure he would not mind indulging us for half an hour.</t415>
<t416>––Mr. Darcy is very polite ––said Elizabeth, smiling.</t416>
<t417>––It is indeed; but considering what induces him, dear Eliza, we cannot doubt his courtesy; because, who could reject such a charming couple?</t417>
<t418>Elizabeth looked at them coquettishly and withdrew.</t418> <t419>Her resistance had not harmed her at all in the eyes of the gentleman, who was thinking of her with satisfaction when he was approached by Miss Bingley.</t419>
<t420>––I guess why you are so thoughtful.</t420>
<t421>––I don't think so.</t421>
<t422>––He is thinking about how unbearable it would be to spend more evenings like this, in a society like this; and of course, I share his opinion.</t422> <t423>I have never been angrier.</t423> <t424>What insipid people and what a fuss they make!</t424> <t425>How insignificant they are and yet how important they think they are.</t425> <t426>I would give anything to hear their criticisms about them.</t426>
<t427>––His conjectures are completely wrong.</t427> <t428>My mind was occupied with more pleasant things.</t428> <t429>I was meditating on the great pleasure that a pair of beautiful eyes on the face of a lovely woman can cause.</t429>
<t430>Miss Bingley stared at him, wishing he would tell her which lady had inspired such thoughts.</t430> <t431>Mr. Darcy, undaunted, replied:</t431>
<t432>––Miss Elizabeth Bennet.</t432>
<t433>––Miss Bennet!</t433> <t434>You astonish me.</t434> <t435>Since when is she your favorite?</t435> <t436>And tell me, when will I have to congratulate you?</t436>
<t437>––That is exactly the question I was expecting you to ask me.</t437> <t438>A lady's imagination moves very quickly and jumps from admiration to love and from love to marriage in a moment.</t438> <t439>I knew you would congratulate me.</t439>
<t440>––If you take it so seriously, I will believe it is already a done deal.</t440> <t441>You will have a charming mother-in-law, truly, and needless to say, she will always be at Pemberley with you.</t441>
<t442>He listened to her with perfect indifference, while she continued to enjoy the things she was saying; and seeing, by Darcy's attitude, that everything was safe, she let her wit flow for a long time.</t442>
<t443>CHAPTER VII</t443>
<t444>Mr. Bennet's property consisted almost entirely of an estate of two thousand pounds a year, which, unfortunately for his daughters, was destined, due to the lack of male heirs, to a distant relative; and the fortune of their mother, although ample for her position, could hardly supplement that of her husband.</t444> <t445>Her father had been a lawyer in Meryton and had left her four thousand pounds.</t445>
<t446>Mrs. Bennet had a sister married to a certain Mr. Phillips who had been employed by her father and had succeeded him in business, and a brother in London who held a respectable position in trade.</t446>
<t447>The village of Longbourn was only a mile from Meryton, a very convenient distance for the young ladies, who were usually tempted to go there three or four times a week to visit their aunt and, incidentally, stop by a millinery shop near her house.</t447> <t448>The ones who frequented Meryton the most were the two youngest, Catherine and Lydia, who tended to be more idle than their sisters, and when nothing better was offered, they decided that a little walk to town was necessary to pass the morning well and thus have conversation for the afternoon; because, although news was not usually abundant in the countryside, their aunt always had something to tell.</t448> <t449>At the moment, they were well supplied with gossip and joy at the recent arrival of a military regiment that was going to stay all winter and had its headquarters in Meryton.</t449>
<t450>Now the visits to Mrs. Phillips provided the most interesting information.</t450> <t451>Each day they added something more to what they already knew about the names and families of the officers.</t451> <t452>The place where they were staying was no longer a secret and soon they began to meet the officers in person.</t452>
<t453>Mr. Phillips knew them all, which was an unexpected source of satisfaction for his nieces.</t453> <t454>He spoke of nothing but officers.</t454> <t455>Mr. Bingley's great fortune, which his mother so loved to talk about, was no longer worth comparing to the uniform of an ensign.</t455>
<t456>After hearing one morning the enthusiasm with which his daughters spoke about the subject, Mr. Bennet observed coldly:</t456>
<t457>––From everything I can make out from the way you talk, you must be the silliest girls in the whole country.</t457> <t458>I had my suspicions sometimes, but now I am convinced.</t458>
<t459>Catherine was left bewildered and did not reply.</t459> <t460>Lydia, with complete indifference, continued to express her admiration for Captain Carter and said that she hoped to see him that very day, as he was leaving for London the next morning.</t460>
<t461>––I am astonished, dear ––said Mrs. Bennet––, at how ready you always are to believe that your daughters are foolish.</t461> <t462>If I were to look down on anyone, it would be the daughters of others, not my own.</t462>
<t463>––If my daughters are foolish, the least I can do is acknowledge it.</t463>
<t464>––Yes, but you see, it turns out they are very smart.</t464>
<t465>––I presume that is the only point on which we disagree.</t465> <t466>I always wished to agree with you on everything, but on this I differ, because our two younger daughters are complete fools.</t466>
<t467>My dear Mr. Bennet, you cannot expect these girls to have as much sense as their parents.</t467> <t468>When they are our age, I would bet that they think about officers as much as we do.</t468> <t469>I remember a time when I was very fond of a red coat, and the truth is that I still carry it in my heart.</t469> <t470>And if a young colonel with five or six thousand pounds a year wanted one of my daughters, I would not say no.</t470> <t471>I found Colonel Forster very well the other night at Sir William's house.</t471>
<t472>––Mom ––said Lydia, Aunt says that Colonel Forster and Captain Carter don't go to the Watsons' house as much as before.</t472> <t473>Now she sees them a lot at Clarke's library.</t473>
<t474>Mrs. Bennet could not answer as she was interrupted by the entrance of a footman who brought a note for Miss Bennet; it came from Netherfield and the servant awaited a reply.</t474> <t475>Mrs. Bennet's eyes sparkled with joy and she was impatient for her daughter to finish reading.</t475>
<t476>––Well, Jane, whose is it? What is it about? What does it say?</t476> <t477>Hurry up and tell us, hurry up, darling.</t477>
<t478>––It is from Miss Bingley ––said Jane, and then she read aloud:</t478>
<t479>My dear friend,</t479>
<t480>If you have compassion for us, come to dinner today with Louisa and me, otherwise, we will be in danger of hating each other for the rest of our lives, because two women together all day cannot end without fighting.</t480> <t481>Come as soon as possible after receiving this note.</t481> <t482>My brother and the other gentlemen will dine with the officers.</t482> <t483>Regards,</t483>
<t484>Caroline Bingley</t484>
<t485>––With the officers! ––exclaimed Lydia––.</t485> <t486>How strange that Aunt didn't tell us!</t486>
<t487>––Dinner out! ––said Mrs. Bennet––.</t487> <t488>What bad luck!</t488>
<t489>––Can I take the carriage? ––asked Jane.</t489>
<t490>––No, dear; it's better if you go on horseback, because it looks like it's going to rain and that way you'll have to stay the night.</t490>
<t491>––It would be a good plan ––said Elizabeth––, if you were sure they wouldn't offer to bring her home.</t491>
<t492>––Oh, the gentlemen will take Mr. Bingley's landau to Meryton and the Hursts do not have their own horses.</t492>
<t493>––I would prefer to go in the carriage.</t493>
<t494>––But dear, your father cannot lend you the horses.</t494> <t495>I am certain of it.</t495> <t496>They are needed on the farm.</t496> <t497>Isn't that right, Mr. Bennet?</t497>
<t498>––They are needed more on the farm than what I can offer them.</t498>
<t499>––If you can offer them today ––said Elizabeth––, my mother's wishes will be fulfilled.</t499>
<t500>In the end, he encouraged the father to admit that the horses were occupied.</t500> <t501>And, finally, Jane was forced to go on horseback.</t501> <t502>Her mother accompanied her to the door, very happily predicting a terrible day.</t502>
<t503>Their hopes were fulfilled; Jane had not been gone long when it started to pour rain.</t503> <t504>The sisters were worried about her, but their mother was delighted.</t504> <t505>It didn't stop raining all afternoon; it was obvious that Jane wouldn't be able to return...</t505>
<t506>––Truly, I had a very good idea ––repeated Mrs. Bennet.</t506>
<t507>However, it was not until the next morning that she knew anything about the result of her timely stratagem.</t507> <t508>She had barely finished breakfast when a servant from Netherfield brought the following note for Elizabeth:</t508>
<t509>My dear Lizzy:</t509>
<t510>I am not feeling very well this morning, which, I suppose, is because I got soaked to the bone yesterday.</t510> <t511>My kind friends do not want to hear about me going back home until I am better.</t511> <t512>They insist that I see Mr. Jones; therefore, do not be alarmed if you hear that he has come to visit me.</t512> <t513>I have nothing more than a sore throat and a headache.</t513> <t514>Yours always,</t514>
<t515>Jane</t515>
<t516>––Well, my dear ––said Mr. Bennet once Elizabeth had read the note aloud––, if Jane were to contract a dangerous illness or die, it would be a consolation to know that it was all to get Mr. Bingley and under your orders.</t516>
<t517>––Oh!</t517> <t518>I'm not afraid that she will die.</t518> <t519>People don't die from insignificant little colds.</t519> <t520>She will have good care.</t520> <t521>As long as she is there, everything will go wonderfully.</t521> <t522>I would go see her if I could use the car.</t522>
<t523>Elizabeth, who was truly worried, made up her mind to go see her.</t523> <t524>Since she couldn't have the carriage and wasn't a good rider, walking was her only option.</t524> <t525>And she announced her decision.</t525>
<t526>––How can you be so foolish? exclaimed her mother––.</t526> <t527>How can such a thing occur to you?</t527> <t528>With all the mud there is!</t528> <t529>You would arrive looking a mess, you wouldn't be presentable!</t529>
<t530>––I would be presentable to see Jane, which is all I desire.</t530>
<t531>––Is that a hint for me to send for the horses, Lizzy? ––said her father.</t531>
<t532>––No, not at all.</t532> <t533>I don't mind walking.</t533> <t534>There are no distances when you have a reason.</t534> <t535>It's only three miles.</t535> <t536>I'll be back by dinner time.</t536>
<t537>––I admire the activity of your benevolence ––observed Mary––; but every impulse of feeling must be guided by reason, and in my opinion, the effort must be proportional to what is intended.</t537>
<t538>––We will go with you to Meryton ––said Catherine and Lydia.</t538> <t539>Elizabeth accepted their company and the three young women went out together.</t539>
<t540>––If we hurry ––said Lydia as she walked––, maybe we can see Captain Carter before he leaves.</t540>
<t541>In Meryton they parted; the two youngest headed to the home of one of the officers' wives, and Elizabeth continued on her way alone.</t541> <t542>She crossed field after field at a brisk pace, jumped fences, and navigated puddles impatiently until she finally found herself in front of the house, with soaked ankles, dirty stockings, and a face flushed from the exercise.</t542>
<t543>They took her to the dining room where everyone was gathered except Jane, and where her presence caused great surprise.</t543> <t544>Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley found it incredible that she had walked three miles alone, so early and in such dreadful weather.</t544> <t545>Elizabeth was convinced that they looked down on her for it.</t545> <t546>Nevertheless, they received her with much courtesy, but in the brother's attitude, there was something more than courtesy: there was good humor and kindness.</t546> <t547>Mr. Darcy spoke little and Mr. Hurst nothing at all.</t547> <t548>The former fluctuated between admiration for the brightness that exercise had given her face and doubt as to whether the occasion justified her having come alone from so far.</t548> <t549>The latter only thought about his breakfast.</t549>
<t550>The questions that Elizabeth asked about her sister were not answered favorably.</t550> <t551>Miss Bennet had slept poorly, and although she had gotten up, she had a high fever and was not in a condition to leave her room.</t551> <t552>Elizabeth was glad to be taken to see her immediately; and Jane, who had refrained from expressing in her note how much she desired that visit, for fear of being inconvenient or alarming them, was very happy to see her enter.</t552> <t553>Despite everything, she did not have the spirit for much conversation.</t553> <t554>When Miss Bingley left them alone, she could only express gratitude for the extraordinary kindness with which she was treated in that house.</t554> <t555>Elizabeth attended to her in silence.</t555>
<t556>When breakfast was over, the Bingley sisters joined them; and Elizabeth began to find them likable upon seeing the affection and interest they showed for Jane.</t556> <t557>The doctor came and examined the patient, declaring, as was to be expected, that she had caught a severe cold and that they should do everything possible to take care of her.</t557> <t558>He recommended that she get back into bed and prescribed some medicines.</t558> <t559>They followed the doctor's instructions to the letter, as the fever had increased and the headache was more severe.</t559> <t560>Elizabeth did not leave the room for a single moment, and the other ladies also did not stay away for long.</t560> <t561>The gentlemen were outside because they really had nothing to do there.</t561>
<t562>When it struck three, Elizabeth realized that she had to leave, and, although very much against her will, she expressed it as such.</t562>
<t563>Miss Bingley offered her the carriage; Elizabeth was only waiting for her to insist a little more to accept it, when Jane expressed her desire to leave with her; therefore, Miss Bingley was obliged to turn the offer of the landau into an invitation for her to stay at Netherfield.</t563> <t564>Elizabeth accepted very gratefully, and they sent a servant to Longbourn to inform the family that she was staying and to send her clothes.</t564>
<t565>CHAPTER VIII</t565>
<t566>At five o'clock, the ladies retired to dress, and at half-past six, they called Elizabeth to come down for dinner.</t566> <t567>She could not respond favorably to the attentive questions they asked her, in which she had the satisfaction of discerning Mr. Bingley's special interest.</t567> <t568>Jane had not improved at all; upon hearing this, the sisters repeated three or four times how sorry they were, how awful it was to have a bad cold, and how much they disliked being ill themselves.</t568> <t569>After that, they no longer concerned themselves with the matter.</t569> <t570>Their indifference towards Jane, as soon as she was out of sight, rekindled in Elizabeth the antipathy she had initially felt towards them.</t570>
<t571>In reality, Bingley was the only one in the group whom she viewed with favor.</t571> <t572>His concern for Jane was evident, and the attentions he paid to Elizabeth were what prevented her from feeling like an intruder, which was how the others regarded her.</t572> <t573>Only he seemed to notice her presence.</t573> <t574>Miss Bingley was absorbed with Mr. Darcy; her sister, more or less, the same; as for Mr. Hurst, who was seated next to Elizabeth, he was an indolent man who lived only to eat, drink, and play cards.</t574> <t575>When he learned that Elizabeth preferred a simple dish to a ragout, he had nothing more to talk about with her.</t575> <t576>When dinner was over, Elizabeth immediately returned to Jane.</t576> <t577>As soon as they left the dining room, Miss Bingley began to criticize her.</t577> <t578>Her manners were, indeed, terrible, a mix of pride and impertinence; she had no conversation, no style, no taste, no beauty.</t578> <t579>Mrs. Hurst felt the same and added:</t579>
<t580>––In summary, the only thing that can be said about her is that she is an excellent walker.</t580> <t581>I will never forget how she appeared this morning.</t581> <t582>She really looked half-wild.</t582>
<t583>Indeed, Louisa.</t583> <t584>When I saw her, I could hardly contain myself.</t584> <t585>What folly to come all the way here!</t585> <t586>What need was there for her to run through the fields just because her sister has a cold?</t586> <t587>How she had her hair, so disheveled, so untidy!</t587>
<t588>––Yes.</t588> <t589>And the petticoats!</t589> <t590>If you had seen them!</t590> <t591>With more than a handspan of mud.</t591> <t592>And the coat she had put on to cover them, of course, did not fulfill its purpose.</t592>
<t593>––Your portrait may be very accurate, Louisa ––said Bingley––, but all of that went unnoticed by me.</t593> <t594>I think Miss Elizabeth Bennet looked impeccable when she entered the room this morning.</t594> <t595>I hardly noticed that her skirts were dirty.</t595>
<t596>––I am sure that you did notice, Mr. Darcy ––said Miss Bingley––; and I imagine that you would not like your sister to put on such a display.</t596>
<t597>––Of course not.</t597>
<t598>––To walk three miles, or four, or five, or however many, with mud up to your ankles and alone, completely alone!</t598> <t599>What could she mean by that?</t599> <t600>To me, that shows an abominable independence and presumption, and an indifference to the decorum typical of country folk.</t600>
<t601>––What it shows is a considerable affection for her sister ––said Bingley.</t601>
<t602>––I fear, Mr. Darcy ––observed Miss Bingley in a low voice––, that this adventure may have considerably affected the admiration you felt for her beautiful eyes.</t602>
<t603>––Not at all ––replied Darcy––; the exercise made them even brighter.</t603>
<t604>This intervention was followed by a brief pause, and Mrs. Hurst began again.</t604>
<t605>––I hold Jane Bennet in high regard, she is truly a charming girl, and I wish with all my heart that she has much luck.</t605> <t606>But with such parents and relatives of such low class, I fear she will not have many opportunities.</t606>
<t607>––I believe I heard you say that his uncle is a lawyer in Meryton.</t607>
<t608>––Yes, and he has another one who lives somewhere near Cheapside.</t608>
<t609>––"Colossal!" added her sister.</t609> <t610>And the two burst into laughter.</t610>
<t611>––Even if all of Cheapside were full of their uncles ––exclaimed Bingley––, the Bennets would be no less pleasant for it.</t611>
<t612>––But it will lessen their chances of marrying men who are of some standing in the world ––replied Darcy.</t612>
<t613>Bingley made no comment on this observation of Darcy.</t613> <t614>But his sisters nodded delightedly, and spent some time amusing themselves at the expense of their dear friend's vulgar relatives.</t614>
<t615>However, in an act of renewed kindness, upon leaving the dining room, they went to the sick room and sat with her until they were called for coffee.</t615> <t616>Jane was still very ill, and Elizabeth would not leave her until later, when she felt reassured seeing that she was asleep, and then it seemed to her that she should go downstairs, although she did not feel like it at all.</t616> <t617>Upon entering the living room, she found everyone playing loo, and they immediately invited her to join them.</t617> <t618>But she, fearing that they were playing for high stakes, did not accept, and, using her sister as an excuse, said that she would amuse herself with a book during the short time she could stay downstairs.</t618> <t619>Mr. Hurst looked at her in astonishment.</t619>
<t620>––Do you prefer reading over playing?––he said––.</t620> <t621>It's very strange.</t621>
<t622>––Miss Elizabeth Bennet ––said Miss Bingley–– despises letters.</t622> <t623>She is a great reader and finds pleasure in nothing else.</t623>
<t624>––I deserve neither that praise nor that criticism, exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t624> <t625>I am not a great reader and I find pleasure in many things.</t625>
<t626>––Like, for example, in taking care of her sister ––Bingley intervened––, and I hope that pleasure increases when she sees her fully recovered.</t626>
<t627>Elizabeth thanked him wholeheartedly and went to a table where there were several books.</t627> <t628>He instantly offered to go fetch others, all the ones he had in his library.</t628>
<t629>––I wish my collection were larger for your benefit and for my own prestige; but I am a lazy man, and although I do not have many books, I have more than I could ever read.</t629>
<t630>Elizabeth assured her that with those in the room, she had plenty.</t630>
<t631>––It surprises me ––said Miss Bingley–– that my father left such a small collection of books.</t631> <t632>What a splendid library you have at Pemberley, Mr. Darcy!</t632>
<t633>––It must be good ––he replied––; it is the work of many generations.</t633>
<t634>––And besides, you have increased it considerably; you are always buying books.</t634>
<t635>––I cannot understand how a family's library can be neglected in times like these.</t635>
<t636>––Don't worry!</t636> <t637>I'm sure that you don't neglect anything that pertains to enhancing the beauty of that noble place.</t637> <t638>Charles, when you build your house, I would be satisfied if it were half as beautiful as Pemberley.</t638>
<t639>––I hope I can.</t639>
<t640>––But I would advise you to buy the land near Pemberley and use it as a model.</t640> <t641>There is no county more beautiful in England than Derbyshire.</t641>
<t642>––Of course, I would do it.</t642> <t643>And I would buy Pemberley itself if Darcy were to sell it.</t643>
<t644>––I am talking about possibilities, Charles.</t644>
<t645>––Honestly, Caroline, I would rather acquire Pemberley by purchasing it than by imitating it.</t645>
<t646>Elizabeth was too absorbed in what was happening to pay the slightest attention to her book; she soon abandoned it, approached the game table, and positioned herself between Bingley and her older sister to observe the game.</t646>
<t647>––Has Miss Darcy grown since the spring? ––asked Miss Bingley––.</t647> <t648>Is she as tall as I am now?</t648>
<t649>––I think so.</t649> <t650>Now she must be as tall as Miss Elizabeth Bennet, or even taller.</t650>
<t651>––How eager I am to see her again!</t651> <t652>I've never met anyone I like so much.</t652> <t653>What a figure, what manners, and what talent for her age!</t653> <t654>She plays the piano exquisitely.</t654>
<t655>––It amazes me ––said Bingley–– that young women have so much patience to learn so much, and become as perfect as they all are.</t655>
<t656>––All the young women are perfect!</t656> <t657>My dear Charles, what do you say?</t657>
<t658>––Yes, all of them.</t658> <t659>They all paint, cover screens, and make mesh bags.</t659> <t660>I don't know any who can't do all these things, and I've never heard of a young lady for the first time without being informed that she was perfect.</t660>
<t661>––Your list of what those perfections commonly encompass ––said Darcy–– has much truth in it.</t661> <t662>The adjective is applied to women whose knowledge is no more than making mesh bags or covering screens.</t662> <t663>But I am far from agreeing with you regarding your estimation of ladies in general.</t663> <t664>Of all those I have known, I cannot boast of knowing more than half a dozen who are truly perfect.</t664>
<t665>––Nor I, certainly ––said Miss Bingley.</t665>
<t666>––Then observed Elizabeth–– it must be that his concept of the perfect woman is very demanding.</t666>
<t667>––Yes, it is very demanding.</t667>
<t668>––"Oh, of course!" exclaimed her faithful collaborator––.</t668> <t669>No one can truly consider themselves perfect if they do not far exceed what is normally found.</t669> <t670>A woman must have a deep knowledge of music, singing, drawing, dancing, and modern languages.</t670> <t671>And in addition to all this, she must possess a certain something in her air and manner of walking, in the tone of her voice, in her demeanor and way of expressing herself; otherwise, she would only deserve the title halfway.</t671>
<t672>––She must possess all this ––added Darcy––, and to it must be added something more substantial in the development of her intelligence through abundant reading.</t672>
<t673>––It doesn't surprise me now that I know only six perfect women.</t673> <t674>What surprises me is that I know any.</t674>
<t675>––Are you so severe with your own sex that you doubt this is possible?</t675>
<t676>––I have never seen a woman like that.</t676> <t677>I have never seen so much capability, so much taste, so much dedication, and so much elegance together as you describe.</t677>
<t678>Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley protested against the injustice of his implicit doubt, asserting that they knew many women who fit that description, when Mr. Hurst called them to order, bitterly complaining that they were not paying attention to the game.</t678> <t679>As the conversation seemed to have ended, Elizabeth soon left the room.</t679>
<t680>––Elizabeth ––said Miss Bingley when the door had closed behind her–– is one of those girls who try to make themselves agreeable to the opposite sex by discrediting their own; I won't say it doesn't work with many men, but in my opinion, it's a vile trick, a bad habit.</t680>
<t681>––Undoubtedly ––replied Darcy, to whom this observation was mainly directed–– there is baseness in all the arts that ladies sometimes lower themselves to employ to captivate men.</t681> <t682>Anything that has to do with cunning is despicable.</t682>
<t683>Miss Bingley was not satisfied enough with the answer to continue with the topic.</t683> <t684>Elizabeth rejoined them only to tell them that her sister was worse and that she could not leave her.</t684> <t685>Bingley decided to send someone immediately to fetch Dr. Jones; while his sisters, convinced that medical assistance in the countryside was useless, proposed sending someone to the capital to bring one of the most eminent doctors.</t685> <t686>Elizabeth did not want to hear of the latter, but she did not oppose doing what the brother suggested.</t686> <t687>So it was agreed to send for Dr. Jones early the next morning if Jane was not better.</t687> <t688>Bingley was quite worried and his sisters were very distressed.</t688> <t689>However, later they consoled themselves by singing some duets, while Bingley could find no better relief for his concern than giving orders to his housekeeper to pay all possible attention to the sick woman and her sister.</t689>
<t690>CHAPTER IX</t690>
<t691>Elizabeth spent most of the night in her sister's room, and in the morning she had the pleasure of being able to send a satisfactory response to the numerous questions she had been receiving very early, through one of Bingley's servants; and also to those she later received from the two elegant ladies-in-waiting of the sisters.</t691> <t692>Despite the improvement, Elizabeth asked for a note to be sent to Longbourn, as she wanted her mother to come visit Jane so she could judge the situation for herself.</t692> <t693>The note was dispatched immediately and the response to its content was fulfilled with the same speed.</t693> <t694>Mrs. Bennet, accompanied by her two younger daughters, arrived at Netherfield shortly after the family's breakfast.</t694>
<t695>If she had found Jane in apparent danger, Mrs. Bennet would have been very upset; but being satisfied to see that the illness was not alarming, she had no desire for her to recover quickly, as her cure would mean leaving Netherfield.</t695> <t696>For this reason, she refused to heed her daughter's request to be taken home, something that the doctor, who had arrived almost at the same time, also did not deem prudent.</t696> <t697>After sitting with Jane for a while, Miss Bingley appeared and invited them to the dining room.</t697> <t698>The mother and the three daughters followed her.</t698> <t699>Bingley received them and asked about Jane, hoping that Mrs. Bennet had not found her daughter worse than expected.</t699>
<t700>––Well, truly, I have found her very unwell ––replied Mrs. Bennet––.</t700> <t701>So unwell that it is not possible to take her home.</t701> <t702>Doctor Jones says we must not think of moving her.</t702> <t703>We will have to impose a little more on your kindness.</t703>
<t704>––Move her! ––exclaimed Bingley––.</t704> <t705>Not a chance!</t705> <t706>I'm sure my sister will also oppose her going home.</t706>
<t707>––You can trust, madam ––replied Miss Bingley with cold courtesy––, that Miss Bennet will lack nothing while she is with us.</t707>
<t708>––I am sure ––she added–– that if it weren't for such good friends, I don't know what would have become of her, because she is very ill and suffers a lot; although, that said, with the greatest patience in the world, as she always does, because she has the sweetest character I know.</t708> <t709>I often tell my other daughters that they are worth nothing compared to her.</t709> <t710>What a beautiful room this is, Mr. Bingley, and what a lovely view it has of the garden paths!</t710> <t711>I have never seen a place in the whole country comparable to Netherfield.</t711> <t712>I hope you are not thinking of leaving it suddenly, even though you have rented it for a short time.</t712>
<t713>––I do everything suddenly ––replied Bingley––.</t713> <t714>So if I decided to leave Netherfield, I would probably be gone in five minutes.</t714> <t715>But, for now, I am fine here.</t715>
<t716>––That is exactly what I expected from you ––said Elizabeth.</t716>
<t717>––You are beginning to understand me, aren't you? ––exclaimed Bingley, turning towards her.</t717>
<t718>––Oh, yes!</t718> <t719>I understand you perfectly.</t719>
<t720>––I would like to take it as a compliment; but I am afraid that being easily recognized is unfortunate.</t720>
<t721>––It is what it is.</t721> <t722>That does not necessarily mean that a deep and complex character is more or less estimable than his.</t722>
<t723>––Lizzy ––exclaimed her mother––, remember where you are and stop behaving with that intolerable conduct that we are accustomed to at home.</t723>
<t724>––I didn't know you were dedicated to studying people's character ––Bingley continued immediately––.</t724> <t725>It must be a fascinating study.</t725>
<t726>––Yes; and complex characters are the most fascinating of all.</t726> <t727>At least, they have that advantage.</t727>
<t728>––The countryside ––said Darcy–– cannot provide many subjects for such a study.</t728> <t729>In a village, one moves in an unchanging and very limited society.</t729>
<t730>––But people change so much that there is always something new to observe in them.</t730>
<t731>––Of course it does ––exclaimed Mrs. Bennet, offended by the way he had spoken about country folk––; I assure you that it happens the same in the country as in the city.</t731>
<t732>Everyone was surprised.</t732> <t733>Darcy looked at her for a moment and then turned away without saying anything.</t733> <t734>Mrs. Bennet believed she had achieved a crushing victory over him and continued triumphantly:</t734>
<t735>––For my part, I do not think that London has any advantage over the countryside, except for the shops and public places.</t735> <t736>The countryside is much more pleasant.</t736> <t737>Isn't that so, Mr. Bingley?</t737>
<t738>––When I am in the countryside ––he replied–– I do not wish to leave, and when I am in the city, the same thing happens to me.</t738> <t739>Each place has its advantages, and I feel equally comfortable in both.</t739>
<t740>––Of course, because you have a very good disposition.</t740> <t741>On the other hand, that gentleman ––she said looking at Darcy –does not seem to have a very good opinion of the countryside.</t741>
<t742>––Mom, you are very mistaken ––Elizabeth intervened, blushing at her mother's imprudence––, you misunderstand Mr. Darcy.</t742> <t743>He only meant to say that in the countryside you don't find as much variety of people as in the city.</t743> <t744>Which you must admit is true.</t744>
<t745>––Certainly, dear, no one said otherwise, but as for there not being many people in this neighborhood, I believe there are few as large as ours.</t745> <t746>I have dined with twenty-four families.</t746>
<t747>Nothing, if it weren't for his consideration for Elizabeth, could have made Bingley restrain himself.</t747> <t748>His sister was less delicate, and looked at Darcy with a very expressive smile.</t748> <t749>Elizabeth wanted to say something to change the conversation and asked her mother if Charlotte Lucas had been to Longbourn since she had left.</t749>
<t750>––Yes, he visited us yesterday with his father.</t750> <t751>What a pleasant man Sir William is! Isn't he, Mr. Bingley?</t751> <t752>So distinguished, so gentle, and so simple!</t752> <t753>He always has a kind word for everyone.</t753> <t754>That is my idea of what good manners are; those people who think they are very important and never open their mouths have no idea of manners.</t754>
<t755>––Did Charlotte have dinner with you?</t755>
<t756>––No, she went home.</t756> <t757>I think they needed her to make the meatloaf.</t757> <t758>As for me, Mr. Bingley, I always have servants who know how to do their job.</t758> <t759>My daughters are educated differently.</t759> <t760>But everyone should judge for themselves.</t760> <t761>The Lucas girls are very good girls, I assure you.</t761> <t762>It's a pity they aren't pretty!</t762> <t763>It's not that I think Charlotte is very ugly; anyway, she is a very good friend of ours.</t763>
<t764>––She seems like a very pleasant young woman ––said Bingley.</t764>
<t765>––Oh! Yes, but you must admit she is rather plain.</t765> <t766>Even Lady Lucas says so many times, and she envies me for Jane's beauty.</t766> <t767>I don't like to praise my own daughters, but the truth is that you don't often find someone as pretty as Jane.</t767> <t768>I can't be impartial, of course; but everyone says so.</t768> <t769>When she was only fifteen, there was a gentleman who lived at my brother Gardiner's house in the city, and he was so in love with Jane that my sister-in-law assured me he would propose before we left.</t769> <t770>But he didn't.</t770> <t771>He probably thought she was too young.</t771> <t772>However, he wrote her some verses, and they were quite beautiful.</t772>
