<t773>––And so ended his love ––said Elizabeth impatiently––.</t773> <t774>I believe there have been many who overcame it in the same way.</t774> <t775>I wonder who was the first to discover the efficacy of poetry in ending love.</t775>
<t776>––I have always considered that poetry is the food of love ––said Darcy.</t776>
<t777>––From a great love, solid and strong, it can.</t777> <t778>Everything nourishes what is already strong by itself.</t778> <t779>But if it is only a slight inclination, without any foundation, a good sonnet would end up starving it to death.</t779>
<t780>Darcy merely smiled.</t780> <t781>A general silence followed, making Elizabeth fear that her mother might speak again.</t781> <t782>Mrs. Bennet wished to do so but did not know what to say, until after a brief pause she began to reiterate her gratitude to Mr. Bingley for his kindness to Jane and apologized for any inconvenience Lizzy might also be causing.</t782> <t783>Mr. Bingley was courteous in his response and obliged his younger sister to be polite and say what the occasion required.</t783> <t784>She played her part, though with little grace, but Mrs. Bennet was satisfied and shortly thereafter asked for her carriage.</t784> <t785>Upon hearing this, the youngest of her daughters stepped forward to say something.</t785> <t786>The two young girls had been whispering throughout the visit, and the result was that the youngest was to remind Mr. Bingley that when he first came to the countryside, he had promised to give a ball at Netherfield.</t786>
<t787>Lydia was strong, very mature for being fifteen years old, she had a good figure and a very cheerful character.</t787> <t788>She was her mother's favorite, who, out of love for her, had introduced her to society at a very young age.</t788> <t789>She was very impulsive and gave herself a lot of importance, which had increased with the attentions she received from the officers, to which her aunt's dinners and her simple manners contributed.</t789> <t790>Therefore, she was the most suitable to approach Bingley and remind him of his promise; adding that it would be a shame before the world if he did not keep it.</t790> <t791>His response to this sudden attack was delightful to Mrs. Bennet's ears.</t791>
<t792>––I assure you that I am willing to keep my commitment, as soon as your sister is well; you yourself, if you like, can set the date of the ball: You wouldn't want to be dancing while your sister is ill.</t792>
<t793>Lydia was satisfied:</t793>
<t794>––Oh! Yes, it will be much better to wait until Jane is well; and by then it is most likely that Captain Carter will be back in Meryton.</t794> <t795>And when you have given your ball ––she added––, I will insist that they give one too.</t795> <t796>I will tell Colonel Forster that it would be unfortunate if he did not.</t796>
<t797>Finally, Mrs. Bennet and her daughters left, and Elizabeth instantly returned to Jane, leaving the two ladies and Mr. Darcy to make their comments about her behavior and that of her family.</t797> <t798>However, Darcy could not share in the others' censure of Elizabeth, despite Miss Bingley's sharpness in making jokes about pretty eyes.</t798>
<t799>CHAPTER X</t799>
<t800>The day passed the same as the previous one.</t800> <t801>Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley had spent a few hours in the morning by the side of the sick woman, who was improving, though slowly.</t801> <t802>In the afternoon, Elizabeth joined them in the drawing room.</t802> <t803>But the usual card table was not set up.</t803> <t804>Darcy was writing, and Miss Bingley, sitting beside him, followed the course of the letter, repeatedly interrupting him with messages for her sister.</t804> <t805>Mr. Hurst and Bingley were playing piquet, and Mrs. Hurst was watching the game.</t805>
<t806>Elizabeth dedicated herself to needlework and had enough entertainment attending to what was happening between Darcy and his company.</t806> <t807>The constant praises from the latter about Darcy's handwriting, the symmetry of his lines, or the length of the letter, as well as the absolute indifference with which they were received, constituted a curious dialogue that was exactly in line with Elizabeth's opinion of each of them.</t807>
<t808>––How happy Miss Darcy will be when she receives this letter!</t808>
<t809>He did not answer.</t809>
<t810>––You write faster than anyone.</t810> <t811>––You are mistaken.</t811> <t812>I write very slowly.</t812>
<t813>––How many letters you will have the chance to write by the end of the year!</t813> <t814>Including business letters.</t814> <t815>How I detest them!</t815>
<t816>––It is fortunate, then, that it is I and not you who has to write them.</t816>
<t817>––I beg you to tell your sister that I very much wish to see her.</t817>
<t818>––I have already told him once, at his request.</t818>
<t819>––I’m afraid your pen isn’t working well.</t819> <t820>Let me sharpen it for you, I do it incredibly well.</t820>
<t821>––Thank you, but I always sharpen my own pen.</t821>
<t822>––How can you achieve such uniform handwriting?</t822>
<t823>Darcy made no comment.</t823>
<t824>––Tell your sister that I am glad to know that she has made a lot of progress with the harp; and I also ask you to tell her that I am enthusiastic about the table design she made, and that I think it is infinitely superior to Miss Grantley's.</t824>
<t825>––May I ask you to postpone your enthusiasm for another letter?</t825> <t826>In this one, I no longer have space for more compliments.</t826>
<t827>––Oh, it's not important.</t827> <t828>I will see her in January.</t828> <t829>But, do you always write such long and charming letters, Mr. Darcy?</t829>
<t830>––Generally they are long; but whether they are charming or not, it is not for me to judge.</t830>
<t831>––For me, it's like a rule, when a person writes such long letters with such ease, they can't write poorly.</t831>
<t832>––That compliment does not apply to Darcy, Caroline ––interrupted her brother––, because he does not write easily.</t832> <t833>He studies the words too much.</t833> <t834>He always looks for complicated words with more than four syllables, isn't that right, Darcy?</t834>
<t835>––My style is very different from yours.</t835>
<t836>––Oh! ––exclaimed Miss Bingley––.</t836> <t837>Charles writes without any care.</t837> <t838>He eats up half the words and smudges the rest.</t838>
<t839>––Ideas come to me so quickly that I don't have time to express them; so, sometimes, my letters don't convey any idea to the recipient.</t839>
<t840>––Your humility, Mr. Bingley ––intervened Elizabeth––, must disarm all reproaches.</t840>
<t841>––Nothing is more deceptive ––said Darcy–– than the appearance of humility.</t841> <t842>Normally, it is nothing more than a lack of opinion, and sometimes it is an indirect form of boasting.</t842>
<t843>––And which of those two adjectives do you apply to my recent act of modesty?</t843>
<t844>––An indirect way of boasting; because you, in reality, are proud of your flaws as a writer, since you attribute them to your quickness of thought and a lack of care in execution, something you consider, if not very estimable, at least very interesting.</t844> <t845>The ability to do anything quickly is always highly appreciated, and no attention is paid to the imperfection with which it is done.</t845> <t846>When you told Mrs. Bennet this morning that if you ever decided to leave Netherfield, you would leave in five minutes, it was a kind of self-praise; and yet, what is praiseworthy about leaving hastily, undoubtedly leaving matters unresolved, which cannot be beneficial for you or anyone else?</t846>
<t847>––No! ––exclaimed Bingley––.</t847> <t848>It seems too much to remember at night the nonsense that is said in the morning.</t848> <t849>And I give you my word, I was convinced that what I said about myself was true, and I still am now.</t849> <t850>At least, I did not unnecessarily adopt a hasty character to show off in front of the ladies.</t850>
<t851>––Yes, I think you were convinced; but it is I who am not convinced that you would leave so hastily.</t851> <t852>Your behavior would depend on the circumstances, like that of any person.</t852> <t853>And if, already mounted on the horse, a friend were to say to you: "Bingley, stay until next week," you would probably do so, you probably wouldn't leave, and just one more word would be enough for you to stay a month.</t853>
<t854>––With this, you have only proven ––said Elizabeth–– that Bingley did not do justice to his temperament.</t854> <t855>You have favored him more now than he had done.</t855>
<t856>––I am enormously grateful ––said Bingley for turning what my friend says into a compliment.</t856> <t857>But I fear that you do not interpret it in the way my friend intended; because he would have a better opinion of me if, in that circumstance, I refused outright and left as quickly as possible.</t857>
<t858>––Would Mr. Darcy then consider the imprudence of his first intention repaired by the obstinacy of maintaining it?</t858>
<t859>––It is not I, but Darcy, who must explain it.</t859>
<t860>––You want me to account for some opinions that you attribute to me, but that I have never acknowledged.</t860> <t861>Returning to the case, you must remember, Miss Bennet, that the supposed friend who wishes you to stay and delay your plan, simply desires it and asks it without offering any argument.</t861>
<t862>––Yielding quickly and easily to a friend's persuasion has no merit for you.</t862> <t863>––Yielding without conviction speaks little in favor of the intelligence of either.</t863>
<t864>––It seems to me, Mr. Darcy, that you never allow affection or friendship to influence you.</t864> <t865>Respect or esteem for the person making the request may lead us to comply without expecting any reason or argument.</t865> <t866>I am not speaking of the particular case you have mentioned regarding Mr. Bingley.</t866> <t867>Moreover, we should perhaps wait for the circumstance to arise to then discuss his behavior.</t867> <t868>But in general and in normal cases among friends, when one wants the other to change a decision, would you see it as wrong for that person to comply with the wish without expecting the other's reasons?</t868>
<t869>––Wouldn't it be advisable, before continuing with the topic, to clarify more precisely the importance of the request and the intimacy between the friends?</t869>
<t870>––Perfectly ––said Bingley––, let's pay attention to all the details without forgetting to compare height and size; because that, Miss Bennet, may carry more weight in the discussion than it seems.</t870> <t871>I assure you that if Darcy were not so tall compared to me, I would not have half the respect for him that I do.</t871> <t872>I confess that I know nothing more imposing than Darcy on certain occasions and in certain places, especially in his house and on Sunday afternoons when he has nothing to do.</t872>
<t873>Mr. Darcy smiled; but Elizabeth noticed that he was quite offended and held back her laughter.</t873> <t874>Miss Bingley was very upset by the offense made to Darcy and scolded her brother for saying such nonsense.</t874>
<t875>––I know your system, Bingley ––said his friend––.</t875> <t876>You don't like arguments and you want to end this one.</t876>
<t877>––Maybe.</t877> <t878>Discussions are too much like disputes.</t878> <t879>If you and Miss Bennet postpone yours until I am not in the room, I will be very grateful; besides, that way you can say whatever you want about me.</t879>
<t880>––For my part ––said Elizabeth––, I have no objection to doing what he asks, and it is better that Mr. Darcy finishes the letter.</t880>
<t881>Darcy followed his advice and finished the letter.</t881> <t882>Task completed, he approached Miss Bingley and Elizabeth to delight them with some music.</t882> <t883>Miss Bingley hurried to the piano, but before sitting down, she courteously invited Elizabeth to play first; the latter, with equal courtesy and complete sincerity, declined the invitation; then, Miss Bingley sat down and began the concert.</t883>
<t884>Mrs. Hurst sang with her sister, and while they were engaged in this activity, Elizabeth could not help but notice, each time she turned the pages of some music books that were on the piano, how frequently Darcy's eyes were fixed on her.</t884> <t885>It was difficult for her to suppose that she was an object of admiration to a man of such a rank; and it would be even stranger if he looked at her because she displeased him.</t885> <t886>Finally, she could only imagine that she caught his attention because there was something in her worse and more reprehensible, according to his concept of virtue, than in the rest of those present.</t886> <t887>This assumption did not sadden her.</t887> <t888>She liked him so little, that his opinion of her did not concern her.</t888>
<t889>After playing some Italian songs, Miss Bingley switched the repertoire to a more cheerful Scottish tune; and at that moment Mr. Darcy approached Elizabeth and said to her:</t889>
<t890>––Would you like to take this opportunity to dance a reel, Miss Bennet?</t890>
<t891>She smiled and did not answer.</t891> <t892>He, somewhat surprised by her silence, repeated the question.</t892>
<t893>––Oh! ––she said––, I had already heard the question.</t893> <t894>I was pondering the answer.</t894> <t895>I know you would have liked me to say yes, and thus you would have had the pleasure of criticizing my tastes; but I love to thwart such traps and disappoint people who are premeditating a slight.</t895> <t896>Therefore, I have decided to tell you that I do not wish to dance at all.</t896> <t897>And now, slight me if you dare.</t897>
<t898>––I don't dare, I assure you.</t898>
<t899>She, who believed she had offended him, was astonished by his gallantry.</t899> <t900>But there was such a mixture of sweetness and malice in Elizabeth's manners that it was difficult for her to offend anyone; and Darcy had never been so captivated by a woman as he was with her.</t900> <t901>He truly believed that if it were not for the inferiority of her family, he would be in danger.</t901>
<t902>Miss Bingley saw or suspected enough to become jealous, and her anxiety for her dear friend Jane to recover increased with the desire to get rid of Elizabeth.</t902>
<t903>He was trying to provoke Darcy into becoming disillusioned with the young woman, by talking to him about his supposed marriage to her and the happiness that such an alliance would bring him.</t903>
<t904>––I hope ––he said to her the next day while they were walking in the garden–– that when that desired event takes place, you will give your mother-in-law a few warnings to moderate her tongue; and if you can manage it, prevent the younger daughters from running after the officers.</t904> <t905>And, if you allow me to mention such a delicate subject, try to restrain that something, bordering on presumption and impertinence, that your lady possesses.</t905>
<t906>––Do you have anything else to propose for my domestic happiness?</t906>
<t907>––Oh, yes!</t907> <t908>Let the portraits of your uncles, the Phillips, be hung in the gallery of Pemberley.</t908> <t909>Place them next to your great-uncle, the judge.</t909> <t910>They are of the same profession, although of different ranks.</t910> <t911>As for the portrait of your Elizabeth, you must not allow it to be made, because what painter could do justice to her beautiful eyes?</t911>
<t912>––Of course, it wouldn't be easy to capture her expression, but the color, the shape, and her pretty eyelashes could be reproduced.</t912>
<t913>At that moment, by another path in the garden, Mrs. Hurst and Elizabeth came across their way.</t913>
<t914>––I didn't know you were out walking ––said Miss Bingley, a little confused at the thought that they might have overheard them.</t914>
<t915>––You have behaved very badly towards us ––Mrs. Hurst replied–– by not telling us that you were going out.</t915>
<t916>And, taking Mr. Darcy's free arm, she let Elizabeth walk alone.</t916> <t917>The path could only accommodate three.</t917> <t918>Mr. Darcy noticed such rudeness and immediately said:</t918>
<t919>––This path is not wide enough for the four of us, let's go out to the avenue.</t919>
<t920>But Elizabeth, who had no intention whatsoever of continuing with them, replied with a big smile:</t920>
<t921>––No, no; stay where you are.</t921> <t922>You make a charming group, it's much better this way.</t922> <t923>A fourth person would spoil it.</t923> <t924>Goodbye.</t924>
<t925>He left cheerfully rejoicing at the thought, while walking, that in one or two more days he would be home.</t925> <t926>Jane was already so well that that very afternoon she intended to leave her room for a couple of hours.</t926>
<t927>CHAPTER XI</t927>
<t928>When the ladies got up from the table after dinner, Elizabeth went upstairs to visit her sister, and seeing that she was well wrapped up, accompanied her to the drawing room, where her friends welcomed her with great demonstrations of joy.</t928> <t929>Elizabeth had never seen them so amiable as in the hour that passed until the gentlemen arrived.</t929> <t930>They talked about everything.</t930> <t931>They described the party in great detail, told anecdotes with much grace, and made fun of their acquaintances with humor.</t931>
<t932>But as soon as the gentlemen entered, Jane ceased to be the primary object of attention.</t932> <t933>Miss Bingley's eyes instantly turned towards Darcy, and she had not taken four steps before she had something to say to him.</t933> <t934>He went directly to Miss Bennet and courteously congratulated her.</t934> <t935>Mr. Hurst also gave her a slight nod, saying he was very glad; but the effusion and warmth were reserved for Bingley's greeting, who was very happy and full of attentions towards her.</t935> <t936>The first half hour he spent stoking the fire so that Jane would not notice the change from one room to another, and he asked her to sit by the fireplace, as far away from the door as possible.</t936> <t937>Then he sat next to her and hardly spoke to anyone else.</t937> <t938>Elizabeth, opposite, with her work, watched the scene with satisfaction.</t938>
<t939>When they finished having tea, Mr. Hurst reminded his sister-in-law about the card table, but it was in vain; she sensed that Darcy did not feel like playing, and Mr. Hurst saw his request immediately rejected.</t939> <t940>He assured her that no one felt like playing; the silence that followed his statement seemed to corroborate it.</t940> <t941>Therefore, Mr. Hurst had nothing left to do but lie down on a sofa and sleep.</t941> <t942>Darcy picked up a book, Miss Bingley picked up another, and Mrs. Hurst, mainly occupied with playing with her bracelets and rings, occasionally joined her brother's conversation with Miss Bennet.</t942>
<t943>Miss Bingley paid more attention to Darcy's reading than to her own.</t943> <t944>She kept asking him questions or looking at the page he had in front of him.</t944> <t945>However, she did not manage to draw him into any conversation; he simply answered and continued reading.</t945> <t946>Finally, distressed by the idea of having to entertain herself with her book, which she had chosen only because it was the second volume of what Darcy was reading, she yawned broadly and exclaimed:</t946>
<t947>––How pleasant it is to spend an evening like this!</t947> <t948>All things considered, I think there's nothing as fun as reading.</t948> <t949>Anything else tires you quickly, but a book, never.</t949> <t950>When I have––my own house, I will be very unhappy if I don't have a large library.</t950>
<t951>Nobody said anything.</t951> <t952>Then he yawned again, closed the book, and looked around the room for something to occupy his time; when, upon hearing his brother mention a ball to Miss Bennet, he suddenly turned to him and said:</t952>
<t953>––Do you seriously think about giving a ball at Netherfield, Charles?</t953> <t954>Before deciding, I would advise you to consult with those present, for either I am very mistaken or there is someone among us who would find a ball more of a punishment than an amusement.</t954>
<t955>––If you mean Darcy ––his brother replied––, he can go to bed before it starts, if he prefers; but as for the dance, it's a done deal, and as soon as Nicholls has arranged everything, I will send the invitations.</t955>
<t956>––I would like dances much more ––replied her sister–– if they were different, but those kinds of gatherings are usually so tedious that they become unbearable.</t956> <t957>It would be more rational if the main focus in them were conversation and not dancing.</t957>
<t958>––Much more rational, yes, Caroline; but then it wouldn't resemble a dance at all.</t958>
<t959>Miss Bingley did not reply; she got up shortly after and began to walk around the room.</t959> <t960>Her figure was elegant and her gait graceful; but Darcy, to whom everything was directed, remained engrossed in his reading.</t960> <t961>She, desperate, decided to make one more effort, and, turning to Elizabeth, said:</t961>
<t962>––Miss Eliza Bennet, allow me to persuade you to follow my example and take a turn about the room.</t962> <t963>I assure you it is very beneficial after sitting for so long in the same position.</t963>
<t964>Elizabeth was surprised, but she agreed immediately.</t964> <t965>Miss Bingley achieved what she had intended with her kindness; Mr. Darcy looked up.</t965> <t966>He was as astonished by the novelty of this invitation as Elizabeth herself could be; unconsciously, he closed his book.</t966> <t967>Then, they invited him to walk with them, to which he refused, explaining that there could only be two reasons for them to walk through the hall together, and if he joined them, he would interfere with both.</t967> <t968>“What does he mean?”</t968> <t969>Miss Bingley was dying to know what the meaning could be and asked Elizabeth if she could understand it.</t969>
<t970>––Not at all ––he replied––; but, whatever it is, it's certain that he wants to make us look bad, and the best way to disappoint him will be not to ask him anything.</t970>
<t971>However, Miss Bingley was unable to disappoint Darcy and therefore insisted on asking him to explain the two reasons.</t971>
<t972>––I have not the slightest objection to explaining it ––he said as soon as she allowed him to speak––.</t972> <t973>You choose this way to pass the time either because you need to confide in each other or to talk about your secret affairs, or because you know that walking shows off your figure better; if it is the first, by going with you I would only be a nuisance; and if it is the second, I can admire you much better sitting by the fire.</t973>
<t974>––What a horror! ––cried Miss Bingley––.</t974> <t975>I have never heard anything so abominable.</t975> <t976>How could we give him what he deserves?</t976>
<t977>––Nothing so easy, if you are willing to do it ––said Elizabeth––.</t977> <t978>We all know how to annoy and mortify each other.</t978> <t979>Mock him, laugh at him.</t979> <t980>Being such a close friend of his, you will know very well how to do it.</t980>
<t981>––I don't know, I give you my word.</t981> <t982>I assure you that my great friendship with him has not taught me what his weak points are.</t982> <t983>Mocking a phlegmatic person, with such cold blood!</t983> <t984>And as for laughing at him just like that, we shouldn't expose ourselves; he could challenge us and we would be the ones to lose.</t984>
<t985>––We cannot laugh at Mr. Darcy! ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t985> <t986>It is a very strange privilege, and I hope it remains strange; I wouldn't like to have many acquaintances like that.</t986> <t987>I love to laugh.</t987>
<t988>––Miss Bingley ––replied Darcy–– has given me more importance than I deserve.</t988> <t989>The wisest and best of men or the wisest and best of actions can be ridiculous in the eyes of a person who thinks of nothing in this life but laughing.</t989>
<t990>––I agree ––replied Elizabeth––, there are people like that, but I don't think I am among them.</t990> <t991>I hope I never come to ridicule what is good or wise.</t991> <t992>Foolishness, nonsense, whims, and inconsistencies are the things that truly amuse me, I confess, and I laugh at them whenever I can.</t992> <t993>But I suppose these are the things you lack.</t993>
<t994>––Perhaps it is not possible for anyone, but I have spent my life striving to avoid these weaknesses that expose any intelligent person to ridicule.</t994>
<t995>––Like vanity and pride, for example.</t995>
<t996>––Yes, indeed, vanity is a flaw.</t996> <t997>But pride, in the case of people with superior intelligence, I believe is valid.</t997>
<t998>Elizabeth had to turn away to hide a smile.</t998>
<t999>––I suppose you have just finished examining Mr. Darcy ––said Miss Bingley, and I beg you to tell me what you have concluded.</t999>
<t1000>––I am fully convinced that Mr. Darcy has no faults.</t1000> <t1001>He himself clearly acknowledges it.</t1001>
<t1002>––No ––said Darcy––, I did not mean to say that.</t1002> <t1003>I have many faults, but they have nothing to do with intelligence.</t1003> <t1004>As for my character, I do not dare to vouch for it; I am too uncompromising, in fact, too uncompromising for what is convenient for people.</t1004> <t1005>I cannot forget as quickly as I should the foolishness and vices of others, nor the offenses committed against me.</t1005> <t1006>My feelings do not fade no matter how much effort is made to change them.</t1006> <t1007>Perhaps I can be accused of being resentful.</t1007> <t1008>When I lose the good opinion I have of someone, it is forever.</t1008>
<t1009>––That is really a flaw ––replied Elizabeth––.</t1009> <t1010>Implacable resentment is truly a shadow on one's character.</t1010> <t1011>But you have chosen your flaw very well.</t1011> <t1012>I cannot laugh at it.</t1012> <t1013>As for me, you are safe.</t1013>
<t1014>––I believe that in every individual there is a certain tendency towards a particular flaw, an innate defect, that not even the best education can overcome.</t1014>
<t1015>––And that flaw is the propensity to hate everyone.</t1015>
<t1016>––And yours, he replied with a smile–– is to intentionally misinterpret everyone.</t1016> <t1017>––Let's hear some music ––proposed Miss Bingley, tired of a conversation in which she took no part––.</t1017> <t1018>Louisa, would you mind if I wake up Mr. Hurst?</t1018>
<t1019>His sister did not raise the slightest objection and opened the piano; Darcy, after a few moments of reflection, did not mind.</t1019> <t1020>He was beginning to feel the danger of paying too much attention to Elizabeth.</t1020>
<t1021>CHAPTER XII</t1021>
<t1022>According to her sister, Elizabeth wrote to her mother the next morning, asking her to send the carriage that very day.</t1022> <t1023>But Mrs. Bennet had calculated that her daughters would stay at Netherfield until Tuesday, which would make it exactly a week since Jane had arrived there, and she was not willing for them to return before the stated date.</t1023> <t1024>Thus, her response was not very favorable or, at least, it was not the response that Elizabeth would have wished for, as she was eager to return home.</t1024> <t1025>Mrs. Bennet replied that it was not possible for her to send the carriage before Tuesday; in the postscript, she added that if Mr. Bingley and his sister insisted that they stay longer, they should not hesitate, as she could very well manage without them.</t1025> <t1026>However, Elizabeth was determined not to stay there no matter how much they asked; fearing, on the contrary, to be a nuisance by staying unnecessarily longer, she begged Jane to ask Bingley for the carriage immediately; and, finally, they decided to present their plan to leave Netherfield that very morning and ask to borrow the carriage.</t1026>
<t1027>The news caused many expressions of concern; they repeatedly expressed their desire for them to stay at least until the next day, and there was no choice but to delay the departure until then.</t1027> <t1028>Miss Bingley later regretted having proposed the delay, because the jealousy and antipathy she felt for one of the sisters was far greater than the affection she felt for the other.</t1028>
<t1029>The master of the house was very saddened to learn that they were going to leave so soon, and he persistently tried to convince Jane that it would not be good for her, because she was not yet fully recovered; but Jane was firm when she knew she was acting as she should.</t1029>
<t1030>The news seemed good to Darcy.</t1030> <t1031>Elizabeth had already been at Netherfield for quite some time.</t1031> <t1032>She attracted him more than he wanted, and Miss Bingley was rude to her and more annoying to him than ever.</t1032> <t1033>He resolved to be especially careful not to let any sign of admiration escape him, nor anything that might make Elizabeth believe she had any influence on his happiness.</t1033> <t1034>Aware that he might have suggested such an idea, his behavior during the last day had to be decisive in either confirming it or removing it from her mind.</t1034> <t1035>Firm in his purpose, he barely spoke ten words to her all Saturday, and despite being left alone for half an hour, he immersed himself in his book and did not even look at her.</t1035>
<t1036>On Sunday, after the morning religious service, the separation, so pleasant for almost everyone, took place.</t1036> <t1037>Miss Bingley's courtesy towards Elizabeth increased rapidly at the last moment, as did her affection for Jane.</t1037> <t1038>Upon saying goodbye, after assuring the latter of the pleasure it would always give her to see her both at Longbourn and at Netherfield and giving her a tender hug, she only shook hands with the former.</t1038> <t1039>Elizabeth bid farewell to everyone with a more cheerful spirit than ever.</t1039>
<t1040>The mother was not very cordial in welcoming them.</t1040> <t1041>She did not understand why they had returned so soon and told them that they were very wrong to cause her such distress; she was sure that Jane had caught a cold again.</t1041> <t1042>But the father, although very laconic in expressing joy, was truly happy to see them.</t1042> <t1043>He had realized the importance they had in the family circle.</t1043> <t1044>The evening gatherings, when everyone got together, had lost their animation and even their meaning with the absence of Jane and Elizabeth.</t1044>
<t1045>They found Mary, as usual, deeply engrossed in the profound study of human nature; they had to admire her new summaries and listen to the observations she had recently made about a very unconvincing morality.</t1045> <t1046>What Catherine and Lydia had to tell them was very different.</t1046> <t1047>Many things had been done and said in the regiment since the previous Wednesday; several officers had recently dined with their uncle, a soldier had been flogged, and there was a rumor that Colonel Forster was going to get married.</t1047>
<t1048>CHAPTER XIII</t1048>
<t1049>––I hope, my dear ––Mr. Bennet said to his wife while they were having breakfast the next morning––, that you have prepared a good meal, because I have reason to believe that one more will join us at our table today.</t1049>
<t1050>––Who are you referring to, dear?</t1050> <t1051>I have no news of anyone coming, unless Charlotte Lucas decides to visit us, and I think my meals are good enough for her.</t1051> <t1052>I don't believe they are better at her house.</t1052>
<t1053>––The person I am talking about is a gentleman and a stranger.</t1053>
<t1054>Mrs. Bennet's eyes sparkled like sparks.</t1054>
<t1055>––A gentleman and a stranger?</t1055> <t1056>It's Mr. Bingley, no doubt.</t1056> <t1057>Why do you never say a word about these things, Jane?</t1057> <t1058>How sly you are! Well, I will be very glad to see him.</t1058> <t1059>But, my God, what bad luck!</t1059> <t1060>Today, it's impossible to get even a bit of fish.</t1060> <t1061>Lydia, dear, ring the bell; I need to speak with Hill immediately.</t1061>
<t1062>––It is not Mr. Bingley ––said her husband––; it is a person I have never seen in my life.</t1062> <t1063>These words aroused general astonishment; and he had the pleasure of being anxiously questioned by his wife and his five daughters at once.</t1063>
<t1064>After having some fun, piquing their curiosity, he explained to them:</t1064>
<t1065>––A month ago I received this letter, and I replied to it about fifteen days ago, because I thought it was a very delicate matter and needed time to reflect.</t1065> <t1066>It is from my cousin, Mr. Collins, who, when I die, can throw you out of this house whenever he pleases.</t1066>
<t1067>––Oh, dear! ––lamented his wife––.</t1067> <t1068>I can't bear to hear about the subject.</t1068> <t1069>Don't mention that hateful man.</t1069> <t1070>It's the worst thing that can happen in the world, that your daughters can't inherit your property.</t1070> <t1071>If I were you, I would have done something about it a long time ago.</t1071>
<t1072>Jane and Elizabeth tried to explain to her why the inheritance did not belong to them.</t1072> <t1073>They had tried many times, but it was a topic that made their mother completely lose her reason; and she continued to complain bitterly about the cruelty of disinheriting a family of five daughters in favor of a man who meant nothing to any of them.</t1073>
<t1074>––Certainly, it is a very unfair matter ––said Mr. Bennet––, and there is nothing that can prove Mr. Collins' guilt for inheriting Longbourn.</t1074> <t1075>But if you listen to his letter, his way of expressing himself might calm you a bit.</t1075>
<t1076>––No, I won't listen to her; and besides, I find it impertinent that she writes to you, and hypocritical.</t1076> <t1077>I can't stand those false friends.</t1077> <t1078>Why doesn't she continue litigating with you like her father did?</t1078>
<t1079>––Because it seems to have some pangs of conscience, as you are about to hear:</t1079>
<t1080>Hunsford, near Westerham, Kent, October 15.</t1080>
<t1081>Dear Sir,</t1081>
<t1082>The ongoing disagreement between you and my recently deceased father has always made me feel somewhat uneasy, and since I had the misfortune of losing him, I have wished to settle the matter. However, for some time, I was held back by doubts, fearing it would be disrespectful to his memory to make amends with someone he was always at odds with, so soon after his death.</t1082> <t1083>But now I have made a decision on the matter, having been ordained at Easter, as I have been fortunate enough to be distinguished with the patronage of the very honorable Lady Catherine de Bourgh, widow of Sir Lewis de Bourgh, whose generosity and beneficence have chosen me to take charge of the esteemed rectory of her parish, where my firmest purpose will be to serve Her Ladyship with gratitude and respect, and to always be ready to perform the rites and ceremonies instituted by the Church of England.</t1083> <t1084>Moreover, as a clergyman, I believe it is my duty to promote and establish the blessing of peace in all the families within my influence; and based on this, I hope that my present intention of goodwill will be well received, and that the fact that I am the heir to Longbourn will be forgotten by you and will not lead you to reject the olive branch I offer.</t1084> <t1085>I cannot help but be concerned about causing harm to your pleasant daughters, and I beg to be excused for this. I also want to attest to my good disposition to make all possible amends from now on.</t1085> <t1086>If you do not object to receiving me in your home, I hope to have the satisfaction of visiting you and your family on Monday, November 18th at four o'clock, and I may take advantage of your hospitality until the following Saturday, which I can do without any inconvenience, as Lady Catherine de Bourgh will not object and would not even disapprove of my being absent fortuitously on Sunday, as long as there is another clergyman ready to fulfill the duties of that day.</t1086> <t1087>I send affectionate regards to your wife and daughters, your friend who wishes you all the best,</t1087>
<t1088>William Collins.</t1088>
<t1089>––Therefore, at four o'clock it is possible that this conciliatory gentleman will appear ––said Mr. Bennet while folding the letter––.</t1089> <t1090>He seems to be a well-mannered and attentive young man; I have no doubt that his friendship will be valuable to us, especially if Lady Catherine is indulgent enough to allow him to come visit us.</t1090>
<t1091>––You see, it seems that what he says about our daughters makes sense.</t1091> <t1092>If he is willing to make amends, I won't be the one to discourage him.</t1092>
<t1093>––Although it is difficult ––observed Jane–– to guess what he means by that reparation he thinks we deserve, we must give credit to his intentions.</t1093>
<t1094>Elizabeth was greatly impressed by that extraordinary deference towards Lady Catherine and that earnest intention to baptize, marry, and bury his parishioners whenever necessary.</t1094>
<t1095>––It must be a bit strange ––he said––.</t1095> <t1096>I can't imagine it.</t1096> <t1097>His style is somewhat pompous.</t1097> <t1098>And what does he mean by apologizing for being the heir of Longbourn?</t1098> <t1099>I suppose he wouldn't try to avoid it, if he could.</t1099> <t1100>Dad, could he be a cunning man?</t1100>
<t1101>––No, dear, I don't think so.</t1101> <t1102>I have great hopes that it will be the opposite.</t1102> <t1103>There is a mixture of servility and presumption in his letter that confirms it.</t1103> <t1104>I am eager to see him.</t1104>
<t1105>––As for the writing ––said Mary––, your letter doesn't seem to have any flaws.</t1105> <t1106>The olive branch part isn't very original, but even so, it is well expressed.</t1106>
<t1107>Catherine and Lydia were not in the least interested in either the letter or its author.</t1107> <t1108>It was practically impossible for their cousin to show up in a scarlet coat, and it had been a few weeks since they had felt any fondness for any man dressed in another color.</t1108> <t1109>As for their mother, Mr. Collins's letter had extinguished her resentment, and she was prepared to receive him with such moderation that it would leave her husband and daughters perplexed.</t1109>
<t1110>Mr. Collins arrived punctually at the announced time and was received with great courtesy by the whole family.</t1110> <t1111>Mr. Bennet spoke little, but the ladies were very willing to talk, and Mr. Collins did not seem to need encouragement nor was he fond of silence.</t1111> <t1112>He was a man of twenty-five years of age, tall, with a deep gaze, a grave and static air, and ceremonious manners.</t1112> <t1113>Shortly after sitting down, he congratulated Mrs. Bennet on having such beautiful daughters; he said he had heard much about their beauty, but that the fame fell short compared to reality; and he added that he did not doubt he would see them all married in due time.</t1113> <t1114>The gallantry was not to the liking of all the listeners; but Mrs. Bennet, who was not one for formalities, replied immediately:</t1114>
<t1115>––You are very kind, and I sincerely hope that it is as you say, because otherwise the poor ones would be quite helpless, given the strange way things are arranged.</t1115>
<t1116>––Are you perhaps referring to the inheritance of this property?</t1116>
<t1117>––Ah! Indeed, sir.</t1117> <t1118>You cannot deny that it is a very distressing matter for my daughters.</t1118> <t1119>I do not blame you; you know that in this world these things are just a matter of luck.</t1119> <t1120>No one has any notion of what will happen with the properties once they have to be inherited.</t1120>
<t1121>––I deeply regret the misfortune of your lovely daughters; but I will be cautious, I do not want to rush and seem hasty.</t1121> <t1122>What I can assure these young ladies is that I have come prepared to admire them.</t1122> <t1123>For now, I will say no more, but perhaps, when we get to know each other better...</t1123>
<t1124>He was interrupted to be invited to the dining room; and the young ladies smiled at each other.</t1124> <t1125>Not only were they the object of Mr. Collins' admiration: he examined and praised the hall, the dining room, and all the furniture; and the praises he gave to everything would have touched Mrs. Bennet's heart, if it weren't for the fact that she was mortified thinking that Collins saw all of it as his future property.</t1125> <t1126>He also praised the dinner and begged to be told which of his beautiful cousins was responsible for preparing it.</t1126> <t1127>But here, Mrs. Bennet interrupted him bluntly, telling him that her means allowed her to have a good cook and that her daughters had nothing to do in the kitchen.</t1127> <t1128>He apologized for having bothered her, and she, in a very gentle tone, told him that she was not at all offended.</t1128> <t1129>But Collins continued apologizing for almost a quarter of an hour.</t1129>
<t1130>CHAPTER XIV</t1130>
<t1131>Mr. Bennet barely spoke during dinner; but when the servants had already withdrawn, he thought the opportune moment had arrived to converse with his guest.</t1131><t1132>He began with a topic he believed would be to his liking, and told him that he had been very fortunate with his patroness.</t1132><t1133>Lady Catherine de Bourgh's attention to his wishes and her concern for his well-being were extraordinary.</t1133><t1134>Mr. Bennet could not have chosen anything better.</t1134><t1135>Mr. Collins praised Lady Catherine with great eloquence.</t1135><t1136>The subject elevated the usual solemnity of his manners, and, giving himself much importance, he asserted that he had never seen behavior like hers in a person of her rank, nor such affability and condescension.</t1136><t1137>She had deigned to give her approval to the two sermons he had already had the honor of delivering in her presence; she had invited him to dine twice at Rosings, and just the previous Saturday had sent for him to complete her quadrille game during the evening.</t1137><t1138>He knew many people who considered Lady Catherine to be proud, but he had never seen in her anything but affability.</t1138><t1139>She always spoke to him as she would to any other gentleman; she did not object to his associating with the people of the neighborhood, nor to his leaving the parish for one or two weeks to visit his relatives.</t1139><t1140>She always saw fit to recommend that he marry as soon as possible, provided he chose wisely, and she had visited him at his humble home, where she approved of all the changes he had made, even going so far as to suggest some herself, such as, for example, putting some shelves in the closets of the upstairs rooms.</t1140>
<t1141>––All that is very well and very courteous on her part ––commented Mrs. Bennet––.</t1141> <t1142>She must be a very pleasant woman.</t1142> <t1143>It's a pity that great ladies in general do not resemble her much.</t1143> <t1144>Does she live near you?</t1144>
<t1145>––Rosings Park, Her Ladyship's residence, is only separated by a road from the estate where my humble abode is situated.</t1145>
<t1146>––I believe you said you were a widow.</t1146> <t1147>Do you have family?</t1147>
<t1148>––He has only one daughter, the heiress of Rosings and other vast estates.</t1148>
<t1149>––Oh! ––sighed Mrs. Bennet, shaking her head––.</t1149> <t1150>She is in a better situation than many other young women.</t1150> <t1151>What kind of girl is she? Is she pretty?</t1151>
<t1152>––She is truly a charming young lady.</t1152> <t1153>Lady Catherine herself says that, to be honest, in terms of beauty, she far surpasses the most beautiful of her sex; because there is something in her features that reveals her distinguished birth.</t1153> <t1154>Unfortunately, she is of a sickly constitution, which has prevented her from progressing in certain aspects of her education that, if not for this, would be very remarkable, according to the lady who directed her education and who still lives with them.</t1154> <t1155>But she is very kind and often has the goodness to stop by my humble residence with her little phaeton and her ponies.</t1155>
<t1156>––Has she been introduced to society yet?</t1156> <t1157>I don't remember hearing her name among the ladies of the court.</t1157>
<t1158>––The poor state of his health has unfortunately not allowed him to go to the capital, and because of this, as I once told Lady Catherine, he has deprived the British court of its most radiant ornament.</t1158> <t1159>Her Ladyship seemed very flattered by this appreciation; and you can understand that I take pleasure in directing, whenever I have the chance, these small and delicate compliments that are usually pleasing to the ladies.</t1159> <t1160>More than once I have pointed out to Lady Catherine that her charming daughter seemed born to be a duchess and that the highest rank, instead of giving her importance, would be elevated by her.</t1160> <t1161>These kinds of little things are what please Her Ladyship and I consider myself especially obliged to have such attentions with her.</t1161>
<t1162>––You judge very well ––said Mr. Bennet––, and it is fortunate that you have the talent to flatter with delicacy.</t1162> <t1163>May I ask if those pleasant compliments occur to you spontaneously or if they are the result of prior study?</t1163>
<t1164>––Normally they come to me on the spot, and although sometimes I take the time to meditate and prepare these small and elegant compliments to be able to adapt them to the occasions that arise, I always try to give them a tone that is as unstudied as possible.</t1164>
<t1165>Mr. Bennet's assumptions had been confirmed.</t1165> <t1166>His cousin was as absurd as he believed.</t1166> <t1167>He listened to him with intense pleasure, maintaining, however, the most perfect composure; and, except for the occasional glance he cast at Elizabeth, no one else needed to share in his joy.</t1167>
<t1168>However, by tea time he had had enough, and Mr. Bennet had the pleasure of taking his guest back to the drawing room.</t1168> <t1169>When tea was over, he invited him to read something aloud to the ladies.</t1169> <t1170>Collins agreed immediately and they brought a book; but as soon as he saw it ––it was immediately noticeable that it was from a circulating library–– he stopped, asked for forgiveness, and said he never read novels.</t1170> <t1171>Kitty looked at him in surprise and Lydia let out an exclamation.</t1171> <t1172>They brought other volumes and after some hesitation he chose Fordyce's sermons.</t1172> <t1173>He had barely opened the book when Lydia began to yawn, and before Collins, with monotonous solemnity, had read three pages, the girl interrupted him saying:</t1173>
<t1174>––Do you know, Mom, that Uncle Phillips is talking about dismissing Richard?</t1174> <t1175>And if he does, Colonel Forster will hire him.</t1175> <t1176>Aunt told me on Saturday.</t1176> <t1177>I will go to Meryton tomorrow to find out more and to ask when Mr. Denny is coming from the city.</t1177>
<t1178>The two older sisters begged Lydia to be quiet, but Collins, very offended, put down the book and exclaimed:</t1178>
<t1179>––I have often observed how little young women are interested in books on serious subjects, despite the fact that they were written for their benefit.</t1179> <t1180>I confess that it amazes me, for there can be nothing as advantageous for them as education.</t1180> <t1181>But I do not want to keep bothering my little cousin.</t1181>
<t1182>He approached Mr. Bennet and proposed a game of backgammon.</t1182> <t1183>Mr. Bennet accepted the challenge and found that he was acting very wisely by letting the girls amuse themselves with their frivolities.</t1183> <t1184>Mrs. Bennet and her daughters apologized profusely for Lydia's interruption and promised that it would not happen again if he wanted to continue reading.</t1184> <t1185>But Collins assured them that he was not angry with his cousin and that he could never interpret what she had done as an offense; and, sitting at another table with Mr. Bennet, he prepared to play backgammon.</t1185>
<t1186>CHAPTER XV</t1186>
<t1187>Mr. Collins was not an intelligent man, and the deficiencies of his nature had not been helped at all by his education or his social life.</t1187> <t1188>He spent most of his life under the authority of an uneducated and miserly father; and although he went to university, he only stayed there for the merely necessary courses and did not acquire any truly useful knowledge.</t1188> <t1189>The subjection with which his father had raised him had initially given him great humility in his character, but this was now counteracted by a vanity obtained thanks to his limited intelligence, his reclusive life, and the feelings inherent to a sudden and unexpected prosperity.</t1189> <t1190>A fortunate chance had placed him under the patronage of Lady Catherine de Bourgh when the rectory of Hunsford became vacant, and his respect for the lady's high rank and the veneration she inspired in him for being his patroness, combined with a great self-concept, his authority as a clergyman, and his rights as rector, had turned him into a mixture of pride and servility, of presumption and modesty.</t1190>
<t1191>Since he now already owned a good house and more than sufficient income, Collins was thinking about getting married.</t1191> <t1192>In his reconciliation with the Longbourn family, he sought the possibility of realizing his plan, as he intended to choose one of the daughters, provided they were as beautiful and pleasant as was said.</t1192> <t1193>This was his plan of amendment, or reparation, for inheriting the father's properties, a plan that seemed excellent to him, as it was legitimate, very appropriate, and at the same time very generous and selfless on his part.</t1193>
<t1194>His plan did not change at all upon seeing them.</t1194> <t1195>Jane's charming face confirmed his intentions and corroborated all his strict notions about the preference that should be given to the elder daughters; and thus, during the first evening, he definitively decided on her.</t1195> <t1196>However, the next morning he had to make an alteration; for before breakfast, he had a quarter-hour conversation with Mrs. Bennet.</t1196> <t1197>They began by talking about his parsonage, which naturally led him to confess his hopes of finding in Longbourn the one who was to be its mistress.</t1197> <t1198>Amidst complacent smiles and general encouragements, Mrs. Bennet gave him a warning about Jane: "As for the younger daughters, it was not for her to argue; she could not answer positively, though she did not know that anyone had made them proposals; but regarding Jane, she had to warn him, though, after all, it was something that concerned only her, that she might soon be engaged."</t1198>
<t1199>Collins only had to replace Jane with Elizabeth; and, spurred on by Mrs. Bennet, he made the change quickly.</t1199> <t1200>Elizabeth, who followed Jane in age and beauty, was the new candidate.</t1200>
<t1201>Mrs. Bennet took note of it, and she hoped that soon she would have two married daughters.</t1201> <t1202>The man she didn't even want to hear about the day before suddenly became the object of her highest esteem.</t1202>
<t1203>Lydia's plan to go to Meryton was still on.</t1203> <t1204>All the sisters, except Mary, agreed to go with her.</t1204> <t1205>Mr. Collins was going to accompany them at Mr. Bennet's request, who was eager to get rid of his relative and have the library all to himself; for Mr. Collins had followed him there after breakfast and would continue to do so, apparently occupied with one of the largest folios in the collection, though in reality, incessantly talking to Mr. Bennet about his house and garden at Hunsford.</t1205> <t1206>Such things greatly unsettled him.</t1206> <t1207>The library was the place where he knew he could enjoy his free time in peace.</t1207> <t1208>He was willing, as he told Elizabeth, to endure stupidity and conceit in any other room of the house, but in the library, he wanted to be free of all that.</t1208> <t1209>So he employed all his courtesy in inviting Collins to accompany his daughters on their walk; and Collins, who was much better at walking than reading, saw the heavens open.</t1209> <t1210>He closed the book and left.</t1210>
<t1211>And between pompous and insipid phrases on his part, and courteous nods on the part of his cousins, time passed until they reached Meryton.</t1211> <t1212>From then on, the younger sisters no longer paid him any attention.</t1212> <t1213>They had eyes only for seeking officers in the streets.</t1213> <t1214>And unless it was a truly elegant hat or a genuinely new muslin, nothing could distract them.</t1214>
<t1215>But the attention of all the young ladies was instantly captured by a young man they had not seen before, who had the appearance of being quite the gentleman, and who was walking with an officer on the opposite side of the street.</t1215> <t1216>The officer was Mr. Denny himself, whose return from London Lydia had come to inquire about, and who bowed to greet them as he passed.</t1216> <t1217>They were all impressed by the stranger's bearing and wondered who he could be.</t1217> <t1218>Kitty and Lydia, determined to find out, crossed the street under the pretext that they wanted to buy something at the shop opposite, reaching the sidewalk so fortunately that, at that very moment, the two gentlemen, on their way back, arrived at exactly the same spot.</t1218> <t1219>Mr. Denny addressed them directly and asked if he might introduce his friend, Mr. Wickham, who had come from London with him the day before and had kindly accepted a position in the Corps.</t1219> <t1220>This was the last straw, for belonging to the regiment was the only thing he lacked to complete his charm.</t1220> <t1221>His appearance spoke highly in his favor; he was handsome and slender, with very affable manners.</t1221> <t1222>After the introduction, Mr. Wickham initiated a conversation with great ease, with the utmost correctness and without pretensions.</t1222> <t1223>They were all still standing there pleasantly chatting when the sound of horses drew their attention, and they saw Darcy and Bingley, on their mounts, coming down the street.</t1223> <t1224>Upon recognizing the young ladies in the group, the two gentlemen approached them and began the customary greetings.</t1224> <t1225>Bingley spoke more than anyone, and Jane was the main subject of his conversation.</t1225> <t1226>At that moment, he said, they were on their way to Longbourn to see how she was; Darcy confirmed this with a nod and was trying not to fix his gaze on Elizabeth when suddenly they were both paralyzed upon seeing the stranger.</t1226> <t1227>Elizabeth, who saw both their faces as they looked at each other, was very surprised by the effect the encounter had on them.</t1227> <t1228>Both changed color, one turned pale and the other red.</t1228> <t1229>After a slight hesitation, Wickham raised his hand to his hat, to which Darcy deigned to respond.</t1229> <t1230>What could that mean?</t1230> <t1231>It was impossible to imagine, but it was also impossible not to feel a great curiosity to know.</t1231>
<t1232>A moment later, Bingley, who seemed not to have noticed what had happened, said goodbye and continued on with his friend.</t1232>
<t1233>Denny and Wickham continued walking with the girls until they reached Mr. Philips' house door, where they made the appropriate bows and left despite Lydia's insistent pleas for them to come in and despite Mrs. Philips opening the vestibule window and leaning out to vocally support the invitation.</t1233>
<t1234>Mrs. Philips was always delighted to see her nieces.</t1234> <t1235>The two eldest were especially well received due to their recent absence.</t1235> <t1236>She expressed her surprise at their quick return home, of which she would have known nothing, since they did not come back in their own carriage, had it not been for the chance encounter with Dr. Jones's young man, who told her that she no longer needed to send more medicines to Netherfield because the Miss Bennets had left.</t1236> <t1237>Then Jane introduced Mr. Collins to whom she dedicated all her attention.</t1237> <t1238>She welcomed him with the most exquisite courtesy, to which Collins responded with even more refinement, apologizing for having presented himself at her house without prior notice, although he felt proud that it was the relationship with her nieces that justified such an intrusion.</t1238> <t1239>Mrs. Philips was completely overwhelmed by such an excess of good manners.</t1239> <t1240>But she soon had to set aside this stranger, due to the exclamations and questions regarding the other.</t1240> <t1241>Mrs. Philips could not tell her nieces more than they already knew: that Mr. Denny had brought him from London and that he was going to stay at the county garrison with the rank of lieutenant.</t1241> <t1242>She added that she had been observing him while he walked down the street; and if Mr. Wickham had appeared then, both Kitty and Lydia would have also approached the window to watch him, but unfortunately, at that moment only a few officers passed by who, compared to the stranger, seemed "stupid and unpleasant fellows."</t1242> <t1243>Some of these officers were going to dine the next day with the Philips, and the aunt promised them that she would tell her husband to visit Wickham to invite him too, if the Longbourn family wanted to come in the evening.</t1243> <t1244>Thus it was agreed, and Mrs. Philips offered them to play the lottery and then have a hot dinner.</t1244> <t1245>The prospect of such delights was magnificent, and the girls left very happy.</t1245> <t1246>Collins apologized again upon leaving, and he was assured that it was unnecessary.</t1246>
<t1247>On the way home, Elizabeth told Jane what had happened between the two gentlemen, and although Jane would have defended them if she had noticed anything strange, in this case, like her sister, she could not explain such behavior.</t1247>
<t1248>Collins praised Mrs. Bennet by commending the manners and education of Mrs. Philips.</t1248> <t1249>He assured that apart from Lady Catherine and her daughter, he had never seen a more elegant woman, for not only did she receive him with the utmost courtesy, but she also included him in the invitation for the next evening, despite being a complete stranger to her.</t1249> <t1250>Of course, he knew he should attribute it to his relationship with them, but nevertheless, he had never been treated with such kindness in his life.</t1250>
<t1251>CHAPTER XVI</t1251>
<t1252>Since no objection was raised to the young women's commitment to their aunt and Mr. Collins's reservations about not leaving the Bennets for even a single evening during his visit were firmly rejected, at the appropriate time the carriage set off with him and his five cousins towards Meryton.</t1252> <t1253>Upon entering the Philips' drawing room, the girls had the satisfaction of learning that Wickham had accepted their uncle's invitation and was in the house.</t1253>
<t1254>After receiving this information, and when everyone had taken their seats, Collins was able to observe everything at his leisure; the dimensions and the furniture of the room caused him such admiration that he confessed to having believed he was in the summer dining room of Rosings.</t1254> <t1255>This comparison did not arouse any enthusiasm at first; but when Mrs. Philips heard from Collins' lips what Rosings was and who its owner was, when she heard the description of one of Lady Catherine's drawing rooms and learned that just the fireplace had cost eight hundred pounds, she appreciated the full value of that compliment and would hardly have been bothered if he had compared her drawing room to the housekeeper's room at the Bourghs'.</t1255>
<t1256>Collins amused himself by telling Mrs. Philips all about Lady Catherine's grandeur and her mansion, occasionally mentioning his humble home and the improvements he was making to it, until the gentlemen arrived.</t1256> <t1257>Collins found in Mrs. Philips an attentive listener whose good opinion of the rector increased with everything he explained to her, and she was already thinking about telling her neighbors all about it as soon as possible.</t1257> <t1258>To the young ladies, who could not stand their cousin and had nothing else to do but wish for a musical instrument at hand and examine the china imitations on the mantelpiece, the wait was becoming too long.</t1258> <t1259>But finally, the gentlemen appeared.</t1259> <t1260>When Wickham entered the room, Elizabeth noticed that she had neither paid attention to him before nor remembered him afterward with sufficient admiration.</t1260> <t1261>The county garrison officers generally enjoyed extraordinary prestige; they were very handsome, and the best of them were now at the present gathering.</t1261> <t1262>But Wickham, with his gallantry, his ease, and his graceful walk, was as superior to them as they were to the stout Uncle Philips, who entered the room last, reeking of port wine.</t1262>
<t1263>Mr. Wickham was the fortunate man to whom almost all female eyes turned; and Elizabeth was the fortunate woman beside whom he decided to take a seat.</t1263> <t1264>Wickham initiated the conversation in such a pleasant manner, despite limiting himself to saying that the night was damp and that it would probably rain a lot throughout the season, that Elizabeth realized that the most common, trivial, and worn-out topics can be interesting if said skillfully.</t1264>
<t1265>With rivals like Wickham and the other officers monopolizing the ladies' attention, Collins seemed to sink into insignificance.</t1265> <t1266>To the young women, he meant nothing.</t1266> <t1267>But Mrs. Philips still listened to him from time to time and made sure he never lacked coffee or pastries.</t1267>
<t1268>When the gaming tables were arranged, Collins saw an opportunity to return her attentions and sat down to play whist with her.</t1268>
<t1269>––I know little about this game now ––he said to him––, but I would like to learn it better, due to my situation in life.</t1269>
<t1270>Mrs. Philips thanked him for his condescension, but she could not understand those reasons.</t1270>
<t1271>Wickham did not play whist and was received with genuine enthusiasm at the other table, between Elizabeth and Lydia.</t1271> <t1272>At first, it seemed there was a danger that Lydia would completely monopolize him, as she liked to talk incessantly, but since she was also very fond of the lottery, she soon focused all her interest on the game and was too busy betting and exclaiming when prizes were won to be distracted by anything else.</t1272> <t1273>As everyone was concentrated on the game, Wickham could dedicate his time to talking with Elizabeth, and she was eager to listen to him, although she had no hope that he would tell her what she most wanted to know, the story of his relationship with Darcy.</t1273> <t1274>She did not even dare to mention his name.</t1274> <t1275>However, her curiosity was unexpectedly satisfied.</t1275> <t1276>It was Mr. Wickham himself who brought up the subject.</t1276> <t1277>He asked how far it was from Meryton to Netherfield, and after hearing Elizabeth's response and a few seconds of hesitation, he also wanted to know how long Mr. Darcy had been there.</t1277>
<t1278>––About a month ––answered Elizabeth.</t1278>
<t1279>And with eagerness that the topic wouldn't end there, he added:</t1279>
<t1280>––I believe that gentleman owns large estates in Derbyshire.</t1280>
<t1281>––Yes ––replied Wickham––, his estate is significant, it provides him with ten thousand pounds annually.</t1281> <t1282>No one better than I could give you authentic information about Mr. Darcy, for I have been particularly connected with his family since my childhood.</t1282>
<t1283>Elizabeth couldn't help but show her surprise.</t1283>
<t1284>––It will surprise you what I say, Miss Bennet, after having seen, as you probably did, the coldness of our meeting yesterday.</t1284> <t1285>Do you know Mr. Darcy well?</t1285>
<t1286>––More than I would wish ––replied Elizabeth affectionately––.</t1286> <t1287>I have spent four days in the same house as him and I find him very unpleasant.</t1287>
<t1288>––I have no right to say whether he is or isn't disagreeable ––continued Mr. Wickham––.</t1288> <t1289>I am not the most suitable person for that.</t1289> <t1290>I have known him for too long and too well to be a fair judge.</t1290> <t1291>It would be impossible for me to be impartial.</t1291> <t1292>But I believe that the opinion you have of him would surprise anyone, and you might not express it so categorically anywhere else.</t1292> <t1293>Here you are among your own.</t1293>
<t1294>––I give you my word that what I say here I would say in any other house in the neighborhood, except in Netherfield.</t1294> <t1295>Darcy has disgusted everyone with his pride.</t1295> <t1296>You will not find anyone who speaks well of him.</t1296>
<t1297>––I cannot pretend that I am sorry ––said Wickham after a brief pause––.</t1297> <t1298>I do not feel that he or anyone else should be esteemed solely for their merits, but with Darcy, it usually does not happen that way.</t1298> <t1299>People are blinded by his fortune and his importance or fear him for his distinguished and haughty manners, and they see him only as he wishes to be seen.</t1299>
<t1300>––Well, despite the little I know him, I consider him a bad person.</t1300>
<t1301>Wickham merely nodded his head.</t1301> <t1302>Then he added: "I wonder if he plans to stay in this county for long."</t1302>
<t1303>––I have no idea; but I didn't hear anything about him leaving while he was at Netherfield.</t1303> <t1304>I hope Darcy's presence won't alter his plans to stay at the county garrison.</t1304>
<t1305>––Of course not.</t1305> <t1306>I will not be the one to leave because of Mr. Darcy, and it always saddens me to see him, but I have only one reason to avoid him and I can proclaim it in front of everyone: a painful regret for his mistreatment and for being the way he is.</t1306> <t1307>His father, Miss Bennet, the late Mr. Darcy, was the best of men and my best friend; I cannot speak with Darcy without my heart breaking with a thousand tender memories.</t1307> <t1308>His conduct towards me has been improper; but I sincerely confess that I would forgive him everything except that he has frustrated his father's hopes and dishonored his memory.</t1308>
<t1309>Elizabeth found that her interest was increasing and she listened with all her senses, but the delicate nature of the matter prevented her from asking more questions.</t1309>
<t1310>Wickham began to talk about more general topics: Meryton, the neighborhood, the society; and he seemed extremely pleased with what he already knew, speaking especially of the latter with gentle but understandable gallantry.</t1310>
<t1311>––The main incentive for my joining the county garrison ––continued Wickham–– was the hope of being in constant contact with society, and people of good society.</t1311> <t1312>I knew it was a very respected and pleasant Corps, and my friend Denny tempted me further by describing his current residence and the great attentions and excellent friendships he has found in Meryton.</t1312> <t1313>I confess that I need a bit of social life.</t1313> <t1314>I am a disappointed man and my state of mind could not bear solitude.</t1314> <t1315>I need occupation and company.</t1315> <t1316>It was not my intention to join military life, but current circumstances made me choose it.</t1316> <t1317>The Church should have been my profession; I was educated for it and today I would be in possession of a valuable rectory if it had not been for the gentleman I was talking about a moment ago.</t1317>
<t1318>––Really?</t1318>
<t1319>––Yes; the late Mr. Darcy had arranged for me to be presented with the best ecclesiastical benefice on his estates.</t1319> <t1320>He was my godfather and loved me dearly.</t1320> <t1321>I can never do justice to his kindness.</t1321> <t1322>He wanted to leave me well situated, and he believed he had done so; but when the position became vacant, it was granted to someone else.</t1322>
<t1323>––My God! ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t1323> <t1324>But how could that be?</t1324> <t1325>How could they contradict his will?</t1325> <t1326>Why didn't you seek justice?</t1326>
<t1327>––There was so much informality in the terms of the legacy that the law would not have given me any hope.</t1327> <t1328>A man of honor would not have doubted the intention of those terms; but Darcy preferred to doubt it or take it as a merely conditional recommendation and asserted that I had lost all my rights due to my extravagance and imprudence; in short, either for one reason or another, the fact is that the rectory became vacant two years ago, just when I was old enough to occupy it, and it was given to someone else; and it is no less true that I cannot blame myself for having done anything to deserve losing it.</t1328> <t1329>I have a fiery temperament, I am indiscreet, and perhaps I have expressed my opinion about Darcy a few times, and even to him, with excessive frankness.</t1329> <t1330>I do not remember anything else I could be accused of.</t1330> <t1331>But the fact is that we are very different and that he hates me.</t1331>
<t1332>––It's shameful!</t1332> <t1333>He deserves to be discredited in public.</t1333>
<t1334>––One day or another his time will come, but it won't be me who discredits him.</t1334> <t1335>As long as I can't forget his father, I will never be able to challenge or unmask him.</t1335>
<t1336>Elizabeth honored him for such sentiments and found him more attractive than ever as he expressed them.</t1336>
<t1337>––But ––he continued after a pause––, what could be the reason?</t1337> <t1338>What could have induced him to act with such cruelty?</t1338>
<t1339>––A deep and energetic antipathy towards me that I can only attribute to jealousy to a certain extent.</t1339> <t1340>If the late Mr. Darcy had not been so fond of me, his son would have tolerated me better.</t1340> <t1341>But the extraordinary affection that his father felt for me irritated him, I believe, from his earliest childhood.</t1341> <t1342>He did not have the character to withstand the kind of rivalry in which we found ourselves, nor the preference that his father often showed me.</t1342>
<t1343>––I remember that one day, in Netherfield, he was boasting about the relentlessness of his feelings and having an unforgiving character.</t1343> <t1344>His way of being is dreadful.</t1344>
<t1345>––I must not speak of this topic, replied Wickham––; I find it difficult to be fair with him.</t1345>
<t1346>Elizabeth reflected again and after a few moments exclaimed:</t1346>
<t1347>––To treat the godson, the friend, the father's favorite in that way!</t1347>
<t1348>He could have added: "To a young man, moreover, like you, whose face alone offers ample proof of your kindness."</t1348> <t1349>But he limited himself to saying:</t1349>
<t1350>––To a man who was surely the companion of his childhood and with whom, as I believe you have said, he had close ties.</t1350>
<t1351>––We were born in the same parish, on the same estate; we spent most of our youth together, living in the same house, sharing games, and being the object of the same parental care.</t1351> <t1352>My father started in the profession in which it seems his uncle, Mr. Philips, has achieved so much prestige; but he left it all to serve Mr. Darcy and dedicated all his time to managing the Pemberley estate.</t1352> <t1353>Mr. Darcy held him in high esteem and he was his trusted man and closest friend.</t1353> <t1354>Mr. Darcy himself often acknowledged that he owed much to my father's active supervision, and when, shortly before he died, Mr. Darcy spontaneously promised to take care of me, I am convinced he did so to repay my father a debt of gratitude as well as out of the affection he had for me.</t1354>
<t1355>––How strange! ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t1355> <t1356>How abominable!</t1356> <t1357>It astonishes me that Mr. Darcy's own pride hasn't compelled him to be just with you.</t1357> <t1358>Because, even if only for that reason, he is too proud not to be honorable; and dishonesty is what I must call what he has done to you.</t1358>
<t1359>It's curious ––answered Wickham––, because almost all of his actions have been guided by pride, which has often been his best advisor.</t1359> <t1360>For him, it is more connected to virtue than any other feeling.</t1360> <t1361>But neither of us are consistent; and in his behavior towards me, there were impulses even stronger than pride.</t1361>
<t1362>––Is it possible that such a detestable pride as his has ever led him to do some good?</t1362> <t1363>––Yes; it has often led him to be liberal and generous, to give his money freely, to be hospitable, to help his tenants, and to aid the poor.</t1363> <t1364>Family pride, his pride as a son, because he is very proud of what his father was, has made him act in this way.</t1364> <t1365>The desire to show that he did not fall short of his own, that he was no less esteemed than they were, and that he did not diminish the influence of the house of Pemberley, was a powerful motive for him.</t1365> <t1366>He also has a brotherly pride which, combined with some fraternal affection, has made him a most kind and solicitous guardian of Miss Darcy, and you will often hear it said that he is considered the most attentive and best of brothers.</t1366>
<t1367>––What kind of girl is Miss Darcy?</t1367>
<t1368>Wickham nodded.</t1368>
<t1369>––I wish I could say she is charming.</t1369> <t1370>It pains me to speak ill of a Darcy.</t1370> <t1371>But now she resembles her brother too much, she is very proud.</t1371> <t1372>As a child, she was very affectionate and accommodating and had great affection for me.</t1372> <t1373>Oh, the hours I have spent entertaining her!</t1373> <t1374>But now she is indifferent to me.</t1374> <t1375>She is a beautiful girl of fifteen or sixteen years, I believe very well-educated.</t1375> <t1376>Since her father's death, she has been living in London with a governess.</t1376>
<t1377>After many pauses and many attempts to talk about other topics, Elizabeth could not help but return to the first subject, and said:</t1377>
<t1378>––What amazes me is his friendship with Mr. Bingley.</t1378> <t1379>How can Mr. Bingley, who is the very embodiment of good humor, and is, I am convinced, truly kind, have anything to do with a man like Mr. Darcy? How can they get along? Do you know Mr. Bingley?</t1379>
<t1380>––No, I don't know him.</t1380>
<t1381>––He is a charming man, kind, with a sweet disposition.</t1381> <t1382>You must not know what Mr. Darcy is really like.</t1382>
<t1383>––Probably not; but Mr. Darcy knows how to please when he feels like it.</t1383> <t1384>He doesn't need to make an effort.</t1384> <t1385>He can be a pleasant conversationalist if he thinks it's worth his while.</t1385> <t1386>Among people of his own rank, he is very different from how he is with those of lower status.</t1386> <t1387>Pride never leaves him, but with the wealthy, he adopts a liberal mindset; he is fair, sincere, reasonable, honorable, and perhaps even pleasant, partly due to his fortune and good looks.</t1387>
<t1388>Shortly after, the whist game ended and the players gathered around the other table.</t1388> <t1389>Collins positioned himself between his cousin Elizabeth and Mrs. Philips.</t1389> <t1390>The latter asked him the usual questions about the outcome of the game.</t1390> <t1391>It was not much; he had lost all the points.</t1391> <t1392>But when Mrs. Philips began to tell him how sorry she was, Collins assured her with the utmost seriousness that it did not matter and that money was the least of his concerns, begging her not to worry about it.</t1392>
<t1393>––I know very well, madam ––he said––, that when one sits at a gaming table, one must submit to chance, and fortunately I am not in circumstances to have to worry about five shillings.</t1393> <t1394>Undoubtedly there are many who cannot say the same, but thanks to Lady Catherine de Bourgh, I am far from having to give importance to such trifles.</t1394>
<t1395>Wickham caught his attention, and after watching Collins for a few minutes, he quietly asked Elizabeth if her relative was a friend of the Bourgh family.</t1395>
<t1396>Lady Catherine de Bourgh has recently given him a rectory ––he replied––.</t1396> <t1397>I don't know very well who introduced them, but he hasn't known her for very long.</t1397> <t1398>––I suppose you know that Lady Catherine de Bourgh and Lady Anne Darcy were sisters, and that, consequently, Lady Catherine is the aunt of the current Mr. Darcy.</t1398> <t1399>––No, no idea; I didn't know anything about Lady Catherine's family.</t1399> <t1400>I had no notion of her existence until two days ago.</t1400>
<t1401>––Her daughter, Miss de Bourgh, will inherit a vast fortune, and it is said that she and her cousin will unite the two estates.</t1401>
<t1402>This news made Elizabeth smile at the thought of poor Miss Bingley.</t1402> <t1403>In vain were all her attentions, in vain and useless all her affection for Darcy's sister and all the praises she lavished on him if he was already destined for another.</t1403>
<t1404>––Mr. Collins ––said Elizabeth–– speaks very well of Lady Catherine and her daughter; but from some details he has shared about Her Ladyship, I suspect that gratitude blinds him and that, despite being his protector, she is an arrogant and vain woman.</t1404>
<t1405>––I believe it is both, and to a high degree ––Wickham replied––.</t1405> <t1406>I haven't seen her for many years, but I remember that I never liked her and that her manners were authoritarian and insolent.</t1406> <t1407>She has a reputation for being judicious and intelligent; but I get the feeling that part of her qualities derive from her rank and fortune; another part, from her despotism, and the rest, from the pride of her nephew who believes that everyone related to him must possess superior intelligence.</t1407>
<t1408>Elizabeth recognized that he had portrayed her very well, and they continued chatting together until dinner ended the game and allowed the other ladies to partake in Wickham's attentions.</t1408> <t1409>A conversation could not be started due to the noise made by Mr. Philips' guests; but his manners charmed everyone.</t1409> <t1410>Everything he said was well said and everything he did was well done.</t1410> <t1411>Elizabeth was captivated by him.</t1411> <t1412>On the way back home, she could think of nothing but Mr. Wickham and everything he had told her; but throughout the journey, she was not given a chance to even mention his name, as neither Lydia nor Mr. Collins stopped talking for a second.</t1412> <t1413>Lydia kept talking about the lottery, what she had lost, what she had won; and Collins, with praising the hospitality of the Philips, assuring that he didn't mind his losses in whist, listing all the dinner dishes, and constantly repeating that he feared his cousins were cramped because of him, had more to say than he could before the carriage stopped in front of the Longbourn house.</t1413>
