<t1414>CHAPTER XVII</t1414>
<t1415>The next day Elizabeth told Jane everything that she and Wickham had talked about.</t1415> <t1416>Jane listened with astonishment and interest.</t1416> <t1417>She could not believe that Darcy was so unworthy of Bingley's esteem; and yet, she did not dare to doubt the veracity of a man with such an affable appearance as Wickham.</t1417> <t1418>The mere possibility that he had suffered such cruelty was enough to stir her most tender feelings; so she had no choice but to think ill of neither, defend the conduct of both, and attribute to chance or error what otherwise could not be explained.</t1418>
<t1419>––I have the impression ––he said–– that both have been let down, they are people, somehow disappointed by something that we cannot guess.</t1419> <t1420>Perhaps it was people interested in distorting things who set them against each other.</t1420> <t1421>In any case, we cannot speculate on the causes or circumstances that have separated them without either being at fault.</t1421>
<t1422>––You are very right; and tell me, my dear Jane: What do you have to say in favor of those interested people who probably had something to do with the matter?</t1422> <t1423>Defend them too, otherwise we will be forced to speak ill of someone.</t1423>
<t1424>––Laugh at me all you want, but you won't make me change my mind.</t1424> <t1425>Dear Lizzy, consider what a dishonorable position it places Mr. Darcy in; to treat the favorite of his father this way, someone to whom he had promised a future.</t1425> <t1426>It is impossible.</t1426> <t1427>No one even moderately good, who values their conduct at all, is capable of doing it.</t1427> <t1428>Is it possible that his closest friends are so deceived about him?</t1428> <t1429>Oh, no!</t1429>
<t1430>––I believe it is easier for Mr. Bingley's friendship to be imposed than for Mr. Wickham to have invented such a story with names, facts, and to tell it so naturally.</t1430> <t1431>And if it is not so, let Mr. Darcy be the one to deny it.</t1431> <t1432>Besides, there was sincerity in his eyes.</t1432>
<t1433>––It's really difficult, it's unfortunate.</t1433> <t1434>One doesn't know what to think.</t1434>
<t1435>––Excuse me; one knows exactly what to think.</t1435>
<t1436>The two young women were chatting in the garden when they were informed of the arrival of some of the people they were just talking about.</t1436> <t1437>Mr. Bingley and his sisters were coming to personally invite them to the much-anticipated Netherfield ball that had been scheduled for the following Tuesday.</t1437> <t1438>The Bingley sisters were very happy to see their dear friend; it seemed to them that a century had passed since they had been together, and they continually asked her what had happened to her since their separation.</t1438> <t1439>They paid little attention to the rest of the family; they avoided Mrs. Bennet as much as they could, spoke very little with Elizabeth, and did not even address the others.</t1439> <t1440>They left immediately, rising from their seats with a speed that astonished their brother; they exited so quickly that they seemed eager to escape Mrs. Bennet's attentions.</t1440>
<t1441>The prospect of the Netherfield ball was extraordinarily appealing to all the female members of the family.</t1441> <t1442>Mrs. Bennet took it as a compliment to her eldest daughter and felt particularly flattered to have received the invitation from Mr. Bingley in person rather than through a ceremonious card.</t1442> <t1443>Jane imagined a happy evening in the company of her two friends and with the attentions of the brother, and Elizabeth thought with delight of dancing the whole time with Mr. Wickham and of seeing the entire story confirmed in Mr. Darcy's looks and behavior.</t1443> <t1444>The happiness that Catherine and Lydia anticipated depended less on a single fact or a particular person, because, although both, like Elizabeth, thought of dancing half the night with Wickham, he was by no means the only partner who could satisfy them, and, after all, a ball was a ball.</t1444> <t1445>Even Mary went so far as to assure her family that she did not dislike the idea of going either.</t1445>
<t1446>––As long as I can have the mornings for myself ––he said––, that's enough for me.</t1446> <t1447>It doesn't require any sacrifice on my part to occasionally accept evening commitments.</t1447> <t1448>We all owe something to society, and I confess that I am one of those who believe that intervals of recreation and relaxation are advisable for everyone.</t1448>
<t1449>Elizabeth was so excited about the occasion that, despite usually only speaking to Collins when necessary, she couldn't help but ask him if he intended to accept Mr. Bingley's invitation and, if so, whether he thought it appropriate to attend evening parties.</t1449> <t1450>Elizabeth was surprised when he replied that he had no objections in this regard and that he did not fear that the archbishop or Lady Catherine de Bourgh would censure him for venturing to the dance.</t1450>
<t1451>––I assure you that I absolutely do not believe ––he said–– that a ball like this, organized by a man of distinction for respectable people, could have anything wrong with it.</t1451> <t1452>I have no objection to dancing and I hope to have the honor of doing so with all my beautiful cousins.</t1452> <t1453>I take this opportunity to ask you, specifically you, Miss Elizabeth, for the first two dances, a preference which I trust my cousin Jane will attribute to the proper cause, and not to any disregard for her.</t1453>
<t1454>Elizabeth was completely disillusioned.</t1454> <t1455>She had intended to dedicate those two very special dances to Mr. Wickham!</t1455> <t1456>And now she had to dance them with Mr. Collins!</t1456> <t1457>She had chosen the wrong moment to become so happy.</t1457> <t1458>Well, what could she do?</t1458> <t1459>She had no choice but to postpone her happiness and Wickham's for a little later and accept Collins' proposal with the best possible attitude.</t1459> <t1460>She found no pleasure in his gallantry because there was something more hidden behind it.</t1460> <t1461>For the first time, it occurred to her that she was the chosen one among all her sisters to be the lady of the parsonage at Hunsford and to attend the quadrille parties at Rosings in the absence of more select visitors.</t1461> <t1462>This idea soon turned into conviction when she observed Collins' increasing attentions towards her and heard his frequent attempts to praise her wit and vivacity.</t1462> <t1463>Although the effect her charms had in this case left her more astonished than pleased, her mother soon made it clear that the possibility of that marriage pleased her excessively.</t1463> <t1464>However, Elizabeth preferred to ignore it, because she was sure that any reply would result in a serious argument.</t1464> <t1465>Probably Mr. Collins would never make such a proposal, and until he did, it was a waste of time to argue about him.</t1465>
<t1466>If they had not had to make preparations for the Netherfield ball, the younger Bennets would have reached a pitiable state, since from the day of the invitation until the day of the ball, the rain did not stop for a moment, preventing them from going to Meryton even once.</t1466> <t1467>No aunt, no officers, no gossip to share.</t1467> <t1468>Even the rose arrangements for the Netherfield ball had to be made to order.</t1468> <t1469>Elizabeth herself saw her patience tested by that bad weather which completely suspended the progress of her friendship with Wickham.</t1469> <t1470>Only the Tuesday ball could make a Friday, Saturday, Sunday, and Monday like those bearable for Catherine and Lydia.</t1470>
<t1471>CHAPTER XVIII</t1471>
<t1472>Until Elizabeth entered the Netherfield drawing-room and searched in vain among the group of red coats gathered there for Wickham, it did not occur to her that he might not be among the guests.</t1472> <t1473>The certainty of finding him had made her forget what would have rightly alarmed her.</t1473> <t1474>She had groomed herself with more care than usual and was prepared with a very high spirit to conquer whatever remained untamed in her heart, trusting that it was the best reward she could achieve during the evening.</t1474> <t1475>But in an instant, the horrible suspicion came over her that Wickham might have been omitted from Bingley's list of invited officers to please Darcy.</t1475> <t1476>That was not exactly the case.</t1476> <t1477>His absence was definitively confirmed by Mr. Denny, whom Lydia anxiously approached, and who told them that Mr. Wickham had been obliged to go to the capital to resolve some matters the day before and had not yet returned.</t1477> <t1478>And with a significant smile, he added:</t1478>
<t1479>––I don't believe those matters would have detained him today, unless he wanted to avoid meeting a certain gentleman here.</t1479>
<t1480>Lydia did not hear these words, but Elizabeth did; although her initial suspicion had not been true, Darcy was equally responsible for Wickham's absence, her antipathy towards the former was so exacerbated that she could barely respond politely to the kind questions Darcy asked her when he approached her shortly after.</t1480> <t1481>Any attention or tolerance towards Darcy meant an injury to Wickham.</t1481> <t1482>She decided not to have any conversation with Darcy and put herself in such a mood that she could not even conceal it when talking to Bingley, as his blind partiality irritated her.</t1482>
<t1483>But bad humor was not made for Elizabeth, and despite all her plans for the night being ruined, it soon passed.</t1483> <t1484>After telling her sorrows to Charlotte Lucas, whom she hadn't seen for a week, she soon found herself in the mood to tolerate all her cousin's oddities and approached him.</t1484> <t1485>However, the first two dances brought back her distress; they were like a penance.</t1485> <t1486>Mr. Collins, clumsy and solemn, apologizing instead of keeping to the rhythm, and losing the step without realizing it, gave her all the pity and embarrassment that an unpleasant partner can give in a couple of dances.</t1486> <t1487>Getting rid of him was like reaching ecstasy.</t1487>
<t1488>Then she had the relief of dancing with an officer with whom she could talk about Mr. Wickham, learning that everyone appreciated him.</t1488> <t1489>When this dance ended, she returned to Charlotte Lucas, and they were chatting when she suddenly realized that Mr. Darcy had approached her and was asking for the next dance; she was so surprised that, without knowing what she was doing, she accepted.</t1489> <t1490>Darcy left immediately, and she, having become very nervous, stayed there wishing to regain her composure.</t1490> <t1491>Charlotte tried to console her.</t1491>
<t1492>––Maybe you'll find it charming.</t1492>
<t1493>––May God forbid it!</t1493> <t1494>That would be the greatest of all misfortunes.</t1494> <t1495>To find charming a man who must be hated!</t1495> <t1496>Do not wish me such ill.</t1496>
<t1497>When the dance resumed, Darcy approached her to take her hand, and Charlotte couldn't help but whisper in her ear not to be foolish and not to let her infatuation with Wickham make her appear unpleasant in the eyes of a man who was worth ten times more than him.</t1497> <t1498>Elizabeth did not reply.</t1498> <t1499>She took her place on the dance floor, astonished by the dignity bestowed upon her by standing face to face with Darcy, reading in the eyes of all her neighbors the same astonishment at witnessing the event.</t1499> <t1500>They were silent for a while; Elizabeth began to think that the silence would last until the end of the two dances.</t1500> <t1501>At first, she was determined not to break it, when suddenly she thought that the worst punishment for her partner would be to force him to talk, and she made a small remark about the dance.</t1501> <t1502>Darcy replied and fell silent again.</t1502> <t1503>After a pause of a few minutes, Elizabeth spoke for the second time and said to him:</t1503>
<t1504>––Now it's your turn to say something, Mr. Darcy.</t1504> <t1505>I've already talked about the dance, and you should make some comment about the size of the hall and the number of couples.</t1505>
<t1506>He smiled and assured her that he would say everything she wished to hear.</t1506>
<t1507>––Very well.</t1507> <t1508>That response isn't bad for now.</t1508> <t1509>Maybe little by little I'll be convinced that private dances are more pleasant than public ones; but for now, we can remain silent.</t1509>
<t1510>––Do you usually talk while you dance?</t1510>
<t1511>––Sometimes.</t1511> <t1512>It's necessary to talk a little, don't you think?</t1512> <t1513>It would be strange to be together for half an hour without saying a word.</t1513> <t1514>But out of consideration for some, you have to steer the conversation so that they don't feel compelled to say more than necessary.</t1514>
<t1515>––Are you referring to yourself or are you saying it about me?</t1515>
<t1516>––For both ––replied Elizabeth coquettishly––, for I have found a great similarity in our ways of being.</t1516> <t1517>We are both unsociable, taciturn, and averse to speaking unless we expect to say something that will dazzle everyone present and go down in history with all the brilliance of a proverb.</t1517>
<t1518>––I am sure that you are not like that.</t1518> <t1519>As for me, I wouldn't know what to say.</t1519> <t1520>You, without a doubt, believe that you have made a faithful portrait of me.</t1520>
<t1521>––I cannot judge my own work.</t1521>
<t1522>He did not answer, and it seemed that they would not open their mouths again until the dance ended, when he asked her if she and her sisters often went to Meryton.</t1522> <t1523>Elizabeth answered affirmatively and, unable to resist the temptation, added:</t1523>
<t1524>––When you found us the other day, we had just met a new friend.</t1524> <t1525>The effect was immediate.</t1525> <t1526>An intense shadow of arrogance darkened Darcy's countenance.</t1526> <t1527>But he did not say a word; Elizabeth, though reproaching herself for her weakness, preferred not to continue.</t1527> <t1528>Finally, Darcy spoke and said in a forced manner:</t1528>
<t1529>––Mr. Wickham is endowed with such pleasant manners that he can certainly make friends easily.</t1529> <t1530>What is less certain is that he is equally capable of keeping them.</t1530>
<t1531>––He has had the misfortune of losing his friendship ––said Elizabeth emphatically––, in such a way that he will suffer for it all his life.</t1531>
<t1532>Darcy did not answer and it was noticeable that he was eager to change the subject.</t1532> <t1533>At that moment, Sir William Lucas was passing near them as he crossed the dance floor with the intention of going to the other end of the hall, and upon seeing Mr. Darcy, he stopped and made a bow with all courtesy to congratulate him on his way of dancing and his partner.</t1533>
<t1534>––I am extremely pleased, my esteemed sir, such an excellent way of dancing is not often seen.</t1534> <t1535>It is evident that you belong to the most distinguished circles.</t1535> <t1536>Allow me to say, however, that your beautiful partner is in no way inferior to you, and I hope to enjoy this pleasure again, especially when a certain much-desired event, dear Elizabeth (looking at Jane and Bingley), takes place.</t1536> <t1537>How many congratulations there will be then!</t1537> <t1538>I appeal to Mr. Darcy.</t1538> <t1539>But I do not want to interrupt you, sir.</t1539> <t1540>You will thank me for not depriving you further of the captivating conversation of this young lady whose beautiful eyes are also reproaching me.</t1540>
<t1541>Darcy barely heard this last part of her speech, but the allusion to his friend seemed to impress him greatly, and with a grave expression, he directed his gaze towards Bingley and Jane who were dancing together.</t1541> <t1542>Nevertheless, he quickly recovered and, turning to Elizabeth, said:</t1542>
<t1543>––Sir William's interruption has made me forget what we were talking about.</t1543>
<t1544>––I don't think we were talking.</t1544> <t1545>Sir William could not have interrupted another couple in the entire room who had less to say to each other.</t1545> <t1546>We have already tried two or three topics without success.</t1546> <t1547>I have no idea what we can talk about now.</t1547>
<t1548>––What do you think about books? ––he asked her, smiling.</t1548>
<t1549>––The books! Oh, no!</t1549> <t1550>I'm sure we never read the same ones or, at least, we don't get the same impressions.</t1550>
<t1551>––I regret that you think that; but if that were the case, anyway, we would not lack a topic.</t1551> <t1552>We can compare our various opinions.</t1552>
<t1553>––No, I can't talk about books in a ballroom.</t1553> <t1554>My mind is occupied with other things.</t1554>
<t1555>––In these places, one thinks only of the present, right? ––he said with a doubtful look.</t1555>
<t1556>––Yes, always ––she replied without knowing what she was saying, as her thoughts had wandered elsewhere, as she suddenly demonstrated by exclaiming––: I remember hearing you say once that you rarely forgave; that when you had conceived a resentment, it was impossible for you to appease it.</t1556> <t1557>I suppose, therefore, that you are very cautious in conceiving resentments...</t1557>
<t1558>––Indeed ––replied Darcy with a firm voice.</t1558> <t1559>––And do you never let yourself be blinded by prejudice?</t1559> <t1560>––I hope not.</t1560>
<t1561>––Those who never change their opinion must make sure before judging.</t1561>
<t1562>––Can I ask you what the intention of these questions is?</t1562>
<t1563>––To know his character, simply ––said Elizabeth, trying to conceal her seriousness––.</t1563> <t1564>I am trying to decipher it.</t1564>
<t1565>––And what conclusions have you reached?</t1565>
<t1566>––None ––he said, shaking his head––.</t1566> <t1567>I've heard such different things about you that I can't make sense of it.</t1567>
<t1568>––I acknowledge ––he replied gravely–– that opinions about me may vary greatly; and I would wish, Miss Bennet, that you do not sketch my character at this moment, because I have reasons to fear that the result would not reflect the truth.</t1568>
<t1569>––But if I don't do it now, I might not have another chance.</t1569>
<t1570>––By no means would I wish to prevent any of your satisfactions ––he replied coldly.</t1570>
<t1571>Elizabeth did not speak further, and when the dance ended, they parted in silence, both dissatisfied, though to different degrees, for in Darcy's heart there was a powerful feeling of tolerance towards her, which soon led him to forgive her and focus all his anger on another.</t1571>
<t1572>It wasn't long after they had parted ways when Miss Bingley approached Elizabeth and, with an expression of both kindness and disdain, said to her:</t1572>
<t1573>––So, Miss Eliza, you are delighted with Mr. Wickham.</t1573> <t1574>I have learned from your sister who has spoken to me about him and asked me a thousand questions.</t1574> <t1575>It seems to me that this young man forgot to tell you, among many other things, that he is the son of old Wickham, the last steward of Mr. Darcy.</t1575> <t1576>Let me advise you, as a friend, not to trust too much in everything he tells you, because the idea that Mr. Darcy treated him badly is completely false; on the contrary, he has always been extraordinarily kind to him, although George Wickham has behaved towards Mr. Darcy in the most infamous manner.</t1576> <t1577>I do not know the details, but I know very well that Mr. Darcy is in no way to blame, that he cannot bear to even hear the name of George Wickham, and that, although my brother considered that he could not avoid including him in the list of invited officers, he was greatly relieved to see that he himself had stepped aside.</t1577> <t1578>The mere fact that he has come here to the countryside is a true insolence, and I cannot understand how he dared to do so.</t1578> <t1579>I pity you, Miss Eliza, for this discovery of the guilt of your favorite; but in reality, considering his origin, nothing better could be expected.</t1579>
<t1580>––His guilt and his origin seem to be the same thing to you ––Elizabeth said angrily––; because the worst thing I have heard you accuse him of is being the son of Mr. Darcy's steward, and I can assure you, he had already informed me of that.</t1580>
<t1581>––I beg you to excuse me ––replied Miss Bingley, turning away with disdain––.</t1581> <t1582>Forgive my intrusion; it was with the best intention.</t1582>
<t1583>“Insolent!” said Elizabeth to herself.</t1583> <t1584>“You are very mistaken if you think you will influence me with such a petty attack.</t1584> <t1585>I see nothing in it but your stubborn ignorance and Darcy's malice.”</t1585>
<t1586>Then she looked at her older sister who had dared to question Bingley about the same matter.</t1586> <t1587>Jane returned her gaze with such a sweet smile, with an expression of happiness and so much satisfaction that it clearly indicated she was very pleased with what had happened during the evening.</t1587> <t1588>Elizabeth instantly read her feelings; and in a moment all her concern for Wickham, her hatred towards his enemies, and everything else disappeared before the hope that Jane was on the best path to her happiness.</t1588>
<t1589>"I want to know," said Elizabeth as smiling as her sister, "what you have heard about Mr. Wickham."</t1589> <t1590>"But perhaps you have been too busy with more pleasant things to think about a third person..."</t1590> <t1591>"If that has been the case, you can be sure that I forgive you."</t1591>
<t1592>––No ––answered Jane––, I have not forgotten him, but I have nothing pleasant to tell you.</t1592> <t1593>Mr. Bingley does not know the whole story and is unaware of the circumstances that have so offended Mr. Darcy, but he vouches for the good conduct, integrity, and honesty of his friend, and is firmly convinced that Mr. Wickham has received more attention from Mr. Darcy than he deserved; and I regret to say that, according to Mr. Bingley and his sister, Mr. Wickham is far from being a respectable young man.</t1593> <t1594>I fear he has been imprudent and well deserves to have lost Mr. Darcy's consideration.</t1594>
<t1595>––Does Mr. Bingley not know Mr. Wickham personally?</t1595>
<t1596>––No, I had never seen him before the other day in Meryton.</t1596>
<t1597>––So what you know is what Mr. Darcy has told you.</t1597> <t1598>I am satisfied.</t1598> <t1599>And what does he say about the rectory?</t1599>
<t1600>––He doesn't remember exactly how it was, although he has heard his friend tell it more than once; but he believes it was bequeathed to him only conditionally.</t1600>
<t1601>––I do not doubt Mr. Bingley's sincerity ––said Elizabeth heatedly––, but forgive me if his assertions do not convince me.</t1601> <t1602>He does well to defend his friend; but since he is unaware of some parts of the story and the only thing he knows is what he has been told, I will continue to think of the two gentlemen the same as I did before.</t1602>
<t1603>Having said this, both sisters started another conversation much more pleasant for both of them.</t1603> <t1604>Elizabeth listened delightedly to the happy yet modest hopes that Jane harbored regarding Bingley, and she said everything she could to encourage her confidence.</t1604> <t1605>When Mr. Bingley joined them, Elizabeth withdrew and went to talk with Miss Lucas, who asked her if she had liked her last partner.</t1605> <t1606>Elizabeth hardly had time to answer, because Collins presented himself to them, enthusiastically saying that he had been fortunate enough to make a very important discovery.</t1606>
<t1607>––I have learned ––he said––, by a singular chance, that there is in this room a close relative of my benefactress.</t1607> <t1608>I had the pleasure of hearing the gentleman himself mention to the lady who hosts this house the names of his cousin, Miss de Bourgh, and her mother, Lady Catherine.</t1608> <t1609>How wonderfully these things happen!</t1609> <t1610>Who would have told me that I would find a nephew of Lady Catherine de Bourgh at this gathering!</t1610> <t1611>I am very glad to have made this discovery in time to pay my respects, which I will do right now.</t1611> <t1612>I trust you will forgive me for not having done so earlier, but my total ignorance of this relationship excuses me.</t1612>
<t1613>––Aren't you going to introduce yourself to Mr. Darcy?</t1613>
<t1614>––Of course!</t1614> <t1615>I will ask him to excuse me for not having done it earlier.</t1615> <t1616>Don't you see that he is Lady Catherine's nephew?</t1616> <t1617>I will be able to tell him that Her Ladyship was very well the last time I saw her.</t1617>
<t1618>Elizabeth tried to dissuade him from doing such a thing by assuring him that Mr. Darcy would consider addressing him without a prior introduction as an impertinence and a boldness, rather than a compliment to his aunt; that there was no need to make himself known, and if there were, it would be up to Mr. Darcy, due to the superiority of his rank, to take the initiative.</t1618> <t1619>Collins listened to her, determined to follow his own impulses, and when Elizabeth stopped speaking, he replied:</t1619>
<t1620>––My dear Miss Elizabeth, I have the highest opinion in the world of your excellent judgment in all kinds of matters, as befits your intelligence; but allow me to tell you that there must be a great difference between the established forms of courtesy for laypeople and those accepted for clergymen; let me warn you that the office of clergyman is, in terms of dignity, equivalent to the highest rank in the kingdom, provided that those who hold it behave with the appropriate humility.</t1620> <t1621>So allow me to follow the dictates of my conscience which on this occasion lead me to do what I consider a duty.</t1621> <t1622>Excuse me, then, for not following your advice which in all other matters will constantly serve as my guide, but I believe that in this case I am more qualified, due to my education and habitual study, than a young lady like you, to decide what is proper.</t1622>
<t1623>Collins made a bow and walked away to greet Darcy.</t1623> <t1624>Elizabeth did not lose sight of him to see Darcy's reaction, whose astonishment at being approached in such a manner was evident.</t1624> <t1625>Collins began his speech with a solemn bow, and, although she could not hear him, it was as if she could, for she could read the words "apologies," "Hunsford," and "lady Catherine de Bourgh" on his lips.</t1625> <t1626>It irritated her that he would make a fool of himself in front of a man like Darcy.</t1626> <t1627>Darcy observed him without suppressing his astonishment, and when Collins allowed him to speak, he responded with distant courtesy.</t1627> <t1628>However, Collins was not discouraged and continued speaking.</t1628> <t1629>Darcy's disdain grew with the length of his second speech, and, in the end, he only made a slight bow and went elsewhere.</t1629> <t1630>Collins then turned back to Elizabeth.</t1630>
<t1631>––I assure you ––he said to her–– that I have no reason to be dissatisfied with the reception Mr. Darcy has given me.</t1631> <t1632>My attention has pleased him greatly and he has responded with the utmost politeness, even doing me the honor of stating that he was so convinced of Lady Catherine's good judgment that he took it for granted she would never bestow a favor unless it was deserved.</t1632> <t1633>It was truly a very beautiful phrase.</t1633> <t1634>In short, I am very pleased with him.</t1634>
<t1635>Elizabeth, who had no interest whatsoever in continuing to talk with Collins, devoted her attention almost entirely to her sister and Bingley; the multitude of pleasant thoughts that her observations gave rise to made her almost as happy as Jane.</t1635> <t1636>She imagined her settled in that great house with all the happiness that a marriage for true love can provide, and felt so delighted that she even believed that Bingley's two sisters might come to like her.</t1636> <t1637>It was not difficult for her to guess that her mother's thoughts followed the same path, and she decided not to risk approaching her to avoid hearing her comments.</t1637> <t1638>Unfortunately, at dinner time, they had to sit next to each other.</t1638> <t1639>Elizabeth was very upset to see how her mother kept talking to Lady Lucas, freely and openly, about her hope that Jane would soon marry Bingley.</t1639> <t1640>The topic was captivating, and Mrs. Bennet seemed never to tire of enumerating the advantages of that alliance.</t1640> <t1641>Just considering the youth of the groom, his attractiveness, his wealth, and the fact that he lived only three miles from Longbourn, made Mrs. Bennet happy.</t1641> <t1642>But in addition, one had to take into account how delighted Bingley's two sisters were with Jane, who would undoubtedly be as pleased with the union as she herself was.</t1642> <t1643>Moreover, Jane's marriage to someone of such high status was very promising for her younger daughters, who would thus have more opportunities to meet wealthy men.</t1643> <t1644>Finally, it was a relief, at her age, to be able to entrust her single daughters to the care of their sister, and not have to accompany them herself except when she felt like it.</t1644> <t1645>There was no choice but to take this circumstance as a reason for satisfaction, for in such cases, etiquette demands it; but there was no one who liked staying comfortably at home more at any time in her life.</t1645> <t1646>She concluded by wishing Lady Lucas that she would soon be as fortunate as she was, though triumphantly thinking that there was not much hope.</t1646>
<t1647>Elizabeth tried in vain to repress her mother's words and to convince her to express her joy a little more quietly; because, to her greater annoyance, she noticed that Darcy, who was sitting opposite them, was hearing almost everything.</t1647> <t1648>The only thing her mother did was scold her for being so foolish.</t1648>
<t1649>––What does Mr. Darcy mean to me?</t1649> <t1650>Tell me, why should I be afraid of him?</t1650> <t1651>We don't owe him any special attention to feel obliged not to say anything that might upset him.</t1651>
<t1652>––For the love of God, mom, speak more quietly!</t1652> <t1653>What do you gain by offending Mr. Darcy?</t1653> <t1654>The only thing you'll achieve, if you do, is to look bad in front of his friend.</t1654>
<t1655>But nothing he said had any effect.</t1655> <t1656>The mother continued to express her opinion with the same nonchalance.</t1656> <t1657>Elizabeth was becoming more and more flushed with embarrassment and displeasure.</t1657> <t1658>She couldn't stop looking at Darcy frequently, although each glance convinced her more of what she was fearing.</t1658> <t1659>Darcy rarely fixed his eyes on the mother, but Elizabeth had no doubt that his attention was on what was being said.</t1659> <t1660>The expression on his face gradually shifted from disdain and indignation to an unperturbed seriousness.</t1660>
<t1661>However, there came a moment when Mrs. Bennet had nothing more to say, and Lady Lucas, who had long been yawning at the repetition of delights in which she saw no possibility of participating, indulged in the pleasures of chicken and ham.</t1661> <t1662>Elizabeth breathed a sigh of relief.</t1662> <t1663>But this interval of tranquility did not last long; after dinner, there was talk of singing, and she had to endure the ordeal of seeing Mary, after very little persuasion, prepare to treat those present with her singing.</t1663> <t1664>With significant looks and silent pleas, Elizabeth tried to prevent this display of condescension, but it was useless.</t1664> <t1665>Mary could not understand what she meant.</t1665> <t1666>Such an opportunity to showcase her talent captivated her, and she began her song.</t1666> <t1667>Elizabeth kept watching her with a painful sensation, observing the progress of the concert with an impatience that was not rewarded in the end, for Mary, upon receiving a slight hint to continue among the expressions of gratitude from her audience, after a minute's pause, began another song.</t1667> <t1668>Mary's abilities were not the most suitable for such an exhibition; she had little voice and an affected style.</t1668> <t1669>Elizabeth endured a true agony.</t1669> <t1670>She looked at Jane to see how she was bearing it, but she was talking calmly with Bingley.</t1670> <t1671>She looked at his sisters and saw that they were making mocking gestures to each other, and at Darcy, who remained serious and unperturbed.</t1671> <t1672>Finally, she looked at her father, imploring his intervention to prevent Mary from singing all night.</t1672> <t1673>He took the hint and when Mary finished her second song, he said loudly:</t1673>
<t1674>––Girl, that's enough.</t1674> <t1675>You've done very well, you've delighted us quite enough; now let the other young ladies shine.</t1675>
<t1676>Mary, although she pretended not to hear, was a little disconcerted.</t1676> <t1677>Elizabeth felt sorry for her and regretted that her father had said that.</t1677> <t1678>She realized that due to her uneasiness, she had not acted well at all.</t1678> <t1679>Now it was someone else's turn to sing.</t1679>
<t1680>––If I ––said Collins then–– had the good fortune to be apt for singing, I would very much like to entertain the audience with a romance.</t1680> <t1681>I consider music to be an innocent distraction and completely compatible with the clerical profession.</t1681> <t1682>By this, I do not mean to say that it is right to devote too much time to music, for there are, of course, other things to attend to.</t1682> <t1683>The rector of a parish has a lot of work.</t1683> <t1684>First of all, he has to make an adjustment of the tithes that is beneficial to him and not burdensome to his patron.</t1684> <t1685>He has to write the sermons, and the time left is never enough for the parish duties and for the care and improvement of his parishioners whose lives he is obliged to make as bearable as possible.</t1685> <t1686>And I consider it of great importance that he be attentive and conciliatory with everyone, especially with those to whom he owes his position.</t1686> <t1687>I consider this indispensable and cannot hold in good esteem the man who would waste the opportunity to pay his respects to anyone related to the family of his benefactors.</t1687>
<t1688>And with a bow to Mr. Darcy, he concluded his speech, spoken so loudly that half the room heard it.</t1688> <t1689>Many stared at him intently, many smiled, but no one was as amused as Mr. Bennet, while his wife seriously praised Collins for having spoken so sensibly, and whispered to Lady Lucas that he was a very good person and extremely clever.</t1689>
<t1690>To Elizabeth, it seemed that if her family had agreed to make themselves as ridiculous as possible that night, they could not have done it better nor achieved such success; and she was very glad that Bingley and his sister had not witnessed most of the spectacle and that Bingley was not the kind of person who cared about or was bothered by the madness he might have witnessed.</t1690> <t1691>It was bad enough that the sisters and Darcy had the opportunity to mock her family; and she did not know what she found more intolerable: Darcy's silent contempt or the ladies' insolent smirks.</t1691>
<t1692>The rest of the night passed for her without the slightest interest.</t1692> <t1693>Collins drove her crazy with his insistence on not leaving her side.</t1693> <t1694>Although he did not manage to convince her to dance with him again, he prevented her from dancing with others.</t1694> <t1695>It was useless for her to beg him to go chat with other people and to offer to introduce him to some young ladies at the party.</t1695> <t1696>Collins assured her that dancing did not matter to him and that his main desire was to make himself agreeable to her with delicate attentions, so he had decided to stay by her side all night.</t1696> <t1697>There was nothing to argue about such a plan.</t1697> <t1698>Her friend Miss Lucas was the only one who consoled her by frequently sitting by her side and diverting Collins' conversation towards her.</t1698>
<t1699>At least in this way she was free of Darcy, who, although sometimes found himself at a short distance from them completely unoccupied, did not approach to speak to them.</t1699> <t1700>Elizabeth attributed it to the result of her allusions to Wickham and was glad of it.</t1700>
<t1701>The Longbourn family was the last to leave.</t1701> <t1702>Mrs. Bennet managed to make them wait for the carriages for a quarter of an hour after everyone else had left, which allowed them to notice how eager some members of the Bingley family were for them to disappear.</t1702> <t1703>Mrs. Hurst and her sister barely opened their mouths except to complain of fatigue; they were visibly impatient to be alone in the house.</t1703> <t1704>They rejected all of Mrs. Bennet's attempts at conversation and the liveliness dwindled, despite Collins' long speeches congratulating Bingley and his sisters on the elegance of the party and the hospitality and finesse with which they had treated their guests.</t1704> <t1705>Darcy said absolutely nothing.</t1705> <t1706>Mr. Bennet, as silent as he, enjoyed the scene.</t1706> <t1707>Bingley and Jane were together and a little apart from the others, talking to each other.</t1707> <t1708>Elizabeth maintained the same silence as Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley.</t1708> <t1709>Even Lydia was too exhausted to say more than "Good heavens! How tired I am!" amid great yawns.</t1709>
<t1710>When they finally got up to say goodbye, Mrs. Bennet insisted very courteously on her desire to soon see the whole family at Longbourn. She specifically addressed Bingley to tell him that they would be very honored if one day he came to their house to have lunch with them as a family, without the formality of a formal invitation.</t1710> <t1711>Bingley thanked her delightedly and immediately committed to taking the first opportunity that presented itself to visit them upon his return from London, where he had to go the next day, although he would not be long in coming back.</t1711>
<t1712>Mrs. Bennet was beside herself with joy and left the house convinced that, accounting for the necessary time for the celebration preparations, purchase of new carriages and wedding dresses, she would see her daughter settled in Netherfield within three or four months.</t1712> <t1713>With the same certainty and with considerable, though not equal, pleasure, she expected to soon have another daughter married to Collins.</t1713> <t1714>Elizabeth was the one she loved the least of all her daughters, and although the suitor and the wedding were more than enough for her, they were overshadowed by Bingley and by Netherfield.</t1714>
<t1715>CHAPTER XIX</t1715>
<t1716>The next day, there was another event at Longbourn.</t1716> <t1717>Collins formally proposed.</t1717> <t1718>He resolved to do it without delay, as his leave was expiring the following Saturday; and having full confidence in his success, he undertook the task methodically and with all the formalities he deemed necessary in such cases.</t1718> <t1719>Shortly after breakfast, he found Mrs. Bennet, Elizabeth, and one of the younger daughters together, and addressed the mother with these words:</t1719>
<t1720>––May I expect, madam, given your interest in your beautiful daughter Elizabeth, that I will be granted the honor of a private interview with her, during the course of this very morning?</t1720>
<t1721>Before Elizabeth had time to do anything more than blush with surprise, Mrs. Bennet instantly replied:</t1721>
<t1722>––Oh, dear! That was the last thing missing!</t1722> <t1723>I'm sure Elizabeth will be delighted and won't have any objections.</t1723> <t1724>Come, Kitty, I need you upstairs.</t1724>
<t1725>And gathering her work, she hurried to leave them alone.</t1725> <t1726>Elizabeth called her, saying:</t1726>
<t1727>––Mom, dear, don't go.</t1727> <t1728>I beg you, don't go.</t1728> <t1729>Mr. Collins will excuse me; but he has nothing to say to me that everyone can't hear.</t1729> <t1730>It's me who is leaving.</t1730>
<t1731>––No, don't be silly, Lizzy.</t1731> <t1732>Stay where you are.</t1732> <t1733>And seeing that Elizabeth, upset and agitated, was about to leave, he added:</t1733>
<t1734>––Lizzy, I order you to stay and listen to Mr. Collins.</t1734>
<t1735>Elizabeth could not disobey such a command.</t1735> <t1736>For a moment, she reconsidered and thought it wiser to end all of this as soon as possible in peace and tranquility.</t1736> <t1737>She sat down again and tried hard to conceal, on one hand, the feeling of discomfort, and on the other, how amusing she found the matter.</t1737> <t1738>Mrs. Bennet and Kitty left, and then Collins began:</t1738>
<t1739>––Believe me, my dear Miss Elizabeth, that your modesty, instead of harming you, adds to your other perfections.</t1739> <t1740>I would have found you less adorable if you had not shown that little resistance.</t1740> <t1741>But allow me to assure you that your mother has given me permission for this interview.</t1741> <t1742>You must already know the object of my discourse; although your natural delicacy leads you to conceal it; my intentions have been too evident to be mistaken.</t1742> <t1743>Almost from the moment I set foot in this house, I chose you as the future companion of my life.</t1743> <t1744>But before expressing my feelings, it might be advisable to explain the reasons I have for marrying, and why I came to Hertfordshire with the idea of finding a wife precisely here.</t1744>
<t1745>Elizabeth almost burst into laughter at the thought of him expressing his feelings; and she couldn't take advantage of the brief pause he made to prevent him from continuing.</t1745> <t1746>Collins continued:</t1746>
<t1747>––The reasons I have for marrying are: first, that the obligation of a clergyman in favorable circumstances like mine is to set an example of marriage in his parish; second, that I am convinced that it will contribute greatly to my happiness; and third, something that perhaps I should have mentioned first, that it is the particular advice and recommendation of the most noble lady whom I have the honor to call my patroness.</t1747> <t1748>Twice she has deigned to suggest it to me, even without my hinting at it, and on the very Saturday night before I left Hunsford and during our game of quadrille, while Mrs. Jenkinson was adjusting Miss de Bourgh's chair, she said to me: "Mr. Collins, you must marry.</t1748> <t1749>A clergyman like you must be married.</t1749> <t1750>Choose well, choose thinking of me and yourself; make sure she is an active and useful person, not highly educated, but capable of making the most of small incomes.</t1750> <t1751>This is my advice.</t1751> <t1752>Find that woman as soon as possible, bring her to Hunsford, and let me see her."</t1752> <t1753>Allow me, by the way, to say, fair cousin, that I do not consider as the least of the advantages I can offer you, the acquaintance and enjoyment of the kindness of Lady Catherine de Bourgh.</t1753> <t1754>Her manners will seem to you far above anything I can describe, and your liveliness and wit will seem very acceptable to her, especially when moderated by the discretion and respect that her high rank inevitably imposes.</t1754> <t1755>This is all regarding my general intentions in favor of marriage; I have nothing more to say except the reason why I have directed myself directly to Longbourn instead of seeking in my own locality, where, I assure you, there are many charming young ladies.</t1755> <t1756>But it is the case that being as I am the heir of Longbourn upon the death of your honorable father, whom I hope will live many years, I would not be satisfied if I did not choose a wife from among his daughters, to mitigate as much as possible the loss they will suffer upon the occurrence of such a sad event which, as I have already told you, I wish will not happen for many years.</t1756> <t1757>This has been the reason, fair cousin, and I hope it will not lower me in your esteem.</t1757> <t1758>And now I have only to express to you, in the most emphatic words, the strength of my affection.</t1758> <t1759>As for your dowry, it is absolutely indifferent to me, and I will not ask your father for anything that I know he cannot fulfill; so you will only have to bring the thousand pounds at four percent that will come to you upon your mother's death.</t1759> <t1760>But I will not be demanding and you can be assured that no interested reproach will come from my lips once we are married.</t1760>
<t1761>It was absolutely necessary to interrupt him immediately.</t1761>
<t1762>––You are going too fast ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t1762> <t1763>You forget that I have not answered you.</t1763> <t1764>Let me do so without further ado.</t1764> <t1765>I appreciate your attention and the honor that your proposal signifies, but I cannot help but decline it.</t1765>
<t1766>––I know full well ––replied Collins with a grave gesture of his hand–– that among young women it is very common to reject the proposals of the man whom, deep down, they intend to accept, when he asks for their preference for the first time, and that the refusal is repeated a second or even a third time.</t1766> <t1767>For this reason, what you have just told me does not discourage me at all, and I hope to lead you to the altar soon.</t1767>
<t1768>––Good heavens, sir! ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t1768> <t1769>I don't know what hopes you could have left after my response!</t1769> <t1770>I assure you that I am not one of those women, if such women even exist, so reckless as to risk their happiness on the chance of being asked a second time.</t1770> <t1771>My refusal is very serious.</t1771> <t1772>You could not make me happy, and I am convinced that I am the last woman in the world who could make you happy.</t1772> <t1773>Moreover, if your friend Lady Catherine knew me, I have a feeling she would think that I am, in every respect, the least suitable for you.</t1773>
<t1774>––If it were true that Lady Catherine thought so... ––said Collins with the utmost seriousness–– but I am sure Her Ladyship would approve.</t1774> <t1775>And believe me ––when I have the honor of seeing her again, I will speak in the most commendatory terms of your modesty, your economy, and your other good qualities.</t1775>
<t1776>––Please, Mr. Collins, all the compliments you give me are unnecessary.</t1776> <t1777>Let me judge for myself and grant me the honor of believing what I say.</t1777> <t1778>I wish you to be very happy and very rich, and by rejecting your hand I am doing everything in my power to ensure it is so.</t1778> <t1779>In making me this proposal, you must consider the delicacy of your feelings towards my family satisfied, and when the time comes, you can take possession of the Longbourn inheritance without any pangs of conscience.</t1779> <t1780>Therefore, let us consider this matter definitively settled.</t1780>
<t1781>While he was finishing saying this, he stood up and was about to leave the room, when Collins insisted again:</t1781>
<t1782>––The next time I have the honor of speaking to you about this topic again, I hope to receive a more favorable response than the one you have given me now; although I am far from believing that you are cruel to me, for I already know that it is an incorrigible custom of women to reject men the first time they declare themselves, and you may have said all that just to make my request more consistent as befits the true delicacy of the female character.</t1782>
<t1783>––Really, Mr. Collins ––exclaimed Elizabeth somewhat heatedly–– you confuse me exceedingly.</t1783> <t1784>If you interpret everything I have said so far as encouragement, I do not know how to express my repulsion to convince you completely.</t1784>
<t1785>––You must let me presume, my dear cousin, that your rejection has been merely verbal.</t1785> <t1786>The reasons I have for believing this are as follows: I do not believe that my hand is unworthy of being accepted by you, nor that the position I offer is anything less than highly desirable.</t1786> <t1787>My situation in life, my connection with the Bourgh family, and my kinship with you are important circumstances in my favor.</t1787> <t1788>Consider, moreover, that despite your many attractions, it is not certain that you will receive another marriage proposal.</t1788> <t1789>Your fortune is so meager that it will unfortunately nullify the effects of your beauty and good qualities.</t1789> <t1790>Therefore, as I cannot deduce from all this that you have sincerely rejected me, I will choose to attribute it to your desire to increase my love with suspense, in accordance with the customary practice among elegant women.</t1790>
<t1791>––I assure you, sir, that I do not find it at all elegant to torment a respectable man.</t1791> <t1792>I would prefer that you do me the courtesy of believing me.</t1792> <t1793>I thank you a thousand times for the honor you have done me with your proposal, but it is absolutely impossible for me to accept it.</t1793> <t1794>My feelings, in every respect, prevent me from doing so.</t1794> <t1795>Could I be any clearer?</t1795> <t1796>Do not consider me as an elegant woman who intends to torture you, but as a rational being who speaks from the heart.</t1796>
<t1797>––She is always charming! ––he exclaimed with coarse gallantry––.</t1797> <t1798>I cannot doubt that my proposal will be accepted when it is sanctioned by the authority of her excellent parents.</t1798>
<t1799>Given such an effort to deceive himself, Elizabeth did not reply and left immediately without saying a word, determined that, if Collins persisted in considering her repeated refusals as a frivolous system of encouragement, she would turn to her father, whose rejection would be formulated in such a way that it would be unappealable and whose attitude, at least, could not be mistaken for the affectation and coquetry of an elegant lady.</t1799>
<t1800>CHAPTER XX</t1800>
<t1801>Collins was not left much time to silently ponder the success of his love; because Mrs. Bennet, who had stayed in the hallway waiting for the conversation to end, as soon as she saw Elizabeth open the door and head quickly towards the stairs, entered the dining room and congratulated Collins, rejoicing over the fortunate prospect of the imminent union.</t1801> <t1802>After accepting and returning those congratulations with the same enthusiasm, Collins proceeded to explain the details of the interview, with which he was satisfied, as his cousin's firm refusal could naturally only stem from her shy modesty and the delicacy of her character.</t1802>
<t1803>But her news startled Mrs. Bennet.</t1803> <t1804>She too would have liked to believe that her daughter had only tried to encourage Collins by rejecting his proposals; but she did not dare to admit it, and thus she conveyed it to Collins.</t1804>
<t1805>––The important thing ––he added–– is that Lizzy comes to her senses.</t1805> <t1806>I will personally talk to her about this matter.</t1806> <t1807>She is a very stubborn and crazy girl and doesn't know what's good for her, but I'll make sure she understands.</t1807>
<t1808>––Excuse me for interrupting ––exclaimed Collins––, but if she is indeed stubborn and crazy, I don't know if, all things considered, she is a desirable wife for a man in my situation, who naturally seeks happiness in marriage.</t1808> <t1809>Therefore, if she insists on rejecting my proposal, it might be better not to force her to accept me, because if she has those flaws, she wouldn't contribute much to my happiness.</t1809>
<t1810>––You have misunderstood me ––said Mrs. Bennet alarmed––.</t1810> <t1811>Lizzy is stubborn only in these matters.</t1811> <t1812>In everything else, she is the most reasonable girl in the world.</t1812> <t1813>I will go directly to Mr. Bennet, and I have no doubt that we will soon come to an agreement with her.</t1813>
<t1814>Without giving him time to answer, she flew to meet her husband and upon entering the library exclaimed: "Oh, Mr. Bennet!"</t1814> <t1815>We need you urgently.</t1815> <t1816>We are in a predicament.</t1816> <t1817>You must go and convince Elizabeth to marry Collins, for she has sworn she will not and if you don't hurry, Collins will change his mind and won't want her anymore.</t1817>
<t1818>When his wife entered, Mr. Bennet lifted his eyes from the book and fixed them on her face with a calm indifference that the news did not alter in the slightest.</t1818> <t1819>––I have not had the pleasure of understanding you ––he said when she finished her tirade––.</t1819> <t1820>What are you talking about? ––About Mr. Collins and Lizzy.</t1820> <t1821>Lizzy says she will not marry Mr. Collins, and Mr. Collins is beginning to say he will not marry Lizzy.</t1821>
<t1822>––And what am I going to do?</t1822> <t1823>It seems to me that there's no solution.</t1823>
<t1824>––Talk to Lizzy.</t1824> <t1825>Tell her you want her to marry him.</t1825>
<t1826>––Tell him to come down.</t1826> <t1827>He will hear my opinion.</t1827>
<t1828>Mrs. Bennet rang the bell and Elizabeth was called to the library.</t1828>
<t1829>––Come, my daughter ––said her father as soon as the young woman entered––.</t1829> <t1830>I have sent for you regarding an important matter.</t1830> <t1831>They say that Collins has made you a marriage proposal, is it true?</t1831>
<t1832>Elizabeth said yes.</t1832>
<t1833>––Very well; and they say that you have rejected them.</t1833>
<t1834>––That's right, Dad.</t1834>
<t1835>––Well.</t1835> <t1836>Now let's get to the point.</t1836> <t1837>Your mother wishes you to accept it.</t1837> <t1838>Isn't that right, Mrs. Bennet?</t1838>
<t1839>Yes, or otherwise I don't want to see her anymore.</t1839>
<t1840>––You have a sad alternative before you, Elizabeth.</t1840> <t1841>From this day forward, you will have to renounce one of your parents.</t1841> <t1842>Your mother does not want to see you again if you do not marry Collins, and I do not want to see you again if you marry him.</t1842>
<t1843>Elizabeth could not help but smile at such a beginning; but Mrs. Bennet, who was convinced that her husband would advocate for that marriage, was disappointed.</t1843>
<t1844>––What does that way of speaking mean, Mr. Bennet?</t1844> <t1845>You had promised me that you would force her to marry Mr. Collins.</t1845>
<t1846>––My dear ––replied her husband––, I have to ask you for two small favors: first, that you let me use my understanding freely in this matter, and second, that you let me enjoy my library alone as soon as you can.</t1846>
<t1847>However, Mrs. Bennet, despite the disappointment she had with her husband, still did not give up.</t1847> <t1848>She spoke to Elizabeth again and again, flattering and threatening her alternately.</t1848> <t1849>She tried to get Jane on her side; but Jane, with all possible gentleness, preferred not to get involved.</t1849> <t1850>Elizabeth, sometimes with true seriousness, and other times jokingly, responded to her attacks; and although her mood changed, her determination remained unshakable.</t1850>
<t1851>Collins, meanwhile, silently pondered everything that had happened.</t1851> <t1852>He had too high a regard for himself to understand what reasons his cousin might have for rejecting him, and although his pride was wounded, he did not suffer in the least.</t1852> <t1853>His interest in his cousin was merely imaginary; the possibility that she might be deserving of her mother's reproaches prevented him from feeling any regret.</t1853>
<t1854>While this confusion reigned in the family, Charlotte Lucas arrived, who was coming to spend the day with them.</t1854> <t1855>She met Lydia in the hall, who ran to her to quietly tell her what was happening.</t1855>
<t1856>––I'm glad you came, because there's a commotion here...!</t1856> <t1857>What do you think happened this morning?</t1857> <t1858>Mr. Collins proposed to Elizabeth and she turned him down.</t1858>
<t1859>Before Charlotte had time to answer, Kitty appeared, coming to give her the same news.</t1859> <t1860>And as soon as they entered the dining room, where Mrs. Bennet was alone, she also began to talk to her about the matter.</t1860> <t1861>She begged her to have compassion and to try to convince Lizzy to yield to the wishes of the whole family.</t1861>
<t1862>––I beg you to intercede, dear Charlotte ––he added in a melancholic tone––, since no one is on my side, they treat me cruelly, no one sympathizes with my poor nerves.</t1862>
<t1863>Charlotte saved herself the response, for at that moment Jane and Elizabeth entered.</t1863>
<t1864>––There he is ––continued Mrs. Bennet––, as if nothing happened, he doesn't care about us at all, he disengages from everything just to get his way.</t1864> <t1865>I'll tell you one thing: if you get it into your head to keep rejecting all the marriage proposals you receive, you'll end up an old maid; and I don't know who will support you when your father dies.</t1865> <t1866>I won't be able to, I warn you.</t1866> <t1867>From today, I'm done with you forever.</t1867> <t1868>I told you in the library that I would never speak to you again; and what I say, I mean.</t1868> <t1869>I don't find any pleasure in talking to disobedient daughters.</t1869> <t1870>Nor with anyone.</t1870> <t1871>People like me who suffer from nerves are not fond of chatting.</t1871> <t1872>No one knows what I suffer!</t1872> <t1873>But it's always the same.</t1873> <t1874>Those who don't complain, no one pities them.</t1874>
<t1875>The daughters listened in silence to their mother's laments.</t1875> <t1876>They knew that if they tried to reason with her or calm her down, they would only succeed in irritating her more.</t1876> <t1877>So she continued speaking without anyone interrupting her, until Collins entered with a more solemn air than usual.</t1877> <t1878>Upon seeing him, Mrs. Bennet said to the girls:</t1878>
<t1879>––Now I ask you to shut your mouths and leave Mr. Collins and me so we can talk for a while.</t1879>
<t1880>Elizabeth left the room in silence; Jane and Kitty followed her, but Lydia did not move, determined to listen to everything she could.</t1880> <t1881>Charlotte, held back by Mr. Collins's courtesy, whose questions about her and her family followed one after another without interruption, and also a bit by curiosity, merely approached the window pretending not to listen.</t1881> <t1882>In a sad voice, Mrs. Bennet thus began her conversation:</t1882>
<t1883>––Oh, Mr. Collins!</t1883>
<t1884>––My dear madam ––he replied––, not another word on this matter.</t1884> <t1885>I am very far ––he continued with an accent that denoted his indignation–– from harboring any resentment towards your daughter's attitude.</t1885> <t1886>It is everyone's duty to resign themselves to inevitable misfortunes; and it is especially a duty for me, who has had the fortune of seeing myself so young in such an elevated position; I trust I will know how to resign myself.</t1886> <t1887>It may be that my beautiful cousin, by not wanting to honor me with her hand, has not diminished my positive happiness.</t1887> <t1888>I have often observed that resignation is never as perfect as when the denied happiness begins to lose some value in our estimation.</t1888> <t1889>I hope you do not suppose that I am disrespecting your family, my dear madam, by withdrawing my plans regarding your daughter without asking you and Mr. Bennet to interpose your authority on my behalf.</t1889> <t1890>I fear that my conduct, by accepting my rejection from your daughter's lips and not from yours, may be censurable.</t1890> <t1891>But we are all capable of making mistakes.</t1891> <t1892>I am sure I have acted with the best intention in this matter.</t1892> <t1893>My objective was to procure myself a kind companion with due consideration to the advantages this would bring to your entire family.</t1893> <t1894>If my conduct has been reproachable, I beg you to forgive me.</t1894>
<t1895>CHAPTER XXI</t1895>
<t1896>The discussions about Collins' offer were coming to an end; Elizabeth no longer had to endure more than that uncomfortable feeling, which inevitably derives from such situations, and, from time to time, some pointed allusions from her mother.</t1896> <t1897>As for the gentleman, he did not show himself to be disturbed, nor dejected, nor did he try to avoid Elizabeth, but expressed his feelings with an attitude of rigidity and a resentful silence.</t1897> <t1898>He hardly spoke to her; and those assiduous attentions so appreciated on his part, he dedicated all day to Miss Lucas who listened to him kindly, providing everyone and especially her friend Elizabeth with great relief.</t1898>
<t1899>The next morning, Mrs. Bennet's bad mood and poor health had not subsided.</t1899> <t1900>Mr. Collins also suffered from the wound to his pride.</t1900> <t1901>Elizabeth thought that his resentment would shorten his visit; but Mr. Collins's plans did not seem to be altered in the slightest.</t1901> <t1902>He had initially planned to leave on Saturday and intended to stay until Saturday.</t1902>
<t1903>After lunch, the girls went to Meryton to find out if Wickham had returned, and to lament his absence at the Netherfield ball.</t1903> <t1904>They found him upon entering the town and he accompanied them to their aunt's house, where they talked at length about his absence and misfortune and the consternation it had caused everyone.</t1904> <t1905>But in front of Elizabeth, he willingly admitted that his absence had been premeditated.</t1905>
<t1906>––As the moment approached ––he said–– it seemed to me that it would be better not to meet Darcy, for being together in a room for so many hours would have been beyond my strength and the situation could have become unpleasant, moreover, for other people.</t1906>
<t1907>Elizabeth fully approved of Wickham's conduct and they discussed it extensively, exchanging mutual praises as they headed towards Longbourn, where Wickham and another officer accompanied the young ladies.</t1907> <t1908>During the walk, Wickham devoted himself entirely to Elizabeth, providing her with a double satisfaction: receiving his compliments and having the opportunity to introduce him to her parents.</t1908>
<t1909>Shortly after arriving, they brought a letter for Jane.</t1909> <t1910>It came from Netherfield, and the young woman opened it immediately.</t1910> <t1911>The envelope contained a very elegant and glossy sheet of paper, covered with the handwriting of a beautiful and agile woman's hand.</t1911> <t1912>Elizabeth noticed that her sister's expression changed as she read and that she lingered over certain paragraphs.</t1912> <t1913>Jane quickly composed herself; she put down the letter and tried to join in the conversation with her usual cheerfulness; but Elizabeth was so curious that she even stopped paying attention to Wickham.</t1913> <t1914>And as soon as he and his companion left, Jane invited her with a look to accompany her upstairs.</t1914> <t1915>Once in her room, Jane showed her the letter and said:</t1915>
<t1916>––It is from Caroline Bingley; its content has surprised me greatly.</t1916> <t1917>Everyone in the house has left Netherfield and at this hour they are on their way to the capital, from where they do not plan to return.</t1917> <t1918>Listen to what it says.</t1918>
<t1919>Jane read aloud the first paragraph where it was stated that they had decided to go to London with her brother and that they intended to eat that same day on Grosvenor Street, where Mr. Hurst had his house.</t1919> <t1920>The following was written as follows: "I do not regret leaving Hertfordshire except for you, dearest friend; but I hope to enjoy again later the delightful moments we spent together and in the meantime we can lessen the sorrow of separation with very frequent and effusive letters.</t1920> <t1921>I count on your correspondence."</t1921> <t1922>Elizabeth listened to all these grand expressions with impassivity due to the distrust they inspired in her.</t1922> <t1923>She was surprised by the haste with which they had left, but in reality, she saw no reason to regret it.</t1923> <t1924>It could not be supposed that the fact that they were not in Netherfield would prevent Bingley from coming; and as for the absence of the ladies, she was sure that Jane would console herself with the presence of the brother.</t1924>
<t1925>––It's a shame ––he said to her after a brief pause–– that you couldn't see your friends before they left.</t1925> <t1926>But can't we hope that this "later on" of future happiness that your friend so desires will come sooner than she thinks and that this wonderful relationship you had as friends will be renewed with greater satisfaction as sisters?</t1926> <t1927>They are not going to detain Mr. Bingley in London.</t1927>
<t1928>––Caroline says that decidedly none of them will return to Hertfordshire this winter.</t1928> <t1929>I will read it to you: «When my brother left us yesterday, he imagined that the matters calling him to London could be handled in three or four days; but as we know that this will not be the case and convinced, at the same time, that when Charles goes to the capital he is in no hurry to return, we have decided to go with him so that he does not have to spend his free hours in a hotel, without any comfort.</t1929> <t1930>Many of our acquaintances are already there to spend the winter; I would like to know if you, dearest friend, plan to do the same; but I don't think it possible.</t1930> <t1931>I sincerely wish that Christmas in Hertfordshire is bountiful in the joys typical of these festivities, and that your suitors are so numerous that they prevent you from feeling the loss of the three gentlemen we are taking away from you.»</t1931>
<t1932>––Therefore, it is evident ––added Jane–– that Mr. Bingley is not coming back this winter.</t1932>
<t1933>––The only thing that is evident is that Miss Bingley is the one who says he is not coming back.</t1933>
<t1934>––Why do you believe that?</t1934> <t1935>It must be Mr. Bingley's doing: It doesn't depend on anyone.</t1935> <t1936>But you don't know everything yet.</t1936> <t1937>I'm going to read you the passage that hurts me the most.</t1937> <t1938>I don't want to hide anything from you.</t1938> <t1939>«Mr. Darcy is eager to see his sister, and the truth is that we are no less eager to see her.</t1939> <t1940>I believe that Georgina Darcy has no equal in beauty, elegance, and talent, and the affection she inspires in Louisa and me increases with the hope we harbor that she will be our sister in the future.</t1940> <t1941>I don't know if I have ever expressed to you my feelings on this matter; but I don't want to leave without confiding it to you, and I imagine you will find it very reasonable.</t1941> <t1942>My brother already feels great admiration for her, and now he will have frequent opportunities to see her with the greatest intimacy.</t1942> <t1943>Georgina's family desires this union as much as we do, and I don't think I am blinded by sisterly passion in believing that Charles is very capable of winning any woman's heart.</t1943> <t1944>With all these circumstances in favor of this relationship and nothing to prevent it, I cannot be mistaken, dearest Jane, if I hope for the event that would bring happiness to so many people.»</t1944>
<t1945>––What do you think of this paragraph, Lizzy? ––asked Jane after finishing reading––.</t1945> <t1946>Isn't it quite clear?</t1946> <t1947>Doesn't it clearly express that Caroline neither expects nor desires that I be her sister, that she is completely convinced of her brother's indifference, and that if she suspects the nature of my feelings towards him, she intends, with all kindness, of course, to warn me?</t1947> <t1948>Can this matter be interpreted in any other way?</t1948>
<t1949>––Yes, it can be done.</t1949> <t1950>I interpret it very differently.</t1950> <t1951>Do you want to know how?</t1951>
<t1952>––Of course.</t1952>
<t1953>––I will tell you in a few words.</t1953> <t1954>Miss Bingley has realized that her brother is in love with you and she wants him to marry Miss Darcy.</t1954> <t1955>She has gone to the capital after him, hoping to keep him there, and is trying to convince you that Bingley does not care about you at all.</t1955>
<t1956>Jane shook her head.</t1956>
<t1957>––That's right, Jane; you must believe me.</t1957> <t1958>No one who has seen you together can doubt Bingley's affection.</t1958> <t1959>His sister doesn't doubt it either, she's not that foolish.</t1959> <t1960>If she had seen half of that affection in Darcy towards her, she would have already ordered the wedding dress.</t1960> <t1961>But the thing is this: we are neither rich enough nor distinguished enough for them.</t1961> <t1962>If Miss Bingley is so eager to marry Miss Darcy to her brother, it's because it would make it easier for her to marry Darcy himself; which seems a bit naive on her part.</t1962> <t1963>But I would dare to believe that she would achieve her desires if Miss de Bourgh were not in the way.</t1963> <t1964>However, you can't seriously think that just because Miss Bingley tells you that her brother likes Miss Darcy, he is less in love with you than he was on Thursday when he said goodbye; nor that it is possible for his sister to convince him to love Miss Darcy instead of you.</t1964>
<t1965>––If our opinion of Miss Bingley were the same ––replied Jane––, your explanation would reassure me.</t1965> <t1966>But I know that you are being unfair to her.</t1966> <t1967>Caroline is incapable of deceiving anyone; the only thing I can hope for in this case is that she is deceiving herself.</t1967>
<t1968>––That's right.</t1968> <t1969>You couldn't have come up with a better idea, since mine doesn't comfort you.</t1969> <t1970>Suppose she's mistaken.</t1970> <t1971>That way you'll be in good standing with her and you'll see that you have nothing to worry about.</t1971>
<t1972>––But Lizzy, can I be happy, even assuming the best, by accepting a man whose sisters and friends wish him to marry another?</t1972>
<t1973>––You must decide that for yourself ––said Elizabeth––, if after mature reflection you find that the misfortune of displeasing his sisters is more than equivalent to the happiness of being his wife, I advise you, of course, to reject Bingley.</t1973>
<t1974>––What things you say! said Jane with a slight smile––.</t1974> <t1975>You must know that although their disapproval would sadden me greatly, I would not hesitate.</t1975>
<t1976>––I already figured as much, and being so, I don't think I can sympathize with your situation.</t1976>
<t1977>––But if he doesn't come back all winter, my choice will be useless.</t1977> <t1978>So many things can happen in six months!</t1978>
<t1979>Elizabeth rejected the idea that Bingley would not return; it seemed to her simply a suggestion born of Caroline's self-interested wishes, and she could not suppose for a moment that such wishes, whether expressed openly or covertly, would influence the mind of a man so independent.</t1979>
<t1980>He explained to his sister as eloquently as he could his point of view, and soon observed the good effect of his words.</t1980> <t1981>Jane was by nature an optimist, which gradually led her to hope that Bingley would return to Netherfield and fulfill all the desires of her heart, although doubt occasionally assailed her.</t1981>
<t1982>They agreed that they would only inform Mrs. Bennet about the family's departure, so she wouldn't be too alarmed; but she was quite alarmed anyway; and lamented the tremendous misfortune that the ladies had left just when they had become so close.</t1982> <t1983>She was very upset about it, but consoled herself by thinking that Bingley would soon return to dine at Longbourn, and ended up declaring that despite having invited him to a family dinner, she would make sure to prepare two first-rate dishes for that day.</t1983>
<t1984>CHAPTER XXII</t1984>
<t1985>The Bennets were invited to dine with the Lucases, and once again Miss Lucas had the kindness to listen to Collins for most of the day.</t1985> <t1986>Elizabeth took the first opportunity to thank her.</t1986>
<t1987>––This puts him in a good mood.</t1987> <t1988>I am more grateful to you than you can imagine ––she said to him.</t1988>
<t1989>Charlotte assured her that she was glad to be able to do something for her, and that it compensated her for the small sacrifice of dedicating her time.</t1989> <t1990>It was very kind of her, but Charlotte's kindness went further than Elizabeth could suspect: her goal was none other than to prevent Collins from directing his compliments to her friend again, attracting them to herself instead.</t1990> <t1991>This was Charlotte's plan, and appearances were so favorable to her that upon parting that night, she could almost have taken success for granted, if Collins did not have to leave Hertfordshire so soon.</t1991> <t1992>But in conceiving this doubt, she did not do justice to Collins' fiery and independent character; the next morning he slipped away from Longbourn with admirable stealth and ran to the Lucas' house to surrender at her feet.</t1992> <t1993>He wanted to conceal his departure from his cousins because if they had seen him, they would have discovered his intention, and he did not want to announce it until he was sure of success; although he felt almost certain of it, for Charlotte had encouraged him enough, but since his adventure on Wednesday, he was a little lacking in confidence.</t1993> <t1994>Nevertheless, he received a very flattering welcome.</t1994> <t1995>Miss Lucas saw him arriving from a window, and immediately went out to the path to meet him as if by chance.</t1995> <t1996>But little could she imagine how much love and eloquence awaited her.</t1996>
<t1997>In the short span of time left by Collins' endless speeches, everything was arranged between them to mutual satisfaction.</t1997> <t1998>Upon entering the house, Collins earnestly begged her to name the day when she would make him the happiest of men; and although such a request had to be postponed for the moment, the lady did not wish to play with his happiness.</t1998> <t1999>The stupidity with which nature had endowed her deprived her courtship of the charms that might incline a woman to prolong it; Miss Lucas, who had accepted him solely out of the pure and selfless desire to marry, did not care how soon this event would take place.</t1999>
<t2000>They quickly informed Sir William and Lady Lucas to obtain their consent, which was granted with the greatest promptness and joy.</t2000><t2001>Collins' situation made him a very desirable match for their daughter, to whom they could leave only a small fortune, and the prospects of future well-being were too tempting.</t2001><t2002>Lady Lucas immediately began to calculate with more interest than ever how many more years Mr. Bennet might live, and Sir William expressed his opinion that when Collins became the owner of Longbourn, it would be very convenient for him and his wife to make their appearance at St. James.</t2002><t2003>In short, the whole family rejoiced greatly at the news.</t2003><t2004>The younger daughters hoped to be presented in society a year or two earlier than they would have been otherwise due to this circumstance.</t2004><t2005>The sons were relieved of the fear that Charlotte might remain single.</t2005><t2006>Charlotte was calm.</t2006><t2007>She had won the game and had time to consider it.</t2007><t2008>Her reflections were generally satisfactory.</t2008><t2009>To tell the truth, Collins was neither intelligent nor pleasant, his company was tedious, and his affection for her must have been imaginary.</t2009><t2010>But, after all, he would be her husband.</t2010><t2011>Although Charlotte did not have a high opinion of men or marriage, she had always desired it because it was the only honorable position for a well-educated young woman with little fortune, and, although it could not be guaranteed to be a source of happiness, it would always be the most agreeable resource against need.</t2011><t2012>This resource was what she had just obtained, for at twenty-seven years of age, never having been pretty, it was a real stroke of luck for her.</t2012><t2013>The least pleasant part of all was the surprise that Elizabeth Bennet would receive, whose friendship she valued more than that of any other person.</t2013><t2014>Elizabeth would be astonished and probably would not approve; and, although the decision was already made, Elizabeth's disapproval would hurt her greatly.</t2014><t2015>She resolved to tell her herself, so she recommended to Collins, when he returned to Longbourn for dinner, not to say anything about what had happened.</t2015><t2016>Naturally, he duly promised to keep the secret; but it was difficult for him, because the curiosity aroused by his long absence burst upon his return in such direct questions that it required great skill to evade them; moreover, it represented a real self-denial for Collins, as he was impatient to proclaim his romantic success to the four winds.</t2016>
<t2017>The next day he had to leave, but since he had to set off too early to see any member of the family, the farewell ceremony took place when the ladies went to bed.</t2017> <t2018>Mrs. Bennet, with great courtesy and warmth, told him that she would be very happy to see him in Longbourn again when his other commitments allowed him to visit them.</t2018>
<t2019>––My dear lady ––replied Collins––, I particularly appreciate this invitation because I very much wanted to receive it; rest assured that I will take advantage of it as soon as possible.</t2019>
<t2020>Everyone was astonished, and Mr. Bennet, who in no way desired such a quick return, hastened to say:</t2020>
<t2021>––But, isn't there a danger that Lady Catherine will disapprove this time?</t2021> <t2022>It is better to be negligent with his relatives than to risk offending his patroness.</t2022>
<t2023>––Dear sir ––replied Collins––, I am very grateful to you for this friendly warning, and you can be assured that I will not take a single step that is not authorized by His Lordship.</t2023>
<t2024>––All precautions are few.</t2024> <t2025>Risk anything except inconveniencing her, and if you believe that your visits to our house could cause that, which I consider more than possible, stay quietly in yours and console yourself thinking that we will not be offended.</t2025>
<t2026>––Believe me, my dear sir, my gratitude increases with your affectionate advice, so I warn you that you will soon receive a letter of thanks for the same and for all the other proofs of consideration that you have given me during my stay in Hertfordshire.</t2026> <t2027>As for my beautiful cousins, although my absence will not be so long as to make it necessary, I will take the liberty of wishing them health and happiness, not excluding my cousin Elizabeth.</t2027>
