<t2028>After the customary pleasantries, the ladies withdrew.</t2028> <t2029>They were all equally surprised to see that he intended to return soon.</t2029> <t2030>Mrs. Bennet wanted to attribute it to his intention of courting one of her younger daughters, so she decided to convince Mary to accept him.</t2030> <t2031>Indeed, Mary appreciated Collins more than the others; she found a solidity in his reflections that often dazzled her, and although she did not consider him as intelligent as herself, she believed that if he were encouraged to read and to follow an example like hers, he could become a very pleasant companion.</t2031> <t2032>But the next morning, the entire plan fell apart, for Miss Lucas came to visit them just after lunch and in a private conversation with Elizabeth, she recounted the events of the previous day.</t2032>
<t2033>The possibility that Collins might believe himself in love with her friend had already occurred to Elizabeth one or two days before, but that Charlotte would encourage him seemed as impossible as if she herself would do it.</t2033> <t2034>Her astonishment, therefore, was so great that it exceeded all bounds of decorum and she could not refrain from shouting at her:</t2034>
<t2035>––Engaged to Mr. Collins! How is that possible, Charlotte?</t2035>
<t2036>Charlotte had told the story with much serenity, but now she felt momentarily confused for having received such a direct reproach; although it was what she had expected.</t2036> <t2037>But she recovered quickly and said calmly:</t2037>
<t2038>––What are you surprised about, Elizabeth?</t2038> <t2039>Does it seem incredible to you that Mr. Collins was able to gain the esteem of a woman just because he was not fortunate with you?</t2039>
<t2040>But, in the meantime, Elizabeth had regained her composure, and with a tremendous effort, she was able to assure him with enough firmness that she was delighted by the idea of their kinship and wished him all the happiness in the world.</t2040>
<t2041>––I know how you feel ––replied Charlotte––.</t2041> <t2042>You must be surprised, very surprised, considering how little Mr. Collins wanted to marry you.</t2042> <t2043>But when you have had time to think it over, I hope you will understand what I have done.</t2043> <t2044>You know I am not romantic.</t2044> <t2045>I never have been.</t2045> <t2046>I seek nothing more than a comfortable home, and considering Collins' character, his connections, and his position, I am convinced that I have as much chance of being happy with him as most people have when they marry.</t2046>
<t2047>Elizabeth answered sweetly:</t2047>
<t2048>––It is undeniable.</t2048>
<t2049>And after a somewhat awkward pause, they went to join the rest of the family.</t2049> <t2050>Charlotte left immediately, and Elizabeth stayed behind, pondering what she had just heard.</t2050> <t2051>It took her a long time to come to terms with such a ludicrous marriage.</t2051> <t2052>The oddity of Collins having made two marriage proposals in three days was nothing compared to the fact that he had been accepted.</t2052> <t2053>She always believed that Charlotte's theories about marriage were not exactly like hers, but she never supposed that in putting them into practice, she would sacrifice her best feelings to worldly matters.</t2053> <t2054>And to the pain caused by seeing how her friend had discredited herself and lost much of the esteem she had for her, was added the distressing conviction that it would be impossible for her to be happy with the fate she had chosen.</t2054>
<t2055>CHAPTER XXIII</t2055>
<t2056>Elizabeth was sitting with her mother and sisters, pondering over what she had heard and unsure whether to tell it or not, when Sir William Lucas himself appeared, sent by his daughter, to announce the engagement to the family.</t2056> <t2057>Amid many compliments and congratulating the union of the two houses, he revealed the matter to an audience not only astonished but also incredulous, as Mrs. Bennet, with more obstinacy than courtesy, insisted that he must be completely mistaken, and Lydia, always indiscreet and often ill-mannered, exclaimed boisterously:</t2057>
<t2058>––Holy God! What are you saying, Sir William?</t2058> <t2059>Don't you know that Mr. Collins wants to marry Elizabeth?</t2059>
<t2060>Only the condescension of a courtier could have endured that behavior without getting angry; but Sir William's good manners were above all.</t2060> <t2061>He begged to be allowed to guarantee the truth of what he said, but he listened to all those impertinences with the utmost correctness.</t2061>
<t2062>Elizabeth felt compelled to help him out of such an awkward situation, and confirmed his words, revealing what she knew from Charlotte herself.</t2062> <t2063>She tried to put an end to the exclamations of her mother and sisters warmly congratulating Sir William, in which she was soon seconded by Jane, and commenting on the happiness that could be expected from the event, given Mr. Collins's excellent character and the convenient distance from Hunsford to London.</t2063>
<t2064>Mrs. Bennet was certainly too overwhelmed to speak much while Sir William remained in the house; but as soon as he left, she quickly vented.</t2064> <t2065>First, she insisted on not believing a word; second, she was sure that Collins had been deceived; third, she trusted that they would never be happy together; and fourth, the wedding would not take place.</t2065> <t2066>However, two things clearly emerged from all this: that Elizabeth was the true cause of all the misfortune, and that she, Mrs. Bennet, had been treated in a barbaric manner by everyone.</t2066> <t2067>The rest of the day was spent ranting, and there was nothing that could console or calm her.</t2067> <t2068>A week had to pass before she could see Elizabeth without reprimanding her; a month, before she spoke to Sir William or Lady Lucas without being rude; and much longer, before she forgave Charlotte.</t2068>
<t2069>Mr. Bennet's mood upon hearing the news was calmer; moreover, he was even pleased, because in this way he could confirm, as he said, that Charlotte Lucas, whom he never considered very smart, was as foolish as his wife, and much more so than his daughter.</t2069>
<t2070>Jane confessed that she had been surprised; but she spoke less of her astonishment than of her sincere wishes that both would be happy, not even Elizabeth managed to make her see that such happiness was unlikely.</t2070> <t2071>Catherine and Lydia were far from envying Miss Lucas, for Collins was nothing more than a clergyman and the event held no more interest for them than that of being able to spread it around Meryton.</t2071>
<t2072>Lady Lucas could not resist the joy of being able to get back at Mrs. Bennet by expressing the comfort she felt in having a married daughter; she went to Longbourn more frequently than usual to tell how happy she was, although Mrs. Bennet's unfriendly looks and malicious comments could have ended all that happiness.</t2072>
<t2073>Between Elizabeth and Charlotte, there was a barrier that made them remain silent on the subject, and Elizabeth had the impression that true trust would never exist between them again.</t2073> <t2074>The disappointment she had felt from Charlotte made her turn to her sister with more affection and admiration than ever; her sister's integrity and delicacy guaranteed that her opinion of her would never change, and her happiness concerned her more each day, as it had been a week since Bingley had left and nothing was known about his return.</t2074>
<t2075>Jane immediately replied to Caroline Bingley's letter, and she was calculating the days it might take to receive the response.</t2075> <t2076>The promised letter from Collins arrived on Tuesday, addressed to the father and written with all the solemnity of gratitude that only a year of living with the family could have justified.</t2076> <t2077>After apologizing at the beginning, he proceeded to inform him, with much grandiloquence, of his happiness for having obtained the affection of his charming neighbor Miss Lucas, and then expressed that it was only with the intention of enjoying her company that he had felt so inclined to accede to their kind wishes to see him again in Longbourn, where he hoped to return on Monday in a fortnight; for Lady Catherine, he added, so cordially approved of his marriage that she wished it to be celebrated as soon as possible, something he trusted would be an irrefutable argument for his dear Charlotte to set the day when she would make him the happiest of men.</t2077>
<t2078>Mrs. Bennet no longer found Collins's return to Hertfordshire a cause for satisfaction.</t2078> <t2079>On the contrary, she deplored it more than her husband: "It was very odd that Collins came to Longbourn instead of going to the Lucas's house; it was very inconvenient and extremely embarrassing.</t2079> <t2080>She hated having visitors given her poor health, and engaged couples were the most unbearable beings in the world."</t2080> <t2081>These were Mrs. Bennet's constant murmurs, which only ceased in the face of an even greater distress: Mr. Bingley's long absence.</t2081>
<t2082>Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were calm about this matter.</t2082> <t2083>The days passed without any more news except for what soon spread through Meryton: that the Bingleys would not return for the entire winter.</t2083> <t2084>Mrs. Bennet was indignant and did not stop denying it, asserting that it was the most atrocious falsehood one could hear.</t2084>
<t2085>Even Elizabeth began to fear, not that Bingley had forgotten Jane, but that his sisters might succeed in separating him from her.</t2085> <t2086>Despite not wanting to admit such a disastrous idea for Jane's happiness and so unworthy of her lover's firmness, Elizabeth could not help but frequently think of it.</t2086> <t2087>She feared that the combined effort of his heartless sisters and his influential friend, along with the attractions of Miss Darcy and the pleasures of London, could be too many things at once against Bingley's affection.</t2087>
<t2088>As for Jane, the anxiety that this doubt caused her was, naturally, more painful than Elizabeth's; but no matter what she felt, she wanted to hide it, and therefore the matter was never mentioned between her and her sister.</t2088> <t2089>Her mother, however, was not restrained by the same delicacy and not an hour passed without her talking about Bingley, expressing her impatience for his arrival or insisting that Jane confess that if he did not return, they would have been treated in the most indecorous manner.</t2089> <t2090>It took all of Jane's gentleness to endure these attacks with tolerable calmness.</t2090>
<t2091>Collins returned promptly on Monday in fifteen days; the reception he received at Longbourn was not as cordial as the first time.</t2091> <t2092>But the man was too happy for anything to affect him, and fortunately for everyone, he was so busy with his courtship that they were free of his company for much of the time.</t2092> <t2093>He spent most of the day at the Lucas house, and sometimes returned to Longbourn just in time to excuse his absence before the family went to bed.</t2093>
<t2094>Mrs. Bennet was truly in a lamentable state.</t2094> <t2095>The mere mention of anything concerning the wedding would put her in a bad mood, and wherever she went, she could be sure to hear about the event.</t2095> <t2096>Seeing Miss Lucas unsettled her.</t2096> <t2097>She looked at her with horror and jealousy, imagining her as her successor in that house.</t2097> <t2098>Whenever Charlotte came to visit them, Mrs. Bennet concluded that she was anticipating the time of taking possession, and every time she whispered something to Collins, she was convinced they were talking about the inheritance of Longbourn and planning to throw her and her daughters out as soon as Mr. Bennet passed away.</t2098> <t2099>She complained about it bitterly to her husband.</t2099>
<t2100>––The truth is, Mr. Bennet ––she said to him––, it is very hard to think that Charlotte Lucas will one day be the mistress of this house, and that I will be forced to yield my place to her and live seeing her in my position.</t2100>
<t2101>––Dear, don't think about sad things.</t2101> <t2102>Let's have hope for better things.</t2102> <t2103>Let's cheer up with the idea that I can outlive you.</t2103>
<t2104>It was not very comforting, to say the least, for Mrs. Bennet; however, instead of replying, she continued:</t2104>
<t2105>––I can't bear the thought of them becoming the owners of all this property.</t2105> <t2106>If it weren't for the inheritance, I wouldn't care.</t2106>
<t2107>––What is it that you couldn't care less about?</t2107>
<t2108>––I wouldn't care about absolutely anything.</t2108>
<t2109>––Let's give thanks, then, that they save you from such a state of insensitivity.</t2109>
<t2110>––I will never be able to give thanks for anything related to the inheritance.</t2110> <t2111>I will never understand how someone can have a clear conscience disinheriting their own daughters.</t2111> <t2112>And to top it off, the heir has to be Mr. Collins!</t2112> <t2113>Why him, and not anyone else?</t2113>
<t2114>––I leave it to your own consideration.</t2114>
<t2115>CHAPTER XXIV</t2115>
<t2116>The letter from Miss Bingley arrived and put an end to all doubts.</t2116> <t2117>The first sentence already conveyed that everyone had settled in London for the winter, and at the end, it expressed the brother's regret for not having had time, before leaving the countryside, to pay his respects to his friends in Hertfordshire.</t2117>
<t2118>There was no hope, it had completely vanished.</t2118> <t2119>Jane continued reading, but found few things, aside from the expressions of affection from her author, that could provide her any relief.</t2119> <t2120>The rest of the letter was almost entirely dedicated to praising Miss Darcy.</t2120> <t2121>It insisted again on her many charms, and Caroline boasted very happily about her growing intimacy with her, venturing to predict the fulfillment of the wishes she had already expressed in the first letter.</t2121> <t2122>She also joyfully recounted that her brother was close to the Darcy family, and enthusiastically mentioned certain plans of the latter, related to the new furniture.</t2122>
<t2123>Elizabeth, to whom Jane immediately communicated the most important parts of the news, listened in silence and with great indignation.</t2123> <t2124>Her heart wavered between concern for her sister and hatred for everyone else.</t2124> <t2125>She did not believe Caroline's assertion that her brother was interested in Miss Darcy.</t2125> <t2126>She never doubted, as she had never doubted before, that Bingley was in love with Jane; but Elizabeth, who had always had so much sympathy for him, could not think without anger, and even without contempt, of that weakness of character and lack of decision that made him a slave to his scheming friends and led him to sacrifice his own happiness to the whims of their desires.</t2126> <t2127>If he were only sacrificing his own happiness, he could play with it as he pleased; but it also involved Jane's happiness, and she thought he should take that into account.</t2127> <t2128>In the end, it was one of those things that it is useless to break one's head over.</t2128>
<t2129>Elizabeth could think of nothing else; and whether Bingley's interest had truly died, or had been hindered by the interference of his friends; whether Bingley was aware of Jane's affection, or it had gone unnoticed by him; in any case, and although Elizabeth's opinion of Bingley might vary depending on the differences, Jane's situation remained the same and her peace had been disturbed.</t2129>
<t2130>A day or two passed before Jane had the courage to confess her feelings to her sister; but finally, in a moment when Mrs. Bennet had left them alone after becoming more irritated than usual with the subject of Netherfield and its owner, the young woman could not resist and exclaimed:</t2130>
<t2131>––If my dear mother had more self-control!</t2131> <t2132>You cannot imagine how much her constant comments about Mr. Bingley hurt me.</t2132> <t2133>But I will not be sad.</t2133> <t2134>It cannot last long.</t2134> <t2135>I will forget it and we will all be as we were before.</t2135>
<t2136>Elizabeth, solicitous and incredulous, looked at her sister, but said nothing.</t2136>
<t2137>––Do you doubt it? ––asked Jane, slightly blushing––.</t2137> <t2138>You have no reason to.</t2138> <t2139>I will always remember him as the best man I have ever known, that's all.</t2139> <t2140>I have nothing to hope for nor to fear, and I have nothing to reproach him for.</t2140> <t2141>Thank God, I am spared that sorrow.</t2141> <t2142>So, in a short time, I will be much better.</t2142>
<t2143>In a louder voice, he added afterwards:</t2143>
<t2144>––I have the consolation of thinking that it was nothing more than an error of imagination on my part and that it has harmed no one but myself.</t2144>
<t2145>––Dear Jane! ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t2145> <t2146>You are too good.</t2146> <t2147>Your sweetness and selflessness are truly angelic.</t2147> <t2148>I don't know what to say to you.</t2148> <t2149>I feel as if I have never done you justice, or as if I have not loved you as much as you deserve.</t2149>
<t2150>Jane vehemently denied having any extraordinary merit and rejected her sister's praises, which were only a product of her great affection.</t2150>
<t2151>––No ––said Elizabeth––, that is not right.</t2151> <t2152>Everyone seems respectable to you and you get offended if I speak ill of someone.</t2152> <t2153>You are the only one I find perfect and you don't want me to tell you that either.</t2153> <t2154>Do not fear that I will exceed by appropriating your privilege of universal kindness.</t2154> <t2155>There is no danger.</t2155> <t2156>I truly care for very few people, and I have a good opinion of very few.</t2156> <t2157>The more I know the world, the more it displeases me, and time confirms my belief in the inconsistency of human character, and in how little one can trust the appearances of goodness or intelligence.</t2157> <t2158>Lately, I have had two examples: one that I do not want to mention, and the other, Charlotte's wedding.</t2158> <t2159>It's incredible!</t2159> <t2160>No matter how you look at it, it's incredible!</t2160>
<t2161>––Dear Lizzy, you must not have those feelings, they will end your happiness.</t2161> <t2162>You do not take into consideration the different situations and the nature of people.</t2162> <t2163>Keep in mind the respectability of Mr. Collins and the firm and prudent character of Charlotte.</t2163> <t2164>Remember that she belongs to a large family, and as far as fortune is concerned, it is a very desirable marriage. You must believe, for God's sake, that she might feel some affection and esteem for our cousin.</t2164>
<t2165>––To please you, I would try to believe what you say, but no one would benefit, because if I suspected that Charlotte had any interest in Mr. Collins, I would think less of her intelligence than I currently do of her heart.</t2165> <t2166>Dear Jane, Mr. Collins is a conceited, pedantic, stubborn, and foolish man; you know this as well as I do; and you must also know, as I do, that the woman who marries him cannot be in her right mind.</t2166> <t2167>Do not defend her just because she is Charlotte Lucas.</t2167> <t2168>For the sake of one person, you should not distort the meaning of principle and integrity, nor try to convince yourself or me that selfishness is prudence or that insensitivity to danger is a guarantee of happiness.</t2168>
<t2169>––You speak of both with too much harshness ––replied Jane––, and I hope you will admit it when you see that they are happy together.</t2169> <t2170>But let's leave this aside.</t2170> <t2171>You alluded to something else.</t2171> <t2172>You mentioned two examples.</t2172> <t2173>I know what it's about, but I beg you, dear Lizzy, not to make me suffer by blaming that person and saying that you have lost the good opinion you had of him.</t2173> <t2174>We should not be so inclined to imagine that we have been intentionally hurt.</t2174> <t2175>We cannot expect a young and so vital man to always be so circumspect and restrained.</t2175> <t2176>Often what deceives us is only our own vanity.</t2176> <t2177>We women believe that admiration means more than it actually does.</t2177>
<t2178>––And men make sure that it is so.</t2178>
<t2179>––If they do it deliberately, they have no justification; but it seems to me that there is not as much premeditation in the world as many people imagine.</t2179>
<t2180>––I do not mean to attribute Mr. Bingley's conduct to premeditation; but without intending to do wrong or cause others to suffer, one can make mistakes and do a great deal of harm.</t2180> <t2181>Inattention, disregard for the feelings of others, and indecision are responsible for that.</t2181>
<t2182>––Do you attribute what happened to any of that?</t2182>
<t2183>––Yes, at the end.</t2183> <t2184>But if I keep talking, I will upset you by saying what I think of people you hold in high regard.</t2184> <t2185>It's better if you try to make me stay quiet.</t2185>
<t2186>Do you still suppose, then, that the sisters influence him?</t2186>
<t2187>––Yes, along with his friend.</t2187>
<t2188>––I can't believe it.</t2188> <t2189>Why would he do it?</t2189> <t2190>They can only wish for his happiness; and if he loves me, no other woman will be able to provide it.</t2190>
<t2191>Your first assumption is false.</t2191> <t2192>They may desire many things besides their happiness; they may desire to increase their wealth, with all that it brings; they may desire for you to marry a girl who has all the importance that money, great families, and pride can give.</t2192>
<t2193>––So, you mean that they want me to choose Miss Darcy ––replied Jane––; but perhaps they are driven by better intentions than you think.</t2193> <t2194>They have spent much more time with her than with me, it's logical that they would like her more.</t2194> <t2195>But whatever their wishes may be, it is very unlikely that they have opposed their brother's.</t2195> <t2196>What sister would believe she has the right to do so, unless there was something very serious to object to?</t2196> <t2197>If they had seen that he was very interested in me, they would not have tried to separate us; and if he were indeed that interested, all their efforts would be useless.</t2197> <t2198>By assuming that he loves me, you only manage to attribute bad behavior and a wrong attitude to everyone and make me suffer even more.</t2198> <t2199>I am not ashamed of having been wrong, and if I were ashamed, my suffering would be nothing compared to the pain that thinking ill of Bingley or his sisters would cause me.</t2199> <t2200>Let me interpret it in the best possible way, in the way that makes it most understandable.</t2200>
<t2201>Elizabeth could not oppose such wishes; and from then on, the name of Bingley was seldom mentioned between them.</t2201>
<t2202>Mrs. Bennet was still puzzled and murmuring upon seeing that Bingley did not return; and although not a day went by without Elizabeth clearly showing her what was happening, it seemed that the mother could not stop being puzzled.</t2202> <t2203>Her daughter tried to convince her of what she herself did not believe, telling her that Bingley's attentions towards Jane had been the result of a common and fleeting whim that ceased once he stopped seeing her; but although Mrs. Bennet did not hesitate to admit that possibility, she could not help but repeat the same story every day.</t2203> <t2204>The only thing that consoled her was that Bingley had to return in the summer.</t2204>
<t2205>Mr. Bennet saw the matter in a very different light.</t2205>
<t2206>So, Lizzy ––he said one day––, your sister has had a romantic failure.</t2206> <t2207>I congratulate her.</t2207> <t2208>Before getting married, it's good for a girl to have some failure; it gives her something to think about, and it gives her a certain distinction among her friends.</t2208> <t2209>And you, when is it your turn?</t2209> <t2210>You wouldn't want to be less than Jane.</t2210>
<t2211>Take advantage now.</t2211> <t2212>There are enough officers in Meryton to deceive all the girls in the area.</t2212> <t2213>Choose Wickham.</t2213> <t2214>He is a pleasant fellow, and he is sure to reject you.</t2214>

<t2217>––It is true ––said Mr. Bennet––, but it is a comfort to think that, whatever happens, you have a loving mother who will always help you.</t2217>
<t2218>Wickham's company was of great utility in dispelling the sadness that the recent unfortunate events had caused several members of the Longbourn family.</t2218> <t2219>They saw him often, and on that occasion, he added to his other virtues that of absolute frankness.</t2219> <t2220>Everything Elizabeth had heard, his complaints against Darcy and the grievances he had inflicted upon him, became public knowledge; everyone delighted in recalling how disagreeable Darcy had always been, even before knowing any of this.</t2220>

<t2223>CHAPTER XXV</t2223>
<t2224>After a week spent between promises of love and plans of happiness, Collins had to bid farewell to his beloved Charlotte to arrive on Saturday at Hunsford.</t2224> <t2225>But the sorrow of the separation was alleviated for Collins by the preparations he had to make for the reception of his fiancée; for he had reasons to believe that shortly after his next return to Hertfordshire, the day would be set that would make him the happiest of men.</t2225> <t2226>He bid farewell to his relatives at Longbourn with the same solemnity as before; once again wished his beautiful cousins health and happiness, and promised the father another letter of gratitude.</t2226>
<t2227>On the following Monday, Mrs. Bennet had the pleasure of receiving her brother and his wife, who came, as usual, to spend Christmas at Longbourn.</t2227> <t2228>Mr. Gardiner was an intelligent and gentlemanly man, far superior to his sister by nature and education.</t2228> <t2229>The ladies of Netherfield would have found it difficult to believe that this man, who lived off commerce and was always involved in his warehouse, could be so well-educated and pleasant.</t2229> <t2230>Mrs. Gardiner, much younger than Mrs. Bennet and Mrs. Philips, was a charming and elegant woman, adored by her nieces at Longbourn.</t2230> <t2231>Especially the two eldest, with whom she had a particular friendship.</t2231> <t2232>Elizabeth and Jane had often been to her house in the capital.</t2232> <t2233>The first thing Mrs. Gardiner did upon arriving was to distribute her gifts and describe the new fashions.</t2233> <t2234>Once this was done, she ceased to lead the conversation; now it was her turn to listen.</t2234> <t2235>Mrs. Bennet had to tell her about her many misfortunes and complaints.</t2235> <t2236>She had suffered many humiliations since the last time she saw her sister-in-law.</t2236> <t2237>Two of her daughters had been on the verge of getting married, but then everything had come to nothing.</t2237>
<t2238>––I do not blame Jane, he continued––, because she would have married Mr. Bingley if she could; but Elizabeth... Ah, my sister! <t2252>––If that had been the case, it would be a great comfort ––said Elizabeth––, but our situation is different.</t2252> <t2253>What happened to us was not by chance.</t2253> <t2254>It is not so common for friends to intervene and convince an independent young man to stop thinking about a girl he was madly in love with just a few days before.</t2254>
<t2255>––But that expression, "madly in love," is so overused, so ambiguous and so vague, that it tells me nothing.</t2255> <t2256>The same applies to feelings born half an hour after meeting, as to a strong and true affection.</t2256> <t2257>Explain to me what Mr. Bingley's love was like.</t2257>
<t2258>––I have never seen a more promising attraction.</t2258> <t2259>When he was with Jane, he paid no attention to anyone else, he devoted himself entirely to her.</t2259> <t2260>Every time they saw each other, it became more certain and evident.</t2260> <t2261>At his own ball, he snubbed two or three young ladies by not asking them to dance, and I spoke to him twice without getting a response.</t2261> <t2262>Could there be clearer symptoms?</t2262> <t2263>Isn't rudeness to everyone else the very essence of love?</t2263>

<t2285>The Gardiners stayed in Longbourn for a week; and among the Philipses, the Lucases, and the officers, there was not a day without an engagement.</t2285> <t2286>Mrs. Bennet had taken such care to prepare everything so that her brother and sister-in-law would have a good time, that not once could they enjoy a family meal.</t2286> <t2287>When the gathering was at home, there were always some officers present among whom Wickham could not be absent.</t2287> <t2288>On these occasions, Mrs. Gardiner, who was curious about the many praises Elizabeth bestowed upon him, observed the two of them closely.</t2288> <t2289>Realizing, from what she saw, that they were not seriously in love; their mutual preference was too evident.</t2289> <t2290>She did not feel very at ease, so before leaving Hertfordshire she decided to speak with Elizabeth about the matter, warning her of her imprudence in encouraging that relationship.</t2290>

<t2294>Mrs. Gardiner had been to Pemberley and had known the late Mr. Darcy perfectly.</t2294> <t2295>This was, therefore, an inexhaustible topic of conversation.</t2295> <t2296>She compared her memories of Pemberley with the detailed description Wickham gave, and by praising the character of its last owner, they both delighted in it.</t2296> <t2297>Upon learning of Darcy's behavior with Wickham, Mrs. Gardiner believed she remembered something of the bad reputation he had when he was still a boy, which fit this case; finally, she confessed that she remembered that even then young Fitzwilliam Darcy was spoken of as a bad and proud boy.</t2297>
<t2298>CHAPTER XXVI</t2298>
<t2299>Mrs. Gardiner gave Elizabeth the aforementioned warning promptly and kindly, at the first opportunity she had to speak with her alone.</t2299> <t2300>After having honestly told her what she thought, she added:</t2300>

<t2340>Shortly after the Gardiners and Jane had left, Collins returned to Hertfordshire; but since he went to the Lucas house, Mrs. Bennet was not bothered by his arrival.</t2340> <t2341>The wedding was approaching, and Mrs. Bennet had resigned herself so much that she already considered it inevitable and even repeated, albeit in a bad mood, that she wished them to be happy.</t2341> <t2342>The wedding was to be held on Thursday, and on Wednesday Miss Lucas came to make her farewell visit.</t2342> <t2343>When the young woman got up to leave, Elizabeth, sincerely moved and embarrassed by her mother's inattentive attitude and feigned good wishes, left the room with her and accompanied her to the door.</t2343> <t2344>As they were going down the stairs, Charlotte said:</t2344>

<t2346>––You will have them.</t2346>
<t2347>––And I want to ask you another favor.</t2347> <t2348>Will you come to see me?</t2348>
<t2349>––We will see each other often in Hertfordshire, I hope.</t2349>
<t2350>––It seems to me that I won't be able to leave Kent for a while.</t2350> <t2351>Therefore, promise me to come to Hunsford.</t2351>

<t2355>The wedding took place; the groom and the bride departed for Kent from the church door, and everyone had some comment to make or hear on the matter, as usual.</t2355> <t2356>Elizabeth soon received a letter from her friend, and their correspondence was as regular and frequent as ever.</t2356> <t2357>But no longer as frank.</t2357> <t2358>It was impossible for Elizabeth to address Charlotte without noticing that all her former confidence had disappeared, and although she did not want to interrupt the correspondence, she did so more for what their friendship had been than for what it actually was now.</t2358> <t2359>Charlotte’s first letters were received with much impatience; she was very curious to see what she would say about her new home, to know if she would have liked Lady Catherine and to what extent she would dare to confess that she was happy.</t2359> <t2360>But upon reading those letters, Elizabeth observed that Charlotte expressed herself exactly as she had anticipated.</t2360> <t2361>She wrote cheerfully, seemed to be surrounded by comforts, and did not mention anything that was not worthy of praise.</t2361> <t2362>The house, the furniture, the neighborhood, and the roads, everything was to her liking, and Lady Catherine could not be more sociable and attentive.</t2362> <t2363>It was the same portrait of Hunsford and Rosings that Mr. Collins had made, although reasonably mitigated.</t2363> <t2364>Elizabeth understood that she would have to wait for her own visit to learn the rest.</t2364>
<t2365>Jane had already sent a few lines to her sister announcing her happy arrival in London; and when she wrote to her again, Elizabeth hoped that she would be able to tell her something about the Bingleys.</t2365>
<t2366>Her impatience for this second letter received the usual reward for all impatience: Jane had been in the capital for a week without having seen or heard anything from Caroline.</t2366> <t2367>However, she explained it by assuming that the last letter she sent to her friend from Longbourn must have been lost.</t2367>
<t2368>“My aunt,” he continued, “will go to that part of the city tomorrow, and I will have the opportunity to visit Caroline on Grosvenor Street.”</t2368>

<t2382>I am sure, my dearest Lizzy, that you will be unable to boast at my expense about your good judgment when I confess that I have been completely disillusioned with Miss Bingley's affection.</t2382> <t2383>Anyway, although the facts have proven you right, do not think me obstinate if I still assert that, given her behavior towards me, my trust was as natural as your suspicions.</t2383> <t2384>Despite everything, I cannot understand why she wanted to be my friend;
