<t2404>Jane</t2404>
<t2405>Elizabeth felt a bit sorry about this letter, but she regained her spirits by thinking that at least she would no longer be fooled by Miss Bingley.</t2405> <t2406>All hope regarding the brother had completely vanished.</t2406> <t2407>She didn't even wish for their relationship to be renewed.</t2407> <t2408>Every time she thought of him, his character disappointed her more.</t2408> <t2409>And as a punishment for him and for Jane's benefit, Elizabeth wished he would marry Mr. Darcy's sister as soon as possible, for, according to what Wickham said, she would make him regret greatly what he had scorned.</t2409>
<t2410>Meanwhile, Mrs. Gardiner reminded Elizabeth of her promise regarding Wickham and wanted to know how things were going.</t2410> <t2411>Elizabeth's news was more favorable for her aunt than for herself.</t2411> <t2412>Wickham's apparent interest had disappeared, as had his attentions.</t2412> <t2413>Now it was another whom he admired.</t2413> <t2414>Elizabeth was observant enough to notice everything, but she saw it and wrote about it without much sorrow.</t2414> <t2415>It had not made much of an impression on her heart, and her vanity was satisfied with believing that she would have been his favorite if her fortune had allowed it.</t2415> <t2416>The sudden acquisition of ten thousand pounds was the most notable charm of the young woman to whom Wickham now paid his attention.</t2416> <t2417>But Elizabeth, perhaps less perceptive in this case than in Charlotte's, did not blame him for his desire for independence.</t2417> <t2418>On the contrary, it seemed the most natural thing in the world to her, and as she presumed it cost him some effort to give her up, she was willing to consider it the wisest and most desirable measure for both, and could sincerely wish him much happiness.</t2418>
<t2419>She communicated all this to Mrs. Gardiner; and after recounting all the details, she added: "I am convinced, dear aunt, that I have never been truly in love, for if I had really felt that pure and elevated passion of love, I would detest even his name and wish him the greatest misfortunes.</t2419> <t2420>But not only do I still appreciate him, I do not feel any aversion towards Miss King.</t2420> <t2421>I do not hate her, I do not want to believe she is a bad girl.</t2421> <t2422>This cannot be love.</t2422> <t2423>My precautions have been effective; and although my friends would worry much more about me if I were madly in love with him, I cannot say that I regret my relative insignificance.</t2423> <t2424>Importance is sometimes paid for too dearly.</t2424> <t2425>Kitty and Lydia take Wickham's betrayal more to heart than I do.</t2425> <t2426>They are still too young to see the reality of the world and to acquire the humiliating conviction that handsome men must have something to live on, just like the ugly ones."</t2426>
<t2427>CHAPTER XXVII/h3></t2427>
<t2428>Without any significant events in the Longbourn family, and with no more variation than walks to Meryton, sometimes muddy and sometimes cold, the months of January and February passed.</t2428> <t2429>March was the month in which Elizabeth would go to Hunsford.</t2429> <t2430>At first, she did not seriously think of going.</t2430> <t2431>But she saw that Charlotte took it for granted, and little by little she became pleasantly accustomed to the idea until she decided to go.</t2431> <t2432>With the absence, her desire to see Charlotte had increased and her aversion to Collins had diminished.</t2432> <t2433>The project involved a certain novelty, and since with such a mother and such unbearable sisters, her home was not a very pleasant place, she could not disregard this change of scenery.</t2433> <t2434>The trip also provided her with the pleasure of going to give Jane a hug; so much so that when the date approached, she would have regretted having to postpone it.</t2434>
<t2435>But everything went smoothly and the trip was carried out according to Charlotte's plans.</t2435> <t2436>Elizabeth would accompany Sir William and his second daughter.</t2436> <t2437>And to top it off, they decided to spend a night in London; the plan was so perfect that nothing more could be asked for.</t2437>
<t2438>The only thing that saddened Elizabeth was parting from her father, because she knew he would miss her, and when the time came to leave, he became so sad that he asked his daughter to write to him and even promised to reply to her letter.</t2438>
<t2439>The farewell between Wickham and Elizabeth was very cordial, even more so on Wickham's part.</t2439> <t2440>Although at that moment he was occupied with other things, he could not forget that she was the first to excite and deserve his attention, the first to listen to him and sympathize with him, and the first to please him.</t2440> <t2441>And in his manner of saying goodbye, wishing her well, reminding her of what Lady Catherine de Bourgh seemed like to him, and repeating that his opinions about her and everyone else would always coincide, there was such solicitude and interest that Elizabeth felt filled with the most sincere affection towards him and left convinced that she would always consider Wickham, single or married, as a model of sympathy and simplicity.</t2441>
<t2442>Her travel companions for the next day were not the most suitable for Elizabeth to remember Wickham with less displeasure.</t2442> <t2443>Sir William and his daughter Maria, a cheerful girl but as empty-headed as her father, said nothing worth listening to; so hearing them was the same for Elizabeth as hearing the rattling of the carriage.</t2443> <t2444>Elizabeth was amused by the nonsense, but she had known Sir William for too long and he could tell her nothing new about the wonders of his presentation at court and his title of "Sir," and his courtesies were as stale as his news.</t2444>
<t2445>The journey was only twenty-four miles, and they set out so early that by noon they were already on Gracechurch Street.</t2445> <t2446>As they approached the Gardiners' door, Jane was at the sitting room window watching their arrival; when they entered the hall, she was already there to welcome them.</t2446> <t2447>Elizabeth examined her anxiously and was pleased to find her as healthy and charming as ever.</t2447> <t2448>On the stairs, there was a throng of boys and girls too impatient to see their cousin to wait for her in the sitting room, but their shyness kept them from fully descending and going to meet her, as it had been more than a year since they last saw her.</t2448> <t2449>Everything was joy and attentions.</t2449> <t2450>The day passed pleasantly; in the afternoon, they strolled around and visited the shops, and in the evening, they went to a theater.</t2450>
<t2451>Elizabeth then managed to sit next to her aunt.</t2451> <t2452>The first topic of conversation was Jane; after hearing the answers to the detailed questions she asked about her sister, Elizabeth was more saddened than surprised to learn that Jane, although always striving to keep her spirits high, was going through moments of great despondency.</t2452> <t2453>Nevertheless, it was reasonable to expect that they would not last long.</t2453> <t2454>Mrs. Gardiner also told her details of Miss Bingley's visit to Gracechurch, and repeated some conversations she had later with Jane that showed the latter had ended their friendship.</t2454>
<t2455>Mrs. Gardiner consoled her niece for Wickham's betrayal and congratulated her on how well she had taken it.</t2455>
<t2456>––But tell me, dear Elizabeth ––he added––, what kind of girl is Miss King?</t2456> <t2457>I would be very sorry to think that our friend is a fortune hunter.</t2457>
<t2458>––Let's see, dear aunt, what is the difference in matrimonial matters between selfish motives and prudent ones?</t2458> <t2459>Where does discretion end and greed begin?</t2459> <t2460>Last Christmas you feared he would marry me because it would have been imprudent, and now because he is seeking a young woman with only ten thousand pounds of income, you assume he is a fortune hunter.</t2460>
<t2461>––Just tell me what kind of person Miss King is, and I will be able to form a judgment.</t2461>
<t2462>––I think she is a good girl.</t2462> <t2463>I haven't heard anything bad about her.</t2463>
<t2464>––But he didn't pay her the slightest attention until her grandfather's death made her the owner of that fortune...</t2464>
<t2465>––Of course, why would he do it?</t2465> <t2466>If he couldn't afford to win me over because I had no money, what reason would he have to court a girl who meant nothing to him and was as poor as I was?</t2466>
<t2467>––But it seems improper for him to direct his attentions to her so soon after that event.</t2467>
<t2468>––A man who is in a bad situation does not have time, like others, to observe those elegant delicacies.</t2468> <t2469>Besides, if she does not reproach him, why should we reproach him?</t2469>
<t2470>––The fact that she doesn't care doesn't justify Wickham.</t2470> <t2471>It only shows that this young lady lacks sense or sensitivity.</t2471>
<t2472>––Well ––exclaimed Elizabeth––, as you wish.</t2472> <t2473>Let's say he is a fortune hunter and she is a fool.</t2473>
<t2474>––No, Elizabeth, that's what I don't want.</t2474> <t2475>You know it would hurt me to think badly of a young man who lived so long in Derbyshire.</t2475>
<t2476>––Ah! Well, if that's the case, I have a very poor opinion of the young men who live in Derbyshire, whose close friends, who live in Hertfordshire, are not much better.</t2476> <t2477>I'm fed up with all of them.</t2477> <t2478>Thank God, tomorrow I'm going to a place where I'll meet a man who has no pleasant qualities, who has neither manners nor the ability to be likable.</t2478> <t2479>After all, stupid men are the only ones worth knowing.</t2479>
<t2480>––Watch out, Lizzy!</t2480> <t2481>Those words sound too much like disappointment.</t2481>
<t2482>Before parting ways after finishing the work, Elizabeth had the unexpected joy of being invited by her uncles to accompany them on a trip they were planning to take in the summer.</t2482>
<t2483>––We still don't know how far we will go ––said Mrs. Gardiner––, but we might reach the Lakes.</t2483>
<t2484>No other project could be as pleasing to Elizabeth.</t2484> <t2485>She accepted the invitation instantly, extremely grateful.</t2485>
<t2486>––Dear, dearest aunt, she exclaimed enthusiastically––, what a delight!, what happiness!</t2486> <t2487>You make me come alive again, this gives me strength.</t2487> <t2488>Goodbye to disappointment and resentment!</t2488> <t2489>What are men compared to rocks and mountains?</t2489> <t2490>Oh, what hours of escape we will have!</t2490> <t2491>And when we return, we will not be like those travelers who are unable to give an accurate idea of anything.</t2491> <t2492>We will know where we have gone, and we will remember what we have seen.</t2492> <t2493>The lakes, the rivers, and the mountains will not be confused in our memory, nor will we argue about their relative positions when we want to describe a specific landscape.</t2493> <t2494>Let our first outpourings not be like those of most travelers!</t2494>
<t2495>CHAPTER XXVIII</t2495>
<t2496>The next day everything was new and interesting for Elizabeth.</t2496> <t2497>She was determined to have a good time and was very cheerful, as she had found her sister in very good health, and all the fears she had about her health had vanished.</t2497> <t2498>Moreover, the prospect of a trip to the North was for her a constant source of joy.</t2498>
<t2499>When they left the main road to enter the Hunsford path, everyone's eyes searched for the parsonage and at every turn they thought they were going to see it.</t2499> <t2500>On one side of the path ran the fence of the Rosings estate.</t2500> <t2501>Elizabeth smiled as she remembered everything she had heard about its inhabitants.</t2501>
<t2502>At last, they caught sight of the parsonage.</t2502> <t2503>The garden that extended to the road, the house that stood in the middle, the green palisade, and the laurel hedge indicated that they had arrived.</t2503> <t2504>Collins and Charlotte appeared at the door, and the carriage stopped at a small entrance that led to the house through a gravel path, amidst general greetings and smiles.</t2504> <t2505>In a moment, everyone got down from the landau, mutually rejoicing at seeing each other.</t2505> <t2506>Mrs. Collins welcomed her friend with the sincerest pleasure, and Elizabeth, being received with such affection, was increasingly glad she had come.</t2506> <t2507>She immediately observed that her cousin's manners had not changed with marriage; his rigid courtesy was exactly the same as before, and he kept her at the door for several minutes to ask questions about the entire family.</t2507> <t2508>Without further delay than Collins's remarks to his guests about the neatness of the entrance, they entered the house.</t2508> <t2509>Once in the hall, Collins, with bombastic formality, welcomed them to his humble abode for the second time, repeating point by point his wife's offer to serve them a refreshment.</t2509>
<t2510>Elizabeth was prepared to see him now in his environment, and she couldn't help but think that by showing them the good proportions of the room, its appearance, and its furnishings, Collins was addressing her specifically, as if he wanted to make her feel what she had lost by rejecting him.</t2510> <t2511>But although everything seemed bright and comfortable, Elizabeth could not gratify him with any sign of regret; rather, she marveled that her friend could appear so cheerful with such a companion.</t2511> <t2512>When Collins said something that must have embarrassed his wife, which happened quite often, Elizabeth involuntarily turned her eyes towards Charlotte.</t2512> <t2513>Once or twice she could see that Charlotte blushed slightly; but, for the most part, Charlotte pretended not to hear him.</t2513> <t2514>After sitting for a while, long enough to admire each and every piece of furniture, from the sideboard to the fireplace grate, and to recount the journey and everything that had happened in London, Mr. Collins invited them to take a walk in the garden, which was large and well laid out and which he personally took care of.</t2514> <t2515>Working in the garden was one of his most respected pleasures; Elizabeth admired the seriousness with which Charlotte spoke of how healthy it was for Collins and confessed that she herself encouraged him to do it whenever possible.</t2515> <t2516>Guiding them through all the paths and corners and barely giving them time to express the praises he demanded, he pointed out all the views with a meticulousness that far exceeded their beauty.</t2516> <t2517>He enumerated the fields that could be seen in all directions and said how many trees were in each one.</t2517> <t2518>But of all the views that his garden, or the countryside, or the entire kingdom could boast of, there was none that could compare to that of Rosings, which was revealed through a clearing in the trees that bordered the estate on the side opposite to the front of his house.</t2518> <t2519>The mansion was pretty, modern, and very well situated, on an elevation of the land.</t2519>
<t2520>From the garden, Collins would have liked to take them to tour his two meadows, but the ladies were not properly shod to walk on the still frozen grass and they declined.</t2520> <t2521>Sir William was the only one who accompanied him.</t2521> <t2522>Charlotte returned to the house with her sister and Elizabeth, probably extremely happy to be able to show it to them without her husband's help.</t2522> <t2523>It was small but well laid out, everything was arranged with order and cleanliness, a merit that Elizabeth attributed to Charlotte.</t2523> <t2524>When one could forget Collins, the house had a more pleasant atmosphere; and from her friend's evident satisfaction, Elizabeth thought she should forget him more often.</t2524>
<t2525>They had already told him that Lady Catherine was still in the country.</t2525> <t2526>She was mentioned again while they were dining, and Collins, joining the conversation, said:</t2526>
<t2527>––Yes, Elizabeth; you will have the honor of seeing Lady Catherine de Bourgh next Sunday at church, and I don't need to tell you how much you will enjoy it.</t2527> <t2528>She is all affability and condescension, and I have no doubt that she will honor you by speaking to you as soon as the service is over.</t2528> <t2529>I also almost have no doubt that you and my sister-in-law Maria will be included in all the invitations with which she honors us during your stay here.</t2529> <t2530>Her attitude towards my dear Charlotte is most kind.</t2530> <t2531>We dine at Rosings twice a week and she never allows us to walk back.</t2531> <t2532>She always requests her carriage to take us, or rather, one of her carriages, because she has several.</t2532>
<t2533>––Lady Catherine is really a very respectable and affectionate lady ––added Charlotte––, and a very attentive neighbor.</t2533>
<t2534>––Very true, dear; it is exactly what I say: she is a woman who can never be considered with enough deference.</t2534>
<t2535>During the evening, there was almost constant talk about Hertfordshire, and what had already been said in writing was repeated.</t2535> <t2536>Upon retiring, Elizabeth, in the solitude of her room, reflected on Charlotte's well-being and her skill and discretion in making the best of and enduring her husband, acknowledging that she did it very well.</t2536> <t2537>She also thought about how her visit would go, what they would do, the tedious interruptions from Collins, and how much she would enjoy dealing with the Rosings family.</t2537> <t2538>Her vivid imagination planned it all at once.</t2538>
<t2539>The next day, around noon, she was in her room getting ready to go out for a walk, when she heard a sudden noise downstairs that seemed to throw the whole house into confusion.</t2539> <t2540>She listened for a moment and noticed that someone was hurriedly coming up the stairs and calling out to her loudly.</t2540> <t2541>She opened the door and in the hallway she found María, very agitated and out of breath, who exclaimed:</t2541>
<t2542>––Oh, dear Elizabeth! Hurry up, come down to the dining room and you'll see!</t2542> <t2543>I can't tell you what it is.</t2543> <t2544>Run, come at once!</t2544>
<t2545>Elizabeth asked in vain what was happening.</t2545> <t2546>Maria did not want to tell her more; both went to the dining room, whose windows faced the road, to see the marvel.</t2546> <t2547>This consisted simply of two ladies who were standing at the garden gate in a low phaeton.</t2547>
<t2548>––And is that all? ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t2548> <t2549>I was at least expecting the pigs to have invaded the garden, and all I see is Lady Catherine and her daughter!</t2549>
<t2550>––Oh, dear! ––replied María, extremely surprised by the mistake––.</t2550> <t2551>It is not Lady Catherine.</t2551> <t2552>The older one is Mrs. Jenkinson, who lives with them.</t2552> <t2553>The other one is Miss de Bourgh.</t2553> <t2554>Look at her closely.</t2554> <t2555>She is a little creature.</t2555> <t2556>Who would have believed she was so small and so thin!</t2556>
<t2557>––It is rude to have Charlotte at the door with the wind blowing like this.</t2557> <t2558>Why doesn't that young lady come in?</t2558>
<t2559>––Charlotte says she almost never does it.</t2559> <t2560>It would be the greatest of favors if Miss de Bourgh were to enter the house.</t2560>
<t2561>––I like her appearance ––said Elizabeth, thinking about other things––.</t2561> <t2562>She looks sick and ill-tempered.</t2562> <t2563>Yes, she is the right woman for him, she suits him well.</t2563>
<t2564>Collins and his wife were conversing with the two ladies at the garden gate, and Elizabeth was greatly amused watching Sir William at the entrance door, absorbed in the contemplation of the grandeur before him and bowing every time Miss de Bourgh glanced in his direction.</t2564>
<t2565>Once the conversation ended, the ladies continued on their way, and the others entered the house.</t2565> <t2566>Collins, as soon as he saw the two girls, congratulated them on their good fortune.</t2566> <t2567>This good fortune, as Charlotte clarified, was that they were all invited to dine at Rosings the next day.</t2567>
<t2568>CHAPTER XXIX</t2568>
<t2569>Collins's satisfaction with this invitation was complete.</t2569> <t2570>There was nothing that delighted him more than being able to showcase the grandeur of his patroness to his admired guests and to make them see the courtesy with which this lady treated him and his wife; and the fact that he was given an opportunity to do so so soon was an example of Lady Catherine's condescension that he wouldn't know how to thank.</t2570>
<t2571>––I confess ––he said–– that I would not have been surprised if His Lordship had invited us on Sunday to have tea and spend the afternoon at Rosings.</t2571> <t2572>In fact, I was expecting it, because I know his affability.</t2572> <t2573>But who could have imagined such an attention as this?</t2573> <t2574>Who could have imagined that we would receive an invitation to dinner; an invitation, moreover, extended to everyone in the house, so very shortly after you arrived?</t2574>
<t2575>––I am not surprised ––replied Sir William––, because my position in life has allowed me to know the true nature of the great.</t2575> <t2576>In the court, such examples of elegant manners are very common.</t2576>
<t2577>All day long and the following morning, hardly anything else was talked about except the visit to Rosings.</t2577> <t2578>Collins carefully instructed them on what they were going to see, so that the sight of those rooms, so many servants, and such splendid food would not leave them open-mouthed.</t2578>
<t2579>When the ladies went to get dressed, he said to Elizabeth:</t2579>
<t2580>––Do not worry about your attire, dear cousin.</t2580> <t2581>Lady Catherine is far from demanding from us the elegance in dressing that befits her and her daughter.</t2581> <t2582>I would only like to advise you to wear the best outfit you have; there is no need for more.</t2582> <t2583>Lady Catherine will not think ill of you for being dressed simply.</t2583> <t2584>She likes to be reserved the distinction due to her rank.</t2584>
<t2585>While they were getting dressed, Collins went two or three times to knock on the various doors, to recommend that they hurry up, as Lady Catherine was very uncomfortable having to wait to eat.</t2585> <t2586>Such formidable reports about Her Ladyship and her way of living had intimidated Maria Lucas, who was not used to social life, and awaited her entrance into Rosings with the same apprehension that her father had experienced when being presented at St. James.</t2586>
<t2587>Since the weather was good, the half-mile walk through the Rosings estate was very pleasant.</t2587> <t2588>All estates have their beauty and views, and Elizabeth was delighted with everything she saw, although she did not show the enthusiasm that Collins expected, and listened with little interest to his enumeration of the windows on the facade, and the account of what the stained glass had cost Sir Lewis de Bourgh.</t2588>
<t2589>As they ascended the staircase leading to the lobby, María's excitement was increasing, and even Sir William was not entirely at ease.</t2589> <t2590>Elizabeth, on the other hand, did not lack courage.</t2590> <t2591>She had not heard anything about Lady Catherine that made her believe she possessed any extraordinary talents or miraculous virtues, and she knew that the mere majesty of money and lineage would not make her lose her composure.</t2591>
<t2592>From the entrance hall, whose harmonious proportions and delicate ornamentation Collins enthusiastically pointed out, the servants led them, through an antechamber, to the room where Lady Catherine, her daughter, and Mrs. Jenkinson were.</t2592> <t2593>Her Ladyship rose with great kindness to receive them.</t2593> <t2594>And as Mrs. Collins had agreed with her husband that she would make the introductions, they took place normally, without the excuses or expressions of gratitude that he would have deemed necessary.</t2594>
<t2595>Despite having been to St. James, Sir William was so overwhelmed by the grandeur surrounding him that he barely had the courage to make a deep bow and sat down without saying a word.</t2595> <t2596>His daughter, frightened and beside herself, also sat on the edge of a chair, not knowing where to look.</t2596> <t2597>Elizabeth was as usual, and was able to calmly observe the three ladies in front of her.</t2597> <t2598>Lady Catherine was a very tall and stout woman, with extremely pronounced features that must have been beautiful in her youth.</t2598> <t2599>She had an air of sufficiency, and her manner of receiving them was not the most appropriate to make her guests forget their inferior rank.</t2599> <t2600>When she was silent, she was not at all terrible; but when she spoke, she did so in such an authoritative tone that her importance became overwhelming.</t2600> <t2601>Elizabeth remembered Wickham, and her observations during the evening confirmed that Lady Catherine was exactly as he had described her.</t2601>
<t2602>After examining the mother, in whose countenance and behavior he immediately found a certain resemblance to Darcy, he turned his eyes towards the daughter, and was almost as astonished as Maria to see her so thin and so small.</t2602> <t2603>Both her figure and her face had nothing to do with her mother.</t2603> <t2604>Miss de Bourgh was pale and sickly; her features, although not ugly, were insignificant; she spoke little and only whispered to Mrs. Jenkinson, whose appearance was unremarkable and who did nothing but listen to what the girl said to her and place a screen in the right direction to protect her eyes from the sun.</t2604>
<t2605>After sitting for a few minutes, they were taken to one of the windows to admire the view; Mr. Collins accompanied them to properly point out its beauty, and Lady Catherine kindly informed them that in summer the view was much better.</t2605>
<t2606>The dinner was excellent, and all the servants and the silverware that Collins had promised were brought out; and just as he had predicted, he took a seat at the head of the table at Her Ladyship's request, making it seem as if life had nothing more important to offer him.</t2606> <t2607>He carved, ate, and praised everything with delight and alacrity.</t2607> <t2608>Each dish was first praised by him and then by Sir William, who had recovered enough to echo everything his son-in-law said, so much so that Elizabeth couldn't understand how Lady Catherine could tolerate them.</t2608> <t2609>But Lady Catherine seemed pleased with such excessive admiration and smiled kindly, especially when a dish was new to them.</t2609> <t2610>The others said almost nothing.</t2610> <t2611>Elizabeth was ready to speak as soon as she was given the opportunity; but she was seated between Charlotte and Miss de Bourgh, and the former was busy listening to Lady Catherine, while the latter did not open her mouth throughout the meal.</t2611> <t2612>Miss Jenkinson's main occupation was to watch over the little that Miss de Bourgh ate, insistently asking her to take another dish, fearing all the time that she was unwell.</t2612> <t2613>Maria thought it best not to speak, and the gentlemen did nothing but eat and praise.</t2613>
<t2614>When the ladies returned to the drawing room, they had nothing else to do but listen to Lady Catherine, who spoke without interruption until coffee was served, expressing her opinion on all kinds of matters in such a decided manner that it was clear she was not used to being contradicted.</t2614> <t2615>She questioned Charlotte minutely and with great familiarity about her domestic affairs, giving her a multitude of advice; she told her that everything should be very well organized in a family as small as hers, and instructed her even in the care of the cows and chickens.</t2615> <t2616>Elizabeth saw that there was nothing under the attention of this great lady that did not offer her the opportunity to dictate orders to others.</t2616> <t2617>In the intervals of her discourse to Mrs. Collins, she directed several questions to Maria and Elizabeth, but especially to the latter, about whose family she knew nothing, and who, as she told Mrs. Collins, seemed to her a very gentle and pretty girl.</t2617> <t2618>She asked her, on various occasions, how many sisters she had, whether they were older or younger than her, if any of them were about to be married, if they were pretty, where they had been educated, what kind of carriage her father had, and what her mother's maiden name had been.</t2618> <t2619>Elizabeth noticed the impertinence of her questions, but answered all of them with composure.</t2619> <t2620>Lady Catherine then observed:</t2620>
<t2621>––I understand that your father's property is to be inherited by Mr. Collins.</t2621> <t2622>I congratulate you ––he said, turning to Charlotte––; but I see no reason to bequeath the possessions outside the female line.</t2622> <t2623>In Sir Lewis de Bourgh's family, it was not done that way.</t2623> <t2624>Do you play and sing, Miss Bennet?</t2624>
<t2625>––A little.</t2625>
<t2626>––Ah! Then we will have the pleasure of hearing you at some point.</t2626> <t2627>Our piano is excellent, probably better than...</t2627> <t2628>One day you will try it.</t2628> <t2629>And your sisters, do they play and sing as well?</t2629>
<t2630>––One of them, yes.</t2630>
<t2631>––And why not all of them?</t2631> <t2632>All of them should have learned.</t2632> <t2633>The Misses Webb all play, and their parents are not as rich as yours.</t2633> <t2634>Do you draw?</t2634>
<t2635>––No, nothing.</t2635>
<t2636>––How? None of you?</t2636>
<t2637>––None.</t2637>
<t2638>––It's very strange.</t2638> <t2639>I suppose they haven't had the opportunity.</t2639> <t2640>Their mother must have taken them to the city every spring to have good teachers.</t2640>
<t2641>––My mother wouldn't have objected, but my father hates London.</t2641>
<t2642>––And is your governess still with you?</t2642>
<t2643>––We have never had a governess.</t2643>
<t2644>––They have never had a governess!</t2644> <t2645>How is that possible?</t2645> <t2646>Five daughters educated at home without a governess!</t2646> <t2647>I've never seen anything like it.</t2647> <t2648>Their mother must have been a true slave to their education.</t2648>
<t2649>Elizabeth could hardly suppress a smile as she assured him that it had not been so.</t2649>
<t2650>––So, who educated them? Who took care of them?</t2650> <t2651>Without a governess, they must have been neglected.</t2651>
<t2652>––In comparison with some families, I'm not saying otherwise; but for those of us who wanted to learn, we never lacked the means.</t2652> <t2653>We were always encouraged to read, and we had all the teachers we needed.</t2653> <t2654>It is true that those who preferred to be idle, could be.</t2654>
<t2655>––Yes, I don't doubt it! And that is what a governess can prevent, and if I had known your mother, I would have insisted with all my might that she take one.</t2655> <t2656>I always maintain that in matters of education, nothing is achieved without solid and orderly instruction, and only a governess can provide that.</t2656> <t2657>You should see the number of families I have guided in this regard!</t2657> <t2658>I love to see girls well-placed.</t2658> <t2659>Four nieces of Mrs. Jenkinson were very well placed thanks to me, and just the other day I recommended another young woman whom I heard about by chance, and the family is very happy with her.</t2659> <t2660>Mrs. Collins, did I tell you that Lady Metcalfe was here yesterday to thank me?</t2660> <t2661>She assures that Miss Pope is a treasure.</t2661> <t2662>“Lady Catherine,” she said to me, “you have given me a treasure.”</t2662> <t2663>Have any of your younger sisters been presented to society yet, Miss Bennet?</t2663>
<t2664>––Yes, ma'am, all of them.</t2664>
<t2665>––All of them! How! All five at once?</t2665> <t2666>How strange!</t2666> <t2667>And you are only the second.</t2667> <t2668>The younger ones presented in society before the older ones get married!</t2668> <t2669>Your sisters must be very young...</t2669>
<t2670>––Yes; the youngest is not yet sixteen years old.</t2670> <t2671>Perhaps she is too young to have been introduced to society.</t2671> <t2672>But in reality, madam, I believe it would be very unfair for the younger sisters to be unable to enjoy society and its amenities, just because the older ones do not have the means or desire to marry soon.</t2672> <t2673>The youngest daughter has as much right to the pleasures of youth as the first.</t2673> <t2674>Delaying them for that reason, I believe, would not be the most appropriate way to foster fraternal affection and delicacy of thought.</t2674>
<t2675>––"Goodness!" ––said His Lordship––.</t2675> <t2676>For being so young, you express your opinions very decisively.</t2676> <t2677>Tell me, how old are you?</t2677>
<t2678>––With three grown-up sisters behind me ––replied Elizabeth, smiling––, Your Lordship cannot expect me to confess it.</t2678>
<t2679>Lady Catherine was astonished not to have received a direct response; and Elizabeth suspected that she was the first person who had dared to mock such majestic impertinence.</t2679>
<t2680>––You can't be more than twenty, I'm sure; so you don't need to hide your age.</t2680>
<t2681>––I haven't turned twenty-one yet.</t2681>
<t2682>When the gentlemen entered and finished having tea, the game tables were set up.</t2682> <t2683>Lady Catherine, Sir William, and the Collinses sat down to play a game of quadrille, and as Miss de Bourgh preferred to play casino, Elizabeth and Maria had the honor of helping Mrs. Jenkinson complete her table, which was supremely boring.</t2683> <t2684>Hardly a syllable was uttered that did not pertain to the game, except when Mrs. Jenkinson expressed her fears that Miss de Bourgh might be too hot or too cold, too much light or too little.</t2684> <t2685>The other table was much more lively.</t2685> <t2686>Lady Catherine almost never stopped talking, highlighting the mistakes of her fellow players or recounting some anecdote about herself.</t2686> <t2687>Collins did nothing but affirm everything Her Ladyship said, thanking her every time he won and apologizing when he thought his winnings were excessive.</t2687> <t2688>Sir William did not say much.</t2688> <t2689>He devoted himself to recalling in his memory all those anecdotes and so many illustrious names.</t2689>
<t2690>When Lady Catherine and her daughter grew tired of playing, the tables were cleared, and they offered the carriage to Mrs. Collins, who accepted it very gratefully, and immediately gave orders to bring it.</t2690> <t2691>The gathering then congregated by the fire to hear Lady Catherine predict what the weather would be like the next day.</t2691> <t2692>At that moment, they were informed that the carriage was at the door, and with many bows from Sir William and many expressions of gratitude from Mr. Collins, they took their leave.</t2692> <t2693>As soon as they left the hallway behind, Mr. Collins invited Elizabeth to express her opinion on what she had seen at Rosings, to which she agreed, only for Charlotte's sake, exaggerating it more than she felt.</t2693> <t2694>But no matter how hard she tried, her praise did not satisfy Mr. Collins, who soon found himself obliged to take on the task of praising Her Ladyship himself.</t2694>
<t2695>CHAPTER XXX</t2695>
<t2696>Sir William spent no more than a week in Hunsford, but it was enough to convince him that his daughter was very well situated and that a husband like that and a neighborhood like that were not often found.</t2696> <t2697>While he was there, Collins dedicated the morning to taking him around in his carriage to show him the countryside; but as soon as he left, the family returned to their usual occupations.</t2697> <t2698>Elizabeth was grateful that with the change in routine, she no longer had to see her cousin so frequently, as most of the time between lunch and dinner, Collins spent working in the garden, reading, writing, or looking out the window of his study, which faced the road.</t2698> <t2699>The room where the ladies usually stayed faced the back of the house.</t2699> <t2700>At first, it surprised Elizabeth that Charlotte did not prefer to be in the dining room, which was a larger and more pleasant-looking room.</t2700> <t2701>But soon she saw that her friend had excellent reasons for acting this way, as Collins would have undoubtedly spent less time in his room if they had enjoyed one as large as his.</t2701> <t2702>And Elizabeth approved of Charlotte's attitude.</t2702>
<t2703>From the living room, they couldn't see the road, so it was always Collins who informed them about the passing cars and especially about how often Miss de Bourgh crossed in her phaeton, something he never failed to report even though it happened almost every day.</t2703> <t2704>Miss de Bourgh usually stopped at the house to chat with Charlotte for a few minutes, but it was difficult to convince her to get out of the carriage.</t2704>
<t2705>Few days passed without Collins taking a walk to Rosings, and his wife often felt it her duty to do the same; Elizabeth, until she remembered that there might be other families willing to do the same, did not understand the sacrifice of so many hours.</t2705> <t2706>From time to time, he honored them with a visit, during which nothing that happened in the drawing room escaped his notice.</t2706> <t2707>Indeed, he paid attention to what they were doing, looked at their work and advised them to do it differently, found faults in the arrangement of the furniture or discovered negligence in the maid; if he accepted any refreshment, it seemed he did so only to point out that the meat portions were too large for them.</t2707>
<t2708>Elizabeth soon realized that although the peace of the county was not entrusted to that great lady, she was an active magistrate in her own parish, whose details were communicated to her by Collins. Whenever any of the villagers were about to cause trouble or felt discontented or helpless, Lady Catherine would personally go to the required place to settle the differences and reprimand them, restoring harmony or ensuring abundance.</t2708>
<t2709>The invitation to dine at Rosings was repeated a couple of times a week, and since Sir William's departure, as there was only one game table during the evening, the entertainment was always the same.</t2709> <t2710>They did not have many other commitments, because the lifestyle of the rest of the neighbors was below that of the Collinses.</t2710> <t2711>Elizabeth did not mind; she was comfortable with it, spending long periods chatting pleasantly with Charlotte; and since the weather was wonderful, despite the time of year, she amused herself by going out for walks.</t2711> <t2712>Her favorite walk, which she often took while the others visited Lady Catherine, was the avenue that bordered one side of the estate where there was a very beautiful and sheltered path that no one but her seemed to appreciate, and on which she was out of Lady Catherine's curious reach.</t2712>
<t2713>With this tranquility, the first fortnight of her stay in Hunsford quickly passed.</t2713> <t2714>Easter was approaching, and the week before it was going to bring an addition to the family at Rosings, which, in such a small circle, had to be very important.</t2714> <t2715>Shortly after her arrival, Elizabeth heard that Darcy was going to arrive in a few weeks, and although she would have preferred any other of his acquaintances, the truth was that his presence could bring a bit of variety to the evenings at Rosings and that she could amuse herself by seeing the little foundation of Miss Bingley's hopes while observing Darcy's attitude towards Miss de Bourgh, to whom Lady Catherine evidently intended him.</t2715> <t2716>Her Ladyship spoke of his coming with enormous satisfaction, and of him, in terms of the highest admiration; and it seemed to bother her that Miss Lucas and Elizabeth had already seen him frequently before.</t2716>
<t2717>Their arrival was known immediately, for Collins had been strolling all morning with his eyes fixed on the pavilions at the entrance to the Hunsford road; as soon as he saw the carriage enter the estate, he made his corresponding bow, and ran home to deliver the grand news.</t2717> <t2718>The next morning he flew to Rosings to pay his respects.</t2718> <t2719>But there was someone else to whom he had to present them, for there he found two of Lady Catherine's nephews.</t2719> <t2720>Darcy had come with Colonel Fitzwilliam, the younger son of his uncle Lord; and to the great surprise of the whole house, when Collins returned, both gentlemen accompanied him.</t2720> <t2721>Charlotte saw them from her husband's room as they crossed the path, and rushed to the other room to inform the two girls of the great honor that awaited them, and added:</t2721>
<t2722>––Elizabeth, it is you whom I must thank for this display of courtesy.</t2722> <t2723>Mr. Darcy would not have come to visit me so soon.</t2723>
<t2724>Elizabeth barely had time to deny her right to such a compliment, for immediately the bell rang announcing the arrival of the two gentlemen, who shortly after entered the room.</t2724>
<t2725>Colonel Fitzwilliam was ahead; he was about thirty years old, not handsome, but in his manner and person, he distinguished himself as a gentleman.</t2725> <t2726>Darcy was the same as in Hertfordshire; he greeted Mrs. Collins with his usual reserve, and whatever his feelings towards Elizabeth were, he greeted her with apparent impassivity.</t2726> <t2727>Elizabeth merely bowed without saying a word.</t2727> <t2728>Colonel Fitzwilliam took part in the conversation with the ease and fluency of a well-educated man, he was very pleasant; but his cousin, after making a few brief remarks to Mrs. Collins about the garden and the house, sat for a long time without speaking to anyone.</t2728> <t2729>Finally, however, his courtesy extended to asking Elizabeth how her family was.</t2729> <t2730>She replied in the usual terms, and after a moment of silence, added:</t2730>
<t2731>––My older sister has spent these three months in London.</t2731> <t2732>Haven't you seen her by any chance?</t2732>
<t2733>He knew perfectly well that he had not seen her, but he wanted to see if any gesture would betray him and reveal that he was aware of what had happened between the Bingleys and Jane; and it seemed to him that he was a bit embarrassed when he replied that he had never had the luck to meet Miss Bennet.</t2733> <t2734>Nothing more was said about the matter, and shortly after, the gentlemen left.</t2734>
<t2735>CHAPTER XXXI</t2735>
<t2736>Colonel Fitzwilliam was highly praised and all the ladies considered that his presence would be an additional charm to the gatherings at Rosings.</t2736> <t2737>But a few days passed without receiving any invitation, as if, having guests in the house, the Collinses were no longer needed.</t2737> <t2738>Until Easter day, a week after the arrival of the two gentlemen, they were not honored with such attention and even then, upon leaving the church, they were advised not to go until the very late afternoon.</t2738>
<t2739>During the previous week, they saw very little of Lady Catherine and her daughter.</t2739> <t2740>Colonel Fitzwilliam visited the Collins' house more than once, but they only saw Darcy at church.</t2740>
<t2741>The invitation, naturally, was accepted, and at the appropriate time, the Collinses presented themselves in Lady Catherine's drawing room.</t2741> <t2742>Her Ladyship received them attentively, but it was quite clear that their company was no longer as pleasing to her as when she was alone; indeed, she was focused on her nephews and spoke with them, especially with Darcy–– much more than with any other person in the room.</t2742>
<t2743>Colonel Fitzwilliam seemed genuinely pleased to see them; in Rosings, anything seemed like a relief to him, and besides, Mrs. Collins's pretty friend had captivated him.</t2743> <t2744>He sat next to Elizabeth and they chatted so pleasantly about Kent and Hertfordshire, their travels and the time spent at home, new books and music, that Elizabeth had never enjoyed herself so much in that parlor; they spoke with such ease and animation that they attracted the attention of Lady Catherine and Darcy.</t2744> <t2745>The latter had already looked at them several times with curiosity.</t2745> <t2746>Her Ladyship soon shared the same sentiment, and it was clearly seen, for she did not hesitate to ask:</t2746>
<t2747>––What are you saying, Fitzwilliam?</t2747> <t2748>What are you talking about?</t2748> <t2749>What are you telling Miss Bennet?</t2749> <t2750>Let me hear it.</t2750>
<t2751>––We are talking about music, madam ––declared the colonel when he saw that he couldn't avoid the response.</t2751>
<t2752>––About music!</t2752> <t2753>Well, do me the favor of speaking loudly.</t2753> <t2754>Of all the topics of conversation, it is the one I enjoy the most.</t2754> <t2755>I must take part in the conversation if you are talking about music.</t2755> <t2756>I believe there are few people in England more fond of music than I am or who possess better natural taste.</t2756> <t2757>If I had studied, I would have become a great disciple.</t2757> <t2758>The same would happen to Anne if her health permitted it; I am sure she would have played delightfully.</t2758> <t2759>How is Georgiana, Darcy?</t2759>
<t2760>Darcy made a cordial compliment about how advanced his sister was.</t2760>
<t2761>––I am very glad that you give me such good news ––said Lady Catherine––, and I beg you to tell her on my behalf that if she does not practice a lot, she will not improve at all.</t2761>
<t2762>––I assure you that he doesn't need to be warned.</t2762> <t2763>He practices constantly.</t2763>
<t2764>––Better.</t2764> <t2765>That is never too much; and the next time I write to her, I will make sure she does not neglect it.</t2765> <t2766>I often tell young ladies that in music, nothing is achieved without constant practice.</t2766> <t2767>I have often told Miss Bennet that she will never play truly well if she does not practice more; and although Mrs. Collins does not have a piano, Miss Bennet will be very welcome, as I have often told her, if she comes to Rosings every day to play the piano in Mrs. Jenkinson's room.</t2767> <t2768>In that part of the house, she will not bother anyone.</t2768>
<t2769>Darcy seemed a little embarrassed by his aunt's rudeness, and did not reply.</t2769>
<t2770>When they finished their coffee, Colonel Fitzwilliam reminded Elizabeth that she had promised to play, and the young woman immediately sat down at the piano.</t2770> <t2771>The colonel placed his chair beside her.</t2771> <t2772>Lady Catherine listened to half of the song and then continued talking, as before, to her other nephew, until Darcy left her and, cautiously approaching the piano, positioned himself so that he could see the face of the beautiful performer.</t2772> <t2773>Elizabeth noticed what he was doing and at the first opportune pause turned to him with a broad smile and said:</t2773>
<t2774>––Do you intend to frighten me by coming to listen to me with such seriousness?</t2774> <t2775>I am not scared, even though your sister plays so well.</t2775> <t2776>There is a kind of stubbornness in me that never allows anyone to intimidate me.</t2776> <t2777>On the contrary, my courage grows when someone tries to intimidate me.</t2777>
<t2778>––I will not tell you that you are mistaken ––replied Darcy–– because you do not sincerely believe that I had any intention of alarming you; and I have had the pleasure of knowing you well enough to know that you sometimes take pleasure in holding opinions that are not actually yours.</t2778>
<t2779>Elizabeth laughed openly at that description of herself, and said to Colonel Fitzwilliam:</t2779>
<t2780>––Your cousin aims to give you a poor impression of me by teaching you not to believe a word I say.</t2780> <t2781>It dismays me to encounter someone so eager to uncover my true nature in a place where I had hoped to pass for better than I am.</t2781> <t2782>Really, Mr. Darcy, it is very ungenerous of you to reveal the bad things you learned about me in Hertfordshire, and allow me to say that it is also very indiscreet, for this might lead me to retaliate and bring to light things that would scandalize your relatives.</t2782>
<t2783>––I'm not––afraid ––he said, smiling.</t2783>
<t2784>––Tell me, please, what you accuse him of ––exclaimed Colonel Fitzwilliam––.</t2784> <t2785>I would like to know how he behaves among strangers.</t2785>
<t2786>––I will tell you, but prepare yourself to hear something very dreadful.</t2786> <t2787>You must know that the first time I saw him was at a ball, and at that ball, what do you think he did?</t2787> <t2788>Well, he only danced four pieces, despite the scarcity of gentlemen, and more than one lady was left sitting for lack of a partner.</t2788> <t2789>Mr. Darcy, you cannot deny it.</t2789>
<t2790>––I did not have the honor of knowing any of the ladies at the gathering, except for those who accompanied me.</t2790>
<t2791>––True, and at a ball there is never a chance to be introduced... Well, Colonel Fitzwilliam, what shall I play now?</t2791> <t2792>My fingers are awaiting your orders.</t2792>
<t2793>––Perhaps you would have judged me better ––added Darcy–– if I had requested an introduction.</t2793> <t2794>But I am not good at making myself known to strangers.</t2794>
<t2795>––Let's ask his cousin why he is like that ––said Elizabeth, addressing no one but Colonel Fitzwilliam––.</t2795> <t2796>Shall we ask him how it is possible that a talented and well-educated man, who has lived in high society, is not capable of attending to strangers?</t2796>
<t2797>––I can answer this question myself ––replied Fitzwilliam–– without asking Darcy.</t2797> <t2798>That's because he doesn't want to bother.</t2798>
<t2799>––I acknowledge ––said Darcy–– that I do not have the ability that others possess to converse easily with people I have never seen.</t2799> <t2800>I cannot engage in those conversations and pretend to be interested in their affairs as is customary.</t2800>
<t2801>––My fingers ––replied Elizabeth–– do not move over this instrument in the masterful way I have seen other women's fingers move; they do not have the same strength or agility, and they cannot produce the same impression.</t2801> <t2802>But I have always believed it was my fault, for not wanting to take the trouble to do exercises.</t2802> <t2803>Not because my fingers are not capable, like those of any other woman, of playing perfectly.</t2803>
<t2804>Darcy smiled and said to her:</t2804>
<t2805>––You are absolutely right.</t2805> <t2806>You have used your time much better.</t2806> <t2807>No one who has the privilege of listening to you will be able to find fault.</t2807> <t2808>None of us perform in front of strangers.</t2808>
<t2809>Lady Catherine interrupted them by asking what they were talking about.</t2809> <t2810>Elizabeth began to play again.</t2810> <t2811>Lady Catherine approached and after listening to her for a few minutes, said to Darcy:</t2811>
<t2812>––Miss Bennet would not play badly if she practiced more and had enjoyed the advantages of a good teacher from London.</t2812> <t2813>She knows what it is to play the keys, although her taste is not like Anne's.</t2813> <t2814>Anne would have been a wonderful pianist if her health had allowed her to learn.</t2814>
<t2815>Elizabeth looked at Darcy to observe his cordial assent to the praise given to his cousin, but neither then nor at any other time did she discover any sign of love; and from his attitude towards Miss de Bourgh, Elizabeth deduced something consoling in favor of Miss Bingley: that Darcy would have married her if she had belonged to his family.</t2815>
<t2816>Lady Catherine continued making observations about Elizabeth's manner of playing, mixed with numerous instructions on execution and taste.</t2816> <t2817>Elizabeth endured them with all the patience that courtesy demands, and at the gentlemen's request, she continued playing until Her Ladyship's carriage was ready and took everyone home.</t2817>
<t2818>CHAPTER XXXII</t2818>
<t2819>The next morning, Elizabeth was alone writing to Jane, while Mrs. Collins and Maria had gone shopping in town, when she was startled by the ringing of the doorbell, an unmistakable sign of a visitor.</t2819> <t2820>Although she had not heard any carriage, she thought it might be Lady Catherine, and hurried to hide the half-written letter to avoid impertinent questions.</t2820> <t2821>But to her great surprise, the door opened, and Mr. Darcy entered the room.</t2821> <t2822>Darcy alone.</t2822>
<t2823>He seemed surprised to find her alone and apologized for his intrusion, saying that he believed all the ladies were in the house.</t2823>
<t2824>They both sat down and, after the obligatory questions about Rosings, it seemed they were going to remain silent.</t2824> <t2825>Therefore, it was absolutely necessary to think of something, and Elizabeth, faced with this necessity, recalled the last time they had seen each other in Hertfordshire and felt curious to see what he would say about his hasty departure.</t2825>
<t2826>––How suddenly you left Netherfield last November, Mr. Darcy! ––she said––.</t2826> <t2827>It must have been a very pleasant surprise for Mr. Bingley to see you so soon by his side, because, if I remember correctly, he had left a day earlier.</t2827> <t2828>I suppose both he and his sisters were well when you left London.</t2828>
<t2829>––Perfectly.</t2829> <t2830>Thank you.</t2830>
<t2831>Elizabeth warned that she was not going to answer him anymore and, after a brief silence, added:</t2831>
<t2832>––I understand that Mr. Bingley does not intend to return to Netherfield.</t2832>
<t2833>––I have never heard him say such a thing; but it is likely that he will not spend much time there in the future.</t2833> <t2834>He has many friends and is at a stage in life where friends and commitments continuously increase.</t2834>
<t2835>––If you intend to stay for a short time at Netherfield, it would be better for the neighborhood if you left it completely, and thus possibly another family could settle there.</t2835> <t2836>But perhaps Mr. Bingley has taken the house not so much for the convenience of the neighborhood as for his own, and it is to be hoped that he will keep it or leave it based on that same principle.</t2836>
<t2837>––It wouldn't surprise me ––added Darcy–– if he got rid of it as soon as he was offered an acceptable purchase.</t2837>
<t2838>Elizabeth did not answer.</t2838> <t2839>She feared speaking too much about her friend, and since she had nothing else to say, she decided to let Darcy find another topic of conversation.</t2839>
<t2840>He understood it and said immediately:</t2840>
<t2841>––This house seems very comfortable.</t2841> <t2842>I believe Lady Catherine improved it a lot when Mr. Collins first came to Hunsford.</t2842>
<t2843>––It seems so, and I am sure that he could not have given a better proof of his kindness.</t2843>
<t2844>––Mr. Collins seems to have been very fortunate in the choice of his wife.</t2844>
<t2845>––That's right.</t2845> <t2846>His friends can rejoice that he found one of the few intelligent women who would have accepted him or who would have made him happy after accepting him.</t2846> <t2847>My friend is very sensible, although her marriage to Collins seems to me the least sensible of her actions.</t2847> <t2848>However, she seems completely happy: from a prudent point of view, this was a good match for her.</t2848>
<t2849>––It must be very pleasant for Mrs. Collins to live so close to her family and friends.</t2849>
<t2850>––Do you call that a short distance?</t2850> <t2851>It's about fifty miles.</t2851>
<t2852>––And what are fifty miles of good road?</t2852> <t2853>Just over half a day's journey.</t2853> <t2854>Yes, I call that a short distance.</t2854>
<t2855>––I would never have considered that distance was one of the advantages of the match, exclaimed Elizabeth, and it would never have occurred to me that Mrs. Collins lived close to her family.</t2855>
<t2856>––That shows the attachment you have to Hertfordshire.</t2856> <t2857>Everything beyond Longbourn must already seem far away to you.</t2857>
<t2858>While speaking, he smiled in a way that Elizabeth thought she could interpret: Darcy must have assumed she was thinking about Jane and Netherfield; and she replied somewhat blushing:</t2858>
<t2859>––I don't mean to say that a woman cannot live far from her family.</t2859> <t2860>Far and near are relative things and depend on very different circumstances.</t2860> <t2861>If one has the fortune not to worry about travel expenses, the distance is the least of it.</t2861> <t2862>But this is not the case.</t2862> <t2863>The Collinses do not live in straitened circumstances, but they are not so wealthy as to afford frequent travel; I am sure my friend would not say she lives close to her family unless she were at half this distance.</t2863>
<t2864>Darcy moved his seat a little closer to Elizabeth's and said:</t2864>
<t2865>––You have no right to be so attached to your residence.</t2865> <t2866>You will not always be in Longbourn.</t2866> <t2867>Elizabeth seemed to be surprised, and the gentleman thought he should change the conversation.</t2867> <t2868>He put his chair back where it was, took a newspaper from the table, and glancing over it, asked coldly:</t2868>
<t2869>––Do you like Kent?</t2869>
<t2870>This was followed by a short dialogue on the subject of the countryside, concise and moderate on both sides, which soon ended, as Charlotte and her sister entered, having just returned from their walk.</t2870> <t2871>The tête-à-tête left them stunned.</t2871> <t2872>Darcy explained the misunderstanding that had led to his visit to the house; he remained seated for a few more minutes, not talking much to anyone, and then left.</t2872>
<t2873>––What does this mean? ––asked Charlotte as soon as he left––.</t2873> <t2874>Dear Elizabeth, he must be in love with you, for otherwise, he would never have come to see us with such familiarity.</t2874>
<t2875>But when Elizabeth recounted how quiet he had been, it didn't seem very likely, despite Charlotte's good wishes; and after several conjectures, they limited themselves to supposing that his visit had been due to the difficulty of finding something to do, which was very natural at that time of year.</t2875> <t2876>All the sports had ended.</t2876> <t2877>At Lady Catherine's house, there were books and a billiard table, but the gentlemen were frustrated by always being cooped up indoors, and whether it was because the Collins' residence was so close, or because the walk was pleasant, or because of the people who lived there, the two cousins felt tempted to visit them every day.</t2877> <t2878>They would show up at different times in the morning, sometimes separately and other times together, and occasionally accompanied by their aunt.</t2878> <t2879>It was evident that Colonel Fitzwilliam came because he felt comfortable with them, which naturally made him even more pleasant.</t2879> <t2880>The pleasure Elizabeth derived from his company and Fitzwilliam's evident admiration for her made her remember her first favorite, George Wickham.</t2880> <t2881>Comparing them, Elizabeth found that the Colonel's manners were less attractive and sweet than Wickham's, but Fitzwilliam seemed to her to be a more cultured man.</t2881>
<t2882>But understanding why Darcy came to the house so often was more difficult.</t2882> <t2883>It couldn't be to seek company, as he would sit for ten minutes without opening his mouth, and when he spoke, it seemed more out of obligation than pleasure, as if it were a sacrifice rather than a delight.</t2883> <t2884>He was rarely truly animated.</t2884> <t2885>Mrs. Collins didn't know what to think of him.</t2885> <t2886>Since Colonel Fitzwilliam sometimes laughed at Darcy's foolishness, Charlotte understood that he must not always be like that, something her limited acquaintance with the gentleman would not have allowed her to guess; and as she wished to believe that this change was the work of love and that the object of that love was Elizabeth, she set out to discover it.</t2886> <t2887>When they were at Rosings and whenever Darcy came to her house, Charlotte observed him attentively, but she couldn't make anything of it.</t2887> <t2888>It was true that he looked at her friend a lot, but the expression of those looks was ambiguous.</t2888> <t2889>It was a fixed and deep gaze, but Charlotte sometimes doubted that it was enthusiastic, and at times it simply seemed that he was distracted.</t2889>
<t2890>Two or three times he told Elizabeth that he might be in love with her, but Elizabeth would laugh, and Mrs. Collins thought it more prudent not to insist on it to avoid the danger of fostering impossible hopes, for she had no doubt that all of Elizabeth's aversion to Darcy would dissipate with the belief that he loved her.</t2890>
<t2891>In the good and affectionate plans that Charlotte made regarding Elizabeth, sometimes marrying her to Colonel Fitzwilliam was included.</t2891> <t2892>He was, without comparison, the most pleasant of all.</t2892> <t2893>He felt genuine admiration for Elizabeth and his position was excellent.</t2893> <t2894>But Darcy had considerable patronage in the Church, and his cousin had none.</t2894>
<t2895>CHAPTER XXXIII</t2895>
<t2896>During her walks through the grove within the estate, Elizabeth had more than once unexpectedly encountered Darcy.</t2896> <t2897>The first time, she was not at all pleased that bad luck had brought him precisely to a place where no one else usually went, and to prevent it from happening again, she was careful to indicate that it was her favorite spot.</t2897> <t2898>Consequently, it was rare for the encounter to happen again, and yet, it occurred even a third time.</t2898> <t2899>It seemed as if he did it with intentional malice or as a penance, because it wasn't just limited to the usual questions or a simple and annoying stop; Darcy would turn back and walk with her.</t2899> <t2900>He never talked much nor bothered her by making her talk or listen too much.</t2900> <t2901>But at the third encounter, Elizabeth was astonished by the oddity of the questions he asked: if she liked being in Hunsford, if she enjoyed solitary walks, and what her opinion was of the happiness of the Collins' marriage; but the strangest thing was that when talking about Rosings and her limited knowledge of the house, it seemed that he assumed that, upon returning to Kent, Elizabeth would also reside there.</t2901> <t2902>Was he thinking about Colonel Fitzwilliam?</t2902> <t2903>The young woman thought that if he meant anything, it had to be an allusion in that direction.</t2903> <t2904>This unsettled her a bit, and she was glad to find herself at the gate of the fence that was right in front of the Collins' house.</t2904>
<t2905>One day, while walking, she was rereading Jane's last letter and focusing on a passage that denoted the sadness with which it had been written, when, instead of running into Darcy again, she looked up and found herself face to face with Colonel Fitzwilliam.</t2905> <t2906>She quickly hid the letter and, feigning a smile, said:</t2906>
<t2907>––I never knew until now that you walked this path.</t2907>
<t2908>––I have been making the full round of the estate ––replied the colonel––, something I usually do every year.</t2908> <t2909>And I was thinking of finishing it with a visit to the parish priest's house.</t2909> <t2910>Are you going to continue walking?</t2910>
<t2911>––No; I was going to return.</t2911>
<t2912>Indeed, he turned around and together they headed towards the parish house.</t2912>
<t2913>––Are you definitely leaving Kent on Saturday? ––asked Elizabeth.</t2913>
<t2914>––Yes, if Darcy doesn't postpone the trip again.</t2914> <t2915>I am at his disposal; he arranges things as he sees fit.</t2915>
<t2916>––And if he does not like things, at least he takes great pleasure in being able to arrange them as he pleases.</t2916> <t2917>I do not know anyone who seems to enjoy the power to do what he wants more than Mr. Darcy.</t2917>
<t2918>––He likes to have his own way, replied Colonel Fitzwilliam––.</t2918> <t2919>But we all like that.</t2919> <t2920>It's just that he has more means to do it than many others, because he is rich and others are poor.</t2920> <t2921>I say what I feel.</t2921> <t2922>You know that younger children have to get used to dependency and give up many things.</t2922>
<t2923>––I believe that the youngest son of a count doesn't have it as bad as you say.</t2923> <t2924>Let's see, honestly, what do you know about renunciations and dependencies?</t2924> <t2925>When have you been deprived, due to lack of money, of going where you wanted or getting something you fancied?</t2925>
<t2926>––Those are insignificant matters, and perhaps I can acknowledge that I haven't suffered many deprivations of that nature.</t2926> <t2927>But in matters of greater importance, I am subject to a lack of money.</t2927> <t2928>The younger children cannot marry whenever they please.</t2928>
<t2929>––Unless they like rich women, which I think happens often.</t2929>
<t2930>––Our habit of spending makes us too dependent, and there are not many of my rank who marry without paying a little attention to money.</t2930>
<t2931>"Could this be referring to me?" thought Elizabeth, blushing.</t2931> <t2932>But recovering, she replied in a jovial tone:</t2932>
<t2933>––And tell me, what is the usual price for the younger son of an earl?</t2933> <t2934>Unless the elder brother is very ill, you wouldn't ask for more than fifty thousand pounds...</t2934>
<t2935>He responded in the same tone and the topic was exhausted.</t2935> <t2936>To prevent a silence that might make the colonel think that what was said had affected her, Elizabeth spoke shortly after:</t2936>
<t2937>––I imagine that his cousin brought him along mainly to have someone at his disposal.</t2937> <t2938>It surprises me that he doesn't get married, as that way he would have someone constantly subject to him.</t2938> <t2939>Although it may be that his sister suffices for that, for the moment, since as she is under his exclusive custody, he must be able to command her as he pleases.</t2939>
<t2940>––No ––said Colonel Fitzwilliam––, that advantage must be shared with me.</t2940> <t2941>I am entrusted, along with him, with the guardianship of his sister.</t2941>
<t2942>––Really?</t2942> <t2943>And tell me, what kind of tutelage do you provide?</t2943> <t2944>Does it keep you very busy?</t2944> <t2945>Girls of her age can sometimes be a bit difficult to manage, and if she has the same character as Mr. Darcy, she must also like to have her own way.</t2945>
<t2946>While she was speaking, Elizabeth noticed that the colonel was looking at her very seriously, and the way he immediately asked how she supposed Miss Darcy could cause them any trouble convinced Elizabeth that, to some extent, she had come close to the truth.</t2946> <t2947>The young woman answered his question directly:</t2947>

<t2954>––Oh, yes! ––said Elizabeth dryly––.</t2954> <t2955>Mr. Darcy is incredibly kind to Mr. Bingley and takes care of him in an extraordinary way.</t2955>
<t2956>––Does he take care of him?</t2956> <t2957>Yes, really, I think he takes care of him precisely in what requires the most care.</t2957> <t2958>From something he told me when we were coming here, I presume that Bingley owes him a lot.</t2958> <t2959>But I must ask you to forgive me, because I have no right to assume that Bingley was the person Darcy was referring to.</t2959> <t2960>They are just conjectures.</t2960>
<t2961>––What does it mean?</t2961>
<t2962>––It is something that Darcy would not want to be disclosed, for if it reached the ears of the lady's family, it would be very unpleasant.</t2962>
<t2963>Don't worry, I won't disclose it.</t2963>
<t2964>––Keep in mind that I lack evidence to suppose that it is Bingley.</t2964> <t2965>What Darcy told me is that he was glad to have recently saved a friend from a very imprudent marriage; but he did not mention names or details, and I suspected that the friend was Bingley only because he seems to me a young man very suitable for such a case, and because I know they were together all summer.</t2965>
<t2966>––Did Mr. Darcy tell you the reasons he had for interfering in the matter?</t2966>
<t2967>––I understood that there were some significant objections against the young lady.</t2967>
<t2968>––And what tricks did he use to separate them?</t2968>
<t2969>––He didn't speak of his tricks ––said Fitzwilliam, smiling––.</t2969> <t2970>He only told me what I just told you.</t2970>
<t2971>Elizabeth made no comment and continued walking with her heart swollen with indignation.</t2971> <t2972>After observing her for a bit, Fitzwilliam asked her why she was so thoughtful.</t2972>
<t2973>––I am thinking about what you have told me ––Elizabeth replied––.</t2973> <t2974>Your cousin's behavior does not seem right to me at all.</t2974> <t2975>Why did he have to be the judge?</t2975>
<t2976>––Does that mean your intervention was indiscreet? ––I don't see what right Mr. Darcy has to decide on his friend's inclination and why he should be the one to direct and determine, in his judgment, how his friend should be happy.</t2976> <t2977>But ––he continued, composing himself––, not knowing the details, it is not right to criticize him.</t2977> <t2978>One must believe that love had little to do with this case.</t2978>
