<t2980>He said this last part in jest, but to Elizabeth, it seemed such an accurate portrayal of Darcy that she thought it useless to respond.</t2980> <t2981>She changed the subject and began talking about trivial matters until they arrived at the house.</t2981> <t2982>As soon as the colonel left, Elizabeth locked herself in her room and thought without interruption about everything she had heard.</t2982> <t2983>There was no supposing that the colonel was referring to anyone other than Jane and Bingley.</t2983> <t2984>There could not exist two men over whom Darcy exercised such unlimited influence.</t2984> <t2985>She had never doubted that Darcy had been involved in the measures taken to separate Bingley and Jane; but she had always attributed the plan and the main role to Miss Bingley.</t2985> <t2986>However, if her own vanity did not blind her, he was the culprit; his pride and his whim were the cause of all that Jane had suffered and continued to suffer.</t2986> <t2987>Because of him, all hope of happiness had disappeared from the kindest and most generous heart in the world, and no one could calculate all the harm he had done.</t2987>
<t2988>Colonel Fitzwilliam had said that "there were some serious objections against the young lady."</t2988> <t2989>And those objections would surely be having an uncle who was a country lawyer and another who was a merchant in London...</t2989>
<t2990>“Against Jane,” thought Elizabeth, “there was no possible objection.</t2990> <t2991>She is the embodiment of charm and kindness!</t2991> <t2992>Her intelligence is excellent; her talent, unparalleled; her manners, captivating.</t2992> <t2993>There was nothing to object to about her father either, who, despite his eccentricities, possessed qualities that even Darcy would not disdain and a respectability that perhaps Darcy would never attain.”</t2993> <t2994>When she thought of her mother, her confidence wavered a bit; but she did not admit that Darcy could have any serious objection to her, for she was sure that his pride–– valued the lack of rank in his friend's potential relatives more than their lack of sense.</t2994> <t2995>In short, one had to think that he was driven partly by the most stubborn pride and partly by his desire to keep Bingley for his sister.</t2995>
<t2996>The agitation and tears gave Elizabeth a headache that worsened in the afternoon, and adding her ailment to her desire not to see Darcy, she decided not to accompany her cousins to Rosings, where they were invited for tea.</t2996> <t2997>Mrs. Collins, seeing that she was truly unwell, did not insist, and did everything possible to prevent her husband from doing so; but Collins could not hide his fear that Lady Catherine would take offense at Elizabeth's absence.</t2997>
<t2998>CHAPTER XXXIV</t2998>
<t2999>When everyone had left, Elizabeth, as if she intended to further exasperate herself against Darcy, set about reviewing all the letters she had received from Jane since she had been in Kent.</t2999> <t3000>They contained no lamentations nor anything that indicated she remembered the past or that she suffered because of it; but overall, and almost in every line, there was a lack of the joy that used to characterize Jane's style, a joy that, as was natural in such a calm and affectionate character, had almost never been eclipsed.</t3000> <t3001>Elizabeth focused on all the phrases revealing unease, with an attention she had not given them in the first reading.</t3001> <t3002>Darcy's shameful boast about the harm he had caused made her feel her sister's suffering more keenly.</t3002> <t3003>It consoled her a little to think that in two days she would be back by Jane's side and could help her regain her spirits with the care that only affection can provide.</t3003>
<t3004>She could not think of Darcy's departure without remembering that his cousin was leaving with him; but Colonel Fitzwilliam had made it clear that he could not think of her.</t3004>
<t3005>While she was contemplating all this, she was startled by the doorbell, and she hoped it might be Colonel Fitzwilliam himself, who had once visited them in the afternoon and might be coming to ask how she was.</t3005> <t3006>But she soon dismissed that idea and continued with her thoughts when, to her complete surprise, she saw Darcy enter the room.</t3006> <t3007>He immediately began to inquire, very hastily, about her health, attributing his visit to a desire to know that she was feeling better.</t3007> <t3008>She replied courteously but coldly.</t3008> <t3009>Elizabeth was astonished but did not say a word.</t3009> <t3010>After several minutes of silence, he approached her and, very agitated, declared:</t3010>
<t3011>––I have fought in vain.</t3011> <t3012>I can't go on any longer.</t3012> <t3013>I am incapable of containing my feelings.</t3013> <t3014>Allow me to tell you that I admire you and love you passionately.</t3014>
<t3015>Elizabeth's astonishment was inexpressible.</t3015> <t3016>She blushed, stood staring at him, indecisive and mute.</t3016> <t3017>He interpreted it as a favorable sign and continued expressing to her all that he had felt for her for a long time.</t3017> <t3018>He explained himself well, but he had to talk not only about his love, and he was no more eloquent on the subject of tenderness than on that of pride.</t3018> <t3019>Elizabeth's inferiority, the degradation it meant for him, the family obstacles that good judgment had always made him prioritize over esteem.</t3019> <t3020>He spoke of these things with an ardor that reflected how much they hurt him, but all of it was not the most suitable to support his request.</t3020>
<t3021>Despite all the deeply rooted antipathy she had for him, Elizabeth could not remain indifferent to the manifestations of affection from a man like Darcy, and although her opinion did not change in the slightest, she was initially saddened by the disappointment he was going to experience; but the language he used afterward was so insulting that all compassion turned into anger.</t3021> <t3022>However, she tried to reply calmly when he finished speaking.</t3022> <t3023>He concluded by assuring her of the firmness of his love which, despite all his efforts, he had not been able to overcome, and hoping that it would be rewarded with the acceptance of his hand.</t3023> <t3024>By his manner of speaking, Elizabeth noticed that Darcy did not doubt that her response would be favorable.</t3024> <t3025>He spoke of fears and anxiety, but his appearance revealed absolute certainty.</t3025> <t3026>This exasperated her even more, and when he finished, she replied with cheeks flushed with anger:</t3026>
<t3027>––In these cases, I believe it is customary to express some gratitude for the feelings expressed, even if they cannot be equally reciprocated.</t3027> <t3028>It is natural to feel this obligation, and if I felt gratitude, I would thank you.</t3028> <t3029>But I cannot; I have never coveted your regard, and you have granted it very much against your will.</t3029> <t3030>I regret having hurt someone, but it was unconsciously, and I hope that hurt lasts a short time.</t3030> <t3031>The same feelings that, as you say, prevented you from revealing your intentions to me for so long will easily overcome that suffering.</t3031>
<t3032>Darcy, who was leaning against the mantelpiece with his eyes fixed on Elizabeth's face, seemed to receive her words with as much resentment as surprise.</t3032> <t3033>His complexion paled with rage and all his features showed the disturbance of his mind.</t3033> <t3034>He struggled to maintain his composure and would not open his lips until he believed he had achieved it.</t3034> <t3035>This silence was terrible for Elizabeth.</t3035> <t3036>Finally, forcing his voice to appear calm, he said:</t3036>
<t3037>––And is this the only response I will have the honor of expecting?</t3037> <t3038>Perhaps I should ask why I am being rejected with such scant courtesy.</t3038> <t3039>But it doesn't matter in the slightest.</t3039>
<t3040>––I could also ask, replied Elizabeth–– why, with such an evident purpose of offending and insulting me, you tell me that you like me against your will, against your better judgment, and even against your nature.</t3040> <t3041>Isn't this an excuse for my lack of courtesy, if indeed I have committed it?</t3041> <t3042>But, moreover, I have received other provocations, you know that very well.</t3042> <t3043>Even if my feelings had not been contrary to yours, even if they had been indifferent or even favorable, do you think there would be anything that could tempt me to accept the man who has been responsible for ruining, perhaps forever, the happiness of a very dear sister?</t3043>
<t3044>Upon hearing these words, Darcy changed color; but the shock was fleeting and he continued listening without any intention of interrupting her.</t3044>
<t3045>––I have every reason in the world to have a poor opinion of you ––continued Elizabeth––.</t3045> <t3046>There is nothing that can excuse your unjust and vile conduct.</t3046> <t3047>You will not dare to deny that you were the main, if not the only, cause of the separation between Mr. Bingley and my sister, exposing one to the censure of people for being capricious and fickle, and the other to ridicule for her failed hopes, plunging both into the greatest misfortune.</t3047>
<t3048>She paused and saw, indignantly, that Darcy was listening to her with an air that indicated he was not at all moved by any kind of remorse.</t3048> <t3049>He even looked at her with a smile of petulant disbelief.</t3049>
<t3050>––Can you deny that you have done this? ––she repeated.</t3050>
<t3051>Pretending to be calm, Darcy replied:</t3051>
<t3052>––I must not deny that I did everything in my power to separate my friend from his sister, nor that I am glad of the result.</t3052> <t3053>I have been kinder to him than to myself.</t3053>
<t3054>Elizabeth disdained to appear as if she noticed that subtle reflection, but its meaning did not escape her, and she could not reconcile it.</t3054>
<t3055>––But my antipathy is not only based on this ––continued Elizabeth.</t3055> <t3056>My opinion of you was formed long before this matter took place.</t3056> <t3057>Your character was revealed by a story Mr. Wickham told me a few months ago.</t3057> <t3058>What can you say to this?</t3058> <t3059>With what fictitious act of friendship can you defend yourself now?</t3059> <t3060>With what falsehood can you justify your dominance over others in this case?</t3060>
<t3061>––You are very keenly interested in what affects that gentleman ––said Darcy in a less calm tone, his face flushed.</t3061>
<t3062>––Who, knowing the hardships he has endured, can avoid feeling interest in him?</t3062>
<t3063>––"The hardships he has endured!" exclaimed Darcy disdainfully––.</t3063> <t3064>"Yes, truly, immense hardships..."</t3064>
<t3065>––It's your fault! ––exclaimed Elizabeth energetically––.</t3065> <t3066>You reduced him to his current relative poverty.</t3066> <t3067>You denied him the future that, as you well know, was destined for him.</t3067> <t3068>In the best years of his life, you deprived him of an independence to which he not only had a right but also deserved.</t3068> <t3069>You did all this!</t3069> <t3070>And you are still capable of ridiculing and mocking his sorrows...</t3070>
<t3071>––And that is –– shouted Darcy as he paced the room like a whirlwind –– the opinion you have of me!</t3071> <t3072>This is the esteem in which you hold me!</t3072> <t3073>I thank you for explaining it to me so openly.</t3073> <t3074>My faults, according to your calculation, are truly enormous.</t3074> <t3075>But perhaps ––he added, stopping and turning towards her–– these offenses would have been overlooked if I had not wounded your pride with my honest confession of the reservations that for a long time prevented me from making a decision.</t3075> <t3076>I would have spared myself these bitter accusations if I had been more skillful and had hidden my struggle, flattering you by making you believe that I had taken this step driven by reason, by reflection, by an unconditional and pure inclination, by whatever.</t3076> <t3077>But I abhor all kinds of deceit and I am not ashamed of the feelings I have expressed, they were natural and just.</t3077> <t3078>How could you suppose that I would be pleased by the inferiority of your family and that I would congratulate myself on the prospect of having relatives whose condition is so far below mine?</t3078>
<t3079>Elizabeth's irritation grew with each passing moment; even so, she tried with all her might to speak calmly when she said:</t3079>
<t3080>––You are mistaken, Mr. Darcy, if you suppose that what affected me was your manner of proposing; if you imagine that you would have spared me the unpleasant task of rejecting you had you behaved in a more gentlemanlike manner.</t3080>
<t3081>Elizabeth realized that she was about to be interrupted, but she said nothing and continued:</t3081>
<t3082>––You could not have offered me your hand in any way that would have tempted me to accept it.</t3082>
<t3083>Once again, his astonishment was obvious.</t3083> <t3084>He looked at her with an expression of disbelief and humiliation at the same time, and she continued speaking:</t3084>
<t3085>––From the beginning, almost from the first moment I met him, his manners convinced me of his arrogance, his vanity, and his selfish disdain for the feelings of others; they disgusted me so much that they gave rise to the disapproval that subsequent events turned into firm dislike; and it wasn't even a month since I had known him when I realized that you would be the last man on earth I could marry.</t3085>
<t3086>––You have said enough, miss.</t3086> <t3087>I perfectly understand your feelings and I can only be ashamed of my own.</t3087> <t3088>Forgive me for having made you waste so much time, and accept my best wishes for your health and happiness.</t3088>
<t3089>Having said this, he hurriedly left the room, and Elizabeth immediately heard him open the front door and leave the house.</t3089>
<t3090>The confusion of her mind made her suffer intensely.</t3090> <t3091>She could not stand and had to sit down because her legs were giving way.</t3091> <t3092>She cried for half an hour.</t3092> <t3093>Her astonishment at recalling what had happened grew more and more.</t3093> <t3094>Having received a marriage proposal from Darcy, who had been in love with her for so many months, and so in love that he wanted to marry her despite all the objections that had led him to prevent his friend from marrying Jane, and which should have applied equally in his own case, was incredible.</t3094> <t3095>She was pleased to have inspired such a vehement affection.</t3095> <t3096>But the pride, his abominable pride, his shameless confession of what he had done to Jane, his unforgivable brazenness in admitting it without even trying to excuse himself, and the insensitivity with which he had spoken of Wickham despite not denying his cruelty towards him, soon prevailed over the compassion she had felt when thinking about his love.</t3096>
<t3097>She remained immersed in her agitated thoughts until the noise of Lady Catherine's carriage made her realize that she was not in a condition to meet Charlotte, and she ran up to her room.</t3097>
<t3098>CHAPTER XXXV</t3098>
<t3099>Elizabeth woke up the next morning with the same thoughts and reflections she had fallen asleep with.</t3099> <t3100>She couldn't recover from the surprise of what had happened; it was impossible for her to think of anything else.</t3100> <t3101>Unable to do anything, as soon as she had breakfast, she decided to go out to get some fresh air and exercise.</t3101> <t3102>She was heading directly towards her favorite walk when she remembered that Darcy sometimes went there; she stopped and instead of entering the estate, she took another path in the opposite direction to the street where the toll gate was, which was still bordered by the Rosings' fence, and soon passed by one of the gates that gave access to the estate.</t3102>
<t3103>After walking up and down that part of the road two or three times, he felt the temptation, given how delightful the morning was, to stop at the gates and gaze at the estate.</t3103> <t3104>The five weeks he had spent in Kent had greatly transformed the countryside, and each day the early trees grew greener.</t3104> <t3105>He was about to continue his walk when he glimpsed a gentleman in the avenue bordering the estate; the gentleman was walking towards her, and Elizabeth, fearing it was Darcy, instantly retreated.</t3105> <t3106>But the person advancing was already close enough to see her; he continued walking quickly and called her name.</t3106> <t3107>She had turned, but upon hearing that voice in which she recognized Darcy, she continued towards the gate.</t3107> <t3108>The gentleman caught up with her and, showing her a letter which she instinctively took, said to her with a haughty look:</t3108>
<t3109>––I have been strolling through the promenade for a while hoping to find you.</t3109> <t3110>Will you grant me the honor of reading this letter?</t3110>
<t3111>And then, with a slight inclination, he headed back towards the plantations and soon disappeared from sight.</t3111>
<t3112>Without expecting any pleasure, but with great curiosity, Elizabeth opened the letter, and her astonishment grew as she saw that the envelope contained two sheets completely written in very tight handwriting.</t3112> <t3113>Even the envelope was written on.</t3113> <t3114>Continuing her walk along the path, she began to read it.</t3114> <t3115>It was dated at Rosings at eight in the morning and said the following:</t3115>
<t3116>Do not be alarmed, miss, upon receiving this letter, nor believe that I am going to repeat my feelings or renew the propositions that bothered you so much last night.</t3116> <t3117>I write with no intention of distressing you nor of humiliating myself by insisting on desires that, for the happiness of both, cannot be forgotten so easily; the effort of writing and reading this letter could have been avoided if my nature did not compel me to write it and you to read it.</t3117> <t3118>Therefore, forgive me for taking the liberty of requesting your attention; although I know you will grant it reluctantly, I ask for it in fairness.</t3118>
<t3119>Yesterday you accused me of two offenses of very different nature and magnitude.</t3119> <t3120>The first was having separated Mr. Bingley from his sister, without consideration for the feelings of either; and the other, that despite certain rights and ignoring honor and humanity, I ruined Mr. Wickham's immediate prosperity and destroyed his future.</t3120> <t3121>To have abandoned ruthlessly and intentionally the companion of my youth and my father's favorite, a young man who had almost no other future than that of our rectory and who had been educated for its practice, would be a depravity that could not be compared to the separation of two young people whose affection had been the result of only a few weeks.</t3121> <t3122>But I hope you will withdraw the severe censure you so openly directed at me last night, when you have read the following account of my actions regarding these two circumstances and their motives.</t3122> <t3123>If in the explanation that I cannot help but give, I am forced to express feelings that offend you, I can only say that I regret it.</t3123> <t3124>One must submit to necessity and any apology would be absurd.</t3124>
<t3125>It wasn't long after I arrived in Hertfordshire that I noticed, like everyone else, that Mr. Bingley favored your eldest sister much more than any of the other young ladies in the locality; but until the night of the Netherfield ball, I did not see that his affection was formal.</t3125><t3126>I had seen him in love several times before.</t3126><t3127>At that ball, while I had the honor of dancing with you, I learned for the first time, through a casual remark from Sir William Lucas, that Bingley's attentions towards your sister had given rise to hopes of marriage; he spoke of it as a settled matter with only the date to be fixed.</t3127><t3128>From that moment, I carefully observed my friend's behavior and noticed that his inclination towards Miss Bennet was greater than any he had felt before.</t3128><t3129>I also studied your sister.</t3129><t3130>Her appearance and manners were as frank, cheerful, and attractive as ever, but they did not reveal any particular regard.</t3130><t3131>My observations that evening left me convinced that, despite the pleasure with which she received my friend's attentions, she did not reciprocate his feelings.</t3131><t3132>If you were not mistaken about this, then I must have been in error.</t3132><t3133>Since you know your sister better, it is more likely the latter; and if so, if driven by that error I have caused her suffering, your resentment is not unfounded.</t3133><t3134>But I do not hesitate to assert that your sister's appearance and demeanor could have given even the most astute observer the assurance that, despite her affectionate nature, her heart did not seem to have been affected.</t3134><t3135>It is true that I wished to believe in her indifference, but I assure you that my studies and conclusions are not usually influenced by my hopes or fears.</t3135><t3136>I did not believe her indifferent because it suited me to believe so; I believed it with absolute impartiality.</t3136><t3137>My objections to that marriage were not exactly those I acknowledged last night could only be overcome by the force of passion, as in my own case; the disparity in rank would not be as serious for my friend as it would be for me; but there were other obstacles that, despite existing in both my friend's case and mine, I would have tried to forget since they did not affect me directly.</t3137><t3138>I must say what they were, though I will do so briefly.</t3138><t3139>The position of your mother's family, though questionable, was nothing compared to the absolute impropriety so often, almost constantly, displayed by that lady, by her three younger sisters, and occasionally even by her father.</t3139><t3140>Forgive me, it pains me to offend you; but amid the concerns about the faults of your closest relatives and your displeasure at my mentioning them, take comfort in knowing that the fact that both you and your sister conduct yourselves in such a way that no similar reproaches can be made against you, elevates you even more in the esteem you deserve.</t3140><t3141>I will only say that what happened that night confirmed all my suspicions and increased the reasons that might have already prompted me to preserve my friend from what I considered an unfortunate union.</t3141><t3142>Bingley left for London the next day, as you will recall, with the intention of returning very soon.</t3142>
<t3143>Now it remains to explain my involvement in the matter.</t3143><t3144>The displeasure of his sisters had also intensified, and we soon discovered that we agreed in our assessments.</t3144><t3145>We saw that there was no time to lose if we wanted to separate Bingley from his sister, and we decided to go with him to London.</t3145><t3146>We moved there, and immediately I set about making my friend understand the dangers of his choice.</t3146><t3147>I listed and described them to him with diligence.</t3147><t3148>But, although this might have caused his determination to waver or be postponed, I do not believe it would have ultimately prevented the marriage, except for the conviction I managed to instill in him of his sister's indifference.</t3148><t3149>Until then, Bingley had believed that she reciprocated his affection with sincere, though not equal, interest.</t3149><t3150>But Bingley possesses great natural modesty and, moreover, genuinely believes that my sagacity is greater than his.</t3150><t3151>Even so, it was not easy to convince him that he had been mistaken.</t3151><t3152>Once convinced, making him decide not to return to Hertfordshire was a matter of an instant.</t3152><t3153>I see nothing in all this that is reprehensible against me.</t3153><t3154>One thing alone in all I did seems reproachable to me: having agreed to take the necessary measures to keep Bingley unaware of his sister's presence in the city.</t3154><t3155>I knew she was in London, and Miss Bingley knew it too; but my friend has not found out yet.</t3155><t3156>Perhaps if they had met, nothing would have happened; but it did not seem to me that his affection had extinguished enough for him to see her again without any danger.</t3156><t3157>Maybe this concealment is unworthy of me, but I believed it my duty to do so.</t3157><t3158>I have nothing more to say on this matter nor any further apology to offer.</t3158><t3159>If I have hurt his sister's feelings, it has been unintentionally, and although my motives may seem insufficient to her, I do not find them so condemnable.</t3159>
<t3160>Regarding the other more important accusation of having wronged Mr. Wickham, I can only counter it by explaining in detail the relationship of that gentleman with my family.</t3160> <t3161>I am unaware of what he may have specifically accused me of, but there is more than one reliable witness who can corroborate the truth of what I am about to tell you.</t3161>
<t3162>Mr. Wickham is the son of a most respectable man who was in charge of managing all the Pemberley estates for many years, and whose excellent conduct inclined my father to favor him, as was natural; my father's affection was therefore generously manifested in George Wickham, who was his godson.</t3162> <t3163>He funded his education at a school and then at Cambridge, for his father, constantly impoverished by his wife's extravagances, would not have been able to give him a gentleman's education.</t3163> <t3164>My father not only enjoyed the boy's company, who was always very charming, but also formed the highest opinion of him and believed that the Church could be his profession, so he sought to provide him with the means for it.</t3164> <t3165>I, on the other hand, began to have a very different opinion of Wickham many years ago.</t3165> <t3166>The propensity for vices and the lack of principles that he took care to hide from his best friend could not escape the observation of a boy almost his age who had the chance to catch him in moments of carelessness that Mr. Darcy did not see.</t3166> <t3167>Now I will have to sadden you again to a degree that only you can calculate, but whatever feelings Mr. Wickham may have awakened in you, this suspicion will not prevent me from unmasking him; on the contrary, it will be an additional incentive for me.</t3167>
<t3168>My excellent father died five years ago, and his affection for Mr. Wickham remained so constant until the end that in his will he recommended that I support him in the best way that his profession would allow; if he were ordained a priest, my father wished for him to be granted a benefice capable of supporting a family, at the first vacancy.</t3168> <t3169>He also bequeathed him a thousand pounds.</t3169> <t3170>Wickham's father did not survive mine by much.</t3170> <t3171>And half a year after his death, the young Wickham wrote to me informing me that he had finally resolved not to be ordained, and that, in exchange for the benefice he was not to enjoy, he hoped I would give him some more immediate pecuniary advantage.</t3171> <t3172>He added that he intended to pursue a career in law, and that I should understand that the interest on a thousand pounds could not suffice for that.</t3172> <t3173>More than believing him sincere, I wished he were; but in any case, I agreed to his proposal.</t3173> <t3174>I knew that Mr. Wickham was not suited to be a clergyman; so I arranged the matter.</t3174> <t3175>He renounced all claims to assistance regarding the clerical profession, even if he might find himself in a position to adopt it, and accepted three thousand pounds.</t3175> <t3176>Everything seemed settled between us.</t3176> <t3177>I had too poor an opinion of him to invite him to Pemberley or admit his company in the capital.</t3177> <t3178>I believe he lived mostly in London, but his law studies were nothing but a pretext, and as there was nothing to hold him, he freely indulged in idleness and dissipation.</t3178> <t3179>I knew almost nothing of him for three years, but upon the death of the holder of the rectory that had been intended for him, he sent me a letter asking me to grant it to him.</t3179> <t3180>He told me, and it was not difficult for me to believe, that he was in very poor circumstances, opined that the law career was not profitable, and was completely decided to be ordained if I granted him the rectory in question, something he did not doubt I would do, as he knew I had no one else to occupy it and on the other hand, I could not forget the wishes of my venerable father.</t3180> <t3181>I believe you cannot blame me for having refused to comply with this demand and preventing it from being repeated.</t3181> <t3182>Wickham's resentment was proportional to the calamitousness of his circumstances, and he undoubtedly spoke of me to people with the same violence with which he directly insulted me.</t3182> <t3183>After this, all relations between him and me were broken off.</t3183> <t3184>I do not know how he lived.</t3184> <t3185>But last summer I received very unpleasant news about him.</t3185>
<t3186>I have to tell you something now that I myself would like to forget and that no other circumstance than the present one could induce me to reveal to any human being.</t3186> <t3187>I have no doubt that you will keep my secret.</t3187> <t3188>My sister, who is ten years younger than I am, was left under the guardianship of my mother's nephew, Colonel Fitzwilliam, and myself.</t3188> <t3189>About a year ago, she left school and settled in London.</t3189> <t3190>Last summer, she went with her governess to Ramsgate, where Mr. Wickham also went expressly, with complete certainty, for we later learned that Mrs. Younge and he had been in contact.</t3190> <t3191>We had, unfortunately, been deceived about the character of the governess.</t3191> <t3192>With her complicity and help, Wickham set about seducing Georgiana, whose affectionate heart was strongly impressed by his attentions; she was only a child and, believing herself to be in love, consented to elope.</t3192> <t3193>She was then only fifteen years old, which serves as an excuse.</t3193> <t3194>After confessing her imprudence, I have the satisfaction of adding that I learned of that plan from her herself.</t3194> <t3195>I went to Ramsgate and surprised them a day or two before the planned elopement, and then Georgiana, unable to distress and offend her brother whom she almost loved as a father, told me everything.</t3195> <t3196>You can imagine how I felt and how I acted.</t3196> <t3197>Out of consideration for my sister's honor and feelings, I did not make a public scandal, but I wrote to Mr. Wickham, who left immediately.</t3197> <t3198>Mrs. Younge, naturally, was dismissed on the spot.</t3198> <t3199>Mr. Wickham's main objective was undoubtedly my sister's fortune, which amounts to thirty thousand pounds, but I cannot help but suspect that his desire for revenge against me was also part of his purpose.</t3199> <t3200>It really would have been a complete revenge.</t3200>
<t3201>This is, miss, the faithful narration of what happened between him and me; and if you do not reject it as absolutely false, I hope that henceforth you will withdraw the accusation of having behaved cruelly towards Mr. Wickham.</t3201> <t3202>I do not know in what way or with what falsehood he has deceived you; but it is not surprising that he has succeeded, for you were unaware of all these matters.</t3202> <t3203>It was impossible for you to find them out and you were not inclined to suspect them.</t3203>
<t3204>You may wonder why I did not tell you everything last night, but at that time I was not in control of myself and did not know what I could or should reveal to you.</t3204> <t3205>Regarding the truth of everything I have narrated to you, I can appeal to the testimony of Colonel Fitzwilliam, who, due to our close relationship and constant interaction, and even more so for being one of the executors of my father's will, has had to become fully aware of everything that has happened.</t3205> <t3206>If the hatred you feel towards me invalidates my assertions, you may consult with my cousin, against whom you will have no reason for distrust; and to make this possible, I will try to find the opportunity to get this letter into your hands this very morning.</t3206> <t3207>I only have to add: May God bless you.</t3207>
<t3208>Fitzwilliam Darcy.</t3208>
<t3209>CHAPTER XXXVI</t3209>
<t3210>Elizabeth did not expect, when Darcy gave her the letter, that he would repeat his proposal in it, but she had no idea what it might contain.</t3210> <t3211>Upon discovering it, one can well imagine how quickly she read it and what conflicting emotions it aroused in her.</t3211> <t3212>It would have been difficult to define her feelings.</t3212> <t3213>At first, she believed with astonishment that Darcy would want to apologize as best he could, but she immediately became firmly convinced that he could not offer any explanation that the most basic sense of dignity would not advise concealing.</t3213> <t3214>With great prejudice against everything he might say, she began to read his account of what had happened at Netherfield.</t3214> <t3215>Her eyes skimmed the paper with such anxiety that she barely had time to comprehend, and her impatience to know what the next sentence said prevented her from understanding the meaning of the one she was reading.</t3215> <t3216>She instantly assumed that Darcy's belief in her sister's indifference was false, and the worst objections he raised to that marriage angered her too much to be able to do him justice.</t3216> <t3217>She was pleased that he expressed no regret for what he had done; his style revealed no contrition, but arrogance.</t3217> <t3218>In his lines, she saw nothing but pride and insolence.</t3218>
<t3219>But when she came to the part concerning Wickham, she read with greater attention.</t3219> <t3220>Faced with that account of events which, if true, would destroy all her good opinion of the young man, and which bore an alarming similarity to what Wickham himself had told her, her feelings became even more distressing and harder to define; confusion, suspicion, and even horror overwhelmed her.</t3220> <t3221>She wanted to deny it all and repeatedly exclaimed: "That must be false, it can't be true! It must be the greatest of lies!"</t3221> <t3222>She finished reading the letter, and without having taken in the last one or two pages, she quickly put it away, complaining to herself that she would not look at it again, that she did not want to know anything about it.</t3222>
<t3223>In such a state of perturbation, assaulted by a thousand confused thoughts, she continued walking; but it was of no use; after half a minute she took out the letter again and, overcoming herself as best she could, began once more the mortifying reading of what referred to Wickham, mastering herself enough to examine the meaning of each phrase.</t3223> <t3224>His relationship with the Pemberley family was exactly as he had said, and the kindness of the old Mr. Darcy, although Elizabeth had not known until now how far it had gone, also coincided with what Wickham himself had indicated.</t3224> <t3225>Therefore, one account confirmed the other, but when it came to the matter of the will, it was very different.</t3225> <t3226>Everything he had said about his ecclesiastical benefice was fresh in the young woman's memory, and recalling his words, she had to acknowledge that there was double intention on one side or the other, and for a few moments she believed that her wishes were not deceiving her.</t3226> <t3227>But when she read and reread everything that had happened after Wickham had refused the rectory, in exchange for which he had received a sum as considerable as three thousand pounds, she could not help but doubt again.</t3227> <t3228>She folded the letter and weighed all the circumstances with her supposed impartiality, meditating on the probabilities of sincerity of each account, but she made no progress; from both sides she found nothing but assertions.</t3228> <t3229>She began to read again, but each line proved more clearly that the matter, which she had not believed could be explained in any way other than as an infamy to the detriment of Darcy's conduct, could be exposed in such a way that left Darcy completely free of blame.</t3229>
<t3230>The vices and prodigality that Darcy did not hesitate to attribute to Wickham outraged her excessively, all the more so because she had no evidence to refute Darcy's testimony.</t3230> <t3231>Elizabeth had never heard of Wickham before his joining the county garrison, which had been prompted by his casual encounter in London with a young man she only knew superficially.</t3231> <t3232>Of his former life, nothing was known in Hertfordshire except what he himself had told.</t3232> <t3233>As for his true character, despite Elizabeth having the opportunity to analyze it, she never felt the desire to do so; his appearance, voice, and manners instantly endowed him with all virtues.</t3233> <t3234>She tried to recall some noble trait, some special gesture of integrity or kindness that could shield him from Darcy's attacks, or at least that the predominance of good qualities would compensate for those casual errors, which was how she insisted on labeling what Darcy branded as laziness and immorality ingrained in him from the beginning.</t3234> <t3235>She imagined Wickham in front of her and remembered him with all the charm of his demeanor, but apart from the general approval he enjoyed in the locality and the consideration he had gained among his comrades for his sympathy, Elizabeth could find nothing more in his favor.</t3235> <t3236>After having reflected for a long time on this point, she resumed reading.</t3236> <t3237>But what followed about the adventure with Miss Darcy was partly confirmed by the conversation Elizabeth had had the previous morning with Colonel Fitzwilliam; and, at the end of the letter, Darcy appealed, to prove the truth of everything, to the colonel himself, whose involvement in all his cousin's affairs Elizabeth knew in advance, and whose veracity she had no reason to doubt.</t3237> <t3238>She was about to turn to him, but restrained herself at the thought of how awkward it would be to take that step; dismissing it in the end, convinced that Darcy would never have risked proposing it without being absolutely certain that his cousin would corroborate his statements.</t3238>
<t3239>She perfectly remembered everything Wickham told her when they first spoke at Mr. Philips' house; many of his expressions were still entirely in her memory.</t3239> <t3240>Now she realized the impropriety of such confidences to a stranger and was amazed that she had not noticed it before.</t3240> <t3241>She saw the lack of delicacy involved in exposing oneself in that way, and the inconsistency of his statements with his behavior.</t3241> <t3242>She remembered that he boasted of not fearing to see Darcy and that Darcy would have to leave, but that he would not move, which did not prevent him from evading the Netherfield ball the following week.</t3242> <t3243>She also remembered that until the Netherfield family had left the county, he told his story only to her, but after their departure, the said story spread from mouth to mouth, and Wickham had no scruples about ruining Darcy's reputation, despite previously assuring Elizabeth that respect for the father would always prevent him from offending the son.</t3243>
<t3244>How different everything related to Wickham seemed to her now!</t3244> <t3245>His attentions towards Miss King were now solely and exclusively the consequence of his odious fortune-hunting prospects, and the mediocrity of the young lady's fortune was no longer proof of the moderation of his ambitions, but rather his eagerness to cling to anything.</t3245> <t3246>His attitude towards Elizabeth could no longer have an acceptable motive: either he had been mistaken at first about her wealth, or he had tried to flatter his own vanity by nurturing the preference she had unwittingly shown him.</t3246> <t3247>All the efforts she made to defend him were progressively weakening.</t3247> <t3248>And to further justify Darcy, she could not help but recognize that Bingley, when questioned by Jane, had long ago proclaimed Darcy's innocence in that matter; that no matter how proud and repellent he was, never, in the entire course of their relationship ––a relationship that had recently brought them much closer, allowing her to know his character more deeply––, had she seen him do anything ignoble or unjust, nothing for which he could be accused of being irreligious or immoral; that among his friends he was appreciated and loved, and even Wickham himself had acknowledged that he was a good brother.</t3248> <t3249>She had also heard him speak of his sister with such affection that it demonstrated he had good feelings.</t3249> <t3250>If he had been as Wickham painted him, capable of such a violation of all rights, it would have been difficult for no one to know, and the friendship between such a being and a man as amiable as Bingley would have been incomprehensible.</t3250>
<t3251>She came to be ashamed of herself.</t3251> <t3252>She could not think of Darcy or Wickham without acknowledging that she had been biased, absurd, blind, and full of prejudice.</t3252>
<t3253>“How despicably I have acted,” she thought, “I, who prided myself on my discernment!”</t3253> <t3254>“I, who have boasted of my talent, who have scorned my sister’s generous candor, and have flattered my vanity with useless or blameworthy suspicions!”</t3254> <t3255>“How humiliating all this is, but how I deserve this humiliation!”</t3255> <t3256>If I had been in love with Wickham, I would not have acted with such lamentable blindness.</t3256> <t3257>But vanity, not love, has been my folly.</t3257> <t3258>Pleased with the preference of one and offended by the disdain of the other, I have from the beginning given myself up to presumption and ignorance, avoiding reason whenever it concerned either of them.</t3258> <t3259>Until this moment, I did not know myself.”</t3259>
<t3260>From herself to Jane and from Jane to Bingley, her thoughts traveled a path that soon led her to remember that the explanation Darcy had given about their matter had seemed very insufficient to her, and she read it again.</t3260> <t3261>The effect of this second reading was very different.</t3261> <t3262>How could she not give credit to what Darcy said about one of the points, if she had been forced to give it to him on the other?</t3262> <t3263>Darcy declared that he had always suspected that Jane felt no love for Bingley, and Elizabeth remembered what Charlotte's opinion had been.</t3263> <t3264>Nor could she dispute the accuracy of his description of Jane; Elizabeth knew that her sister's feelings, though fervent, had been little expressed; and that the constant complacency in her air and manners often did not go hand in hand with great sensitivity.</t3264>
<t3265>When she reached the part of the letter where Darcy mentioned her family in terms of such humiliating yet deserved reproaches, Elizabeth felt truly ashamed.</t3265> <t3266>The justice of his accusations seemed too evident for her to deny, and the circumstances he particularly alluded to as having occurred at the Netherfield ball could not have impressed him more than they had embarrassed her.</t3266>
<t3267>The praise that Darcy bestowed upon her and her sister did not go unnoticed.</t3267> <t3268>It flattered her, but she could not console herself for the disdain it implied for the rest of the family; and upon considering that Jane's troubles had actually been caused by their own family, and reflecting on how the reputation of both would suffer due to such improper conduct, she felt a despondency she had never known before.</t3268>
<t3269>After walking for two hours along the road, turning over the diversity of her thoughts, reconsidering the events, determining possibilities, and gradually getting used to such a sudden and significant change, fatigue and the memory of how long she had been away made her return to the house.</t3269> <t3270>She entered with the intention of feigning her usual cheerfulness and resolved to suppress the thoughts that besieged her, as otherwise, she would not be able to maintain any conversation.</t3270>
<t3271>They told her that the two gentlemen from Rosings had been there during her absence; Darcy only for a brief moment, to say goodbye; but that Colonel Fitzwilliam had stayed for at least an hour, to see if she arrived and almost ready to go in search of her.</t3271> <t3272>Elizabeth was hardly affected by the colonel's departure; in fact, she was glad.</t3272> <t3273>She could only think about Darcy's letter.</t3273>
<t3274>CHAPTER XXXVII</t3274>
<t3275>Both gentlemen left Rosings the next morning.</t3275> <t3276>Collins was waiting near the entrance gates to bid them farewell, and brought home the pleasant news that they seemed to be well and in a passable mood, as was to be expected after the melancholy scene that must have taken place at Rosings.</t3276> <t3277>Collins then hurried to Rosings to console Lady Catherine and her daughter, and upon returning, he brought with great satisfaction a message from Her Ladyship, who was very sad and wished for everyone to dine with her.</t3277>
<t3278>Elizabeth could not see Lady Catherine without remembering that, if she had wanted, she would have been introduced to her as her future niece; nor could she think, without smiling, about how indignant she would have been.</t3278> <t3279>What would she have said? What would she have done?</t3279> <t3280>It amused her to wonder about all these things.</t3280>
<t3281>The first thing that was discussed was the decline suffered in the gatherings at Rosings.</t3281>
<t3282>––I assure you that I am very sorry ––said Lady Catherine––; I believe that no one laments the loss of friends as much as I do.</t3282> <t3283>But, moreover, I love those boys so much and they love me so much!</t3283> <t3284>They were very sad when they left, as always when we part.</t3284> <t3285>The colonel remained firm until the end, but Darcy's sorrow was much more acute, more than last year, in my opinion.</t3285> <t3286>I do not doubt that his affection for Rosings is increasing.</t3286>
<t3287>Collins had a compliment and an allusion to the matter, which mother and daughter received with a kind smile.</t3287> <t3288>After the meal, Lady Catherine observed that Miss Bennet seemed to be low in spirits.</t3288> <t3289>She immediately explained it in her own way, assuming that the idea of returning so soon to her parents' house did not appeal to her, and said:</t3289>
<t3290>––If that is the case, write to your mother to ask her to let you stay a little longer.</t3290> <t3291>I'm sure Mrs. Collins will be happy to have you by her side.</t3291>
<t3292>––I greatly appreciate Your Lordship's kind invitation ––replied Elizabeth––, but I cannot accept it.</t3292> <t3293>I have to be in London next Saturday.</t3293>
<t3294>––What!</t3294> <t3295>Then you have only been here for six weeks.</t3295> <t3296>I expected you to stay for two months; I told Mrs. Collins so before you arrived.</t3296> <t3297>There is no reason for you to leave so soon.</t3297> <t3298>Mrs. Bennet will not mind if you stay another fortnight.</t3298>
<t3299>––But my father, yes; he wrote to me last week asking me to come back soon.</t3299>
<t3300>––If your mother can do without you, your father will be able to as well.</t3300> <t3301>Daughters are never as necessary to fathers as they are to mothers.</t3301> <t3302>And if you would like to stay here another month, I could take you to London, because I have to go there at the beginning of June for a week; and since Danson won't mind traveling on the coachman's seat, there will be room for one of you, and if the weather is cool, I wouldn't mind taking both of you, as neither of you is stout.</t3302>
<t3303>You are very kind, ma'am; but I believe we will have no choice but to do what we had originally planned.</t3303>
<t3304>Lady Catherine seemed to resign herself.</t3304>
<t3305>––Mrs. Collins, you will have to send a servant with them.</t3305> <t3306>You know that I always say what I feel, and I cannot bear the idea of two young ladies traveling alone in the coach.</t3306> <t3307>It is not proper.</t3307> <t3308>Find a way to have someone accompany them.</t3308> <t3309>There is nothing I dislike as much as that.</t3309> <t3310>Young ladies must always be guarded and attended to according to their position.</t3310> <t3311>When my niece Georgiana went to Ramsgate last summer, I insisted that two male servants go with them; otherwise, it would be improper for Miss Darcy, the daughter of Mr. Darcy of Pemberley and Lady Anne.</t3311> <t3312>I am very careful about these things.</t3312> <t3313>Send John with the young ladies, Mrs. Collins.</t3313> <t3314>I am glad it occurred to me, for it would be disgraceful for you to send them alone.</t3314>
<t3315>––My uncle will send us a servant.</t3315>
<t3316>––Ah! An uncle of yours!</t3316> <t3317>So you have a servant?</t3317> <t3318>I'm glad you have someone who thinks about these things.</t3318> <t3319>Where will you change horses?</t3319> <t3320>Oh!</t3320> <t3321>In Bromley, of course.</t3321> <t3322>If you mention my name at "The Bell," they will take good care of you.</t3322>
<t3323>Lady Catherine had many other questions to ask about the trip, and since not all of them were answered by her, Elizabeth had to pay attention to her; it was fortunate, for otherwise, with her head so occupied, she would have forgotten where she was.</t3323> <t3324>She had to reserve her meditations for her solitary hours; when she was alone, she indulged in them as her greatest relief; not a day passed without her going for a walk to immerse herself in the delight of her unpleasant memories.</t3324>
<t3325>She almost knew Darcy's letter by heart.</t3325> <t3326>She studied its phrases one by one, and her feelings towards its author were sometimes extremely conflicted.</t3326> <t3327>When she focused on the tone in which he addressed her, she was filled with indignation, but when she considered how unjustly she had condemned and vilified him, her anger turned against herself and she pitied Darcy's disillusionment.</t3327> <t3328>His love for her stirred her gratitude, and his general demeanor, her respect; but she could not accept him and never for a moment regretted having rejected him nor felt the slightest desire to see him again.</t3328> <t3329>The way she had behaved filled her with constant shame and regret, and the unfortunate flaws of her family caused her horrible discomfort.</t3329> <t3330>There was no remedy.</t3330> <t3331>Her father merely mocked her younger sisters, but never attempted to curb their impetuous recklessness; and her mother, whose manners were so far from any decorum, was completely insensitive to the danger.</t3331> <t3332>Elizabeth had often conspired with Jane to curb Catherine and Lydia's imprudence, but as long as they had their mother's indulgence, what hope was there that they would be corrected?</t3332> <t3333>Catherine, weak and irritable in character and absolutely submissive to Lydia's direction, had always rebelled against their warnings; and Lydia, capricious and carefree, paid them no heed.</t3333> <t3334>Both were ignorant, lazy, and vain.</t3334> <t3335>As long as there was an officer in Meryton, they would flirt with him, and as long as Meryton was so close to Longbourn, nothing could prevent them from going there all their lives.</t3335>
<t3336>The anxiety for Jane's fate was another of her predominant concerns.</t3336> <t3337>Darcy's explanation, by restoring Bingley to the good opinion she previously had of him, made her better realize what Jane had lost.</t3337> <t3338>Bingley's affection was sincere and his conduct had been impeccable, except for his blind trust in his friend.</t3338> <t3339>How sad, then, it was to think that Jane had been deprived of such a desirable position in every sense, so full of advantages and so promising in happiness, due to the folly and lack of decorum of her own family!</t3339>
<t3340>When to all this was added the discovery of Wickham's true personality, it was easily understood that Elizabeth's jovial spirit, which had rarely felt depressed, had now declined to such an extent that it was almost impossible for her to feign even a little joy.</t3340>
<t3341>Invitations to Rosings were as frequent during the last week of their stay in Hunsford as they were at the beginning.</t3341> <t3342>The last evening was spent there, and Her Ladyship once again asked meticulous questions about the details of the trip, gave them instructions on the best way to pack the trunks, and insisted so much on the necessity of arranging the dresses in the only way she deemed proper, that when they returned to the house, Maria felt obliged to undo all her morning's work and had to repack.</t3342>
<t3343>When they left, Lady Catherine deigned to wish them a pleasant journey and invited them to return to Hunsford the following year.</t3343> <t3344>Miss de Bourgh extended her effort to the courtesy of shaking hands with both of them.</t3344>
<t3345>CHAPTER XXXVIII</t3345>
<t3346>On Saturday morning, Elizabeth and Collins met at breakfast a few minutes before the others appeared; and he took the opportunity to offer her the farewell compliments he considered absolutely necessary.</t3346>
<t3347>––I am unaware, Miss Elizabeth ––he said––, whether Mrs. Collins has expressed to you how grateful she is for your kindness in coming; but I am sure she will do so before you leave this house.</t3347> <t3348>We have greatly appreciated the favor of your company.</t3348> <t3349>We know how unappealing it can be for anyone to come to our humble abode.</t3349> <t3350>Our simple way of living, our small rooms, our few servants, and our isolation must make Hunsford an extremely dreary place for a young lady like yourself.</t3350> <t3351>But I hope you believe in our gratitude for your condescension and that we have done everything within our power to prevent you from being bored.</t3351>
<t3352>Elizabeth thanked her effusively and said she was very happy.</t3352> <t3353>She had spent six very happy weeks; and the pleasure of being with Charlotte and the kind attentions she had received had left her very satisfied.</t3353> <t3354>Collins celebrated it solemnly, but with a more smiling demeanor, he replied:</t3354>
<t3355>––It gives me the greatest pleasure to know that you have spent your time pleasantly.</t3355> <t3356>Everything that could be done has indeed been done; we have had the supreme luck of being able to introduce you to the highest society, and the frequent means of varying the humble domestic scene that our relations with Rosings have facilitated, allow us to hope that your visit has been pleasant.</t3356> <t3357>Our dealings with Lady Catherine's family are truly an extraordinary advantage and a blessing that few can boast of.</t3357> <t3358>You have seen the situation we are in at Rosings, how many times we have been invited there.</t3358> <t3359>I must sincerely acknowledge that, with all the disadvantages of this humble parsonage, no one who comes here can pity themselves as long as they can share our intimacy with the Bourgh family.</t3359>
<t3360>Words were insufficient to express the elevation of his feelings, and he found himself compelled to pace the room, while Elizabeth tried to combine truth with courtesy in brief phrases.</t3360>
<t3361>––So, then, you will be able to bring good news from us to Hertfordshire, dear cousin.</t3361> <t3362>At least this is my hope.</t3362> <t3363>You have been a daily witness to Lady Catherine's great attentions towards Mrs. Collins, and I trust it has not seemed to you that your friend is unhappy.</t3363> <t3364>But regarding this point, it is better that I remain silent.</t3364> <t3365>Allow me only to assure you, dear Miss Elizabeth, that I wholeheartedly wish you the same happiness in your marriage.</t3365> <t3366>My dear Charlotte and I have but one will and one way of thinking.</t3366> <t3367>There exist between us very notable similarities of character and ideas; we seem made for each other.</t3367>
<t3368>Elizabeth could truly say that it was a great joy that it was so, and with the same sincerity, she added that she firmly believed it and was glad for her domestic well-being; however, she did not regret that the description of it was interrupted by the arrival of the lady in question.</t3368> <t3369>Poor Charlotte!</t3369> <t3370>It was sad to leave her in such company!</t3370> <t3371>But she had chosen it consciously.</t3371> <t3372>It was clear that she was pained by the departure of her guests, but she did not seem to want to be pitied.</t3372> <t3373>Her home and domestic chores, her parish, her chicken coop, and all the other associated tasks had not yet lost their charm for her.</t3373>
<t3374>Finally, the stagecoach arrived; the trunks were loaded, the packages were arranged, and they were informed that everything was ready.</t3374> <t3375>The two friends said their affectionate goodbyes, and Collins accompanied Elizabeth to the carriage.</t3375> <t3376>As they crossed the garden, he asked her to convey his warm regards to the entire family and to repeat his gratitude for the kindnesses they had shown him during his stay in Longbourn last winter, and he urged her to also greet the Gardiners even though he did not know them.</t3376> <t3377>He helped her into the carriage and, after her, Maria.</t3377> <t3378>Just as he was about to close the doors, Collins, distressed, reminded them that they had forgotten to ask him to convey something to the ladies of Rosings.</t3378>
<t3379>––But ––he added–– surely they would want us to convey their humble respects along with their gratitude for your kindness towards them.</t3379>
<t3380>Elizabeth did not object; the door closed and the carriage departed.</t3380>
<t3381>––My God! ––exclaimed María after a few minutes of silence––.</t3381> <t3382>It seems like it was just yesterday when we arrived and yet, so many things have happened!</t3382>
<t3383>––Many, it's true ––replied her companion with a sigh.</t3383>
<t3384>––We have dined at Rosings nine times, and we have had tea there twice.</t3384> <t3385>How much I have to tell! Elizabeth added to herself: "And I, how many things I have to keep to myself!"</t3385>
<t3386>The journey passed without much conversation and without any incident, and four hours after leaving Hunsford, they arrived at the Gardiners' house, where they were going to spend a few days.</t3386>
<t3387>Jane looked very well, and Elizabeth hardly had a chance to examine her state of mind, as her aunt had prepared countless invitations for them.</t3387> <t3388>But Jane was going to return to Longbourn with her sister, and once there, there would be plenty of time to observe her.</t3388>
<t3389>Elizabeth barely managed to restrain herself from telling her until then about Darcy's proposals.</t3389> <t3390>What a surprise she was going to get, and how gratifying it would be for the vanity that Elizabeth still could not master!</t3390> <t3391>It was such a strong temptation that she would not have been able to resist it if it were not for the indecision she found herself in, the extent of what she had to communicate, and the fear that if she started talking, she would be forced to mention Bingley, which would only succeed in making her sister even sadder.</t3391>
<t3392>CHAPTER XXXIX</t3392>
<t3393>In the second week of May, the three girls set off together from Gracechurch Street, heading towards the town of X, in Hertfordshire.</t3393> <t3394>Upon arriving near the inn where Mr. Bennet's carriage was supposed to wait for them, they immediately saw, as proof of the coachman's punctuality, Catherine and Lydia who were on the lookout in the upper floor dining room.</t3394> <t3395>They had spent almost an hour at the place happily occupied with visiting the hat shop across the street, watching the guard on duty, and preparing a cucumber salad.</t3395>
<t3396>After welcoming their sisters, they triumphantly showed them a table set with all the cold cuts that can normally be found in the pantry of an inn and exclaimed:</t3396>
<t3397>––Isn't it wonderful?</t3397> <t3398>Isn't it a pleasant surprise?</t3398>
<t3399>––We want to invite all of you ––added Lydia––; but you will have to lend us the money, because we just spent ours in the store out there.</t3399>
<t3400>And, showing her purchases, she added:</t3400>
<t3401>––Look at the hat I bought.</t3401> <t3402>I don't think it's very pretty, but I thought it was the same to buy it or not; I'll take it apart as soon as we get home and see if I can improve it a bit.</t3402>

<t3407>––Really? ––exclaimed Elizabeth very satisfied.</t3407>

<t3413>“Yes,” thought Elizabeth, “it would be a truly splendid plan and very fitting for us.</t3413> <t3414>That’s all we would need.</t3414> <t3415>Brighton and a whole camp of soldiers, with how disrupted we already are by a mere regiment and the monthly Meryton balls.”</t3415>
<t3416>––I have some news to share with you ––said Lydia as they sat down at the table—.</t3416> <t3417>What do you think?</t3417> <t3418>It's the most sensational thing you can imagine; something very important about a certain person we all like.</t3418>
<t3419>Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other and told the servant that they no longer needed him.</t3419> <t3420>Lydia laughed and said:</t3420>

<t3437>As soon as they had eaten and the older ones had paid, they called for the carriage and, after organizing themselves a bit, all the girls, with their boxes, their work bags, their packages, and the poorly received addition of Catherine and Lydia's purchases, settled into the vehicle.</t3437>
<t3438>––How tightly packed we are! ––exclaimed Lydia––.</t3438> <t3439>I'm glad I bought the hat, if only for the pleasure of having another hatbox!</t3439> <t3440>Well, let's get comfortable and chat and laugh all the way home.</t3440> <t3441>First, let's hear what has happened to you since you left.</t3441> <t3442>Have you met any interesting men?</t3442> <t3443>Have you had any flirts?</t3443> <t3444>I had high hopes that one of you would catch a husband before coming back.</t3444> <t3445>Jane is soon going to get old.</t3445> <t3446>She is almost twenty-three years old!</t3446> <t3447>Lord, how ashamed I would be if I didn't get married before twenty-three...!</t3447> <t3448>You can't imagine how much Aunt Philips wants you to get married.</t3448> <t3449>She says Lizzy would have done better to accept Collins; <t3466>At home, they were received with all the affection.</t3466> <t3467>Mrs. Bennet rejoiced to see Jane as beautiful as ever, and Mr. Bennet, during the meal, more than once said to Elizabeth with all his heart:</t3467>

<t3488>In the afternoon, Lydia insisted that they all go to Meryton to see how everyone was; but Elizabeth vehemently opposed.</t3488> <t3489>She did not want it to be said that the Bennet ladies could not stay at home for half a day without running after the officers.</t3489> <t3490>She had another reason for opposing: she feared seeing Wickham again, something she wanted to avoid at all costs.</t3490> <t3491>The satisfaction she felt at the regiment's departure was beyond what could be expressed.</t3491> <t3492>In fifteen days they would no longer be there, and she hoped that this way she would be rid of Wickham forever.</t3492>
<t3493>She hadn't been home for many hours when she realized that the Brighton plan Lydia had informed them about at the inn was often discussed by her parents.</t3493> <t3494>Elizabeth understood that Mr. Bennet had no intention of giving in, but his replies were so vague and equivocal that her mother, although sometimes discouraged, did not lose hope of eventually getting her way.</t3494>
<t3495>CHAPTER XL</t3495>
<t3496>Elizabeth could no longer contain her impatience to tell Jane everything that had happened.</t3496> <t3497>Finally, she decided to omit everything related to her sister, and after preparing her for the surprise, she recounted the most important parts of her scene with Darcy the next morning.</t3497>
<t3498>The great affection that Jane felt for Elizabeth diminished her astonishment, for anything that was admiration for her seemed perfectly natural.</t3498> <t3499>Her feelings were different.</t3499> <t3500>It hurt her that Darcy had expressed himself in such a way that was so little suitable to make himself agreeable, but it pained her even more to think of the unhappiness that her sister's refusal would have caused him.</t3500>
<t3501>––It was a mistake to be so sure of success ––he said–– and of course he shouldn't have given himself away; but just imagine how much he must regret it and how bad he must feel now!</t3501>
<t3502>––It is true ––replied Elizabeth––, I truly feel sorry for him; but his pride is so great that it won't take long for him to forget me.</t3502> <t3503>Do you think it was wrong for me to reject him?</t3503>
<t3504>––Do I mind?</t3504> <t3505>Not at all.</t3505>

<t3591>––Tell me ––continued the mother––, the Collins live very well, don't they?</t3591> <t3592>Well, well, I hope it lasts a long time for them.</t3592> <t3593>And how do they eat?</t3593> <t3594>I'm sure Charlotte is an excellent manager.</t3594> <t3595>If she is half as sharp as her mother, she will save a lot.</t3595> <t3596>I don't think they indulge much.</t3596>
<t3597>––No, not at all.</t3597>
<t3598>––Good administration depends on it.</t3598> <t3599>Yes, yes; they will be very careful not to squander their salary.</t3599> <t3600>They will never have money troubles.</t3600> <t3601>Good for them!</t3601> <t3602>And I imagine they often talk about acquiring Longbourn when your father dies, and that they already consider it theirs as soon as that happens.</t3602>
<t3603>––They never mentioned this topic in front of me.</t3603> <t3604>––Of course, it wouldn't have been appropriate; but I have no doubt that they talk about it many times among themselves.</t3604> <t3605>Well, if they are content with a possession that legally isn't theirs, that's their problem.</t3605> <t3606>It would embarrass me.</t3606>
<t3607>CHAPTER XLI</t3607>
<t3608>The first week of the return passed quickly, and they entered the second, which was the last week of the regiment's stay in Meryton.</t3608> <t3609>The young women of the locality languished; the sadness was almost general.</t3609> <t3610>Only the elder Bennet daughters were capable of eating, drinking, and sleeping as if nothing was happening.</t3610> <t3611>Catherine and Lydia often reproached them for their insensitivity.</t3611> <t3612>They were very dejected and could not understand such hardness of heart in members of their own family.</t3612>
<t3613>––My God! What is going to become of us?</t3613> <t3614>What are we going to do? ––they exclaimed desolately––.</t3614> <t3615>How can you smile like that, Elizabeth?</t3615>
<t3616>Her loving mother shared her sorrow and remembered what she herself had suffered on a similar occasion twenty-five years ago.</t3616>
<t3617>––I remember ––he said–– that I cried for two days straight when Colonel Miller's regiment left, I thought my heart was going to break.</t3617>
<t3618>––Mine will also fall to pieces ––said Lydia.</t3618>
