<t3620>––Oh, yes!</t3620> <t3621>If only we could go to Brighton!</t3621> <t3622>But father is so unaccommodating!</t3622>
<t3623>––A few sea baths would make me feel like new.</t3623> <t3624>––And Aunt Philips says they would do me a lot of good too ––added Catherine.</t3624>
<t3625>These lamentations continually echoed in the Longbourn house.</t3625> <t3626>Elizabeth tried to keep herself isolated, but she could not avoid the shame.</t3626> <t3627>She once again recognized the justice of Darcy's observations, and she had never felt so willing to forgive him for opposing his friend's plans.</t3627>
<t3628>But Lydia's melancholy did not take long to dissipate, for she received an invitation from Mrs. Forster, the wife of the regiment's colonel, to accompany her to Brighton.</t3628> <t3629>This invaluable friend of Lydia was very young and had recently married.</t3629> <t3630>Since both were equally cheerful and lively, they got along perfectly and within three months of meeting, they were already close friends.</t3630>
<t3631>Lydia's enthusiasm and the adoration she felt for Mrs. Forster, Mrs. Bennet's satisfaction, and Catherine's mortification were almost indescribable.</t3631> <t3632>Without the slightest concern for her sister's displeasure, Lydia ran around the house completely ecstatic, asking everyone to congratulate her, laughing and talking more energetically than ever, while poor Catherine remained in the living room lamenting her bad luck in unreasonable terms and in a foul mood.</t3632>
<t3633>––I don't see why Mrs. Forster doesn't invite me as well ––she said––, even though Lydia is her particular friend.</t3633> <t3634>I have the same right as she does to be invited, and even more so, because I am older.</t3634>
<t3635>In vain did Elizabeth try to make her see reason, and in vain did Jane try to make her resign herself.</t3635> <t3636>The happy invitation awakened in Elizabeth feelings quite different from those of Lydia and her mother; she clearly understood that there was no longer any hope that Mrs. Bennet would show any sign of common sense.</t3636> <t3637>She could not help but ask her father not to let Lydia go to Brighton, as such a step could have dire consequences.</t3637> <t3638>She pointed out to him Lydia's unsuitability, the few advantages her friendship with Mrs. Forster could bring, and the danger that, with such company, Lydia's imprudence in Brighton, where temptations would be greater, would be redoubled.</t3638> <t3639>Mr. Bennet listened attentively to his daughter and said to her:</t3639>
<t3640>––Lydia will not be at ease until she makes a fool of herself in public at one place or another, and we can never expect her to do it with so little expense and sacrifice to her family as on this occasion.</t3640>
<t3641>––If you knew ––replied Elizabeth–– the great harm that the indiscreet and improper behavior of Lydia can bring upon all of us, and the harm it has already caused us, I am sure you would think very differently.</t3641>
<t3642>––"It has already caused us trouble!" ––exclaimed Mr. Bennet––.</t3642> <t3643>"Has it scared away any of your suitors? Poor Lizzy!"</t3643> <t3644>"But don't be distressed."</t3644> <t3645>"Those delicate young men who cannot endure such nonsense are not worth it."</t3645> <t3646>"Come, tell me who are the finicky suitors that Lydia's madness has driven away."</t3646>
<t3647>––You don't understand me.</t3647> <t3648>I'm not complaining about that.</t3648> <t3649>I'm not denouncing specific dangers, but general ones.</t3649> <t3650>Our prestige and our respectability in front of people will be harmed by Lydia's extreme frivolity, disdain, and recklessness.</t3650> <t3651>Forgive me, but I have to speak to you clearly.</t3651> <t3652>If you, dear father, do not take the trouble to curb her euphoria, to teach her that she should not devote her life to her current pastimes, soon it will be too late for her to amend.</t3652> <t3653>Her character will be set, and at sixteen she will be an incorrigible flirt who will not only make a fool of herself but also of her entire family; a flirt, moreover, in the worst and most insignificant degree of flirtation, with no more attraction than her youth and her average physical traits; ignorant and empty-headed, incapable of noticing the slightest bit of the general contempt that her desire to be admired will provoke.</t3653> <t3654>Catherine is in the same danger, because she will go where Lydia leads her; vain, ignorant, lazy, and absolutely uncontrollable.</t3654> <t3655>Father, can you believe that they will not be criticized and despised wherever they go, and that they will not drag the other sisters into their disgrace?</t3655>
<t3656>Mr. Bennet realized that Elizabeth was speaking from the heart.</t3656> <t3657>He took her hand affectionately and replied:</t3657>
<t3658>––Don't be troubled, my love.</t3658> <t3659>You and Jane will always be respected and loved everywhere, and you won't seem less advantaged for having two or perhaps three very foolish sisters.</t3659> <t3660>There will be no peace in Longbourn if Lydia does not go to Brighton.</t3660> <t3661>Let her go.</t3661> <t3662>Colonel Forster is a sensible man and will watch over her.</t3662> <t3663>And she is fortunately too poor to be the target of anyone's predation.</t3663> <t3664>Her flirtation will be of less importance in Brighton than here, as the officers will find more attractive women there.</t3664> <t3665>So it will serve to make her understand her own insignificance.</t3665> <t3666>In any case, she can't get much worse, and if she does, we would then have sufficient reason to lock her up for the rest of her life.</t3666>
<t3667>Elizabeth had to be content with this answer; but her opinion remained the same, and she parted from her father sorrowful and disappointed.</t3667> <t3668>But her character prevented her from increasing her troubles by dwelling on them.</t3668> <t3669>She believed she had fulfilled her duty and was not willing to consume herself thinking about inevitable evils or to increase them with her anxiety.</t3669>
<t3670>If Lydia or her mother had known what Elizabeth had been talking about with her father, their indignation would have known no bounds.</t3670> <t3671>A visit to Brighton was for Lydia the epitome of earthly happiness.</t3671> <t3672>With her enormous imagination, she saw the streets of that cheerful coastal town teeming with officers; she saw herself attracting the gazes of dozens and dozens of them whom she had yet to meet.</t3672> <t3673>She imagined herself in the middle of the camp, with its tents laid out in the beautiful uniformity of their lines, filled with cheerful and dazzling young men in their crimson uniforms; and to complete the picture, she imagined herself sitting next to one of those tents and tenderly flirting with no fewer than six officers at once.</t3673>
<t3674>If she had known that her sister intended to take away all those dreams, all those realities, what would have happened?</t3674> <t3675>Only her mother would have been able to understand it, as she almost felt the same as her.</t3675> <t3676>Lydia's trip to Brighton was the only thing that consoled her from her melancholic conviction that she would never manage to take her husband there.</t3676>
<t3677>But neither of them suspected what had happened, and their enthusiasm continued until the very day Lydia left home.</t3677>
<t3678>Elizabeth was now going to see Wickham for the last time.</t3678> <t3679>She had often been in his company since she returned from Hunsford, and her agitation had greatly subsided; her former interest in him had completely vanished.</t3679> <t3680>She had learned to discern in that kindness which initially attracted her a certain affectation that now repelled her.</t3680> <t3681>Moreover, Wickham's attitude towards her had just displeased her, as the young man showed a desire to renew his courtship, and after all that had happened, Elizabeth could not help but feel indignant.</t3681> <t3682>She firmly restrained his vain and frivolous attentions, while still feeling the offense implied by Wickham's belief that no matter how long he had neglected her and whatever the cause of his neglect, he would flatter and win her over again just by seeking her out once more.</t3682>
<t3683>On the last day of the regiment's stay in Meryton, Wickham dined at Longbourn with other officers.</t3683> <t3684>Elizabeth was so unwilling to endure him that when Wickham asked her how she had spent her time in Hunsford, she replied that Colonel Fitzwilliam and Darcy had spent three weeks in Rosings, and she wanted to know if he knew the former.</t3684>
<t3685>Wickham seemed surprised, upset, and alarmed; but he quickly recovered and with a smile replied that he used to see him often.</t3685> <t3686>He said that he was quite a gentleman and asked if she had liked him.</t3686> <t3687>Elizabeth responded enthusiastically that she had.</t3687> <t3688>But then Wickham added, with an indifferent air:</t3688>
<t3689>––How long did you say the colonel was at Rosings?</t3689>
<t3690>––About three weeks.</t3690>
<t3691>––And did you see him often?</t3691>
<t3692>Almost every day.</t3692>
<t3693>––He is very different from his cousin.</t3693>
<t3694>––Yes, indeed.</t3694> <t3695>But I think Mr. Darcy improves a lot upon acquaintance.</t3695>
<t3696>––Wow! ––exclaimed Wickham with a look that did not go unnoticed by Elizabeth––.</t3696> <t3697>In what? ––but, restraining himself, he continued in a more jovial tone––: In manners?</t3697> <t3698>Has he deigned to behave more correctly than usual?</t3698> <t3699>Because I cannot believe ––he continued in a lower and more serious voice–– that he has improved in the essential.</t3699>
<t3700>––Oh, no!</t3700> <t3701>In essence, he is still the same as always.</t3701>
<t3702>Wickham did not know whether to be pleased with her words or to distrust their meaning.</t3702> <t3703>There was something in Elizabeth's demeanor that made him listen with anxious attention and suspicion to what she said next:</t3703>
<t3704>––By saying that he benefits from the interaction, I do not mean to imply that his character or manners have improved, but rather that by getting to know him better, it is easier to understand his attitude.</t3704>
<t3705>Wickham's alarm then betrayed itself through his blush and the agitation in his gaze; he remained silent for a few moments until he managed to overcome his embarrassment and, addressing Elizabeth again, said in the kindest tone:</t3705>
<t3706>––You, who know so well my resentment against Mr. Darcy, will understand how sincerely I must rejoice that he is astute enough to at least assume an outward correction.</t3706> <t3707>With that system, his pride can be useful, if not to him; to many others, for it will keep him away from the bad behavior of which I was a victim.</t3707> <t3708>But I very much fear that the kind of prudence to which you seem to allude is employed only during his visits to his aunt, as it does not suit him to misbehave in her presence.</t3708> <t3709>I know very well that he has always cared about appearances in front of her with the desire to bring to fruition his marriage with Miss de Bourgh, in which he puts all his effort.</t3709>
<t3710>Elizabeth couldn't suppress a smile upon hearing this; but she only responded with a slight nod of her head.</t3710> <t3711>She noticed that Wickham was about to return to the old topic of his misfortunes, and she was not in the mood to allow it.</t3711> <t3712>For the rest of the evening, Wickham pretended his usual cheerfulness, but he no longer attempted to court Elizabeth.</t3712> <t3713>In the end, they parted with mutual courtesy and also probably with the mutual desire never to see each other again.</t3713>
<t3714>When the gathering ended, Lydia went to Meryton with Mrs. Forster, from where they were to depart early the next morning.</t3714> <t3715>Her farewell to the family was more noisy than pathetic.</t3715> <t3716>Catherine was the only one who cried, although out of humiliation and envy.</t3716> <t3717>Mrs. Bennet wished her daughter to have as much fun as possible, advice that the girl was willing to follow to the letter.</t3717> <t3718>And her clamor upon leaving was so loud that she didn't even hear the gentle goodbyes from her sisters.</t3718>
<t3719>CHAPTER XLII</t3719>
<t3720>If Elizabeth's opinion were derived from what she saw in her own family, she could not have formed a very pleasant idea of marital happiness and domestic well-being.</t3720> <t3721>Her father, captivated by youth and beauty, and the apparent illusion and joy that both entail, had married a woman whose weak understanding and petty spirit had put an end to all affection at the very beginning of their marriage.</t3721> <t3722>Respect, esteem, and trust had vanished forever; and all of Mr. Bennet's prospects of happiness within the home had collapsed.</t3722> <t3723>But he was not one of those men who seek comfort for the effects of their own imprudence in the pleasures that often console those who have become unhappy through their follies and vices.</t3723> <t3724>He loved the countryside and books, and they constituted the source of his main joys.</t3724> <t3725>From his wife, he owed nothing more than the laughter that her ignorance and folly provided him from time to time.</t3725> <t3726>That is not the kind of happiness a man would wish to owe to his wife; but in the absence of...</t3726> <t3727>The good philosopher only takes benefit from where it exists.</t3727>
<t3728>Elizabeth, nevertheless, had never ceased to recognize the impropriety of her father's conduct as a husband.</t3728> <t3729>She had always observed it with sorrow, but she respected his talent and was grateful for his affection, so she tried to forget what she could not ignore and to put out of her mind his continuous breach of marital duties and the decorum that, by exposing his wife to the contempt of her own daughters, was so exceedingly reprehensible.</t3729> <t3730>But she had never felt as she did then the harm that a discordant marriage can cause to the children, nor had she ever so clearly realized the dangers inherent in the misdirection of talent, talent that, if well employed, although it might not have been enough to enhance his wife's intelligence, could at least have preserved the respectability of the daughters.</t3730>
<t3731>While it is true that Elizabeth was glad of Wickham's absence, it cannot be said that she rejoiced at the regiment's departure.</t3731> <t3732>Her outings were less frequent than before, and the constant complaints of her mother and sister about the boredom they had fallen into saddened the house.</t3732> <t3733>And although Catherine might have regained the common sense lost with the departure of those who caused her disturbance, her other sister, from whose behavior all calamities could be expected, was in danger of affirming her madness and brazenness, for being near a beach and a camp, her situation was doubly threatening.</t3733> <t3734>In short, she now saw what she had already realized at other times, that an eagerly awaited event could not, upon happening, bring her all the satisfaction that was to be expected.</t3734> <t3735>It was necessary, therefore, to open another period for the beginning of her happiness, to set another goal for the achievement of her desires and hopes, which by cheering her with another anticipated pleasure, would console her for the present and prepare her for another disappointment.</t3735> <t3736>Her trip to the Lakes became the object of her happiest thoughts and constituted her best refuge in the unpleasant hours that her mother's and Catherine's discontent made inevitable.</t3736> <t3737>And if she could have included Jane in the plan, everything would have been perfect.</t3737>
<t3738>––"It's a stroke of luck," he thought, "to have something to desire.</t3738> <t3739>If everything were complete, there would undoubtedly be something that would disappoint me.</t3739> <t3740>But now, carrying with me that source of longing which will be Jane's absence, I can reasonably think that all my expectations of pleasure will be fulfilled.</t3740> <t3741>A plan that in all its parts promises happiness never turns out well; and you can't avoid some mishap if you don't have a small setback."</t3741>
<t3742>Lydia, upon leaving, promised to write very often and in great detail to her mother and Catherine, but her letters were always long overdue and all were brief.</t3742> <t3743>The ones addressed to her mother said little more than that they had just returned from the reading room where they had been greeted by such and such officers, that the decoration of the room was so beautiful that it had taken her breath away, that she had a new dress or a new parasol that she would describe more extensively, but that she couldn't because Mrs. Forster was waiting for her to go to the camp together...</t3743> <t3744>From the correspondence addressed to her sister, even less could be known, for her letters to Catherine, although long, had many underlined lines that could not be made public.</t3744>
<t3745>After the two or three weeks of Lydia's absence, health and good humor began to reign in Longbourn.</t3745> <t3746>Everything looked better.</t3746> <t3747>Families who had spent the winter in the capital were returning, and the summer festivities and invitations were reemerging.</t3747> <t3748>Mrs. Bennet recovered from her complaining state, and by mid-June, Catherine was already consoled enough to enter Meryton without tears.</t3748> <t3749>This fact was so promising that Elizabeth believed that by the next Christmas, Catherine would be so reasonable that she wouldn't mention an officer even once a day, unless by some cruel and malicious order of the War Office, a new regiment was quartered in Meryton.</t3749>
<t3750>The appointed time for the excursion to the North was already approaching; only two weeks were left, when a letter was received from Mrs. Gardiner postponing the date of the trip and, at the same time, shortening its duration.</t3750> <t3751>Mr. Gardiner's business prevented him from leaving until two weeks after the beginning of July, and he had to be back in London within a month; and since this reduced the time too much to go so far and see all the things they had planned, or to see them with enough rest and comfort, there was no choice but to give up the Lakes and think of a more limited excursion, in view of which they would not go beyond Derbyshire.</t3751> <t3752>In that region, there were enough things worth seeing to fill most of the time they had available, and, moreover, Mrs. Gardiner felt a very special attraction for Derbyshire.</t3752> <t3753>The town where she had spent several years of her life might be as interesting to her as all the famous beauties of Matlock, Chatsworth, Dovedale, or the Peak.</t3753>
<t3754>Elizabeth felt very disappointed; she was very excited about going to the Lakes, and she believed there would have been plenty of time for it.</t3754> <t3755>But, in any case, she should be satisfied, they would surely have a good time, and it didn't take long for her to come to terms with it.</t3755>
<t3756>For Elizabeth, the name of Derbyshire was connected to many other things.</t3756> <t3757>It made her think of Pemberley and its owner.</t3757> <t3758>"But," she said to herself, "I can enter his county with impunity and steal a few stones without him noticing."</t3758>
<t3759>The wait then felt doubly long to her.</t3759> <t3760>There were four weeks left until her uncles arrived.</t3760> <t3761>But, finally, the time passed and Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner arrived in Longbourn with their four children.</t3761> <t3762>The children ––two little girls aged six and eight respectively, and two younger boys–– were to be under the special care of their cousin Jane, the favorite of all, whose sweet and calm temperament was ideal for teaching them, playing with them, and loving them.</t3762>
<t3763>The Gardiners slept at Longbourn that night and the next morning they set off with Elizabeth in search of news and amusement.</t3763> <t3764>They had assured pleasure: the three of them were excellent travel companions, which meant they had the health and character to endure inconveniences, the cheerfulness to enhance all kinds of happiness, and the affection and intelligence to overcome any setbacks.</t3764>
<t3765>We are not going to describe Derbyshire here, nor any of the notable places they passed through: Oxford, Blenheim, Warwick, Kenilworth, Birmingham, and all the others, are well known.</t3765> <t3766>We will only refer to a small part of Derbyshire.</t3766> <t3767>Towards the small town of Lambton, the scene of Mrs. Gardiner's youth, where she had recently learned that some acquaintances still resided, the travelers directed their steps after having seen the main wonders of the region.</t3767> <t3768>Elizabeth learned from her aunt that Pemberley was about five miles from Lambton.</t3768> <t3769>It was not on their way, but they only had to deviate one or two miles to visit it.</t3769> <t3770>While discussing their route the previous evening, Mrs. Gardiner expressed a desire to see Pemberley again.</t3770> <t3771>Mr. Gardiner did not object and sought Elizabeth's approval.</t3771>
<t3772>––Dear ––her aunt said to her––, wouldn’t you like to see a place you’ve heard so much about and that is connected with so many of your acquaintances?</t3772> <t3773>You know that Wickham spent all his youth there.</t3773>
<t3774>Elizabeth was distressed.</t3774> <t3775>She felt that she had nothing to do at Pemberley and was forced to say that she was not interested.</t3775> <t3776>She had to confess that she was tired of large houses, after having seen so many; and that she found no pleasure in seeing exquisite carpets and satin curtains.</t3776>
<t3777>Mrs. Gardiner censured her foolishness.</t3777>
<t3778>––If it were just a richly furnished house ––he said–– I wouldn't be interested either; but the estate is a marvel.</t3778> <t3779>It contains one of the most beautiful forests in the country.</t3779>
<t3780>Elizabeth did not speak any more, but she no longer had any peace of mind.</t3780> <t3781>The possibility of encountering Darcy while visiting Pemberley instantly crossed her mind.</t3781> <t3782>It would be horrible!</t3782> <t3783>Just thinking about it made her blush, and she thought it would be better to speak clearly to her aunt than to expose herself to such a risk.</t3783> <t3784>But this decision had its drawbacks, and she resolved that she would only adopt it if her inquiries about the absence of the property owner's family were negative.</t3784>
<t3785>Consequently, upon retiring that night, she asked the maid if Pemberley was a very beautiful place, what the name of its owner was, and finally, with no small amount of concern, if the family was spending the summer there.</t3785> <t3786>The negative response to this last question was the most welcome in the world.</t3786> <t3787>With her anxiety now gone, she felt great curiosity even about the house itself, and when the plan was proposed again the next morning and she was consulted, she instantly replied, with an evident air of indifference, that she did not dislike the idea.</t3787>
<t3788>Therefore, they set out for Pemberley.</t3788>
<t3789>CHAPTER XLIII</t3789>
<t3790>Elizabeth glimpsed the woods of Pemberley with some unease, and when they finally arrived at the gate, her heart was beating strongly.</t3790>
<t3791>The estate was enormous and comprised a great variety of lands.</t3791> <t3792>They entered through one of the lowest points and strolled for a long time through a beautiful forest that extended over its wide surface.</t3792>
<t3793>Elizabeth's mind was too busy to converse; but she observed and admired all the notable spots and all the views.</t3793> <t3794>For half a mile, they climbed a slope that led them to a considerable hill where the forest broke off and from where they immediately saw Pemberley House, situated on the other side of the valley through which a somewhat steep path wound.</t3794> <t3795>It was a stone building, spacious and beautiful, well situated on a knoll that stood out in front of a chain of high, forest-covered hills, and it had in front a rather copious stream that ran increasingly powerful, completely natural and wild.</t3795> <t3796>Its banks were neither regular nor falsely adorned with gardening works.</t3796> <t3797>Elizabeth was amazed.</t3797> <t3798>She had never seen a place more favored by nature or where natural beauty was less marred by poor taste.</t3798> <t3799>Everyone was full of admiration, and Elizabeth then understood what it might mean to be the mistress of Pemberley.</t3799>
<t3800>They went down the hill, crossed a bridge, and continued to the door.</t3800> <t3801>While they examined the appearance of the house up close, Elizabeth again feared encountering the owner.</t3801> <t3802>What if the maid had been mistaken?</t3802> <t3803>After asking for permission to see the mansion, they were led into the vestibule.</t3803> <t3804>While they waited for the housekeeper, Elizabeth had time to marvel at finding herself in such a place.</t3804>
<t3805>The housekeeper was an elderly woman, of respectable appearance, much less stiff and much more courteous than Elizabeth had imagined.</t3805> <t3806>She led them to the dining room.</t3806> <t3807>It was a well-proportioned and elegantly furnished room.</t3807> <t3808>Elizabeth glanced at it briefly and went to one of the windows to take in the view.</t3808> <t3809>The hill crowned with forest they had descended, from a distance, appeared more abrupt and more beautiful.</t3809> <t3810>The entire layout of the land was good; she looked at the landscape with delight: the stream, the trees along the banks, and the curve of the valley as far as the eye could see.</t3810> <t3811>As they moved to other rooms, the landscape appeared from different angles, but magnificent views could be seen from all the windows.</t3811> <t3812>The rooms were tall and beautiful, and their furnishings were in harmony with the fortune of their owner.</t3812> <t3813>Elizabeth noted, admiring his taste, that there was nothing flashy or tacky and that there was less pomp but more elegance than at Rosings.</t3813>
<t3814>"And to think," she said to herself, "that I could have been the mistress of all this!</t3814> <t3815>These rooms could now be mine!</t3815> <t3816>Instead of visiting them as a stranger, I could enjoy them and receive my uncles here!</t3816> <t3817>But no," she replied, recovering herself, "it would not have been possible, I would have had to give up my uncles; I would not have been allowed to invite them."</t3817>
<t3818>This revived her and saved her from something akin to regret.</t3818>
<t3819>He wanted to find out from the housekeeper if his master was truly absent, but he lacked the courage.</t3819> <t3820>Finally, it was his uncle who asked the question, and Elizabeth turned frightened when Mrs. Reynolds said yes, adding:</t3820>
<t3821>––But we expect him tomorrow.</t3821> <t3822>He is going to come with many friends.</t3822>
<t3823>Elizabeth was glad that her trip had not been postponed by a day for any reason.</t3823>
<t3824>Her aunt called her to see a painting.</t3824> <t3825>Elizabeth approached and saw a portrait of Wickham above the mantelpiece among other miniatures.</t3825> <t3826>Her aunt asked her with a smile what she thought of it.</t3826> <t3827>The housekeeper came to tell them that he was a young son of the last steward of her lord, educated by him at his own expense.</t3827>
<t3828>––Now he has joined the army ––––he added–– and I think he is a lost cause.</t3828>
<t3829>Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece with a smile, but Elizabeth remained very serious.</t3829>
<t3830>––And this one ––said Mrs. Reynolds, pointing to another of the miniatures–– is my master, and it is very similar.</t3830> <t3831>It was painted at the same time as the other one, about eight years ago.</t3831>
<t3832>––I have heard much about your master's distinction ––replied Mrs. Gardiner, gazing at the portrait––, he is handsome.</t3832> <t3833>Elizabeth, tell me if it is a good likeness or not.</t3833>
<t3834>Mrs. Reynolds's respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase upon seeing that she knew her master –– "Does the young lady know Mr. Darcy?"</t3834>
<t3835>Elizabeth blushed and replied:</t3835>
<t3836>––A little.</t3836>
<t3837>––And doesn't the young lady think he is a very handsome gentleman?</t3837>
<t3838>––Yes, very handsome.</t3838>
<t3839>––I would swear he is the most handsome I have seen; but in the gallery upstairs you will see a better and larger portrait of him.</t3839> <t3840>This room was the favorite of my previous master, and these miniatures are just as they were during his lifetime.</t3840> <t3841>He liked them very much.</t3841>
<t3842>Elizabeth then explained to herself why Wickham's was among them.</t3842>
<t3843>Mrs. Reynolds then showed them a portrait of Miss Darcy, painted when she was only eight years old.</t3843>
<t3844>––And is Miss Darcy as handsome as her brother?</t3844>
<t3845>––Oh, yes! She is the most beautiful young woman ever seen!</t3845> <t3846>And so diligent!</t3846> <t3847>She plays and sings all day long.</t3847> <t3848>In the next room, there is a new piano that was just brought to her, a gift from my master.</t3848> <t3849>She will also arrive tomorrow with him.</t3849>
<t3850>Mr. Gardiner, with kindness and skill, was drawing her out, and Mrs. Reynolds, out of pride and affection, evidently took pleasure in talking about her master and his sister.</t3850>
<t3851>––Does your master come to Pemberley very often throughout the year?</t3851>
<t3852>––Not as much as I would like, sir; but I would say he spends half of his time here; Miss Darcy is always here during the summer months.</t3852> <t3853>“Except ––thought Elizabeth–– when she goes to Ramsgate.”</t3853>
<t3854>––If your master got married, you would see him more.</t3854>
<t3855>––Yes, sir; but I don't know when it will be.</t3855> <t3856>I don't know if there will be anyone who deserves it.</t3856>
<t3857>Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner smiled.</t3857> <t3858>Elizabeth couldn't help but say:</t3858>
<t3859>––If that is what you believe, it speaks highly of Mr. Darcy.</t3859>
<t3860>––I am only telling the truth and what anyone who knows him would say ––replied Mrs. Reynolds.</t3860> <t3861>Elizabeth thought that things were going too far, and listened with growing astonishment to what the housekeeper continued to say.</t3861>
<t3862>––Never in his life did he have a word of anger with me.</t3862> <t3863>And I have known him since he was four years old.</t3863> <t3864>It was a more important compliment than all the others and more opposed to what Elizabeth thought of Darcy.</t3864> <t3865>She always firmly believed that he was a man of bad character.</t3865> <t3866>With keen curiosity, she waited to keep hearing what the housekeeper was saying, when her uncle observed:</t3866>
<t3867>––There are few people about whom that can be said.</t3867> <t3868>It is a stroke of luck for you to have such a gentleman.</t3868>
<t3869>––Yes, sir; it is a stroke of luck.</t3869> <t3870>Even if you traveled around the world, you wouldn't find a better one.</t3870> <t3871>I've always noticed that those who are kind as children, remain so as adults.</t3871> <t3872>And Mr. Darcy was the sweetest and most generous child on earth.</t3872>

<t3874>––I believe his father was an excellent person ––added Mrs. Gardiner.</t3874>
<t3875>––Yes, ma'am; yes, he was, and his son is exactly like him, just as good with the poor.</t3875>
<t3876>Elizabeth listened, marveled, doubted, and wished to know more.</t3876> <t3877>Mrs. Reynolds could not capture her attention with anything else.</t3877> <t3878>It was useless for her to explain the subject of the paintings, the dimensions of the rooms, and the value of the furniture.</t3878> <t3879>Mr. Gardiner, very amused at what he assumed was family prejudice inspiring the old lady's lavish praises of her master, soon insisted on his questions, and as they ascended the grand staircase, Mrs. Reynolds continued extolling Darcy's many merits.</t3879>

<t3886>––Maybe we are wrong.</t3886>
<t3887>––It’s not likely; we know it from a very reliable source.</t3887> <t3888>In the wide corridor upstairs, they were shown a lovely room recently decorated with greater elegance and a lighter tone than the lower rooms, and they were told that all of it had been done to please Miss Darcy, who had taken a liking to that room the last time she was at Pemberley.</t3888>

<t3895>In the gallery, there were also several family portraits, but it was not easy for them to attract the attention of a stranger.</t3895> <t3896>Elizabeth went through them looking for the only portrait whose features she could recognize.</t3896> <t3897>Upon reaching it, she stopped, noting its surprising accuracy.</t3897> <t3898>Darcy's face had that same smile that Elizabeth had seen when he looked at her.</t3898> <t3899>She remained in front of the painting for several minutes, in the most attentive contemplation, and even looked at it again before leaving the gallery.</t3899> <t3900>Mrs. Reynolds informed her that it had been done during Darcy's father's lifetime.</t3900>
<t3901>Elizabeth felt at that moment a much greater inclination for the original than she had felt at the height of their relationship.</t3901> <t3902>Mrs. Reynolds' praises were no small matter.</t3902> <t3903>What praise can be more valuable than that of an intelligent servant?</t3903> <t3904>How many people had their happiness placed in Darcy's hands as a brother, a proprietor, and a lord!</t3904> <t3905>How much pleasure and how much pain could he bestow!</t3905> <t3906>How much harm and how much good could he do!</t3906> <t3907>Everything said by the housekeeper exalted him.</t3907> <t3908>Standing before the canvas on which he was portrayed, it seemed to Elizabeth that his eyes were looking at her, and she thought of his esteem for her with a much deeper gratitude than she had felt before; Elizabeth recalled the strength and warmth of his words and mitigated her lack of decorum.</t3908>
<t3909>They had already seen everything that was shown to the public in the house; they went downstairs and said goodbye to the housekeeper, who entrusted them to a gardener waiting at the vestibule door.</t3909>
<t3910>When they were crossing the meadow on their way to the stream, Elizabeth turned to look at the house again.</t3910> <t3911>Her uncles stopped as well, and while Mr. Gardiner was speculating about the age of the building, its owner suddenly came out from behind the house on the path leading to the stables.</t3911>
<t3912>They were less than twenty yards away, and their appearance was so sudden that it was impossible to prevent them from being seen.</t3912> <t3913>Elizabeth and Darcy's eyes met instantly, and their faces were covered with intense blushes.</t3913> <t3914>He stopped short and for a moment remained motionless with surprise; he quickly recovered and, stepping forward towards the visitors, spoke to Elizabeth, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least with absolute courtesy.</t3914>
<t3915>She had turned instinctively, but as he approached, he stopped and received her compliments with embarrassment.</t3915> <t3916>If Darcy's appearance at first sight or his resemblance to the portraits they had just been viewing had been insufficient to reveal to Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner that they had Darcy himself before them, the gardener's astonishment at encountering his master would have left no room for doubt.</t3916> <t3917>They waited at a certain distance while their niece spoke with him.</t3917> <t3918>Elizabeth, astonished and confused, hardly dared to raise her eyes to Darcy and did not know how to answer the questions he asked about her family.</t3918> <t3919>Surprised by the change in manners since they had last parted, each phrase he uttered increased her embarrassment, and as she thought about how improper it was to have met him there, the few moments they were together were the most uneasy of her life.</t3919> <t3920>Darcy did not seem more in control of himself than she was; his tone lacked the usual calm, and he kept asking her when she had left Longbourn and how long she had been in Derbyshire, with such disorder and haste that his agitation was clearly evident.</t3920>
<t3921>At last, it seemed that he no longer knew what to say; he remained silent for a few moments, suddenly composed himself, and said goodbye.</t3921>
<t3922>The Gardiners met with Elizabeth and praised Darcy's good appearance; but she heard nothing; absorbed in her thoughts, she followed them in silence.</t3922> <t3923>She was overwhelmed by shame and frustration.</t3923> <t3924>How had it occurred to her to go there?</t3924> <t3925>It had been the most unfortunate and foolish decision in the world!</t3925> <t3926>How strange it must seem to Darcy!</t3926> <t3927>How was a man ––so vain!–– to interpret that?</t3927> <t3928>Her visit to Pemberley would seem deliberately made to seek him out.</t3928> <t3929>Why had she gone?</t3929> <t3930>And he, why had he come a day earlier?</t3930> <t3931>If they themselves had arrived at Pemberley just ten minutes earlier, they would not have met, for it was evident that Darcy had just arrived, that at that moment he was getting off the horse or the carriage.</t3931> <t3932>Elizabeth could not stop feeling ashamed of their unfortunate encounter.</t3932> <t3933>And Darcy's behavior, so remarkably changed, what could it mean?</t3933> <t3934>It was surprising that he had spoken to her, but even more so that he did it with such finesse and asked about her family.</t3934> <t3935>She had never seen such simplicity in his manners nor had she ever heard him express himself with such gentleness.</t3935> <t3936>What a contrast to the last time he approached her at the Rosings estate to hand her the letter!</t3936> <t3937>Elizabeth did not know what to think or how to judge all this.</t3937>
<t3938>Meanwhile, they had entered a beautiful path parallel to the stream, and with each step, a more beautiful slope of the terrain or a more impressive view of the forests they were approaching appeared before them.</t3938> <t3939>But some time passed before Elizabeth realized all of this, and although she mechanically responded to her uncles' repeated questions and seemed to direct her gaze to the objects they pointed out, she did not distinguish any part of the landscape.</t3939> <t3940>Her thoughts could not be diverted from the site of Pemberley mansion, wherever it might be, where Darcy must be.</t3940> <t3941>She longed to know what was going through his mind at that moment, what he thought of her, and if he still loved her.</t3941> <t3942>It might be that his courtesy was merely because he had already forgotten her; but there was something in his voice that denoted unease.</t3942> <t3943>She could not guess whether Darcy felt pleasure or regret at seeing her; but the truth is that he seemed bewildered.</t3943>
<t3944>The observations of her companions about her lack of attention woke her up and made her realize that she needed to appear calm.</t3944>
<t3945>They penetrated the forest and, moving away from the stream for a while, climbed to one of the highest points, from which, through the clearings of the trees, one could extend the view and appreciate magnificent panoramas of the valley and the opposite hills covered with woodland, and parts of the stream could also be seen.</t3945> <t3946>Mr. Gardiner would have liked to tour the entire estate, but he feared the walk would be too long.</t3946> <t3947>With a triumphant smile, the gardener told them that the estate was ten miles long, so they decided not to take the planned tour and headed back down to a slope with trees leaning over the water at one of the narrowest points of the stream.</t3947> <t3948>They crossed it by a simple bridge in harmony with the general aspect of the landscape.</t3948> <t3949>That spot was the least adorned with artifices of all they had seen.</t3949> <t3950>The valley, turned into a glen here, left only space for the stream and a narrow path in the middle of the rustic grove that bordered it.</t3950> <t3951>Elizabeth wanted to explore its twists and turns, but as soon as they crossed the bridge and could appreciate how far they were from the house, Mrs. Gardiner, who was not fond of walking, did not want to go any further and only thought of returning to the carriage as soon as possible.</t3951> <t3952>Her niece was forced to yield, and they began the return to the house on the opposite side of the stream and by the shortest path.</t3952> <t3953>But they walked very slowly because Mr. Gardiner was fond of fishing, although he seldom had the chance to indulge in it, and he was distracted every so often by watching for the appearance of a trout and commenting on it with the gardener.</t3953> <t3954>As they continued their slow march, they were surprised again;
