<t3960>Mrs. Gardiner was coming a little behind, and Darcy took advantage of Elizabeth's silence to ask her to do him the honor of introducing him to her friends.</t3960> <t3961>Elizabeth was not prepared for this gesture of courtesy, and she could not help but smile at seeing that he intended to meet one of those people against whom his pride had rebelled when he declared himself to her.</t3961> <t3962>"What will his surprise be," she thought, "when he finds out who they are? He imagines they are people of high rank."</t3962>
<t3963>He made the introduction promptly and, upon mentioning the relationship, quickly glanced at Darcy to see his reaction, hoping he would hastily flee from such company.</t3963> <t3964>It was evident that Darcy was surprised, but he composed himself and instead of continuing on his way, he turned back with all of them and began conversing with Mr. Gardiner.</t3964> <t3965>Elizabeth couldn't help but feel satisfied and triumphant.</t3965> <t3966>It was comforting that Darcy knew she had relatives of whom there was no need to be ashamed.</t3966> <t3967>She listened attentively to what they were saying and took pride in her uncle's phrases and remarks that demonstrated his intelligence, good taste, and excellent manners.</t3967>
<t3968>The conversation soon turned to fishing, and Elizabeth heard Darcy inviting her uncle to go fishing there whenever he wanted while they were in the neighboring town, even offering to provide him with gear and pointing out the best spots on the river for fishing.</t3968> <t3969>Mrs. Gardiner, who was walking arm in arm with Elizabeth, looked at her with an expression of disbelief.</t3969> <t3970>Elizabeth said nothing, but she was extremely pleased; Darcy's attentions must surely be directed at her.</t3970> <t3971>Her astonishment, however, was extraordinary and she couldn't stop repeating to herself: "Why is he so changed?</t3971> <t3972>It can't be because of me, it can't be for my sake that his manners have softened so much.</t3972> <t3973>My reproaches in Hunsford cannot have effected such a transformation.</t3973> <t3974>It's impossible that he still loves me."</t3974>
<t3975>After walking for a while in this manner, the two ladies in front and the two gentlemen behind, upon resuming their path after descending to the riverbank to get a better look at a curious aquatic plant, there was a change of pairs.</t3975> <t3976>This was initiated by Mrs. Gardiner, who, fatigued by the day's exertions, found Elizabeth's arm too weak to support her and therefore preferred her husband's arm.</t3976> <t3977>Darcy then positioned himself beside the niece, and they continued their walk in this way.</t3977> <t3978>After a short silence, Elizabeth spoke.</t3978> <t3979>She wanted to let him know that before going to Pemberley, she had made sure that he was not there and that their arrival was completely unexpected.</t3979>
<t3980>––Your housekeeper ––he added–– informed us that you wouldn't arrive until tomorrow; and even before leaving Bakewell, we were told that you would be delayed in returning to Derbyshire.</t3980>
<t3981>Darcy acknowledged that it was so, but some matters he had to resolve with his steward had forced him to go ahead of his companions.</t3981>
<t3982>––Tomorrow morning ––he continued–– they will all meet with me.</t3982> <t3983>Among them are your acquaintances who will wish to see you; Mr. Bingley and his sisters.</t3983>
<t3984>Elizabeth did nothing more than a slight nod of her head.</t3984> <t3985>She instantly remembered the last time Bingley's name had been mentioned between them, and judging by Darcy's expression, he must have been thinking the same thing.</t3985>
<t3986>––A person who has a special desire to meet you is also coming with his friends ––he continued after a pause––.</t3986> <t3987>Will you allow me, or is it asking too much, to introduce my sister to you while you are in Lambton?</t3987>
<t3988>Elizabeth was left speechless.</t3988> <t3989>She could not imagine how Miss Darcy could intend such a thing; but she immediately understood that this desire was her brother's doing, and without drawing any further conclusions, it seemed very flattering to her.</t3989> <t3990>It was pleasant to know that Darcy did not hold a grudge against her.</t3990>
<t3991>They continued walking in silence, both deeply absorbed in their thoughts.</t3991> <t3992>Elizabeth could not be calm, but she felt flattered and pleased.</t3992> <t3993>Darcy's intention to introduce her to his sister was an exceptional kindness.</t3993> <t3994>Soon they left the others behind, and when they reached the carriage, Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner were half a quarter of a mile away from them.</t3994>
<t3995>Darcy then invited her to come into the house, but Elizabeth declared that she was not tired and they waited together on the lawn.</t3995> <t3996>In that moment they could have talked about many things, the silence was awkward.</t3996> <t3997>She wanted to speak but her mind was blank and all the topics that came to her seemed to be off-limits.</t3997> <t3998>Finally, she remembered her trip and spoke about Matlock and Dove Dale with great perseverance.</t3998> <t3999>Time passed, her aunt walked very slowly, and Elizabeth's patience and ideas were running out before the tête-à-tête ended.</t3999> <t4000>When Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner arrived, Darcy invited them all to come into the house and have some refreshments; but they excused themselves and parted with the utmost courtesy.</t4000> <t4001>Darcy accompanied them to the carriage, and when it started moving, Elizabeth saw him walking slowly back towards the house.</t4001>
<t4002>Then the uncles began their comments; both declared that Darcy was superior to anything that could be imagined.</t4002>
<t4003>––Her education is perfect and her elegance and simplicity are admirable ––said her uncle.</t4003>
<t4004>––There is a bit of arrogance in him ––added the aunt, but only in his demeanor, and it doesn't look bad on him.</t4004> <t4005>I can say, like the housekeeper, that although he is labeled as proud, it is not noticeable at all.</t4005>
<t4006>––His attitude towards us has left me astonished.</t4006> <t4007>He has been more than courteous, he has been frankly attentive and nothing obliged him to be so.</t4007> <t4008>His friendship with Elizabeth was very superficial.</t4008>
<t4009>––Of course, he is not as handsome as Wickham ––replied the aunt––; or rather, he is not as well-built, but his features are perfect.</t4009> <t4010>How could you tell us he was so unpleasant, Lizzy?</t4010>
<t4011>Elizabeth apologized as best she could; she said that upon seeing him in Kent, she had liked him more than before and had never found him as agreeable as that morning.</t4011>
<t4012>––He might be a bit whimsical in his courtesy ––replied the uncle––; those very haughty gentlemen tend to be like that.</t4012> <t4013>That's why I won't take his word regarding the fishing, in case he changes his mind another day and throws me off the estate.</t4013>
<t4014>Elizabeth realized that they were completely wrong about her character, but she said nothing.</t4014>
<t4015>––After having seen him now, I would never have believed that he could behave as badly as he did with Wickham ––continued Mrs. Gardiner––, he doesn't seem heartless.</t4015> <t4016>On the contrary, he has a very pleasant expression when he speaks.</t4016> <t4017>And there is also a dignity in his face that would make no one think he doesn't have a good heart.</t4017> <t4018>But, to tell the truth, the good woman who showed us the house exaggerated his character a bit.</t4018> <t4019>There were times when I almost burst out laughing.</t4019> <t4020>The thing is, he must be a very generous master and that, in the eyes of a servant, equals all virtues.</t4020>
<t4021>Upon hearing this, Elizabeth believed she should say something in defense of Darcy's conduct with Wickham.</t4021> <t4022>With all the care she could muster, she tried to hint that, based on what she had heard from her relatives in Kent, his actions could be interpreted very differently, and that neither his character was as bad nor Wickham's as good as had been believed in Hertfordshire.</t4022> <t4023>To confirm what she had said, she recounted the details of all the financial transactions that had taken place between them, without mentioning how she had come to know them, but asserting that it was strictly true.</t4023>
<t4024>Mrs. Gardiner was surprised and curious about the topic, but as they were approaching the scene of her former pleasures, she yielded to the charm of her memories and only pointed out to her husband all the interesting places and their surroundings.</t4024> <t4025>Despite being tired from the morning walk, as soon as they had dinner, they went out in search of old acquaintances, and the evening passed with the satisfaction of relationships renewed after many years of interruption.</t4025>
<t4026>The events of that day had been too overwhelming for Elizabeth to pay much attention to any of those new friends, and she could only think with admiration of Darcy's kindnesses, and especially of his desire for her to meet his sister.</t4026>
<t4027>CHAPTER XLIV</t4027>
<t4028>Elizabeth had calculated that Darcy would bring his sister to visit her the day after their arrival at Pemberley, and consequently, she resolved not to lose sight of the inn all that morning.</t4028> <t4029>But she was mistaken, for she received the visit the very day they arrived.</t4029> <t4030>The Gardiners and Elizabeth had been walking around the town with some of their new friends, and were returning at that moment to the inn to dress and go to lunch with them, when the noise of a carriage made them look out the window and they saw a gentleman and a young lady in a cabriolet coming up the street.</t4030> <t4031>Elizabeth instantly recognized the livery of the footmen, guessed what it meant, and left her uncles astonished by informing them of the honor that awaited them.</t4031> <t4032>They were frightened; that visit, Elizabeth's bewilderment, and the circumstances of the previous day made them form a new idea of the matter.</t4032> <t4033>There had been nothing to suggest it before, but now they realized that there was no other way to explain Darcy's attentions than by supposing him interested in their niece.</t4033> <t4034>While they were thinking about all this, Elizabeth's agitation increased by the moment.</t4034> <t4035>She was alarmed by her own bewilderment, and among the other causes of her unease was the idea that Darcy, in his enthusiasm, might have spoken of her to his sister with too much praise.</t4035> <t4036>She wanted to please more than ever, but suspected that she would not be able to achieve it.</t4036>
<t4037>She withdrew from the window for fear of being seen, and, while pacing back and forth in the room, the questioning looks from her uncles made her even more nervous.</t4037>
<t4038>Finally, Miss Darcy and her brother appeared, and the grand introduction took place.</t4038> <t4039>Elizabeth noticed with astonishment that her new acquaintance was at least as disturbed as she was.</t4039> <t4040>Since arriving in Lambton, she had heard that Miss Darcy was extremely proud, but after observing her for a few minutes, she was convinced that she was only extremely shy.</t4040> <t4041>She could hardly get a word out of her, except for a few monosyllables.</t4041>
<t4042>Miss Darcy was taller than Elizabeth and, although she was only sixteen years old, her body was already developed and her appearance was very feminine and graceful.</t4042> <t4043>She was not as beautiful as her brother, but her face revealed intelligence and good character, and her manners were very simple and gentle.</t4043> <t4044>Elizabeth, who had feared that she might be as sharp and outgoing an observer as Darcy, felt great relief to see how different she was.</t4044>
<t4045>They had been conversing for only a short while when Darcy told Elizabeth that Bingley would also be coming to visit her, and she had barely had time to express her satisfaction and prepare to receive him when they heard Bingley's hurried steps on the stairs, and he immediately entered the room.</t4045> <t4046>All of Elizabeth's indignation against him had long since disappeared, but if any resentment had remained, she could not have resisted the frank cordiality that Bingley showed her upon seeing her again.</t4046> <t4047>He inquired about her family in a caring, albeit general, manner, and behaved and spoke with his usual good humor.</t4047>
<t4048>The Gardiners welcomed Bingley with the same interest as Elizabeth.</t4048> <t4049>They had long wanted to meet him.</t4049> <t4050>In truth, all those present inspired the liveliest curiosity in them.</t4050> <t4051>The suspicions they had just conceived about Darcy and his niece led them to focus their attention on them, examining them closely, though discreetly, and very soon they realized that at least one of them was very much in love.</t4051> <t4052>Elizabeth's feelings were somewhat doubtful, but it was evident that Darcy was overflowing with admiration in every way.</t4052>
<t4053>Elizabeth, for her part, had a lot to do.</t4053> <t4054>She had to guess the feelings of each of her visitors and at the same time had to contain her own and make herself pleasant to everyone.</t4054> <t4055>It is true that the last part, which was what scared her the most, was what she could achieve most confidently, as the interested parties were already very predisposed in her favor.</t4055> <t4056>Bingley was ready, Georgiana desired it, and Darcy was completely decided.</t4056>
<t4057>Upon seeing Bingley, Elizabeth's thoughts naturally flew to her sister, and she diligently observed whether any of his thoughts were going in the same direction.</t4057> <t4058>She deluded herself into thinking that he spoke less than on other occasions, and once or twice she took pleasure in the idea that, when he looked at her, Bingley was trying to find a resemblance.</t4058> <t4059>But, even if all that were just her fantasies, she could not be mistaken about his behavior towards Miss Darcy, who had been spoken of as Jane's presumed rival.</t4059> <t4060>She did not notice a single glance from either of them that could justify the hopes of Bingley's sister.</t4060> <t4061>Regarding this matter, she was completely satisfied.</t4061> <t4062>Before they left, she still noticed from two or three small details that Bingley remembered Jane tenderly and seemed to want to say something more but did not dare.</t4062> <t4063>At a moment when the others were conversing, he said it in a sorrowful tone:</t4063>
<t4064>––How long it has been since I had the pleasure of seeing you!</t4064>
<t4065>And, before Elizabeth had time to respond, he added:</t4065>
<t4066>––About eight months ago.</t4066> <t4067>We hadn't seen each other since the twenty-sixth of November when we all danced together at Netherfield.</t4067>
<t4068>Elizabeth was glad to see that her memory did not fail her.</t4068> <t4069>Then, taking advantage of the others being distracted, she asked him if all his sisters were at Longbourn.</t4069> <t4070>Neither the question nor the previous memory were important, but Bingley's look and gesture were very significant.</t4070>
<t4071>Elizabeth did not look at Darcy very often; but when she did, she saw in him an expression of contentment and in what he said she perceived a tone that erased all disdain or haughtiness towards his companions, and convinced her that the improvement in his character that she had witnessed the previous day, even if fleeting, had lasted, at least, until now.</t4071> <t4072>Seeing him trying to be sociable, seeking the good opinion of those present, with whom having any dealings a few months ago would have meant disgrace for him; seeing him so courteous, not only with her, but with the very relatives he had despised, and remembering the violent scene at the parsonage in Hunsford, the difference, the change was so great, that she could barely prevent her astonishment from becoming visible.</t4072> <t4073>Never, neither in the company of his dear friends at Netherfield, nor in that of his haughty relatives at Rosings, had she found him so eager to please, so devoid of self-importance or reserve, as now when no vanity could be gained from the success of his endeavor, and when dealing with those he showered with attentions would have been censured and ridiculed by the ladies of Netherfield and Rosings.</t4073>
<t4074>The visit lasted about half an hour, and when they stood up to say goodbye, Darcy asked his sister to support the invitation to the Gardiners and Miss Bennet, so that they would come to dine at Pemberley before leaving the area.</t4074> <t4075>Miss Darcy, although with a shyness that revealed her little habit of making invitations, obeyed immediately.</t4075> <t4076>Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece to see how she, to whom the invitation was directed, would receive it; but Elizabeth had turned her head away.</t4076> <t4077>She presumed, however, that her studied evasion meant more a momentary confusion than displeasure at the proposition, and seeing her husband, who was very fond of social life, eager to accept, she ventured to accept on behalf of the three; and the date was set for two days later.</t4077>
<t4078>Bingley expressed his delight at knowing he would see Elizabeth again, as he still had many things to tell her and many questions to ask about all the friends in Hertfordshire.</t4078> <t4079>Elizabeth believed she understood that he wished to hear her speak of her sister, and she was very pleased.</t4079> <t4080>This and some other details of the visit left her inclined, as soon as their friends had left, to recall it with pleasure, even though she had felt a bit uncomfortable during the visit.</t4080> <t4081>Eager to be alone and fearful of her uncles' questions or suppositions, she stayed with them just long enough to hear their favorable comments about Bingley, and then hurried to get dressed.</t4081>
<t4082>But she was very wrong to fear the curiosity of the Gardiners, who had no intention of making her talk.</t4082> <t4083>It was evident that her relationship with Darcy was much more serious than they had believed, and it was clearer than water that he was deeply in love with her.</t4083> <t4084>They had seen many things that interested them, but they did not justify their inquiry.</t4084>
<t4085>The important thing now was that Darcy was a good boy.</t4085> <t4086>As far as they could have appreciated, he had no faults.</t4086> <t4087>His kindnesses had moved them, and if they had had to describe his character according to their own opinion and according to the reports of their maid, disregarding any other reference, they would have done so in such a way that the Hertfordshire circle who knew him would not have recognized him.</t4087> <t4088>They now wished to give credit to the housekeeper and soon agreed that the testimony of a maid who had known him since he was four years old and who seemed so respectable, could not be questioned.</t4088> <t4089>On the other hand, there was nothing in what his friends from Lambton said that could diminish the weight of that testimony.</t4089> <t4090>They accused him of nothing more than pride; proud he may have been, but even if he had not been, the inhabitants of that small commercial town, where the Pemberley family never went, would have attributed the label to him just the same.</t4090> <t4091>But they said he was very generous and did a lot of good among the poor.</t4091>
<t4092>As for Wickham, the travelers soon saw that he was not held in high regard there; the main details of his relationship with his master's son were unknown, but it was well known that upon leaving Derbyshire he had left behind a multitude of debts that Darcy had paid.</t4092>
<t4093>Elizabeth thought about Pemberley that night even more than the previous one.</t4093> <t4094>It seemed very long to her, but not long enough to determine her feelings towards one of the inhabitants of the mansion.</t4094> <t4095>After going to bed, she stayed awake for two hours trying to decipher them.</t4095> <t4096>She did not hate him, no; the hatred had vanished long ago, and for almost all that time she had been ashamed of having felt a dislike towards that person that could bear such a name.</t4096> <t4097>The respect due to his valuable qualities, although admitted at first against her will, had contributed to the cessation of the hostility of her feelings, and these had evolved into affectionate ones in light of the significant testimony in his favor that she had heard and the good disposition he himself had shown the previous day.</t4097> <t4098>But above all that, above respect and esteem, Elizabeth felt another impulse of benevolence towards Darcy that could not be overlooked.</t4098> <t4099>It was gratitude; gratitude not only for having loved her, but for still loving her enough to forget all the petulance and sharpness of her rejection and all the unjust accusations that accompanied it.</t4099> <t4100>He, who should consider her ––so Elizabeth supposed–– as his greatest enemy, upon meeting her by chance seemed eager to maintain her friendship, and without any display of indelicacy or affectation in his manner, in a matter that only concerned the two of them, sought the good opinion of her friends and decided to introduce her to his sister.</t4100> <t4101>Such a change in a man so proud not only had to inspire astonishment, but also gratitude, for it had to be attributed to love, to a passionate love.</t4101> <t4102>But, although this impression was encouraging and very contrary to dislike, she could not define it precisely.</t4102> <t4103>She respected him, esteemed him, was grateful to him, and earnestly wished for his happiness.</t4103> <t4104>She only needed to know to what extent she wished that happiness to depend on her, and to what extent it would result in the happiness of both if she used the power she imagined she still possessed to induce him to renew his proposal.</t4104>
<t4105>In the afternoon, the aunt and niece agreed that such an extraordinary attention as the visit from Miss Darcy on the very day of her arrival at Pemberley—where she had arrived shortly after breakfast—should be reciprocated, if not with something equivalent, at least with some special courtesy.</t4105> <t4106>Therefore, they decided to visit her at Pemberley the next morning.</t4106> <t4107>Elizabeth felt happy, although when she asked herself why, she couldn't find an answer.</t4107>
<t4108>After breakfast, Mr. Gardiner left them.</t4108> <t4109>The offer to go fishing had been renewed the previous day, and he had been assured that one of the gentlemen from Pemberley would accompany him at noon.</t4109>
<t4110>CHAPTER XLV</t4110>
<t4111>Elizabeth was now convinced that Miss Bingley's antipathy towards her stemmed from jealousy.</t4111> <t4112>She understood, therefore, how unpleasant it must be for her to see her appear at Pemberley and wondered with curiosity how much courtesy she would muster to resume their relations.</t4112>
<t4113>Upon arriving at the house, they crossed the vestibule and entered the living room, whose northward orientation made it delightful in summer.</t4113> <t4114>The wide-open windows offered a refreshing view of the tall, forested hills behind the building, and of the beautiful oaks and chestnut trees of Spain scattered across the meadow that stretched out in front of the house.</t4114>
<t4115>In that room, they were received by Miss Darcy, who was waiting for them along with Mrs. Hurst, Miss Bingley, and her companion.</t4115> <t4116>Georgiana's welcome was very courteous, but dominated by that shyness due to her timidity and the fear of doing things wrong, which had given her a reputation of being proud and reserved among her inferiors.</t4116> <t4117>But Mrs. Gardiner and her niece understood and sympathized with her.</t4117>
<t4118>Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley made a simple bow and sat down.</t4118> <t4119>An extremely awkward silence ensued, lasting for a few moments.</t4119> <t4120>It was interrupted by Mrs. Annesley, a kind and pleasant person who, in attempting to break the ice, showed better manners than any of the other ladies.</t4120> <t4121>The conversation continued between her and Mrs. Gardiner, with some contributions from Elizabeth.</t4121> <t4122>Miss Darcy seemed to wish she had the confidence to take part in the conversation, and occasionally ventured a short sentence when there was the least danger of being overheard.</t4122>
<t4123>Elizabeth immediately realized that Miss Bingley was closely watching her and that she couldn't say a word, especially to Miss Darcy, without the other sharpening her ears.</t4123> <t4124>However, her tenacious observation would not have prevented her from speaking with Georgiana if they hadn't been so distant from each other; but she was not distressed by not being able to speak much, as it allowed her to think more freely.</t4124> <t4125>She both wished for and feared the arrival of the master of the house, and she could hardly clarify whether she feared it more than she desired it.</t4125> <t4126>After being like this for a quarter of an hour without hearing Miss Bingley's voice, Elizabeth blushed when the latter asked her how her family was.</t4126> <t4127>She replied with the same indifference and brevity, and the other said nothing more.</t4127>
<t4128>The first variety of the visit consisted of the appearance of some servants who brought cold cuts, pastries, and some of the best fruits of the season, but this happened after many significant looks from Mrs. Annesley to Georgiana to remind her of her duties.</t4128> <t4129>This distracted the gathering, for, although not all the ladies could talk, at least all could eat.</t4129> <t4130>The beautiful pyramids of grapes, apricots, and peaches immediately gathered them around the table.</t4130>
<t4131>While they were engaged in this, Elizabeth began to ponder whether she feared or desired Darcy's arrival due to the effect his presence would have on her; and although a moment before she believed she rather desired it, she now began to think the opposite.</t4131>
<t4132>Darcy had been with Mr. Gardiner, who was fishing in the river with two or three other gentlemen, but upon learning that the ladies of his family planned to visit Georgiana that very morning, he went home.</t4132> <t4133>Upon seeing him enter, Elizabeth resolved to appear as natural as possible, a necessary but difficult task, as she was aware that the entire gathering was focused on them, and as soon as Darcy arrived, all eyes turned to examine him.</t4133> <t4134>But no face showed curiosity as strongly as Miss Bingley's, despite the smiles she lavished while speaking with anyone; however, her jealousy had not reached the point of making her desist from her attentions to Darcy––.</t4134> <t4135>Georgiana, as soon as her brother entered, made more effort to speak, and Elizabeth understood that Darcy wanted the two to become close, for which he encouraged all attempts at conversation from both sides.</t4135> <t4136>Miss Bingley also noticed this and with the imprudence typical of her anger, seized the first opportunity to say with mocking finesse:</t4136>
<t4137>––Tell me, Miss Elizabeth, is it true that the Meryton garrison has been relocated?</t4137> <t4138>It must have been a great loss for your family.</t4138>
<t4139>In Darcy's presence, she did not dare to utter Wickham's name, but Elizabeth guessed that he had that name in his thoughts; the various memories it evoked distressed her for a moment, but she composed herself with fortitude to repel that brazen attack and answered the question in a nonchalant tone.</t4139> <t4140>In doing so, an involuntary glance made her see Darcy, with his face flushed, watching her intently, and his sister completely confused and unable to lift her eyes.</t4140> <t4141>If Miss Bingley could have suspected how much it pained her beloved, she would undoubtedly have refrained; but she had only tried to unsettle Elizabeth by bringing up something related to a man for whom she had been partial, and to provoke her into some misstep that would harm her in Darcy's eyes and, incidentally, remind him of the absurdities and follies of the Bennet family.</t4141> <t4142>She did not know a word about Miss Darcy's elopement, as it had been kept strictly secret, and Elizabeth was the only person to whom it had been revealed.</t4142> <t4143>Darcy wanted to conceal it from all of Bingley's relatives for that same desire, which Elizabeth had attributed to him for so long, of becoming part of their family.</t4143> <t4144>Darcy, in fact, had this purpose, and although it was not for this reason that he sought to separate his friend from Jane, it is likely that it added to his keen interest in Bingley's happiness.</t4144>
<t4145>But Elizabeth's attitude reassured him.</t4145> <t4146>Miss Bingley, humiliated and disappointed, did not dare to allude to anything related to Wickham again.</t4146> <t4147>Georgiana began to recover, but she remained definitively silent, not daring to face her brother's gaze.</t4147> <t4148>Darcy did not dwell on what had happened, but instead of putting Elizabeth out of his mind, Miss Bingley's insinuation seemed to excite his passion even more.</t4148>
<t4149>After the aforementioned question and answer, the visit did not last much longer, and while Darcy accompanied the ladies to the carriage, Miss Bingley vented by criticizing Elizabeth's behavior and attire.</t4149> <t4150>But Georgiana paid her no mind.</t4150> <t4151>Her brother's interest in Miss Bennet was more than enough to ensure her approval; his judgment was infallible, and he had spoken of Elizabeth in such terms that Georgiana had to find her amiable and attractive.</t4151> <t4152>When Darcy returned to the drawing room, Miss Bingley could not contain herself and had to repeat some of what she had already said to her sister:</t4152>
<t4153>––How poorly Elizabeth Bennet looked, Mr. Darcy! ––she exclaimed––.</t4153> <t4154>How changed I found her since the winter!</t4154> <t4155>How tanned and unrefined she has become!</t4155> <t4156>Neither Louisa nor I would have recognized her.</t4156>
<t4157>The remark amused Darcy very little, but he restrained himself and replied coldly that he had noticed no other change than being tanned by the sun, which was very natural when traveling in summer.</t4157>
<t4158>––For my part ––continued Miss Bingley, I confess that she has never seemed pretty to me.</t4158> <t4159>Her face is too thin, her complexion is dull, and her features are not at all attractive; her nose has no character and there is nothing remarkable about its lines; she has passable teeth, but they are nothing out of the ordinary, and as for her much-praised eyes, I do not see anything extraordinary about them, they look in a penetrating and stern way that is very unpleasant; and in her whole demeanor, in short, there is so much pretension and a lack of good taste that it is intolerable.</t4159>
<t4160>Knowing as Miss Bingley did that Darcy admired Elizabeth, this was not at all the best way to please him, but irritated people do not usually act wisely; and seeing that she was provoking him, she achieved the success she expected.</t4160> <t4161>However, he remained silent, but Miss Bingley was determined to make him speak and continued:</t4161>
<t4162>––I remember that the first time we saw her in Hertfordshire, we were surprised that she had a reputation for being beautiful; and I especially remember that one night when they had dined at Netherfield, you said: "If she is a beauty, her mother is a genius!"</t4162> <t4163>But afterwards, it seemed that you started to like her, and I believe you came to consider her pretty at some point.</t4163>
<t4164>––Yes ––replied Darcy, unable to contain himself any longer––, but that was when I first started to get to know her, because for many months now I have considered her one of the most beautiful women I have ever seen.</t4164>
<t4165>Having said this, he left, and Miss Bingley was very pleased to have forced him to say what only hurt her.</t4165>
<t4166>On the way back from Lambton, Mrs. Gardiner and Elizabeth discussed everything that had happened during the visit, except for what interested them both the most.</t4166> <t4167>They talked about the appearance and behavior of everyone, without mentioning the person to whom they had paid the most attention.</t4167> <t4168>They spoke about his sister, his friends, his house, his fruits, everything except for him, despite Elizabeth's desire to know what Mrs. Gardiner thought of Darcy, and how much Mrs. Gardiner would have been pleased if her niece had brought up the subject.</t4168>
<t4169>CHAPTER XLVI</t4169>
<t4170>Upon arriving at Lambton, Elizabeth was displeased not to find a letter from Jane; the displeasure was renewed every morning, but on the third day, she received two letters at once, one of which contained a note saying that it had been misplaced and sent to another location, which did not surprise Elizabeth, because Jane had addressed it very poorly.</t4170>
<t4171>At the moment the two letters arrived, they were about to go for a walk, and to let her enjoy them quietly, her uncles went off alone.</t4171> <t4172>Elizabeth first read the lost letter, which was five days late.</t4172> <t4173>At first, it recounted the small gatherings and invitations, and gave the few news that the countryside allowed; but the last half, dated a day later and written with evident agitation, said much more important things:</t4173>
<t4174>After having written the above, dearest Elizabeth, something very serious and unexpected has occurred; but do not be alarmed, we are all well.</t4174> <t4175>What I am going to tell you concerns poor Lydia.</t4175> <t4176>Last night at eleven, when we were going to bed, an express arrived sent by Colonel Forster to inform us that our sister had eloped to Scotland with one of the officers; to be blunt: with Wickham.</t4176> <t4177>Imagine our surprise.</t4177> <t4178>However, Catherine did not find it surprising at all.</t4178> <t4179>I am very sad.</t4179> <t4180>What imprudence on the part of both!</t4180> <t4181>But I want to hope for the best and that Wickham is not as bad as believed, that he is merely frivolous and indiscreet; for what he has done ––let us be glad of it–– does not indicate a bad heart.</t4181> <t4182>His choice, after all, is disinterested, because he knows that our father cannot give Lydia anything.</t4182> <t4183>Our poor mother is dismayed.</t4183> <t4184>Father is taking it better.</t4184> <t4185>How right we were not to tell them what we knew about Wickham!</t4185> <t4186>We ourselves must forget it.</t4186> <t4187>It is supposed that they eloped on Saturday at about twelve, but they were not missed until yesterday at eight in the morning.</t4187> <t4188>They immediately sent the express.</t4188> <t4189>Dear Elizabeth, they must have passed less than ten miles from you!</t4189> <t4190>Colonel Forster says he will come at once.</t4190> <t4191>Lydia left a few lines written for Mrs. Forster informing her of her intentions.</t4191> <t4192>I have to finish, for I cannot extend myself because of my poor mother.</t4192> <t4193>I fear you will not understand what is written, for I do not even know what I have put.</t4193>
<t4194>Without taking time to reflect and barely knowing what she felt after finishing the reading of this letter, Elizabeth impatiently opened the other one and read the following, written a day later:</t4194>
<t4195>By this time, dearest sister, you will have received my hurried letter.</t4195><t4196>I hope this one is more intelligible; but, although I have time, my head is so bewildered that I cannot be coherent.</t4196><t4197>Dear Eliza, I would rather not write to you, but I have bad news to give you and I cannot delay it.</t4197><t4198>However imprudent the marriage of Wickham and our poor Lydia may be, we are anxious to know that it has already taken place, for there are ample reasons to fear that they have not gone to Scotland.</t4198><t4199>Colonel Forster arrived yesterday; he left Brighton a few hours after the couple themselves.</t4199><t4200>Although Lydia's letter to Mrs. Forster implied that they were going to Gretna Green, Denny said that he was aware and that Wickham never intended to go there nor to marry Lydia; upon learning this, Colonel Forster became alarmed and immediately left Brighton with the idea of catching up with them.</t4200><t4201>He indeed followed their trail easily to Clapham, but could not continue further, because upon reaching that point they took a hired carriage, leaving the post chaise that had brought them from Epsom.</t4201><t4202>And nothing more is known except that they were seen taking the road to London.</t4202><t4203>I do not know what to think.</t4203><t4204>After making all possible inquiries from there to London, Colonel Forster came to Hertfordshire to repeat them at all the toll gates and inns in Barnet and Hatfield, but without any result; no one has seen those persons there.</t4204><t4205>With the greatest regret, he arrived at Longbourn to inform us of everything, in a manner that does him honor.</t4205><t4206>I am truly sorry for him and his wife; no one can reproach them.</t4206><t4207>Our grief is very great.</t4207><t4208>Father and mother expect the worst, but I cannot believe that Wickham is so wicked.</t4208><t4209>Many circumstances may have driven them to marry secretly in the capital instead of following their first plan; and even in the case that he had plotted the ruin of a girl from a good family like Lydia, which is not likely, should I believe her so lost?</t4209><t4210>Impossible.</t4210><t4211>It dismays me, however, to see that Colonel Forster does not trust that they have married; when I expressed my hopes to him, he shook his head and expressed his fear that Wickham is not to be trusted.</t4211><t4212>My poor mother is truly ill and does not leave her room.</t4212><t4213>As for my father, I have never seen him so affected.</t4213><t4214>Poor Catherine is desperate for having concealed Lydia and Wickham's romance, but it is not surprising that the girls confided in each other.</t4214><t4215>Dearest Lizzy, I am sincerely glad that you have been spared these painful scenes.</t4215><t4216>But now that the first blow has passed, I confess that I long for your return.</t4216><t4217>I am not selfish, however, to the extent of asking you to come back if you cannot.</t4217><t4218>Goodbye.</t4218><t4219>I take up the pen again to do what I just told you I would not do, but the circumstances are such that I cannot help but implore the three of you to come as soon as possible.</t4219><t4220>I know our dear uncles so well that I do not doubt they will agree.</t4220><t4221>I also have another request to make of our uncle.</t4221><t4222>My father is going to London with Colonel Forster to see if they can find her.</t4222><t4223>I do not know what they plan to do, but he is so dejected that he will not be able to take the best and most expeditious measures, and Colonel Forster has no choice but to be in Brighton by tomorrow night.</t4223><t4224>In this situation, our uncle's advice and assistance would be of great help.</t4224><t4225>He will understand this; I count on his kindness.</t4225>
<t4226>––Where, where is my uncle?</t4226> <t4227>––exclaimed Elizabeth, rising from the chair as soon as she finished reading and determined not to lose a single moment; but as she reached the door, a servant opened it and Darcy entered.</t4227> <t4228>Elizabeth's pale countenance and urgency alarmed him.</t4228> <t4229>Before he could recover enough to speak to her, Elizabeth, who could think of nothing but Lydia's situation, exclaimed hastily:</t4229>
<t4230>––Excuse me, but I have to leave you; I need to speak with Mr. Gardiner immediately about a matter that cannot be delayed; there is no time to lose.</t4230>
<t4231>––My God! What is this about? ––he asked with more feeling than courtesy; then, recovering himself, he said––: I don't want to detain you for even a minute; but allow me to be the one to go in search of Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner or send a servant.</t4231> <t4232>You cannot go in those conditions.</t4232>
<t4233>Elizabeth hesitated; but her knees were trembling and she realized that she would gain nothing by trying to catch up with them.</t4233> <t4234>Consequently, she called the servant and instructed him to bring her masters without delay, although she gave the order in such a faint voice that she was barely heard.</t4234>
<t4235>When the servant left the room, Elizabeth collapsed into a chair, unable to support herself.</t4235> <t4236>She looked so distraught that Darcy could not leave her without saying in an affectionate and compassionate tone:</t4236>
<t4237>––I am going to call your maid.</t4237> <t4238>What could you take to feel better?</t4238> <t4239>A glass of wine?</t4239> <t4240>I am going to bring it to you.</t4240> <t4241>You are sick.</t4241>
<t4242>––No, thank you ––replied Elizabeth, trying to calm herself––.</t4242> <t4243>It's nothing of mine.</t4243> <t4244>I am fine.</t4244> <t4245>The only thing that happens to me is that I am devastated by a horrible news I just received from Longbourn.</t4245>
<t4246>Upon saying this, she burst into tears and was unable to speak for a few minutes.</t4246> <t4247>Darcy, distressed and in suspense, said nothing more than a few vague remarks about his interest in her, and then watched her in silence.</t4247> <t4248>Finally, Elizabeth continued:</t4248>
<t4249>––I have had a letter from Jane, and she gives me some dreadful news that cannot be concealed from anyone.</t4249> <t4250>My younger sister has abandoned us, she has eloped, she has given herself to... Wickham.</t4250> <t4251>The two have escaped from Brighton.</t4251> <t4252>You know Wickham too well to understand what that means.</t4252> <t4253>Lydia has no money nor anything that could have tempted him...</t4253> <t4254>She is lost forever.</t4254>
<t4255>Darcy stood motionless in astonishment.</t4255>
<t4256>––When I think ––added Elizabeth even more agitatedly–– that I could have prevented it!</t4256> <t4257>I who knew who Wickham was!</t4257> <t4258>If I had explained to my family just a part, something of what I knew about him!</t4258> <t4259>If they had known him, this would not have happened.</t4259> <t4260>But now it is too late for everything.</t4260>
<t4261>––I am horrified ––exclaimed Darcy––.</t4261> <t4262>But is it true, absolutely true?</t4262>
<t4263>––Unfortunately!</t4263> <t4264>They left Brighton on Sunday night and their tracks have been followed up to near London, but no further; it is certain that they have not gone to Scotland.</t4264>
<t4265>––And what has been done, what have they tried to do to find her?</t4265>
<t4266>––My father has gone to London and Jane writes requesting my uncle's immediate help; I hope we will leave within half an hour.</t4266> <t4267>But nothing can be done, I know nothing can be done.</t4267> <t4268>How to convince such a man?</t4268> <t4269>How to discover them?</t4269> <t4270>I have not the slightest hope.</t4270> <t4271>Any way you look at it, it is horrible.</t4271>
<t4272>Darcy nodded silently.</t4272>
<t4273>––Oh, if only when I opened my eyes and saw who Wickham was, I had done what I should have!</t4273> <t4274>But I didn't dare, I feared I would go too far.</t4274> <t4275>What an unfortunate mistake!</t4275>
<t4276>Darcy did not respond.</t4276> <t4277>It seemed as if he didn't even hear her; he paced back and forth in the room, absorbed in his thoughts, with a frown and a somber air.</t4277> <t4278>Elizabeth watched him, and in an instant, she understood everything.</t4278> <t4279>The attraction he felt for her had ended; everything had ended before that proof of her family's unworthiness and the certainty of such profound misfortune.</t4279> <t4280>She neither found it strange nor could she blame him.</t4280> <t4281>But the belief that Darcy had recovered did not console her pain nor lessen her despair.</t4281> <t4282>On the contrary, it made the young woman realize her own feelings, and never had she felt so sincerely as at that moment that she could have loved him, when all love was already impossible.</t4282>
<t4283>But not even this consideration managed to distract her.</t4283> <t4284>She could not take Lydia, nor the humiliation and misfortune that had befallen them all, out of her mind.</t4284> <t4285>She covered her face with a handkerchief and forgot everything else.</t4285> <t4286>After several minutes of silence, she heard Darcy's voice, compassionate yet reserved, saying to her:</t4286>
<t4287>––I am afraid you want me to leave, and there is nothing that excuses my presence; but I have been moved by a genuine, though useless, interest.</t4287> <t4288>I wish I could say or do something to console you in such misfortune!</t4288> <t4289>But I do not want to torment you with vain wishes that would seem to be expressed only so that you would thank me.</t4289> <t4290>I believe this unfortunate matter is going to deprive my sister of the pleasure of seeing you today at Pemberley.</t4290>
<t4291>––Oh, yes!</t4291> <t4292>Please excuse us to Miss Darcy.</t4292> <t4293>Tell her that urgent matters call us home without delay.</t4293> <t4294>Hide the sad truth from her, although I know it won't be very easy for you.</t4294>
<t4295>Darcy promised to be discreet, expressed his sympathy once again for the misfortune, wished that the matter would not end as badly as could be expected, and, asking him to send his regards to his relatives, took his leave with just a serious look.</t4295>
<t4296>When Darcy left the room, Elizabeth realized how unlikely it was that they would see each other again with the cordiality that had characterized their encounters in Derbyshire.</t4296> <t4297>She recalled the history of her relations with Darcy, so full of contradictions and changes, and appreciated the perversity of the feelings that now made her wish those relations to continue, when before they had made her glad that they had ended.</t4297>
<t4298>If gratitude or esteem are good bases for affection, the transformation of Elizabeth's feelings will not seem improbable or condemnable.</t4298> <t4299>But if it is not so, if the interest that arises from this is less natural and reasonable than that which springs spontaneously, as often described, from the first meeting and before having exchanged two words with the object of said interest, only one thing can be said in Elizabeth's defense: that she tried this system with Wickham and the bad results it gave her perhaps authorized her to lean towards the other method, even if it was less passionate.</t4299> <t4300>Be that as it may, she saw Darcy leave with great sorrow, and this first example of the misfortunes that Lydia's infamy could cause increased the anguish she felt when thinking about that disastrous matter.</t4300>
<t4301>As soon as she read Jane's second letter, she did not believe that Wickham intended to marry Lydia.</t4301> <t4302>No one but Jane could have had that hope.</t4302> <t4303>Surprise was the last of her feelings.</t4303> <t4304>Upon reading the first letter, she was astonished that Wickham would marry a girl who was not a good match and did not understand how Lydia could have attracted him.</t4304> <t4305>But now she saw everything clearly.</t4305> <t4306>Lydia was pretty, and although she did not suppose she had committed to eloping without any intention of marriage, Elizabeth knew that neither her virtue nor her good judgment could preserve her from falling as an easy prey.</t4306>
<t4307>While the regiment was in Hertfordshire, she never noticed that Lydia was attracted to Wickham; but she was convinced that she only needed a little attention to fall in love with anyone.</t4307> <t4308>She liked one officer as soon as another, depending on the attention they paid her.</t4308> <t4309>She had always flitted about, without any fixed object.</t4309> <t4310>How they were now paying for the neglect and indulgence with which they had raised that girl!</t4310>
<t4311>She couldn't wait to be home to see, hear, and be there, and to share with Jane the care that such a troubled family required, with the father absent and the mother incapable of any effort and needing constant attention.</t4311> <t4312>Although she was almost convinced that nothing could be done for Lydia, her uncle's help seemed of utmost importance to her, so until she saw him enter the room, she endured the torment of an impatient wait.</t4312> <t4313>Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner returned hurriedly and alarmed, believing, from what the servant had told them, that their niece had suddenly fallen ill.</t4313> <t4314>Elizabeth reassured them on this point and immediately informed them of the reason for her call by reading them the two letters and insisting on the postscript with trembling energy.</t4314> <t4315>Although Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner had never been very fond of Lydia, the news deeply affected them.</t4315> <t4316>The misfortune affected not only Lydia but everyone.</t4316> <t4317>After the initial exclamations of surprise and horror, Mr. Gardiner offered all the help he could.</t4317> <t4318>Elizabeth expected no less and thanked them with tears in her eyes.</t4318> <t4319>Moved by the same spirit, the three of them quickly arranged everything for the trip.</t4319>
<t4320>––And what shall we do about Pemberley? ––asked Mrs. Gardiner––.</t4320> <t4321>John has told us that Mr. Darcy was here when you sent him to fetch us.</t4321> <t4322>Is it true?</t4322>
<t4323>––Yes; I told him that we were not in a position to fulfill our commitment.</t4323> <t4324>That's already been settled.</t4324> <t4325>––That's already been settled ––repeated Mrs. Gardiner as she ran to the other room to get ready–.</t4325> <t4326>Are they on such close terms as to have revealed the truth to him?</t4326> <t4327>How I would love to find out what has happened!</t4327>
<t4328>But her curiosity was useless.</t4328> <t4329>At most, it served to entertain her in the rush and confusion of the next hour.</t4329> <t4330>If Elizabeth could have stayed idle, she would have believed that someone as miserable as she was incapable of any work, but she was as busy as her aunt and, to top it off, she had to write cards to all the friends in Lambton to explain their sudden departure with false excuses.</t4330> <t4331>Everything was dispatched within an hour.</t4331> <t4332>Meanwhile, Mr. Gardiner settled the inn's bill, and all that was left was to depart.</t4332> <t4333>After the sadness of the morning, Elizabeth found herself, in less time than she had supposed, seated in the carriage and heading towards Longbourn.</t4333>
<t4334>CHAPTER XLII</t4334>
<t4335>I've been thinking about it again, Elizabeth," her uncle said to her as they were leaving the city, "and finally, after serious consideration, I feel inclined to adopt your elder sister's opinion.</t4335> <t4336>It seems unlikely to me that Wickham would want to harm a girl who is not without protection or friends and who was living with the Forster family.</t4336> <t4337>He wouldn't assume that the girl's friends would stand idly by, nor that he would be readmitted to the regiment after such a grave offense to his colonel.</t4337> <t4338>The temptation is not proportional to the risk.</t4338>
<t4339>––Do you really believe that? ––asked Elizabeth, brightening up for a moment.</t4339>
<t4340>––I am also beginning to share your uncle's opinion ––said Mrs. Gardiner––.</t4340> <t4341>It is too great a violation of decency, honor, and self-interest to have acted so lightly.</t4341> <t4342>I cannot admit that Wickham is so foolish.</t4342> <t4343>And you yourself, Elizabeth, do you hold him in such low regard to believe him capable of such madness?</t4343>
<t4344>––I don't believe he's capable of forgetting his own convenience, but he is capable of forgetting everything that doesn't pertain to it.</t4344> <t4345>I wish it were as you say!</t4345> <t4346>I don't dare to hope for it.</t4346> <t4347>And if not, why haven't they gone to Scotland?</t4347>
<t4348>––First of all ––replied Mr. Gardiner––, there is no evidence that they haven't gone.</t4348>
<t4349>––What better proof than having left the post chaise and taken a hired carriage?</t4349> <t4350>Besides, they did not go through the Barnet road.</t4350>
<t4351>––Well, suppose they are in London.</t4351> <t4352>They may have done it only for the purpose of hiding.</t4352> <t4353>It is not likely that either of them has plenty of money, and they might have thought it would be cheaper to get married in London than in Scotland, even if it is more difficult for them.</t4353>
<t4354>––But why the secrecy?</t4354> <t4355>Why do they have to marry in secret?</t4355> <t4356>You know from Jane that Wickham's closest friend assures that he never thought of marrying Lydia.</t4356> <t4357>Wickham will never marry a woman who doesn't have money, because he can't afford the expenses of a marriage.</t4357> <t4358>And what merits does Lydia have, what attractions, aside from her health, her youth, and her good humor, for Wickham to give up the possibility of making a good match for her?</t4358> <t4359>I can't accurately assess to what extent a dishonorable elopement will harm him in the Corps, as I am unaware of the measures taken in such cases, but as for your other objections, it seems difficult for them to hold up.</t4359> <t4360>Lydia has no brothers to intervene; and given my father's character, his indolence, and the little attention he has always paid to his family, Wickham might have believed he wouldn't take it too seriously.</t4360>
<t4361>––But how do you suppose that Lydia is so inconsiderate about everything except loving him, that she would agree to live with him in any other way than as his legitimate wife?</t4361>
<t4362>––It seems so ––replied Elizabeth with her eyes full of tears––, and it is dreadful to have to doubt the decency and virtue of a sister.</t4362> <t4363>But in reality, I don't know what to say.</t4363> <t4364>Perhaps I judge her wrongly, but she is very young, she has never been accustomed to thinking about serious matters, and for the last half year, or rather for an entire year, she has done nothing but chase after diversions and vanity.</t4364> <t4365>She has been allowed to indulge in idleness and frivolity and to do nothing but what she fancied.</t4365> <t4366>Since the county garrison was quartered in Meryton, she has thought of nothing but love, flirting, and the officers.</t4366> <t4367>She did everything she could to excite, how should I put it?, the susceptibility of her feelings, which are already quite lively by nature.</t4367> <t4368>And we all know that Wickham possesses in his person and manners all the charms that can captivate a woman.</t4368>
<t4369>––But you see ––insisted her aunt–– that your sister does not believe Wickham capable of such an offense.</t4369>
<t4370>––Jane never believes anything bad about anyone.</t4370> <t4371>Much less when it comes to something like this, until it has been proven to her.</t4371> <t4372>But Jane knows as well as I do who Wickham is.</t4372> <t4373>We both know that he is a libertine in every sense of the word, lacking integrity and honor, and that he is as false and deceitful as he is attractive.</t4373>
<t4374>––Are you sure? ––asked Mrs. Gardiner, who was burning with the desire to know the source of her niece's information.</t4374>
<t4375>––Absolutely sure ––replied Elizabeth, blushing––.</t4375> <t4376>I already told you the other day about his infamous behavior towards Mr. Darcy, and you yourself heard the last time in Longbourn how he spoke about the man who has treated him with such indulgence and generosity.</t4376> <t4377>And there is yet another circumstance that I am not authorized... that is not worth mentioning.</t4377> <t4378>The truth is that his lies about the Pemberley family are endless.</t4378> <t4379>From what he had told us about Miss Darcy, I believed she would be a haughty, reserved, and unpleasant girl.</t4379> <t4380>However, he knew that she was quite the opposite.</t4380> <t4381>He must know very well, as we have observed, how affectionate and simple she is.</t4381>
<t4382>––And Lydia isn't aware of any of this?</t4382> <t4383>How does she not know what Jane and you know?</t4383>
<t4384>––You are right.</t4384> <t4385>Until I was in Kent and met Mr. Darcy and his cousin Colonel Fitzwilliam, I did not know either.</t4385> <t4386>When I arrived home, the county garrison was set to leave Meryton within three weeks, so neither Jane, whom I informed of everything, nor I thought it necessary to disclose it; because what use would it be to ruin the good opinion they had of him in Hertfordshire?</t4386> <t4387>And when it was decided that Lydia would go with the Forsters to Brighton, it never occurred to me to reveal Wickham's true character to her, as it never crossed my mind that she would be in any danger of that kind.</t4387> <t4388>You can understand that I was far from suspecting that such disastrous consequences would ensue.</t4388>
<t4389>––When they moved the garrison to Brighton, did you have any idea that there was something between them?</t4389>
<t4390>––Not the slightest bit.</t4390> <t4391>I don't remember noticing any sign of affection from either one of them.</t4391> <t4392>If there had been anything, my family is certainly sharp enough to have noticed it!</t4392> <t4393>When Wickham joined the Corps, Lydia liked him a lot, but no more than the rest of us did.</t4393> <t4394>All the girls in Meryton and the surrounding areas went crazy over him during the first two months, but he never paid Lydia any special attention, so after a period of extravagant and unrestrained admiration, she forgot about him and turned her attention to other officers who paid her more attention.</t4394>
<t4395>Although few new things could be added to their fears, hopes, and conjectures about such an interesting matter, the travelers debated it throughout the entire journey.</t4395> <t4396>Elizabeth could not think of anything else.</t4396> <t4397>The sharpest of all agonies, self-reproach, prevented her from finding the slightest interval of relief or forgetfulness.</t4397>
<t4398>They walked as quickly as they could, spent the night at an inn, and arrived at Longbourn the next day at lunchtime.</t4398> <t4399>Elizabeth's only consolation was that she would not have kept Jane waiting too long.</t4399>
<t4400>The little Gardiners, drawn by the sight of a carriage, stood on the steps of the house as it made its way up the driveway.</t4400> <t4401>When the carriage stopped at the door, the joyful surprise that shone on their faces and made them jump around was the prelude to their welcome.</t4401>
<t4402>Elizabeth gave each one a kiss and ran to the hall, where she met Jane who was hurrying down from their mother's room.</t4402>
<t4403>They embraced warmly, with eyes full of tears, and Elizabeth asked without wasting a second if there had been any news of the fugitives.</t4403>
<t4404>––Not yet ––replied Jane––, but now that our dear uncle has arrived, I hope everything goes well.</t4404>
<t4405>––Is Dad in the capital?</t4405>
<t4406>––Yes, he left on Tuesday, as I wrote to you.</t4406>
<t4407>––And what news have you had of him?</t4407>
<t4408>––Few.</t4408> <t4409>On Wednesday, he sent me a few lines saying that he had arrived safely and giving me his address, as I had asked him to.</t4409> <t4410>He only added that he wouldn't write again until he had something important to tell us.</t4410>
<t4411>––And mom, how is she? How are all of you?</t4411>
<t4412>––Mom is fine, as far as I can see, although very downcast.</t4412> <t4413>She is upstairs and will have great satisfaction in seeing you all.</t4413> <t4414>She still hasn't left her room.</t4414> <t4415>Mary and Catherine are perfectly well, thank God.</t4415>
<t4416>––And you, how are you feeling? ––asked Elizabeth––.</t4416> <t4417>You look pale.</t4417> <t4418>You must have gone through so much!</t4418> <t4419>But Jane assured her that she was very well.</t4419> <t4420>Meanwhile, Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, who had been busy with their children, arrived and put an end to the conversation between the two sisters.</t4420> <t4421>Jane ran to her aunt and uncle and welcomed and thanked them with tears and smiles.</t4421>
<t4422>Once gathered in the living room, the questions asked by Elizabeth were repeated by the others, and they saw that poor Jane had no new information.</t4422> <t4423>But her ardent confidence that everything would end well had not abandoned her; she still hoped that one of these mornings a letter from Lydia or her father would arrive explaining the events and perhaps announcing the marriage.</t4423>
<t4424>Mrs. Bennet, to whose room they all went up after their brief conversation, received them as expected: with tears and lamentations, reproaches against Wickham's villainous conduct, and complaints about her own sufferings, blaming everyone except the one who, due to her tolerance and poor judgment, was mainly responsible for her daughter's mistakes.</t4424>
<t4425>––If I had been able ––he said–– to carry out my plan of going to Brighton with my whole family, this would not have happened; but poor Lydia had no one to look after her.</t4425> <t4426>The Forsters should not have lost sight of her.</t4426> <t4427>If they had watched her closely, she would not have done such a thing, Lydia is not that kind of girl.</t4427> <t4428>I always knew that the Forsters were very unsuitable to take care of her, but no one listened to me, as always.</t4428> <t4429>My poor girl!</t4429> <t4430>And now Bennet has gone and I suppose he will challenge Wickham wherever he finds him, and since he will die in the duel, what will become of us?</t4430> <t4431>The Collins will throw us out of here before he is cold in his grave, and if you, my brother, do not assist us, I don't know what we will do.</t4431>
<t4432>Everyone protested against such terrifying ideas.</t4432> <t4433>Mr. Gardiner assured her that they would not lack his support and said that he planned to be in London the next day to help Mr. Bennet with all his efforts to find Lydia.</t4433>
<t4434>––Do not alarm yourselves needlessly ––he added––; although it is good to prepare for the worst, it should not be taken for granted.</t4434> <t4435>It has not even been a week since they left Brighton.</t4435> <t4436>In a few more days we will find out something; and until we know that they are not married and have no intentions of being so, let us not consider the matter lost.</t4436> <t4437>As soon as I arrive in London, I will pick up my brother and take him to Gracechurch Street; together we will deliberate on what needs to be done.</t4437>
<t4438>––Oh, my dear brother! exclaimed Mrs. Bennet––, that is precisely my greatest wish.</t4438> <t4439>When you get to London, find them wherever they are, and if they are not married, make them get married.</t4439> <t4440>Do not allow them to delay the wedding for the bridal gown, tell Lydia that she will have all the money she wants to buy it afterwards.</t4440> <t4441>And above all, prevent Bennet from dueling with Wickham.</t4441> <t4442>Tell him the horrible state I am in: shattered, distraught, with such trembling and agitation, such convulsions in my side, such headaches and such palpitations that I cannot rest either day or night.</t4442> <t4443>And tell my dear Lydia not to order her dresses until she has seen me, for she does not know which are the best stores.</t4443> <t4444>Oh, brother!</t4444> <t4445>How good you are!</t4445> <t4446>I know you will sort everything out.</t4446>
<t4447>Mr. Gardiner repeated to her that he would do everything he could and advised her to moderate her hopes and fears.</t4447> <t4448>He conversed with her in this manner until the meal was on the table, and left her to vent with the housekeeper who assisted her in the absence of her daughters.</t4448>
<t4449>Although his brother and sister-in-law were convinced that there was no reason for him not to come down to eat, they did not dare ask him to sit with them at the table, because they feared his imprudence in front of the servants and thought it preferable that only one of them, the one they could trust the most, should learn of his troubles.</t4449>
<t4450>In the dining room appeared Mary and Catherine, who had been too busy in their rooms to show up earlier.</t4450> <t4451>One had just put down her books and the other her vanity.</t4451> <t4452>But both were very calm and did not seem upset.</t4452> <t4453>Only the latter had a more irate tone than usual, whether due to the loss of the favorite sister or the anger of not finding herself in her place.</t4453> <t4454>Shortly after sitting at the table, Mary, very sure of herself, whispered to Elizabeth with an air of gravity in her reflection:</t4454>
<t4455>It is a very unfortunate matter and will probably be much talked about; but we must overcome the wave of malice and pour the balm of fraternal comfort over our wounded hearts.</t4455>
<t4456>Upon arriving here, he noticed that Elizabeth did not feel like answering, and added:</t4456>
<t4457>––Although it may be a misfortune for Lydia, for us it can be a valuable lesson: the loss of virtue in a woman is irreparable; a single misstep carries within it the final ruin; her reputation is no less fragile than her beauty, and she can never be cautious enough in her behavior towards the indignities of the other sex.</t4457>
<t4458>Elizabeth, astonished, raised her eyes, but she was too distressed to respond.</t4458> <t4459>Mary continued consoling herself with moral lessons of the sort drawn from the misfortune before them.</t4459>
<t4460>In the afternoon, the two eldest Bennet daughters were able to be alone for half an hour, and Elizabeth immediately took the opportunity to ask some questions that Jane was equally eager to answer.</t4460>
<t4461>After lamenting together about the terrible consequences of the event, which Elizabeth took for granted and the other could not assure were impossible, the former said:</t4461>
<t4462>Tell me everything I don't know.</t4462> <t4463>Give me more details.</t4463> <t4464>What did Colonel Forster say? Didn't he have any suspicion about the escape?</t4464> <t4465>They must have always seen them together.</t4465>
<t4466>––Colonel Forster confessed that he once noticed some interest, especially on Lydia's part, but he saw nothing that alarmed him.</t4466> <t4467>I feel sorry for him.</t4467> <t4468>He was most attentive and kind.</t4468> <t4469>He was planning to come see us before knowing they hadn't gone to Scotland, and when it was presumed they were in London, he hastened his trip.</t4469>

<t4506>Affectionate greetings from your friend,</t4506>
<t4507>Lydia Bennet</t4507>
<t4508>––Oh, Lydia, how thoughtless!</t4508> <t4509>How thoughtless! ––exclaimed Elizabeth after finishing reading––.</t4509> <t4510>What a letter to be written at such a moment!</t4510> <t4511>But at least it seems she was serious about the purpose of her trip; we don't know what Wickham might have led her into, but Lydia's intention wasn't so infamous.</t4511> <t4512>Poor father of mine!</t4512> <t4513>How much he must have felt it!</t4513>

<t4523>––You have sacrificed too much for mom; you don't look well.</t4523> <t4524>I wish I had been by your side!</t4524> <t4525>That way you could have taken care of yourself.</t4525>

<t4530>––It would have been better if he had stayed at home ––said Elizabeth––; his intentions may have been good, but in misfortunes like this, one should avoid neighbors.</t4530> <t4531>They cannot help us and their condolences are offensive.</t4531> <t4532>Let them take pleasure in criticizing us from a distance!</t4532>
<t4533>He then asked what measures his father planned to take in the capital in order to find his daughter.</t4533>
<t4534>––I think he intended to go to Epsom ––replied Jane––, which is where they last changed horses; he will talk to the postilions and see what he can find out from them.</t4534> <t4535>His main goal is to discover the number of the rental carriage they left Clapham with; which had come from London with a passenger; and since my father thinks that the fact a gentleman and a lady change carriages might be noticed, he wants to make inquiries in Clapham.</t4535> <t4536>If he could discover the house where the coachman dropped off the traveler, it wouldn't be difficult to find out what type of carriage it was and the number.</t4536> <t4537>I don't know what other plans he had; but he was in such a hurry to leave and was so distraught that this is all I could get out of him.</t4537>
<t4538>CHAPTER XLVIII</t4538>
<t4539>Everyone expected a letter from Mr. Bennet the next morning; but the mail arrived and did not bring a single line from him.</t4539> <t4540>His family knew that he was not very fond of writing, but on this occasion, they believed he could well make an exception.</t4540> <t4541>They were, therefore, forced to assume that there was no good news; but even in that case, they preferred to have certainty.</t4541> <t4542>Mr. Gardiner waited only for the mail to arrive and then left.</t4542>
<t4543>When he left, everyone remained confident that this way, at least they would have constant information about what was happening.</t4543> <t4544>Mr. Gardiner promised them he would persuade Mr. Bennet to return to Longbourn as soon as possible for the comfort of his wife, who considered his return as the only guarantee that he would not die in the duel.</t4544>
<t4545>Mrs. Gardiner and her children would remain in Hertfordshire a few more days, as she believed her presence would be useful to her nieces.</t4545> <t4546>She helped them take care of Mrs. Bennet and provided great relief during their free hours.</t4546> <t4547>Their other aunt visited them often with the intention, as she said, of encouraging them; but since she always recounted some new example of Wickham's extravagance and lack of scruples, she rarely left without making them even more disheartened.</t4547>
<t4548>The entire town of Meryton was bent on discrediting the man who only three months earlier had been regarded as an angel of light.</t4548> <t4549>It was said that he owed money to every shop in the city, and his intrigues, honored with the name of seductions, extended to all the families of the merchants.</t4549> <t4550>Everyone claimed that he was the most wicked young man in the world, and they began to say that they had always distrusted his apparent goodness.</t4550> <t4551>Elizabeth, despite not believing even half of what was being whispered, believed enough to reinforce her prior belief in her sister's ruin, and even Jane began to lose hope, especially when the time came that, had they gone to Scotland, news of them would have already been received.</t4551>
<t4552>Mr. Gardiner left Longbourn on Sunday and on Tuesday his wife received a letter from him.</t4552> <t4553>He told her that upon his arrival he had immediately gone in search of his brother-in-law and had taken him to Gracechurch Street; that Mr. Bennet had been to Epsom and Clapham, but without any results, and that he now wanted to inquire at all the main inns in the city, as he believed it possible that they might have lodged in one of them upon their arrival in London, before securing other accommodations.</t4553> <t4554>Mr. Gardiner thought this attempt was futile, but since his brother-in-law was determined to carry it out, he would help him.</t4554> <t4555>He added that Mr. Bennet refused to leave London, and promised to write soon.</t4555> <t4556>In a postscript, he said the following:</t4556>
<t4557>I have written to Colonel Forster, begging him to find out from the friends of the regiment if Wickham has any relatives or connections who might know where in the city he will be hiding.</t4557> <t4558>If there were anyone to whom we could turn with any probability of obtaining that clue, it would advance us greatly.</t4558> <t4559>For now, there is nothing to guide us.</t4559> <t4560>I do not doubt that Colonel Forster will do everything in his power to please us, but perhaps Elizabeth can tell us better than anyone if Wickham has any relatives.</t4560>
<t4561>Elizabeth understood the reason for this allusion, but she could not respond to it.</t4561> <t4562>She had never heard if she had any relatives apart from her father and mother, who had died many years ago.</t4562> <t4563>But it was possible that one of her companions might be able to provide better information, and although she was not optimistic, she considered it wise to ask.</t4563>
<t4564>In Longbourn, the days passed with great anxiety, anxiety that grew with the arrival of the mail.</t4564> <t4565>Every morning they awaited the letters with impatience.</t4565> <t4566>By letter, they would learn of the good or bad progress of the matter, and each day they believed they would receive some important news.</t4566>
<t4567>But before they heard again from Mr. Gardiner, a letter arrived from Hunsford for Mr. Bennet from his cousin Collins.</t4567> <t4568>Since Jane had been instructed to read everything received in her father's absence, she opened the letter.</t4568> <t4569>Elizabeth, who knew what Collins's letters were like, also read over her sister's shoulder.</t4569> <t4570>It read as follows:</t4570>
<t4571>"My dear sir,</t4571>
<t4572>Our kinship and my situation in life compel me to offer you my condolences for the grave affliction you are enduring, of which we were informed by a letter from Hertfordshire.</t4572> <t4573>Do not doubt that both Mrs. Collins and I share in the sorrow with you and your respectable family in this present calamity, which must be very bitter, as time cannot erase it.</t4573> <t4574>There will be no lack of arguments on my part to alleviate such a tremendous misfortune or to serve as consolation in circumstances that must be more painful for a father than for anyone else.</t4574> <t4575>The death of a daughter would have been a blessing compared to this.</t4575> <t4576>And it is more lamentable because there are reasons to suppose, according to my dear Charlotte, that this licentious behavior of your daughter stems from a deplorable excess of indulgence; although at the same time, for your and your wife's consolation, I am inclined to think that it must have been due to a perverse nature, for otherwise she would not have committed such an atrocity at such a young age.</t4576> <t4577>In any case, you are deserving of compassion, an opinion shared not only by Mrs. Collins but also by Lady Catherine and her daughter, to whom I have related the matter.</t4577> <t4578>They agree with me that this misstep by your daughter will be detrimental to the fortunes of the others; because, as Lady Catherine herself kindly says, who will want to ally themselves with such a family?</t4578> <t4579>This consideration moves me to recall with the greatest satisfaction a certain event from last November, for had things not gone as they did, I would now be involved in all your sadness and misfortune.</t4579> <t4580>Allow me, then, to advise you, dear sir, to resign yourself as much as possible and to remove your unworthy daughter from your heart forever, and let her reap the fruits of her abominable offense.</t4580>
<t4581>Mr. Gardiner did not write again until he had received a reply from Colonel Forster, but he could not say anything good.</t4581> <t4582>It was not known that Wickham had any relationship with any relatives, and it was assured that he had none close.</t4582> <t4583>He had formerly had many friends, but since his entry into the army, he seemed to have distanced himself from everyone.</t4583> <t4584>There was, therefore, no one capable of providing news of his whereabouts.</t4584> <t4585>There was a powerful reason for him to hide, which added to the fear of being discovered by Lydia's family, and it was that he had left behind a large amount of gambling debts.</t4585> <t4586>Colonel Forster believed that more than a thousand pounds would be necessary to clear his accounts in Brighton.</t4586> <t4587>He owed much in the city, but his debts of honor were even higher.</t4587> <t4588>Mr. Gardiner did not dare to hide these details from the Longbourn family.</t4588> <t4589>Jane was horrified:</t4589>
<t4590>––A player!</t4590> <t4591>I didn't expect that.</t4591> <t4592>I couldn't imagine it!</t4592>
<t4593>Mr. Gardiner added in his letter that Mr. Bennet was going to return to Longbourn the next day, which was Saturday.</t4593> <t4594>Discouraged by the failure of his inquiries, he had yielded to his brother-in-law's insistence to return home and let him handle things while the circumstances were not more favorable for joint action.</t4594> <t4595>When they told Mrs. Bennet, she did not show the satisfaction that her daughters expected given her concerns for her husband's life.</t4595>

<t4599>Since Mrs. Gardiner was already eager to be at home, it was agreed that she would go to London with the children, taking advantage of Mr. Bennet's return.</t4599> <t4600>Consequently, the Longbourn carriage took them to the first stage of their journey and brought Mr. Bennet back.</t4600>
<t4601>Mrs. Gardiner left still perplexed at the thought of the chance encounter between Elizabeth and her friend from Derbyshire in that place.</t4601> <t4602>Elizabeth had refrained from mentioning his name, and that sort of semi-hope that her aunt had nurtured of receiving a letter from him upon arriving at Longbourn had come to nothing.</t4602> <t4603>Since their arrival, Elizabeth had not received any letter from Pemberley.</t4603>
<t4604>The unfortunate state of the entire family made any other excuse unnecessary to explain Elizabeth's dejection; nothing, therefore, could be conjectured about it, although Elizabeth, who by then knew where she stood regarding her feelings, was aware that, if not for Darcy, she would have better endured her fears about Lydia's disgrace.</t4604> <t4605>She would have spared herself one or two sleepless nights.</t4605>
<t4606>Mr. Bennet arrived with his usual philosophical demeanor.</t4606> <t4607>He spoke little, as always; he said nothing about the reason that had prompted his return, and some time passed before his daughters had the courage to speak on the subject.</t4607>
<t4608>In the afternoon, when she met with them at tea time, Elizabeth ventured to touch on the matter; she briefly expressed her sorrow for what her father must have suffered, and he replied:</t4608>
<t4609>––Let it be.</t4609> <t4610>Who else would suffer but me?</t4610> <t4611>It was my fault and it's only right that I pay for it.</t4611>
