<t4620>––Then he must be happy ––said his father coldly–– and he won't leave there for a long time.</t4620> <t4621>After a short silence, he continued:</t4621>
<t4622>Lizzy, don't hold a grudge against me for not having followed your advice last May; what happened shows that you were right.</t4622>
<t4623>At that moment, they were interrupted by Jane, who came to get the tea for her mother.</t4623>
<t4624>––"Look how nice!" exclaimed Mr. Bennet.</t4624> <t4625>"That lends a certain elegance to misfortune!"</t4625> <t4626>"Another day I will do the same: I will stay in the library with my nightcap and dressing gown and give you all the trouble I can, or perhaps I will leave it for when Catherine runs away..."</t4626>
<t4627>––I'm not going to run away, Dad! ––shouted Catherine furiously––.</t4627> <t4628>If I had gone to Brighton, I would have behaved better than Lydia.</t4628>

<t4635>Catherine took all these threats seriously and began to cry.</t4635>
<t4636>––Well, well ––said Mr. Bennet––, don't get upset.</t4636> <t4637>If you are a good girl for the next ten years, as soon as they pass, I will take you to see a parade.</t4637>
<t4638>CHAPTER XLIX</t4638>
<t4639>Two days after Mr. Bennet's return, while Jane and Elizabeth were walking together through the shrubbery behind the house, they saw the housekeeper coming towards them.</t4639> <t4640>They thought she was coming to call them on behalf of their mother and ran to meet her; but the woman said to Jane: Excuse me for interrupting you, miss; but I assumed you might have some good news from the capital and therefore took the liberty of coming to ask you about it.</t4640>
<t4641>––What do you say, Hill?</t4641> <t4642>I haven't heard anything.</t4642>
<t4643>––Dear miss! ––exclaimed Mrs. Hill with great astonishment––.</t4643> <t4644>Do you not know that a messenger has arrived for the master, sent by Mr. Gardiner?</t4644> <t4645>He has been here for half an hour and the master has received a letter.</t4645>
<t4646>The two girls rushed towards the house, too anxious to continue their conversation.</t4646> <t4647>They went from the hall to the dining room and from there to the library, but their father was not in any of those places; they were about to check if he was upstairs with their mother when they ran into the butler who told them:</t4647>
<t4648>––If you are looking for my master, ladies, you will find him strolling through the grove.</t4648>

<t4650>Jane, who was neither as light nor had the habit of running like Elizabeth, fell behind, while her sister arrived breathlessly to their father and exclaimed:</t4650>
<t4651>––What news is there, Dad?</t4651> <t4652>What news is there? Have you heard anything about my uncle?</t4652>
<t4653>––Yes, he has sent me a letter by a messenger.</t4653>
<t4654>––And what news do you bring, good or bad?</t4654>
<t4655>––What good can be expected? ––said the father, taking the letter out of his pocket––.</t4655> <t4656>Here, read it if you want.</t4656>

<t4660>Gracechurch Street, Monday, August 2.</t4660>
<t4661>My dear brother,</t4661>
<t4662>At last, I can send you news about my niece, and such news, overall, that I hope will satisfy you.</t4662> <t4663>Shortly after you left on Saturday, I was fortunate enough to find out where in London they were.</t4663> <t4664>I will reserve the details for when we see each other; suffice it to know that they have been discovered; I have seen both of them.</t4664>

<t4667>Elizabeth continued reading:</t4667>

<t4680>Yours,</t4680>
<t4681>E. Gardiner.</t4681>
<t4682>––Is it possible? ––exclaimed Elizabeth upon finishing the letter––.</t4682> <t4683>Could it be possible that he will marry her?</t4683>

<t4738>––As you wish.</t4738>

<t4785>Mrs. Hill expressed her satisfaction and gave her congratulations to everyone.</t4785> <t4786>Elizabeth, dizzy from all the madness, took refuge in her room to freely indulge her thoughts.</t4786>
<t4787>The situation of poor Lydia was to be, even in the best case, quite bad; but that was not the worst; she had to be grateful, for although looking to the future her sister could not expect any reasonable happiness or any prosperity in the world, looking back, to what only two hours earlier Elizabeth had feared so much, it could not be denied that she had still been lucky.</t4787>
<t4788>CHAPTER L</t4788>
<t4789>Previously, Mr. Bennet had often wanted to save a certain annual amount to improve the fortune of his daughters and his wife, if she survived him, instead of spending all his income.</t4789> <t4790>And now he regretted not having done so.</t4790> <t4791>This would have prevented Lydia from indebting herself to her uncle for everything he now had to do for her, both in terms of honor and money.</t4791> <t4792>Moreover, he could have had the pleasure of tempting any of the brightest young men in Great Britain to marry her.</t4792>
<t4793>He was seriously dismayed that for a matter which offered so few advantages for anyone, his brother-in-law had to make so many sacrifices, and he wanted to find out the amount of his donation in order to repay him when possible.</t4793>
<t4794>In the early days of Mr. Bennet's marriage, it was considered that there was no need for economy, as it was assumed that a male heir would be born and that he would inherit the estate upon reaching the appropriate age, thus ensuring the widow and daughters would be provided for.</t4794> <t4795>But five daughters were born in succession, and the male heir did not appear.</t4795> <t4796>Years after Lydia's birth, Mrs. Bennet still believed that the heir would come, but eventually, she gave up hope.</t4796> <t4797>Now it was too late to save: Mrs. Bennet had no aptitude for economy, and her husband's love of independence was the only thing that prevented them from overspending.</t4797>
<t4798>In the marriage settlements, five thousand pounds were secured for Mrs. Bennet and her daughters; but the distribution depended on the will of the parents.</t4798> <t4799>Finally, this point was to be decided concerning Lydia, and Mr. Bennet did not hesitate to agree to the proposal.</t4799> <t4800>In terms of gratitude for his brother-in-law's kindness, though expressed very concisely, he entrusted to paper his approval of everything done and his desire to fulfill the commitments made in his name.</t4800> <t4801>He would never have believed that Wickham would consent to marry Lydia at the cost of so few inconveniences as those resulting from that arrangement.</t4801> <t4802>Ten pounds annually was the most he was going to lose by giving the hundred he had to give them, for between the fixed ordinary expenses, the loose money he gave Lydia, and the continuous cash gifts her mother made to her, little less than that amount was spent on Lydia.</t4802>
<t4803>Another thing that pleasantly surprised him was that everything was done with such insignificant trouble for him, as his main desire was always to be left alone.</t4803> <t4804>After the initial burst of anger that motivated him to seek out his daughter, he returned, as expected, to his usual indolence.</t4804> <t4805>He quickly dispatched the letter, though he took a long time to start things, he was quick to execute them.</t4805> <t4806>In the letter, he asked for more details about what he owed his brother-in-law, but he was too resentful towards Lydia to send her any message.</t4806>
<t4807>The good news spread quickly throughout the house and, with proportional promptness, through the neighborhood.</t4807> <t4808>It is true that it would have caused more talk if Lydia Bennet had come to town, and it would have been even better if they had confined her to some distant farm; but there was already enough to gossip about regarding her marriage, and the well-intentioned wishes for her happiness that the spiteful old women of Meryton had previously expressed lost only a little of their liveliness in this change of circumstances, for with such a husband Lydia's misfortune was considered certain.</t4808>
<t4809>It had been fifteen days since Mrs. Bennet had come down from her rooms, but in order to celebrate such joyous events, she once again took her radiant place at the head of the table.</t4809> <t4810>In her triumph, there was not the slightest feeling of shame.</t4810> <t4811>The marriage of a daughter, which had been her main desire since Jane turned sixteen, was now about to take place.</t4811> <t4812>She thought and spoke of nothing but elegant weddings, fine muslins, new servants, and new carriages.</t4812> <t4813>She was very busy looking for a suitable house for the couple in the neighborhood, and without knowing or considering what their income would be, she rejected many for lack of space or luxury.</t4813>
<t4814>––Haye Park ––he said–– would do very well if the Gouldings would let it; or the house at Stoke, if the drawing-room were larger; but Asworth is too far away!</t4814> <t4815>I could not bear to have her live ten miles away.</t4815> <t4816>As for Purvis Lodge, the attics are dreadful.</t4816>
<t4817>Her husband let her speak without interrupting her while the servants were present.</t4817> <t4818>But when they left, he said to her:</t4818>
<t4819>––Mrs. Bennet, before taking any of those houses or all of them for your daughter, let's make things clear.</t4819> <t4820>There is a house in this neighborhood where they will never be admitted.</t4820> <t4821>I will not encourage the impudence of either of them by receiving them at Longbourn.</t4821>
<t4822>This declaration was followed by a long dispute, but Mr. Bennet remained firm.</t4822> <t4823>The discussion moved from this point to another, and Mrs. Bennet watched with astonishment and horror as her husband refused to advance even a guinea to buy their daughter’s wedding dress.</t4823> <t4824>He assured that she would not receive any proof of affection from him regarding that matter.</t4824> <t4825>Mrs. Bennet could not comprehend it; it was beyond the possibilities of her imagination that her husband's resentment could reach the point of denying their daughter a privilege without which her marriage would scarcely seem valid.</t4825> <t4826>She was more sensitive to the misfortune of her daughter not having a wedding dress to wear than to the shame of her having eloped and lived with Wickham for fifteen days before the wedding took place.</t4826>
<t4827>Elizabeth regretted more than ever having informed Darcy, driven by the pain of the moment, of her sister's action, for since the wedding was going to cover the scandal of the elopement, it was to be assumed that the ungrateful preliminaries would be hidden from all those who could be unaware of them.</t4827>
<t4828>He did not fear Darcy's indiscretion; few people inspired more confidence in him than Darcy; but it mortified him that he knew his sister's weakness.</t4828> <t4829>And not out of fear that it would cause her any harm, because the chasm that seemed to separate them was insurmountable anyway.</t4829> <t4830>Even if Lydia's marriage had been arranged in the most honorable way, it could not be supposed that Darcy would want to ally himself with a family that, to all its other drawbacks, would now add the most intimate connection with the man whom Darcy so justly despised.</t4830>
<t4831>In the face of such a thing, it was natural for Darcy to retreat.</t4831> <t4832>The desire to win Elizabeth's affection, which she had sensed in him in Derbyshire, could not survive such a blow.</t4832> <t4833>Elizabeth felt humiliated, saddened, and filled with vague remorse.</t4833> <t4834>She longed for his affection when she could no longer hope to obtain it.</t4834> <t4835>She wanted to hear from him when there was not the slightest chance of receiving news from him.</t4835> <t4836>She was convinced that she could have been happy with him, when it was likely that they would not see each other again.</t4836>
<t4837>"What a triumph for him," she thought, "if he knew that the proposals I so proudly rejected just four months ago, I would now gladly accept."</t4837>
<t4838>He did not doubt that he was as generous as anyone, but as long as he lived, that had to be a triumph for him.</t4838>
<t4839>Then she began to understand that Darcy was exactly, by his way of being and his talent, the man who would have suited her best.</t4839> <t4840>Darcy's understanding and character, although not similar to hers, would have fulfilled all her desires.</t4840> <t4841>Their union would have been advantageous for both: with her ease and liveliness, his temperament would have softened and his manners would have improved.</t4841> <t4842>And the judgment, culture, and knowledge of the world that he possessed would have brought her significant benefits.</t4842>
<t4843>But that ideal marriage could no longer teach the admiring crowds what conjugal happiness was; the union that was about to take place in Elizabeth's family was very different and excluded the possibility of the former.</t4843>
<t4844>They could not imagine how Wickham and Lydia would manage to live with a passable independence; but it was not difficult to conjecture how unstable the happiness of a couple united solely because their passions were stronger than their virtue would be.</t4844>
<t4845>Mr. Gardiner soon wrote back to his brother-in-law.</t4845> <t4846>He briefly responded to Mr. Bennet's gratitude by saying that his greatest wish was to contribute to the well-being of the entire family and concluded by asking that the matter not be discussed any further.</t4846> <t4847>The main purpose of the letter was to inform him that Wickham had decided to leave the regiment.</t4847>
<t4848>He was very eager for him to do it ––he added when we finalized the marriage; and I believe you will agree with me that his departure from that Corps is highly beneficial both for him and for my niece.</t4848> <t4849>Mr. Wickham's intention is to join the regular Army, and among his old friends, there are those who can and want to help him achieve it.</t4849> <t4850>He has been promised the rank of ensign in General X's regiment, currently stationed in the North.</t4850> <t4851>It is much better for him to move away from this part of the kingdom.</t4851> <t4852>He firmly promises, and I hope it will be so, that being among other people before whom they should not discredit themselves, the two will be more prudent.</t4852> <t4853>I have written to Colonel Forster informing him of our arrangements and requesting that he tell Mr. Wickham's various creditors in Brighton and its surroundings that they will be paid immediately under my responsibility.</t4853> <t4854>Would you mind taking the trouble to give the same assurances to the creditors in Meryton, of whom I am sending you a list according to what Mr. Wickham has indicated to me?</t4854> <t4855>He has confessed all his debts to us and I hope that at least in this he has not deceived us.</t4855> <t4856>Haggerston already has instructions and within a week everything will be ready.</t4856> <t4857>Then Mr. Wickham will join his regiment, unless he is first invited to go to Longbourn, for my wife tells me that Lydia is very eager to see you all before leaving the South.</t4857> <t4858>She is very well and humbly asks you and her mother to remember her.</t4858>
<t4859>Yours,</t4859>
<t4860>E. Gardiner.</t4860>
<t4861>Mr. Bennet and his daughters understood the advantages of Wickham leaving the county garrison as clearly as Mr. Gardiner; but Mrs. Bennet was not as satisfied as they were.</t4861> <t4862>She was very displeased that Lydia was to settle in the North precisely when she had been looking forward with pleasure and pride to enjoying her company, for she had not given up her illusion that Lydia would reside in Hertfordshire.</t4862> <t4863>And besides, it was a pity that Lydia would be separated from a regiment where everyone knew her and where she had so many admirers.</t4863>
<t4864>––She is so fond of Mrs. Forster that it will be very hard for her to leave her.</t4864> <t4865>And, besides, there are several boys she likes.</t4865> <t4866>It may be that the officers of General X's regiment are not as nice.</t4866>
<t4867>The plea ––for such it had to be considered from his daughter to be readmitted into the family before departing for the North was at first roundly denied; but Jane and Elizabeth, for the sake of their sister's feelings and future, wished that she would notify her parents of her marriage in person, and insisted with such interest, gentleness, and sweetness that Mr. Bennet agreed to receive her and her husband at Longbourn after the wedding, that they convinced him.</t4867> <t4868>Thus, Mrs. Bennet had the satisfaction of knowing that they could present their married daughter to the neighborhood before she was banished to the North.</t4868> <t4869>Consequently, when Mr. Bennet wrote back to his brother-in-law, he gave permission for the couple to come, and it was determined that after the ceremony they would leave for Longbourn.</t4869> <t4870>Elizabeth complained that Wickham accepted this plan, and if she had been guided only by her own desires, Wickham would be the last person she would want to meet.</t4870>
<t4871>CHAPTER LI</t4871>
<t4872>The day of Lydia's wedding arrived, and Jane and Elizabeth were probably more interested in it than she was herself.</t4872> <t4873>The carriage was sent to fetch them from X, and it would return with them at lunchtime.</t4873> <t4874>Jane and Elizabeth dreaded their arrival, especially Jane, who attributed to Lydia the same feelings that would have overwhelmed her if she had been the guilty one, and she tormented herself thinking about what Lydia must be suffering.</t4874>
<t4875>They arrived.</t4875> <t4876>The family was gathered in the sitting room waiting for them.</t4876> <t4877>A smile adorned Mrs. Bennet's face when the carriage stopped in front of the door; her husband was impenetrably serious, and her daughters were alarmed, anxious, and restless.</t4877>
<t4878>The voice of Lydia was heard in the hall; the door opened and the newlywed entered the room.</t4878> <t4879>Her mother stood up, hugged her, and enthusiastically welcomed her, extending her hand to Wickham who followed his wife, wishing them both the greatest happiness, with a promptness that demonstrated her conviction that they would undoubtedly be happy.</t4879>
<t4880>The reception of Mr. Bennet, to whom they then turned, was no longer so cordial.</t4880> <t4881>He reaffirmed his seriousness and barely opened his lips.</t4881> <t4882>The tranquility of the young couple was really enough to provoke him.</t4882> <t4883>Elizabeth was embarrassed and even Jane was scandalized.</t4883> <t4884>Lydia remained Lydia: untamed, shameless, foolish, loud, and daring.</t4884> <t4885>She went from sister to sister asking them to congratulate her, and when they all finally sat down, she looked eagerly around the room, noticing that there had been a small change, and, bursting into laughter, said that it had been a long time since she had been there.</t4885>
<t4886>Wickham did not seem less pleased than she was; but his manners remained so pleasant that if his behavior and his marriage had been as they should, his smiles and his easy gestures in claiming the recognition of his kinship from his sisters-in-law would have charmed them all.</t4886> <t4887>Elizabeth never believed he was capable of such impudence, but she sat determined not to set limits henceforth to the shamelessness of a shameless person.</t4887> <t4888>Both Jane and she were blushing, but the cheeks of those who caused their embarrassment remained unchanged.</t4888>
<t4889>The conversation was not lacking.</t4889> <t4890>The bride and the mother talked without pause, and Wickham, who sat next to Elizabeth, began to ask about their acquaintances in the neighborhood with a cheerfulness and good humor that she could not have matched in her responses.</t4890> <t4891>Both Lydia and Wickham seemed to have wonderful memories.</t4891> <t4892>They remembered everything from the past without any regret, and she willingly spoke of things that her sisters would not have mentioned for anything in the world.</t4892>
<t4893>––It's already been three months since I left! ––she exclaimed––.</t4893> <t4894>And it seems like it was only fifteen days ago!</t4894> <t4895>And yet, how many things have happened!</t4895> <t4896>My God!</t4896> <t4897>When I left, I had no idea that when I returned I would be married; although I thought it would be hilarious if that were the case.</t4897>
<t4898>Her father raised his eyes; Jane was distressed; Elizabeth looked at Lydia significantly, but she, who never saw or heard what did not interest her, continued cheerfully:</t4898>
<t4899>––Mom, do the people around here know that I got married?</t4899> <t4900>I was afraid they didn't, and that's why, when we passed William Goulding's carriage, I wanted him to find out; I lowered the window on his side, took off my glove, and rested my hand on the window frame so he could see the ring.</t4900> <t4901>Then I greeted him and smiled as if nothing had happened.</t4901>
<t4902>Elizabeth couldn't take it anymore.</t4902> <t4903>She got up and went to her room and didn't come down until she heard them passing through the hall towards the dining room.</t4903> <t4904>She arrived in time to see Lydia, strutting, taking her place at the table on their mother's right side and saying to her older sister:</t4904>
<t4905>––Jane, now it is my turn to take your place.</t4905> <t4906>You move to second place, because I am a married woman.</t4906>
<t4907>It was not to be supposed that time would give Lydia that composure which she had always lacked.</t4907> <t4908>Her peace of mind and her ease were increasing.</t4908> <t4909>She was eager to see Mrs. Philips, the Lucases, and all the other neighbors, to hear them call her "Mrs. Wickham."</t4909> <t4910>Meanwhile, after lunch, she went to show her wedding ring to Mrs. Hill and the two maids to boast about being married.</t4910>
<t4911>––Well, Mom ––she said when everyone returned to the sitting room––, what do you think of my husband?</t4911> <t4912>Isn't he charming?</t4912> <t4913>I'm sure all my sisters envy me; I only wish they have half the luck I do.</t4913> <t4914>They should go to Brighton; it's an ideal place to find a husband.</t4914> <t4915>What a pity we all didn't go!</t4915>
<t4916>––It's true.</t4916> <t4917>If I were in charge, we would have gone.</t4917> <t4918>Lydia, my dear, I don't like it at all that you are going so far away.</t4918> <t4919>Does it have to be this way?</t4919>
<t4920>––Oh, Lord!</t4920> <t4921>Yes, there is no other choice.</t4921> <t4922>But I will like it a lot.</t4922> <t4923>You, dad, and my sisters have to come see us.</t4923> <t4924>We will be in Newcastle all winter, and there will surely be some dances; I will try to get good partners for everyone.</t4924>
<t4925>––That's what I would like the most! ––sighed her mother.</t4925>
<t4926>––And when you return, let one or two of my sisters stay with us, and I am sure I will have found them a husband before the winter is over:</t4926>
<t4927>––I appreciate the intention ––replied Elizabeth––, but I don't quite like, to say the least, your way of getting a husband.</t4927>
<t4928>The guests were going to stay at Longbourn for only ten days.</t4928> <t4929>Wickham had received his assignment before leaving London and had to join his regiment within a fortnight.</t4929>
<t4930>Nobody, except Mrs. Bennet, felt that their stay was too short.</t4930> <t4931>Most of the time was spent making visits accompanied by her daughter and organizing parties at the house.</t4931> <t4932>The parties were pleasant for everyone; avoiding the family circle was even more desirable for those who thought than for those who did not think.</t4932>
<t4933>Wickham's affection for Lydia was exactly as Elizabeth had imagined it, and very different from Lydia's affection for him.</t4933> <t4934>Elizabeth needed only to observe her sister a little to realize that the elopement had been driven more by Lydia's love for him than by his love for her.</t4934> <t4935>She would have been surprised that Wickham had eloped with a woman towards whom he felt no special attraction, if she had not been certain that his dire situation had forced that action upon him, and he was not the kind of man, in such a case, to pass up the opportunity of having a companion.</t4935>
<t4936>Lydia was crazy about him; her "dear Wickham" was always on her lips, he was the most perfect man in the world and everything he did was well done.</t4936> <t4937>She assured that at the beginning of September Wickham would kill more birds than anyone in the region.</t4937>
<t4938>One morning, shortly after her arrival, while she was sitting with her older sisters, Lydia said to Elizabeth:</t4938>
<t4939>––I don't think I've told you yet how my wedding was.</t4939> <t4940>You weren't there when I explained it to mom and the others.</t4940> <t4941>Aren't you interested in knowing?</t4941>
<t4942>––Really, no ––replied Elizabeth––; you shouldn't talk much about that matter.</t4942>
<t4943>––Oh, how strange you are!</t4943> <t4944>But I want to tell you about it.</t4944> <t4945>You already know that we got married in San Clemente because Wickham's lodging belonged to that parish.</t4945> <t4946>We had agreed to all be there at eleven.</t4946> <t4947>My uncles and I had to go together and meet the others at the church.</t4947> <t4948>Well, Monday morning arrived and I was beside myself.</t4948> <t4949>You know?</t4949> <t4950>I was so afraid that something would happen to ruin everything, I would have gone crazy!</t4950> <t4951>While I was getting dressed, my aunt kept preaching to me non-stop as if she were reading me a sermon.</t4951> <t4952>But I didn't hear even a tenth of her words because, as you can imagine, I was thinking about my dear Wickham, and whether he would wear his blue suit for the wedding.</t4952>
<t4953>"Well, we had breakfast at ten, as usual."</t4953> <t4954>"I thought it would never end, because you should know that my uncles were extremely annoying with me the whole time I spent with them."</t4954> <t4955>"Believe me, I didn't set foot outside the house in fifteen days; no parties, no excursions, nothing!"</t4955> <t4956>"The truth is that London wasn't very lively; but the Little Theatre was open."</t4956> <t4957>"As soon as the car arrived at the door, my uncle had to attend to that horrible Mr. Stone for some matter."</t4957> <t4958>"And you know that as soon as they meet, it takes a long time."</t4958> <t4959>"Well, I was so scared that I didn't know what to do, because my uncle was going to be the best man, and if we arrived late, we wouldn't be able to get married that day."</t4959> <t4960>"But fortunately, my uncle was ready in two minutes and we left for the church."</t4960> <t4961>"But then I remembered that if Uncle Gardiner hadn't been able to go to the wedding, it wouldn't have been canceled anyway, because Mr. Darcy could have taken his place."</t4961>
<t4962>Mr. Darcy! ––repeated Elizabeth in complete astonishment.</t4962>
<t4963>Of course!</t4963> <t4964>He was accompanying Wickham, you know.</t4964> <t4965>But alas, I had forgotten!</t4965> <t4966>I shouldn't have said it.</t4966> <t4967>I promised faithfully.</t4967> <t4968>What will Wickham say? It was a secret!</t4968>
<t4969>––If it was a secret ––said Jane–– don't say another word.</t4969> <t4970>I don't want to know.</t4970>
<t4971>––Naturally ––added Elizabeth, even though she was dying of curiosity––, we won't ask you anything.</t4971>
<t4972>––Thank you ––said Lydia––, because if you asked me, I would tell you everything and Wickham would get angry.</t4972>
<t4973>With such an incentive to coax him, Elizabeth refrained from doing so and to avoid the temptation, she left.</t4973>
<t4974>But ignoring that was impossible or, at the very least, it was impossible not to try to find out.</t4974> <t4975>Darcy had attended Lydia's wedding.</t4975> <t4976>Both the event and its protagonists seemed precisely the least likely for Darcy to get involved with them.</t4976> <t4977>Quick and confused conjectures about what that meant crossed her mind, but none seemed acceptable to her.</t4977> <t4978>The ones that pleased her the most, because they exalted Darcy, were apparently improbable.</t4978> <t4979>She could not bear such uncertainty, so she hurried and grabbed a sheet of paper to write a brief letter to her aunt asking her to clarify what Lydia had let slip, if it was compatible with the secrecy of the matter.</t4979>
<t4980>"You will understand," he added, "that I need to know why a person who has nothing to do with us and who, properly speaking, is a stranger to our family, was with you at that moment."</t4980> <t4981>"I beg you to reply by return mail and explain it to me, unless there are powerful reasons that impose the secrecy Lydia mentions, in which case I will have to try to resign myself to ignorance."</t4981>
<t4982>“But I will not do it,” she said to herself upon finishing the letter; “and dear aunt, if you do not tell me, I will be forced to resort to tricks and stratagems to find out.”</t4982>
<t4983>Jane's delicate sense of honor prevented her from speaking alone with Elizabeth about what had slipped out to Lydia.</t4983> <t4984>Elizabeth was glad, although in this way, if her inquiries were successful, she would not be able to have a confidant.</t4984>
<t4985>CHAPTER LII</t4985>
<t4986>Elizabeth had the satisfaction of receiving an immediate response to her letter.</t4986> <t4987>She ran with it to the grove, where there was less chance of being disturbed, sat on a bench, and prepared to be happy, as the length of the letter convinced her that it did not contain a refusal.</t4987>
<t4988>Gracechurch Street, September 8.</t4988>
<t4989>My dear niece: I have just received your letter and I am going to dedicate the whole morning to answering it, as I believe that in a few words I will not be able to tell you all that I have to share with you.</t4989> <t4990>I must confess that your question surprised me, as I did not expect it from you.</t4990> <t4991>Do not be upset, I only wish you to know that I did not think such clarifications were necessary on your part.</t4991> <t4992>If you do not want to understand me, forgive my impertinence.</t4992> <t4993>Your uncle is as surprised as I am, and only because of the belief that you are an interested party has he allowed himself to act as he has.</t4993> <t4994>But in case you are indeed innocent and know nothing at all, I will have to be more explicit.</t4994>
<t4995>The same day I arrived from Longbourn, your uncle had a very unexpected visit.</t4995> <t4996>Mr. Darcy came and was locked up with him for several hours.</t4996> <t4997>When I returned, everything was already settled; so my curiosity did not suffer as much as yours.</t4997> <t4998>Darcy came to tell Gardiner that he had discovered the hiding place of Wickham and your sister, and that he had seen and spoken to both of them: to Wickham several times, to your sister only once.</t4998> <t4999>From what I can deduce, Darcy left Derbyshire the day after we did and came to London with the idea of finding them.</t4999> <t5000>The reason he gave is that he felt guilty for not having made Wickham's infamy sufficiently known to prevent a decent girl from loving or trusting him.</t5000> <t5001>He generously attributed it all to his blind pride, saying that he had previously deemed it unworthy of him to publish his private affairs.</t5001> <t5002>His conduct would speak for him.</t5002> <t5003>Therefore, he believed it his duty to intervene and remedy a wrong that he himself had caused.</t5003> <t5004>If he had another motive, I am sure it was not dishonorable...</t5004> <t5005>He had spent several days in the capital without being able to find them, but he had a lead that could guide him and that was more important than all of ours and that, moreover, was another reason that prompted him to come see us.</t5005>
<t5006>It seems that there is a lady, a certain Mrs. Younge, who some time ago was Miss Darcy's governess, and had to be dismissed from her position for some reprehensible reason that he did not tell us.</t5006> <t5007>Upon leaving the Darcy family, Mrs. Younge took a large house on Edwards Street and since then made a living by renting out rooms.</t5007> <t5008>Darcy knew that this Mrs. Younge had close ties with Wickham, and he went to her for news of him as soon as he arrived in the capital.</t5008> <t5009>But two or three days went by without being able to obtain from that lady what he needed.</t5009> <t5010>I suppose she did not want to speak until she was bribed, for, in reality, she knew from the beginning where her friend was.</t5010> <t5011>Wickham, in fact, went to her upon his arrival in London, and if there had been room in her house, he would have stayed there.</t5011> <t5012>But, finally, our good friend obtained the address he was looking for.</t5012> <t5013>They were on X Street. He saw Wickham and then wanted to see Lydia.</t5013> <t5014>He confessed to us that his first intention was to convince her to leave that unfortunate situation and return to her family's bosom if it could be arranged for them to receive her, and he offered his help in everything within his reach.</t5014> <t5015>But he found Lydia absolutely determined to stay as she was.</t5015> <t5016>She did not care a bit about her family and rejected Darcy's help; she did not want to hear about leaving Wickham; she was convinced that they would marry someday and did not care when.</t5016> <t5017>In view of this, Darcy thought that the only thing to do was to facilitate and secure the marriage; in his first dialogue with Wickham, he saw that marriage was not in his plans.</t5017> <t5018>Wickham confessed that he had been forced to leave the regiment due to certain pressing debts of honor; he had no scruples in blaming Lydia's madness for all the unfortunate consequences of the elopement.</t5018> <t5019>He said he would immediately resign from his position, and as for the future, he did not know what would become of him; he had to go somewhere, but he did not know where and admitted that he did not have a penny to his name.</t5019>
<t5020>Mr. Darcy asked him why he had not married your sister on the spot.</t5020> <t5021>Although Mr. Bennet might not be very wealthy, he could do something for him and his situation would improve with the marriage.</t5021> <t5022>But from Wickham's response, Darcy understood that he still harbored the hope of securing a more solid fortune by marrying another girl in some other country; nevertheless, given the circumstances he was in, he did not seem very averse to the temptation of obtaining an immediate solution.</t5022>
<t5023>They met repeatedly because there were many things to discuss.</t5023> <t5024>Wickham, of course, needed much more than could be given to him, but in the end, he agreed to be reasonable.</t5024>
<t5025>When everything was agreed upon between them, the first thing Mr. Darcy did was inform your uncle, for which he came to Gracechurch Street for the first time, the afternoon before my arrival.</t5025> <t5026>But he could not see Gardiner.</t5026> <t5027>Darcy found out that your father was still at our house, but that he was going to leave the next day.</t5027> <t5028>He did not believe that your father was a more suitable person than your uncle to handle the matter, and then postponed his visit until your father had left.</t5028> <t5029>He did not leave his name, and the next day we only knew that a gentleman had come on a business matter.</t5029>
<t5030>He returned on Saturday.</t5030> <t5031>Your father had left and your uncle was at home.</t5031> <t5032>As I said before, the two of them talked for a long time.</t5032>
<t5033>On Sunday they met again and then I saw him too.</t5033> <t5034>It wasn't until Monday that everything was decided, and then it was when the messenger was sent to Longbourn.</t5034> <t5035>But our visitor was very stubborn; I assure you, Elizabeth, that stubbornness is the true flaw in his character.</t5035> <t5036>He has been accused of many faults on various occasions, but that is the only true one.</t5036> <t5037>He wanted to do everything on his own, despite the fact that your uncle ––and I don't say this for you to thank me, so I beg you not to speak of it–– would have arranged everything instantly.</t5037>

<t5042>I imagine you know what has been done for those young people.</t5042> <t5043>The debts of Wickham, which amount, I believe, to much more than a thousand pounds, have been paid; another thousand has been set aside to increase Lydia's dowry, and he has been secured a job.</t5043> <t5044>According to Darcy, the reasons for doing all this are solely the ones I mentioned before: because of his discretion, it was not known who Wickham was, and he was received and regarded in a way he did not deserve.</t5044> <t5045>There may be some truth in this, although I do not doubt that neither Darcy's discretion nor anyone else's has anything to do with the matter.</t5045> <t5046>But despite his nice words, my dear Elizabeth, you can be sure that your uncle would never have yielded had he not believed himself moved by another interest.</t5046>
<t5047>When everything was resolved, Mr. Darcy returned to his friends who were still at Pemberley, but he promised to return to London for the wedding and to settle the financial arrangements.</t5047>

<t5054>Mr. Darcy returned punctually and, as Lydia told you, attended the wedding.</t5054> <t5055>He dined with us the next day.</t5055> <t5056>He was planning to leave London on Wednesday or Thursday.</t5056> <t5057>Will you be angry with me, dear Lizzy, if I take this opportunity to tell you what I never would have dared to say before, which is how much I like Darcy?</t5057> <t5058>His conduct with us has been as pleasant in every way as when we were in Derbyshire.</t5058> <t5059>His intelligence, his opinions, everything pleases me.</t5059> <t5060>He only lacks a bit of liveliness, and if he marries wisely, his wife will teach him that.</t5060> <t5061>It seems to me that he conceals it very well; he hardly mentioned your name.</t5061> <t5062>But it seems that concealment is in fashion.</t5062>
<t5063>I beg you to forgive me if I have been too suspicious, or at least do not punish me to the point of excluding me from Pemberley.</t5063> <t5064>I will not be completely happy until I have taken a full tour of the estate.</t5064> <t5065>A low phaeton with a good pair of ponies would be ideal.</t5065>
<t5066>I can't write to you anymore.</t5066> <t5067>The children have been calling me for half an hour.</t5067>
<t5068>Yours affectionately,</t5068>
<t5069>M. Gardiner</t5069>
<t5070>The content of this letter left Elizabeth in a state of shock where it was impossible to determine whether pleasure or pain took the greater part.</t5070><t5071>The vague suspicions she had conceived in her uncertainty about Darcy's role in her sister's wedding, without daring to entertain them because they implied acts of kindness too great to be possible, and fearing they were true because of the humiliation that gratitude would impose, were thus confirmed.</t5071><t5072>Darcy had gone after them expressly, had assumed all the trouble and mortification inherent in that search, implored a woman he must detest and was forced to deal with frequently, to persuade and ultimately bribe the man he most wished to avoid and whose very name horrified him to pronounce.</t5072><t5073>He had done it all to save a girl who must mean nothing to him and for whom he could feel no esteem.</t5073><t5074>Elizabeth's heart told her he had done it for her, but other considerations repressed this hope and she soon realized she was flattering her vanity by explaining the fact in this way, for Darcy could not feel any affection for a woman who had rejected him and, if he did, he would not be able to overcome such a natural feeling as that of being related to Wickham.</t5074><t5075>Darcy, Wickham's brother-in-law!</t5075><t5076>The most basic pride had to rebel against that bond.</t5076><t5077>It was true that Darcy had done so much that Elizabeth was confused, but he gave a very plausible reason.</t5077><t5078>It was not unreasonable to think that Darcy believed he had acted wrongly; <t5085>She rose from her seat and came out of her meditation upon noticing someone approaching; and before she could reach another path, Wickham accosted her.</t5085>

<t5150>CHAPTER LIII</t5150>
<t5151>Wickham was so chastened by that conversation that he never again exposed himself, nor provoked his dear sister Elizabeth to resume it.</t5151> <t5152>And she was glad to have said enough to ensure that he would not mention the subject again.</t5152>

<t5174>But the discouragement that this event caused him was quickly alleviated, and his mind began to function again with great agitation at the series of news circulating at that time.</t5174> <t5175>The housekeeper of Netherfield had received orders to prepare for the arrival of her master, which was to take place within two or three days, to engage in hunting for a few weeks.</t5175> <t5176>Mrs. Bennet was extremely nervous.</t5176> <t5177>She looked at Jane and alternately smiled and shook her head.</t5177>

<t5198>Elizabeth did not know what to think.</t5198> <t5199>If she had not seen him in Derbyshire, she might have believed that he came only for the mentioned reason, but she did not doubt that he still loved Jane, and she even ventured to think that he came with his friend's approval or that he had dared to come without it.</t5199>

<t5253>"First, I will see how he behaves," he said to himself, "and then God will decide if I can have hopes."</t5253>

<t5297>Elizabeth's discomfort increased with such unnecessary and officious attention.</t5297> <t5298>She had no doubt that all those illusions that were reborn after a year would end again in the same way.</t5298> <t5299>She thought that entire years of happiness could not compensate her and Jane for those moments of painful confusion.</t5299>

<t5303>But that misfortune, which years of happiness could not compensate for, was somewhat alleviated shortly after by noticing that her sister's beauty once again awakened the admiration of her former suitor.</t5303> <t5304>At first, Bingley spoke very little with Jane, but with each moment he seemed more captivated by her.</t5304> <t5305>He found her as beautiful as the previous year, as sensitive and as affable, though not as talkative.</t5305> <t5306>Jane wished that no change would be noticed in her and believed she spoke as always, but her mind was so occupied that sometimes she did not realize her silence.</t5306>
<t5307>When the gentlemen rose to leave, Mrs. Bennet did not forget her planned invitation.</t5307> <t5308>The two young men accepted, and it was agreed that they would dine at Longbourn in a few days.</t5308>
<t5309>––You owed me a visit, Mr. Bingley,–– added Mrs. Bennet––, for when you went to the capital last winter, you promised to dine with us as soon as you returned.</t5309> <t5310>You see, I have not forgotten.</t5310> <t5311>I was very upset that you did not come back to fulfill your commitment.</t5311>

<t5314>Mrs. Bennet had been on the verge of inviting them to dine that very day, but despite the fact that one always ate well in her house, she did not believe that two courses would in any way be sufficient for a man who inspired such ambitious projects in her, nor to satisfy the appetite and pride of another who had ten thousand pounds a year in income.</t5314>
<t5315>CHAPTER LIV</t5315>
<t5316>As soon as they left, Elizabeth went for a walk to recover her spirits or, rather, to ponder the cause that had made her lose them.</t5316> <t5317>Darcy's behavior had her astonished and angry.</t5317> <t5318>Why did he come ––she asked herself–– to be silent, serious, and indifferent?</t5318>

<t5337>Elizabeth, with triumphant satisfaction, looked at Darcy.</t5337> <t5338>He met her gaze with noble indifference; Elizabeth would have imagined that Bingley had already obtained his friend's permission to enjoy his happiness if she had not caught his eyes also turned towards Darcy, with a smiling but alarmed expression.</t5338>

<t5347>"If he doesn't come towards me," she said to herself, "I will give up."</t5347>
<t5348>The gentlemen entered, and it seemed that Darcy was going to do what she longed for; but unfortunately, the ladies had gathered around the table where Mrs. Bennet was preparing tea and Elizabeth was serving coffee. <t5397>CHAPTER LV</t5397>
<t5398>A few days after that visit, Bingley returned to Longbourn, alone.</t5398> <t5399>His friend had gone to London in the morning, but was going to return within ten days.</t5399> <t5400>He spent an hour with them and was in excellent spirits.</t5400> <t5401>Mrs. Bennet invited him to dinner, Bingley said he was sorry, but he was already engaged elsewhere.</t5401>

<t5441>Mrs. Bennet's plans did not come to fruition that day.</t5441> <t5442>Bingley was a model of kindness, but not the declared suitor of her daughter.</t5442> <t5443>His ease and cheerfulness greatly contributed to the liveliness of the evening gathering; he endured all the mother's indiscretions and impertinences and listened to all her foolish warnings with a patience and serenity that left Jane very pleased.</t5443>

<t5456>Jane could not keep secrets from Elizabeth, especially not a piece of news that she knew would make her happy.</t5456> <t5457>She hugged her tightly and confessed with the most vivid emotion that she was the happiest woman in the world.</t5457>
<t5458>––It's too much! ––he added.</t5458> <t5459>It's too much!</t5459> <t5460>I don't deserve it.</t5460> <t5461>Oh! Why can't everyone be as happy as I am?</t5461>

<t5471>He went hurriedly in search of his mother who had deliberately suspended the card game and was upstairs with Catherine.</t5471>

<t5537>The state of the affairs of the Longbourn family could not remain a secret.</t5537> <t5538>Mrs. Bennet had the privilege of communicating it to Mrs. Philips, who then proceeded to broadcast it without prior permission to the houses of all the neighbors in Meryton.</t5538>
<t5539>The Bennets were soon proclaimed the luckiest family in the world, despite the fact that a few weeks earlier, on the occasion of Lydia's elopement, they had been regarded as the most unfortunate people on earth.</t5539>
<t5540>CHAPTER LVI</t5540>
<t5541>One morning, approximately a week after Bingley's declaration, while he was gathered in the sitting room with the ladies of Longbourn, they were drawn by the noise of a carriage and looked out the window, spotting a landau with four horses crossing the lawn in front of the house.</t5541> <t5542>It was too early for visits and besides, the carriage's team did not belong to any of the neighbors; the horses were post horses and neither the carriage nor the livery of the footmen were familiar to them.</t5542> <t5543>But it was evident that someone was coming to the house.</t5543> <t5544>Bingley proposed to Jane that they go for a walk in the shrubbery to avoid being separated by the intruder.</t5544> <t5545>The two of them left, and the three who stayed in the dining room continued their conjectures, though with little satisfaction, until the door opened and the visitor entered.</t5545> <t5546>It was Lady Catherine de Bourgh.</t5546>
<t5547>It is true that everyone was expecting some surprise, but this one exceeded all expectations.</t5547> <t5548>Although Mrs. Bennet and Catherine did not know that lady, they were no less astonished than Elizabeth.</t5548>
<t5549>He entered the room with an even more unpleasant air than usual; he responded to Elizabeth's greeting with a simple nod of his head and sat down without saying a word.</t5549> <t5550>Elizabeth had mentioned his name to Mrs. Bennet when His Lordship entered, although she had not requested any introduction.</t5550>
<t5551>Mrs. Bennet, astonished yet very proud to have a person of such high rank in her home, received her with the utmost courtesy.</t5551> <t5552>They all sat in silence for a while, until at last Lady Catherine said to Elizabeth with great pomp:</t5552>
<t5553>––I suppose you are well, and I assume that lady is your mother.</t5553>
<t5554>Elizabeth answered concisely in the affirmative.</t5554>
<t5555>––And that other one I imagine must be one of his sisters.</t5555>
<t5556>––Yes, ma'am ––Mrs. Bennet responded, very proud to be able to speak with Lady Catherine––.</t5556> <t5557>She is the second youngest; the youngest of all got married recently, and the eldest is in the garden walking with a gentleman who I believe will soon become part of our family.</t5557>
<t5558>––You have a very small farm ––said His Lordship after a short silence.</t5558>
<t5559>––It is nothing compared to Rosings, madam; one must admit it; but I assure you it is much better than Sir William Lucas's.</t5559>
<t5560>––This must be a very uncomfortable room in the summer afternoons; the windows face completely west.</t5560>

<t5563>––Very well; I saw them the night before last.</t5563> <t5564>Elizabeth hoped that she would now give her some letter from Charlotte, as this seemed the only likely reason for her visit; but Lady Catherine did not take out any letter, and Elizabeth remained perplexed.</t5564>
<t5565>Mrs. Bennet delicately entreated Her Ladyship to take something, but Lady Catherine firmly declined the offer without excessive politeness.</t5565> <t5566>Then, rising, she said to Elizabeth:</t5566>
<t5567>––Miss Bennet, it seems to me that over there, beside the meadow, there is a beautiful and secluded spot.</t5567> <t5568>I would like to take a walk there if you would do me the honor of accompanying me.</t5568>
<t5569>––Come on, dear ––exclaimed the mother––, show His Lordship all the walks.</t5569> <t5570>I think he will like the hermitage.</t5570>
<t5571>Elizabeth obeyed, ran to her room to fetch her umbrella, and waited downstairs for her noble visitor.</t5571> <t5572>As she passed through the hall, Lady Catherine opened the doors to the dining room and the drawing room, and after a brief inspection, declared that they were decent rooms, after which she continued walking.</t5572>
<t5573>The carriage was still at the door, and Elizabeth saw that Her Ladyship's maid was in it.</t5573> <t5574>They walked silently along the gravel path that led to the stables.</t5574> <t5575>Elizabeth was determined not to engage in conversation with that lady who seemed even more insolent and unpleasant than usual.</t5575>
<t5576>How could I ever say that she looked like her nephew?, he said to himself as he looked at her face.</t5576>
<t5577>When they entered a thicket, Lady Catherine said the following to him:</t5577>
<t5578>––Surely you must know, Miss Bennet, the reason for my journey here.</t5578> <t5579>Your own heart and conscience must tell you the motive for my visit.</t5579> <t5580>Elizabeth regarded her with natural astonishment:</t5580>
<t5581>––You are mistaken, madam.</t5581> <t5582>In no way can I explain the honor of your presence.</t5582>
<t5583>––Miss Bennet ––replied His Lordship in an angry tone––, you must know that I do not like jokes; no matter how insincere you wish to be, I am not like that.</t5583> <t5584>My character has always been celebrated for its loyalty and frankness, and in a matter of such importance as the one that brings me here, I will deviate much less from my way of being.</t5584> <t5585>It has come to my attention that not only is your sister about to marry very advantageously, but that you, Miss Bennet, may possibly unite with my nephew Darcy afterwards.</t5585> <t5586>Even knowing that this is a dreadful falsehood and although I do not wish to insult my nephew by admitting that there might be some hint of truth in it, I decided at once to come and communicate my feelings to you.</t5586>

<t5589>––First of all, try to have that news corrected in all its parts.</t5589>

<t5591>––Has it circulated?</t5591> <t5592>Do you intend to ignore it?</t5592> <t5593>Haven't you yourselves taken the trouble to spread it?</t5593>
<t5594>––I have never heard anything like it.</t5594>

<t5605>––Miss Bennet, do you know who I am?</t5605> <t5606>I am not accustomed to that language.</t5606> <t5607>I am almost the closest relative my nephew has in the world, and I have reasons to know what his dearest interests are.</t5607>

<t5650>––By marrying your nephew, I would not believe I am stepping out of my sphere.</t5650> <t5651>He is a gentleman, and I am the daughter of another gentleman; therefore, we are equals.</t5651>
<t5652>––That's right; you are the daughter of a gentleman.</t5652> <t5653>But, who is your mother?</t5653> <t5654>Who are your uncles and aunts?</t5654> <t5655>Do you think I am unaware of your status?</t5655>

<t5657>Tell me once and for all, are you engaged to him?</t5657>
<t5658>Although merely out of the desire to be thanked by Lady Catherine, Elizabeth would not have answered her question; she could not help but say, after a moment of deliberation:</t5658>
<t5659>––I'm not.</t5659>
<t5660>Lady Catherine seemed pleased.</t5660>
<t5661>––And do you promise me never to make such a commitment?</t5661>
<t5662>––I will not make any such promise.</t5662> <t5663>Miss Bennet! <t5675>––Do not rush, please, I have not finished yet.</t5675> <t5676>To all the objections I have raised, I must add one more.</t5676> <t5677>I am not unaware of the details of the infamous abduction of your younger sister.</t5677> <t5678>I know everything.</t5678> <t5679>I know that the boy married her thanks to an arrangement made between his father and his uncle.</t5679> <t5680>And that woman is to be my nephew's sister?</t5680> <t5681>And her husband, the son of your father's former steward, is to become Darcy's brother?</t5681> <t5682>For heaven's sake!</t5682> <t5683>What do you think?</t5683> <t5684>Are the ancestors of Pemberley to be desecrated in this way?</t5684>
<t5685>––You have already said it all ––replied Elizabeth indignantly––.</t5685> <t5686>You have insulted me in every possible way.</t5686> <t5687>I beg you, let us return home.</t5687>
<t5688>And upon saying this, he stood up.</t5688> <t5689>Lady Catherine stood up as well and they returned.</t5689> <t5690>Her Ladyship was in a rage.</t5690>
<t5691>––So, you have no regard for the honor and reputation of my nephew?</t5691> <t5692>You insensitive and selfish creature!</t5692> <t5693>Don't you realize that if he marries you, he will be discredited in the eyes of everyone?</t5693>
<t5694>Lady Catherine, I have nothing more to say.</t5694> <t5695>You already know what I think.</t5695>
<t5696>––Are you, then, determined to achieve it?</t5696>
<t5697>––I have not said such a thing. I am only determined to act in the way that I believe is most convenient for my happiness without taking you or anyone who has so little to do with me into account.</t5697>
<t5698>––Very well.</t5698> <t5699>Then you refuse to please me.</t5699> <t5700>You refuse to obey the command of duty, honor, and gratitude.</t5700> <t5701>You are determined to humiliate my nephew in front of all his friends and make him the laughingstock of everyone.</t5701>
<t5702>––Neither duty, nor honor, nor gratitude ––replied Elizabeth––, can demand anything from me in the present circumstances.</t5702> <t5703>None of your principles would be violated by my marriage to Darcy.</t5703> <t5704>And as for the resentment of your family or the indignation of the world, if the former are enraged by my marriage to their nephew, I wouldn't care in the slightest; and the world would have enough good sense to join me in my disdain.</t5704>
<t5705>––And this is your attitude, your final resolution?</t5705> <t5706>Very well; I know what I have to do.</t5706> <t5707>Do not imagine that your ambition, Miss Bennet, will ever be satisfied.</t5707> <t5708>I came to test you.</t5708> <t5709>I hoped you would be a reasonable person.</t5709> <t5710>But rest assured that I will have my way.</t5710>
<t5711>Lady Catherine said all this until they reached the carriage door.</t5711> <t5712>Then she turned and said:</t5712>
<t5713>––I do not bid you farewell, Miss Bennet; I send no regards to your mother; you do not deserve such attention.</t5713> <t5714>You have gravely offended me.</t5714> <t5715>Elizabeth did not respond nor did she try to persuade Her Ladyship to come into the house.</t5715> <t5716>She left alone and slowly.</t5716> <t5717>As she was going up the stairs, she heard the carriage depart.</t5717> <t5718>Her mother, impatient, met her at the door of the dressing room to ask her why Lady Catherine had not come back to rest.</t5718>
<t5719>––He didn't want to ––said his daughter––.</t5719> <t5720>He has left.</t5720>
<t5721>––What a distinguished woman!</t5721> <t5722>And what courtesy on her part to come visit us!</t5722> <t5723>Because I suppose she has come to tell us that the Collinses are well.</t5723> <t5724>She must have been going somewhere and thought she could visit us while passing through Meryton.</t5724> <t5725>I suppose she didn't have anything particular to say to you, right, Lizzy?</t5725>
<t5726>Elizabeth was forced to tell a small lie, because uncovering the subject of her conversation was impossible.</t5726>
<t5727>CHAPTER LVII</t5727>
<t5728>Not without difficulty did Elizabeth manage to overcome the agitation caused by that extraordinary visit.</t5728> <t5729>She spent many hours unable to think of anything else.</t5729> <t5730>Apparently, Lady Catherine had taken the trouble to travel from Rosings to Hertfordshire with the sole purpose of breaking her supposed engagement with Darcy.</t5730> <t5731>Although Lady Catherine was very capable of such a project, Elizabeth could not imagine where she had gotten the news of said engagement, until she remembered that his being such a good friend of Bingley and she being Jane's sister could have given rise to the idea, since the marriage of one couple predisposed to suppose the marriage of the other.</t5731> <t5732>Elizabeth had indeed thought that her sister's marriage would bring her and Darcy closer together.</t5732> <t5733>That must have been why the Lucases—through whose correspondence with the Collinses Elizabeth presumed the conjecture had reached Lady Catherine's ears—considered immediate what she had also believed possible for later.</t5733>
<t5734>But upon reflecting on Lady Catherine's words, she could not avoid a certain uneasiness about the consequences that her interference might have.</t5734> <t5735>From what she said about her resolution to prevent the marriage, Elizabeth deduced that she intended to confront her nephew, and she did not know how Darcy would take the relation of the dangers that their union entailed.</t5735> <t5736>She was unaware of the extent of Darcy's affection for his aunt and how much he valued her opinion; but it was logical to suppose that he had more regard for Her Ladyship than she did, and she was sure that her aunt would touch a sore spot by enumerating the misfortunes of a marriage with a person from a family so unequal to his own.</t5736> <t5737>Given Darcy's ideas on that particular matter, Elizabeth believed it likely that the arguments that had seemed so weak and ridiculous to her might appear to him full of good sense and solid reasoning.</t5737>
<t5738>So if Darcy had hesitated before about what he had to do, which he often seemed to, the warnings and urgings of such a close relative might dispel all his doubts and incline him once and for all to be as happy as an immaculate dignity would allow him.</t5738> <t5739>In that case, Darcy would not return to Hertfordshire.</t5739> <t5740>Lady Catherine would see him as he passed through London, and the young man would rescind his commitment to Bingley to return to Netherfield.</t5740>
<t5741>"Therefore," Elizabeth said to herself, "if in a few days Bingley receives an excuse from Darcy for not coming, I will know what to expect.</t5741> <t5742>And then I will have to rid myself of all hope and illusion about his constancy.</t5742> <t5743>If he is content to lament my loss when he could have obtained my love and my hand, I too will soon cease to lament losing him."</t5743>
<t5744>The surprise of the rest of the family upon learning who the visitor had been was enormous; but they explained everything in the same way as Mrs. Bennet, and Elizabeth was spared having to mention her indignation.</t5744>
<t5745>The next morning, when coming down from his room, he met his father who was coming out of the library with a letter in his hand.</t5745>
<t5746>––Elizabeth ––he said to her––, I was looking for you.</t5746> <t5747>Come with me.</t5747>
<t5748>Elizabeth followed him and her curiosity to know what he had to communicate to her increased, thinking that it might be related to the previous day.</t5748> <t5749>Suddenly, it occurred to her that the letter could be from Lady Catherine, and she anticipated with dismay what it might be about.</t5749>
<t5750>He went with his father to the fireplace and they both sat down.</t5750> <t5751>Then Mr. Bennet said:</t5751>
<t5752>––I received a letter this morning that left me flabbergasted.</t5752> <t5753>Since it mainly concerns you, you should know its contents.</t5753> <t5754>Until now, I didn't know that you had two daughters about to get married.</t5754> <t5755>Allow me to congratulate you on such an achievement.</t5755>
<t5756>Elizabeth was left stunned, believing that the letter was from the nephew instead of the aunt; and she wavered between being glad that Darcy was finally explaining himself, and being offended that he had not addressed the letter to her, when her father continued:</t5756>
<t5757>––It seems like you can guess it.</t5757> <t5758>You girls have great intuition for these matters.</t5758> <t5759>But I think I can challenge your shrewdness by daring you to discover the name of your admirer.</t5759> <t5760>The letter is from Collins.</t5760>
<t5761>––From Collins! And what does he have to say? ––As expected, something very timely.</t5761> <t5762>He begins with congratulations on the upcoming wedding of my eldest daughter, of which he seems to have been informed by one of the kind and talkative Lucases.</t5762> <t5763>I won't bore you by reading what he says about that point.</t5763> <t5764>What concerns you is the following:</t5764>
<t5765>After having congratulated you on behalf of Mrs. Collins and myself for such a fortunate event, allow me to add a brief warning about another matter, of which we have been informed through the same channel.</t5765> <t5766>It is assumed that your daughter Elizabeth will not bear the name Bennet for much longer once her elder sister has left it, and that the partner she has been fortunate enough to secure can reasonably be considered one of our most illustrious personalities.</t5766>
<t5767>––Can you guess, Lizzy, what this means?</t5767>
<t5768>That young man possesses everything one could desire in this world: superb properties, a distinguished family, and an extensive patronage.</t5768> <t5769>But despite all those temptations, allow me to warn my cousin Elizabeth and yourself of the dangers you may face with a hasty acceptance of the proposals of such a gentleman, which, naturally, you might be inclined to consider advantageous.</t5769>
<t5770>––Do you have no idea who the gentleman is, Elizabeth?</t5770> <t5771>He's coming now.</t5771>
<t5772>The reasons I have for informing you are as follows: your aunt, Lady Catherine de Bourgh, does not look favorably upon this marriage.</t5772>
<t5773>––As you see, the gentleman in question is Mr. Darcy.</t5773> <t5774>I believe, Elizabeth, that you must be stunned.</t5774> <t5775>Neither Collins nor the Lucases could have chosen a name from our circle of friends that better reveals that what they are spreading is a falsehood.</t5775> <t5776>Mr. Darcy, who only looks at a woman to criticize her, and who probably has never looked at you in his life!</t5776> <t5777>It's phenomenal!</t5777>
<t5778>Elizabeth tried to joke with her father, but her effort resulted in nothing more than a very timid smile.</t5778> <t5779>Her father's mood had never taken a more unpleasant turn for her.</t5779>
<t5780>––Didn't it amuse you?</t5780>
<t5781>––Of course! Keep reading.</t5781>
<t5782>When last night I mentioned to His Lordship the possibility of that marriage, with his usual condescension he expressed his opinion on the matter.</t5782> <t5783>If it were true, Lady Catherine would never give her consent to what she considers a most imprudent union due to certain objections to my cousin's family.</t5783> <t5784>I felt it my duty to communicate this as soon as possible to my cousin, so that she and her noble admirer know what is happening and do not rush into a marriage that has not been duly authorized.</t5784>
<t5785>And Mr. Collins, moreover, added:</t5785>
<t5786>I sincerely rejoice that the matter of your daughter Lydia has been resolved so well, and I only regret that the news spread that they were living together before the marriage had taken place.</t5786> <t5787>I cannot forget what I owe to my position by refraining from expressing my astonishment upon learning that you received the young couple when they were married.</t5787> <t5788>That was encouraging vice; and if I had been the rector of Longbourn, I would have resolutely opposed it.</t5788> <t5789>It is true that you must forgive them as a Christian, but not admit them into your presence nor allow their names to be mentioned before you.</t5789>
<t5790>––This is his concept of Christian forgiveness!</t5790> <t5791>The rest of the letter refers only to the state of his dear Charlotte, and his hope of having an offspring.</t5791> <t5792>But, Elizabeth, it seems that you have not been amused.</t5792> <t5793>I suppose you wouldn't get angry and take offense at this foolishness.</t5793> <t5794>What do we live for if not to entertain our neighbors and laugh at them in turn?</t5794>
<t5795>––Yes, I had a lot of fun ––exclaimed Elizabeth––.</t5795> <t5796>But it is so strange!</t5796>
<t5797>––Well, that's what makes it funnier.</t5797> <t5798>If they had thought of another man, it wouldn't be anything special; but Darcy's absolute indifference and the deep grudge you hold against him is what makes the joke.</t5798> <t5799>As much as it bothers me to write, I cannot do without Collins' correspondence.</t5799> <t5800>The truth is that when I read one of his letters, he seems superior to Wickham, despite the fact that I consider my son-in-law the epitome of shamelessness and hypocrisy.</t5800> <t5801>And tell me, Eliza, how did Lady Catherine take it?</t5801> <t5802>Did she come to deny you her consent?</t5802>
<t5803>To this question, Elizabeth responded with a laugh, and since her father had asked it without the slightest suspicion, she didn't mind ––that he repeated it.</t5803> <t5804>Elizabeth had never found herself in a situation where she had to pretend that her feelings were something they were not in reality.</t5804> <t5805>But now she had to laugh when she would have rather cried.</t5805> <t5806>Her father had cruelly hurt her by saying that about Darcy's indifference, and she couldn't help but marvel at her father's lack of intuition, or fear that instead of him having seen too little, she had seen too much.</t5806>
<t5807>CHAPTER LVIII</t5807>
<t5808>A few days after Lady Catherine's visit, Bingley not only did not receive any letter of excuse from his friend, but he also brought him to Longbourn in person.</t5808> <t5809>The gentlemen arrived early, and before Mrs. Bennet had time to tell Darcy that his aunt had come to visit them, which Elizabeth feared for a moment, Bingley, who wanted to be alone with Jane, proposed that they all go for a walk.</t5809> <t5810>It was agreed upon, but Mrs. Bennet was not in the habit of walking and Mary could not afford to waste time.</t5810> <t5811>So the remaining five went out.</t5811> <t5812>Bingley and Jane soon let the others go ahead and stayed behind.</t5812> <t5813>Elizabeth, Darcy, and Catherine walked together, but spoke very little.</t5813> <t5814>Catherine was too afraid of Darcy to chat; Elizabeth was making a desperate decision in her mind, and perhaps Darcy was doing the same.</t5814>
<t5815>They headed towards the Lucas' house because Catherine wanted to see Maria, and since Elizabeth thought this might interest her, when Catherine left them, she boldly continued walking alone with Darcy.</t5815> <t5816>Then the moment came to put her decision into practice, and summoning her courage, she immediately said:</t5816>
<t5817>––Mr. Darcy, I am a very selfish creature who cares only for my own feelings, without thinking that I might hurt yours.</t5817> <t5818>But I can no longer go on without thanking you for your unparalleled kindness towards my poor sister.</t5818> <t5819>Since I found out, I have been longing to express my gratitude to you.</t5819> <t5820>If my family knew, they would have done so as well.</t5820>
<t5821>––I am very sorry ––replied Darcy in a tone of surprise and emotion–– that you have been informed of something that, if misinterpreted, could have caused you some unease.</t5821> <t5822>I did not think Mrs. Gardiner would be so indiscreet.</t5822>
<t5823>––Don't blame my aunt.</t5823> <t5824>Lydia's indiscretion was the first thing that revealed to me her involvement in the matter; and, naturally, I did not rest until I knew all the details.</t5824> <t5825>Let me thank you a thousand times, on behalf of my entire family, for the generous interest that led you to take so much trouble and endure so many mortifications to find the whereabouts of the two.</t5825>
<t5826>––If you wish to thank me ––replied Darcy––, do so only in your own name.</t5826> <t5827>I will not deny that the desire to ease your mind was added to the other reasons that drove me to do what I did; but your family owes me nothing.</t5827> <t5828>I have great respect for them, but I thought only of you.</t5828>
<t5829>Elizabeth was so confused that she couldn't speak.</t5829> <t5830>After a short pause, her companion added: "You are too generous to mock me."</t5830> <t5831>If your feelings are still the same as last April, tell me at once.</t5831> <t5832>My affection and desires have not changed, but with a single word from you, I will not insist any further.</t5832>

<t5880>––I cannot believe in that philosophy of yours.</t5880><t5881>Your memories must be so free from any reproach that the satisfaction they bring you does not come from philosophy, but from something better: the tranquility of conscience.</t5881><t5882>But with me, it is different: I am confronted by painful memories that cannot and should not be driven away.</t5882><t5883>I have been a selfish person all my life in practice, though not in principles.</t5883><t5884>As a child, I was taught to think well, but not to correct my temperament.</t5884><t5885>I was instilled with good norms, but they allowed me to follow them laden with pride and presumption.</t5885><t5886>Unfortunately, I was an only child for several years, and my parents, who were good in themselves, particularly my father, who was the personification of kindness and love, allowed me, indulged me, and almost guided me towards selfishness and authoritarianism, towards indifference to everything that was not my own family, towards disdain for the rest of the world or, at least, to believe that the intelligence and merits of others were far inferior to mine.</t5886><t5887>Thus, from the age of eight to twenty-eight, and it would still be so if it were not for you, dearest Elizabeth.</t5887><t5888>I owe you everything.</t5888><t5889>You gave me a lesson that was, indeed, very hard at first, but also very beneficial.</t5889><t5890>You humbled me as was fitting, you showed me how insufficient my pretensions were to flatter a woman who deserves all the flattery.</t5890>
<t5891>––Did you think I was going to accept it?</t5891>

<t5897>mistake.</t5897> <t5898>How she must have hated me after that afternoon!</t5898>

<t5909>Then he explained to her how delighted Georgiana had been to meet her and how much she regretted the sudden interruption of their friendship.</t5909> <t5910>This naturally led them to discuss the cause of said interruption, and Elizabeth learned that Darcy had decided to leave Derbyshire in search of Lydia before leaving the inn, and that his seriousness and pensive appearance were due to no other thoughts than those inherent to the aforementioned project.</t5910>
<t5911>Elizabeth thanked him again, but the matter was too overwhelming for both of them, and they did not pursue it further.</t5911>
<t5912>After walking several miles in complete abandonment and too busy to care about anything else, they looked at their watches and saw that it was time to go back home.</t5912>
<t5913>––What could have become of Bingley and Jane?</t5913>
<t5914>This exclamation led them to talk about each other's affairs.</t5914> <t5915>Darcy was very happy with his engagement, which Bingley had immediately informed him about.</t5915>

<t5946>CHAPTER LIX</t5946>
<t5947>Elizabeth, dear, where have you been walking?</t5947>
<t5948>This is the question that Jane directed to Elizabeth as soon as they were in their room, and the one that everyone else asked when they sat down at the table.</t5948> <t5949>Elizabeth replied that they had been wandering until they reached the end of the path she knew.</t5949> <t5950>As she said this, she blushed, but neither this nor anything else aroused the slightest suspicion about the truth.</t5950>
<t5951>The evening passed quietly without anything extraordinary happening.</t5951> <t5952>The official couples chatted and laughed, while the unofficial ones remained silent.</t5952> <t5953>Darcy's happiness never overflowed into joy; Elizabeth, agitated and confused, knew she was happy more than she felt it, for besides her immediate bewilderment, other things worried her.</t5953> <t5954>She foresaw the commotion that would arise in the family when they learned what had happened.</t5954> <t5955>She knew that no one in her household liked Darcy more than Jane did, and she even feared that neither his fortune nor his position would be enough to satisfy them.</t5955>
<t5956>At night, she opened her heart to Jane, and although Jane was not naturally suspicious, she could not believe what her sister was telling her:</t5956>

<t5972>Jane contemplated her sister with astonishment.</t5972> <t5973>Elizabeth reaffirmed with the utmost seriousness that what she was saying was true.</t5973>

<t5994>Jane once again asked for formality and Elizabeth then spoke solemnly, affirming that she adored Darcy.</t5994> <t5995>Jane was convinced and was entirely satisfied.</t5995>
<t5996>––Now I am completely happy ––he said––, because you are going to be as happy as I am.</t5996> <t5997>I have always had great esteem for Darcy.</t5997> <t5998>Even if it were only for his love for you, I would have to love him; but now that in addition to being Bingley's friend, he will be your husband, I will love only Bingley and you more than him.</t5998> <t5999>But how quiet and reserved you have been with me!</t5999> <t6000>Why didn't you tell me what happened at Pemberley and in Lambton?</t6000> <t6001>I had to learn everything from someone else and not from you.</t6001>
<t6002>Elizabeth explained to her the reasons for her secrecy.</t6002> <t6003>She had not wanted to mention Bingley, and the indecision of her own feelings made her avoid also mentioning the name of his friend.</t6003> <t6004>But now she did not want to hide Darcy's intervention in the matter of Lydia.</t6004> <t6005>Everything was clarified, and the two sisters spent half the night talking.</t6005>

<t6012>As soon as the two gentlemen entered, Bingley looked at Elizabeth expressively and shook her hand with such ardor that the young woman understood that he already knew everything.</t6012> <t6013>Shortly after, Bingley said:</t6013>

<t6024>During the walk, they decided that that very afternoon they would ask for the father's consent.</t6024> <t6025>Elizabeth reserved for herself the task of informing the mother.</t6025> <t6026>She could not imagine how she would take it; sometimes she doubted whether all of Darcy's wealth and lineage would be enough to counteract the hatred she harbored for him; but whether she opposed the marriage violently or approved of it with equal vehemence, there was no doubt that her outbursts would not be any indication of good sense, and for that reason, she could not bear for Darcy to witness either the first raptures of joy or the initial expressions of disapproval.</t6026>

<t6033>Elizabeth dashed out.</t6033>

<t6041>––In other words, you are determined to marry him.</t6041> <t6042>He is rich, that's true; you will be able to have better clothes and better cars than Jane.</t6042> <t6043>But will all that make you happy?</t6043>

<t6073>Then Mr. Bennet remembered how badly Elizabeth had felt while he was reading Collins' letter to her, and after joking with her for a while, he let her go and said as she was leaving the room:</t6073>
<t6074>––If any boy comes for Mary or Catherine, send him to me, as I am completely unoccupied.</t6074>
<t6075>Elizabeth felt as if a huge weight had been lifted off her shoulders, and after half an hour of quiet reflection in her room, she found herself ready to join the others, quite calm.</t6075> <t6076>Things were too recent to give in to joy, but the afternoon passed in the utmost serenity.</t6076> <t6077>She had nothing to fear, and the comfort of ease and familiarity would come in due time.</t6077>

<t6099>This was enough to prove that his approval was undeniable.</t6099> <t6100>Elizabeth, delighted that those effusions had been heard only by her, left immediately.</t6100> <t6101>But she had not been in her room for three minutes when her mother came in.</t6101>
