<t6108>This was a bad omen for what Mrs. Bennet's behavior towards the gentleman in question was going to be, and Elizabeth understood that despite having Darcy's ardent love and the consent of her entire family, something was still missing.</t6108> <t6109>But the next morning went better than she had thought, because, fortunately, her future son-in-law instilled such fear in Mrs. Bennet that she did not dare speak to him except when she could pay him some attention or agree with what he said.</t6109>
<t6110>Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing that her father was making an effort to become close with him, and he assured her, to top it off, that he liked him more each day.</t6110>
<t6111>CHAPTER LX</t6111>
<t6112>Elizabeth soon regained her cheerfulness, and she wanted Darcy to tell her how he had fallen in love with her:</t6112>
<t6113>––How did it all start? ––he said to her––.</t6113> <t6114>I understand that once you were on the path you kept going, but what was the first moment you liked me?</t6114>
<t6115>––I cannot pinpoint the hour, nor the place, nor the look, nor the words that laid the foundations of my love.</t6115> <t6116>It was quite some time ago.</t6116> <t6117>I was already half in love with you before I knew that I wanted you.</t6117>
<t6118>––Well, my beauty hardly moved you.</t6118> <t6119>And as for my manners with you, they bordered on rudeness.</t6119> <t6120>I never spoke to you except to annoy you.</t6120> <t6121>Be honest: did you admire me for my impertinence?</t6121>
<t6122>––For your vigor and your intelligence.</t6122>
<t6123>––You can call it impertinence, for it was little less than that.</t6123> <t6124>The truth is that you were fed up with courtesies, deferences, attentions.</t6124> <t6125>You were annoyed by women who spoke only to attract you.</t6125> <t6126>I irritated and interested you because I didn't seem like them.</t6126> <t6127>That's why, if you hadn't actually been so affable, you would have hated me; but despite the effort you took to hide it, your feelings were noble and just, and deep down you completely despised the people who so assiduously courted you.</t6127> <t6128>See how I have spared you the trouble of explaining it to me.</t6128> <t6129>And, the truth is, after all, I'm beginning to think that it's perfectly reasonable.</t6129> <t6130>I'm sure that now you don't find any merit in me, but no one notices that when they fall in love.</t6130>
<t6131>––Was there no merit in your affectionate behavior towards Jane when she fell ill at Netherfield?</t6131>
<t6132>––My dear Jane!</t6132> <t6133>Anyone would have done the same for her.</t6133> <t6134>But interpret it as a virtue, if you wish.</t6134> <t6135>My good qualities belong to you now, and you can exaggerate them as much as you like.</t6135> <t6136>On the other hand, it is my duty to find occasions to contradict you and argue with you as often as I can.</t6136> <t6137>So I am going to start right now.</t6137> <t6138>Why did you take so long to talk to me about your feelings again?</t6138> <t6139>Why were you so shy when you first came and then when you had dinner with us?</t6139> <t6140>Why, especially, while you were at home, did you behave as if I didn't matter to you at all?</t6140>
<t6141>––Because I saw you serious and silent, and you didn't encourage me.</t6141>
<t6142>––She was very violent.</t6142>
<t6143>––And me too.</t6143>
<t6144>––You could have talked to me more when you came to eat.</t6144>
<t6145>––If I had been less moved, I would have done it.</t6145>
<t6146>––What a pity that you always have a reasonable answer, and that I am also so reasonable to accept it!</t6146> <t6147>But if you had had to decide, we would still be waiting.</t6147> <t6148>When would you have told me something, if it is not I who starts?</t6148> <t6149>My decision to thank you for what you did for Lydia had a good effect; too much: I am scared; because how does morality stand if our happiness sprang from the breach of a promise?</t6149> <t6150>I should not have spoken about that, I will not do it again.</t6150>
<t6151>––Don't torment yourself.</t6151> <t6152>Morality will remain completely intact.</t6152> <t6153>Lady Catherine's unclassifiable behavior to separate us was what dispelled all my doubts.</t6153> <t6154>I do not owe my current happiness to your vehement desire to express your gratitude to me.</t6154> <t6155>I did not need you to tell me anything.</t6155> <t6156>My aunt's account had given me hope and I was determined to know everything at once.</t6156>
<t6157>––Lady Catherine has been, therefore, infinitely useful to us, something that should delight her as she loves being useful to everyone.</t6157> <t6158>But tell me, why did you return to Netherfield?</t6158> <t6159>Was it only to come to Longbourn to torment yourself, or did you think of achieving a more serious outcome?</t6159>
<t6160>––My true purpose was to see you and to find out if I could still hope that you loved me.</t6160> <t6161>What I was confessing or confessing to myself was to see if your sister still cared for Bingley, and, if so, to reiterate the confession I had already made to him once before.</t6161>
<t6162>––Will you have the courage to inform Lady Catherine of what awaits her?</t6162>
<t6163>––Maybe I'm more short on time than courage.</t6163> <t6164>Let's do it right now.</t6164> <t6165>If you give me a sheet of paper, I'll do it immediately.</t6165>
<t6166>––And if I didn't have to write another letter, I could sit by your side and admire the uniformity of your handwriting, as a certain young lady did on another occasion.</t6166> <t6167>But I have an aunt whom I don't want to leave forgotten for any longer.</t6167>
<t6168>For not wanting to confess that they had exaggerated their intimacy with Darcy, Elizabeth had not yet replied to Mrs. Gardiner's long letter.</t6168> <t6169>But now, being able to announce to them what would be so well received, she almost felt ashamed that her uncles had missed out on three days of enjoying that news.</t6169> <t6170>Her letter was as follows:</t6170>
<t6171>Dear Aunt,</t6171>
<t6172>I would have thanked you earlier, as was my duty, for your extensive, kind, and satisfactory description of the event you know about; but you will understand that I was too distressed to do so.</t6172> <t6173>Your suppositions went beyond reality.</t6173> <t6174>But now you can suppose whatever you please, you can let your imagination run wild, you can allow your fancy to fly freely, and you will not be more mistaken than if you imagine that I am already married.</t6174> <t6175>You must write to me soon and praise Darcy much more than you did in your last letter.</t6175> <t6176>I thank God a thousand times over for not having gone to the Lakes.</t6176> <t6177>What folly on my part to have desired it!</t6177> <t6178>Your idea of the ponies is magnificent; every day we will tour the estate.</t6178> <t6179>I am the happiest creature in the world.</t6179> <t6180>Perhaps others have said it before, but no one with as much justice.</t6180> <t6181>I am even happier than Jane.</t6181> <t6182>She only smiles.</t6182> <t6183>I laugh out loud.</t6183> <t6184>Darcy sends you all the love he can spare from me.</t6184> <t6185>You will all come to Pemberley for Christmas.</t6185>
<t6186>Darcy's letter to Lady Catherine was different.</t6186> <t6187>And even more different was the one Mr. Bennet sent to Mr. Collins in response to his last one:</t6187>
<t6188>Dear sir,</t6188>
<t6189>I have to trouble you once more with the matter of congratulations: Elizabeth will soon be Mr. Darcy's wife.</t6189> <t6190>Console Lady Catherine as best as you can; but if I were you, I would stick with the nephew.</t6190> <t6191>He has more to offer.</t6191>
<t6192>I greet you attentively.</t6192>
<t6193>The congratulations from Miss Bingley to her brother on the occasion of his upcoming wedding were very affectionate, but not sincere.</t6193> <t6194>She also wrote to Jane to express her joy and to repeat her previous expressions of affection.</t6194> <t6195>Jane was not deceived, but she felt moved, and although she did not trust her, she could not help but send a reply much kinder than she thought she deserved.</t6195> <t6196>The joy that Miss Darcy felt upon hearing the news was as genuine as her brother's when he communicated it to her.</t6196> <t6197>She sent a four-page letter that still seemed insufficient to express all her satisfaction and her strong desire to gain her sister's affection.</t6197>
<t6198>Before any response from Collins or congratulations from his wife to Elizabeth arrived, the Longbourn family learned that the Collinses were coming to the Lucas' house.</t6198> <t6199>The reason for such a sudden move was soon known.</t6199> <t6200>Lady Catherine had become so furious upon receiving her nephew's letter that Charlotte, who was genuinely happy about the wedding, wanted to leave until the storm subsided.</t6200> <t6201>The arrival of her friend at that moment was a great pleasure for Elizabeth; although during their meetings this pleasure diminished when she saw Darcy exposed to Collins' pompous courtesy.</t6201> <t6202>But Darcy endured it all with admirable serenity.</t6202> <t6203>He even attended to Sir William Lucas when he came to congratulate him on winning the brightest jewel of the county and expressed his hopes that they would all meet at St. James.</t6203> <t6204>Darcy shrugged, but only when Sir William could no longer see him.</t6204>
<t6205>The vulgarity of Mrs. Philips was another and perhaps the greatest of the contributions imposed on her patience, for although this lady, like her sister, had too much respect for her to speak to her with the familiarity that Bingley's good humor allowed, she could not open her mouth without saying something vulgar.</t6205> <t6206>Not even that respect, which earned her a bit, managed to give her any elegance.</t6206> <t6207>Elizabeth did everything she could to protect him from everyone and always tried to keep him close to her or to the members of her family whose conversation did not mortify him.</t6207> <t6208>The annoyances that all this brought took away a good part of the pleasures of the courtship, but added greater hopes for the future.</t6208> <t6209>Elizabeth thought with delight about the future, when they would be away from that society so unpleasant for both of them and enjoying the comfort and elegance of their family gatherings at Pemberley.</t6209>
<t6210>CHAPTER LXI</t6210>
<t6211>The day Mrs. Bennet parted with her two best daughters was of great bliss for all her maternal feelings.</t6211> <t6212>One can imagine with what delightful pride she later visited Mrs. Bingley and spoke of Mrs. Darcy.</t6212> <t6213>I would like to say, for the sake of her family, that the fulfillment of her most fervent desires in seeing so many of her daughters well married had the happy effect of turning her into a sensible, kind, and judicious woman for the rest of her life; but perhaps it was a blessing for her husband (who would not have been able to enjoy the happiness of home in such an unusual form) that she remained occasionally nervous and invariably foolish.</t6213>
<t6214>Mr. Bennet missed his Elizabeth greatly; his affection for her drew him out of the house more frequently than anything else could have.</t6214> <t6215>He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when they least expected him.</t6215>
<t6216>Bingley and Jane stayed in Netherfield for only a year.</t6216> <t6217>The proximity of her mother and the relatives from Meryton was not desirable even considering Bingley's easygoing nature and Jane's loving heart.</t6217> <t6218>Then the golden dream of Bingley's sisters came true; he bought an estate in a county near Derbyshire, and Jane and Elizabeth, to their utmost happiness, were only thirty miles away.</t6218>
<t6219>Catherine, solely for her material interest, spent most of her time with her two older sisters; and by frequenting a society far superior to what she had always known, she made remarkable progress.</t6219> <t6220>Her temperament was not as indomitable as Lydia's, and away from her influence, she became, thanks to proper attention and guidance, less irritable, less ignorant, and less insipid.</t6220> <t6221>As was natural, she was carefully kept away from the previous disadvantages of Lydia's company, and although Mrs. Wickham invited her many times to her house, with the promise of dances and suitors, her father never consented to her going.</t6221>
<t6222>Mary was the only one who stayed in the house and was forced to stick close to Mrs. Bennet, who did not know how to be alone.</t6222> <t6223>For this reason, she had to mingle more with the world, but she could still moralize about all the morning visits, and since she was no longer mortified by comparisons between her beauty and that of her sisters, her father suspected that she had accepted the change without displeasure.</t6223>
<t6224>As for Wickham and Lydia, their sisters' weddings left them just as they were.</t6224> <t6225>He philosophically accepted the conviction that Elizabeth would now know all his falsehoods and all his ingratitude that she had previously ignored; but nevertheless, he still harbored the hope that Darcy would influence his fortune.</t6225> <t6226>The congratulatory letter for her marriage that Elizabeth received from Lydia implied that such hope was cherished, if not by him, at least by his wife.</t6226> <t6227>It read as follows:</t6227>
<t6228>My dear Lizzy,</t6228>
<t6229>I wish you the greatest happiness.</t6229> <t6230>If you love Mr. Darcy half as much as I love my beloved Wickham, you will be very happy.</t6230> <t6231>It is a great comfort to think that you are so rich; and when you have nothing else to do, remember us.</t6231> <t6232>I am sure that Wickham would very much like a court appointment, and we will never have enough money to live there without some help.</t6232> <t6233>I mean a position of about three or four hundred pounds a year; but, in any case, do not mention this to Darcy if you do not think it appropriate.</t6233>
<t6234>And as it happened that Elizabeth found it very inconvenient, in her reply she tried to put an end to all such pleas and dreams.</t6234> <t6235>But she often sent all the help that her practice of what she called economy in her private expenses allowed.</t6235> <t6236>It was always evident that the income managed by people as extravagant as the two of them and so careless about the future, would be insufficient to sustain themselves.</t6236> <t6237>Every time they moved, either Jane or she would receive some plea for help to pay their bills.</t6237> <t6238>Their life, even after peace confined them to a home, was extremely hectic.</t6238> <t6239>They were always moving from one place to another in search of a cheaper house and always spending more than they could afford.</t6239> <t6240>Wickham's affection for Lydia soon turned into indifference; Lydia's lasted a bit longer, and despite her youth and demeanor, she retained all the rights to the reputation that her marriage had given her.</t6240>
<t6241>Although Darcy never received Wickham at Pemberley, he helped him progress in his career out of consideration for Elizabeth.</t6241> <t6242>Lydia paid them a visit now and then when her husband went to have fun in London or went to take baths.</t6242> <t6243>They often spent seasons with the Bingleys, to such an extent that they managed to ruin Bingley's good humor and he hinted that they should leave.</t6243>
<t6244>Miss Bingley was very resentful of Darcy's marriage, but as soon as she felt entitled to visit Pemberley, her resentment passed: she was crazier than ever about Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy as before, and so courteous to Elizabeth that she paid her overdue respects.</t6244>
<t6245>Georgiana then stayed to live at Pemberley and grew fond of her sister as much as Darcy had anticipated.</t6245> <t6246>The two loved each other tenderly.</t6246> <t6247>Georgiana had the highest regard for Elizabeth, although at first she was astonished and almost frightened to see how playful she was with her brother; she saw that man who had always inspired in her a respect that almost surpassed affection, become the object of frank jokes.</t6247> <t6248>Her understanding received insights she had never encountered before.</t6248> <t6249>Enlightened by Elizabeth, she began to understand that a woman can take liberties with her husband that a brother can never tolerate from a sister ten years younger than him.</t6249>
<t6250>Lady Catherine became furious about her nephew's wedding, and as she unleashed all her genuine frankness in her reply to the letter in which he informed her of his engagement, she used such immoderate language, especially when referring to Elizabeth, that their relations were interrupted for some time.</t6250> <t6251>But, in the end, convinced by Elizabeth, Darcy agreed to forgive the offense and sought reconciliation.</t6251> <t6252>His aunt still resisted a little, but she yielded either to her affection for him or her curiosity to see how his wife behaved, so she deigned to visit them at Pemberley, despite the desecration her woods had suffered not only by the presence of such a mistress but also by the visits of her uncles from London.</t6252>
<t6253>The Darcys were always in the most intimate relationship with the Gardiners.</t6253> <t6254>Darcy, like Elizabeth, truly cared for them; both felt the deepest gratitude towards the people who, by bringing Elizabeth to Derbyshire, had been the cause of their union.</t6254>
